Jerusalem Talmud Avodah Zarah



Chapter 1



Halakhah 1

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> Within three days before Gentile holidays one is forbidden to trade with them, to lend them and to borrow from them, to give them loans and to ask them for loans, to pay debts to them and to accept payment from them<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">It is forbidden to buy from an idolator or repay one’s debt because this provides him with money for his idolatrous celebration. It is forbidden to sell to him because he will buy things for his celebration. One may not lend him vessels or animals for he will use them for his celebration. One may not borrow from him because this will make him feel important.</i>. Rebbi Jehudah says, one may accept payment from them because this hurts him. They told him, even though it hurts him now he will enjoy it later<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">The feeling that he got rid of his debts will make his idolatrous celebration more happy.</i>. Rebbi Ismael says, three days before and three days afterwards are forbidden but the Sages say, before their holidays it is forbidden, after their holidays it is permitted.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “Within three days before Gentile holidays,” etc. Rebbi Ḥama bar Uqba understood all from here: <i>Bring your sacrifices in the morning, on the third day your tithes</i><sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Amos 4:4" href="/Amos.4.4"><i>Am</i>. 4:4</a>, speaking of the sinful service of the Golden Calf of Beth El.</i>. Rebbi Yose said to him, if it were so, even in the diasporas. But it was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 7b" href="/Avodah_Zarah.7b">Babli 7b</a>, slightly differently <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Avodah Zarah 1:1" href="/Tosefta_Avodah_Zarah.1.1">Tosephta 1:1</a>.</i>, Nahum the Mede said, in the diasporas one day is forbidden. What about this? There, they checked and found that they supplied their needs in one day and forbade them one day. But here<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">In Palestine. The expression גָּלִיּוֹת usually means Babylonia; the plural does not imply that any of these restrictions were followed in Egypt or other parts of the Roman Empire.</i> they checked and found that they cover their needs in three days and forbade them three days.
How does Rebbi Yose uphold this verse, <i>bring your sacrifices in the morning</i>, etc<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">Since for him the Mishnah has no biblical basis; the cessation of commerce with idolators either is rabbinic or an old popular custom. The entire sermon is reproduced in <i>Yalqut Šim`oni Prophets</i>§542.</i>? The verse speaks about Jeroboam’s kingdom. When Jeroboam became king over Israel he started seducing Israel and said to them come, let us worship pagan worship. Pagan worship is lenient. This is what is written, <i>let us go against Jehudah, cut it down, break it up, and appoint a king in it, the man from Ṭabeal</i><sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Is</i>. 7:6. The tradition that Ṭabeal is a place rather than a personal name was accepted by the Medieval commentators of Prophets. According to Rashi the name is coded; one has to replace letter nby <i>n</i>+/-11; then טבאל becomes רַמְלֵא, a city founded after the Arab conquest.</i>. Rebbi Abba said, we checked all of Scripture but did not find a place named Ṭabeal. But it treats its worshippers well. The Torah said, <i>selected him from all of the tribes of Israel to be a priest for Me</i><sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="I Samuel 2:28" href="/I_Samuel.2.28"><i>1S</i>. 2:28</a>.</i>. Pagan worship says, <i>he made priests from the fringes of the people</i><sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="I Kings 12:31" href="/I_Kings.12.31"><i>1K</i>. 12:31</a>; quoted in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Kiddushin 75b" href="/Kiddushin.75b">Babli, <i>Qiddušin</i>75b</a>, in the name of R. Joḥanan.</i>. Rebbi La said, from the thorns of the people, the rubbish of the people. The Torah said, <i>the fat of My sacrifice shall not remain until the morning</i><sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 23:28" href="/Exodus.23.28"><i>Ex</i>. 23:28</a>.</i>; but pagan worship said, <i>bring your sacrifices in the morning</i><sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Amos 4:4" href="/Amos.4.4"><i>Am</i>. 4:4</a>, speaking of the sinful service of the Golden Calf of Beth El.</i>. The Torah said, <i>it should be eaten on the day of its slaughter and the next day</i><sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 19:6" href="/Leviticus.19.6"><i>Lev</i>. 19:6</a>.</i>; but pagan worship said, <i>on the third day your tithes</i><sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Amos 4:4" href="/Amos.4.4"><i>Am</i>. 4:4</a>, speaking of the sinful service of the Golden Calf of Beth El.</i>. The Torah said, <i>do not sacrifice the blood of My sacrifice on leavened matter</i><sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 23:28" href="/Exodus.23.28"><i>Ex</i>. 23:28</a>.</i>; but pagan worship said, <i>and burn your thanksgiving offer of leavened matter</i><sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Amos 4:4" href="/Amos.4.4"><i>Am</i>. 4:4</a>, speaking of the sinful service of the Golden Calf of Beth El.</i>. The Torah said, <i>if you are vowing a vow to the Eternal, your God, do not tarry to fulfill it</i><sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 23:22" href="/Deuteronomy.23.22"><i>Deut</i>. 23:22</a>.</i>, but pagan worship said, <i>pledge gifts, publicize them</i><sup class="footnote-marker">3,</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Amos 4:4" href="/Amos.4.4"><i>Am</i>. 4:4</a>, speaking of the sinful service of the Golden Calf of Beth El.</i><sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">But it is not necessary to fulfill one’s pledge. The reason of the Northern Kingdom’s apostasy is traced to the cost and onerous rules of Torah practice.</i>. Rebbi Yudan, Rebbi Mattaniah’s father, said, the verse only serves to mention the shame of Israel. <i>The day of our king, the princes are sick from wine’s heat, he draws the mockers by his hand</i><sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Hosea 4:5" href="/Hosea.4.5"><i>Hos</i>. 4:5</a>.</i>. On the day when Jeroboam became king over Israel, all of Israel came to him late in the evening and told him, come and worship pagan worship. He told them, it is late in the evening; I am drunk and not drunk<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">He claimed that his mental faculties were slightly impaired.</i>. Everybody is drinking; but if you wish, go and come in the morning. That is what is written, <i>for their heart is like an oven while they are lying in ambush; all night long their baker is sleeping.</i><sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Hosea 4:6" href="/Hosea.4.6"><i>Hos</i>. 4:6</a>.</i> All night long their baker did not sleep. <i>In the morning he is burning like fire of a conflagration</i><sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Hosea 4:6" href="/Hosea.4.6"><i>Hos</i>. 4:6</a>.</i>. In the morning they came to him. He told them, I do understand what you want but I am afraid of your Synhedrion lest they kill me. They told him, we shall kill them; this is what is written, <i>that all are glowing like an oven and eat their judges</i><sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Hosea 4:7" href="/Hosea.4.7"><i>Hos.</i>4:7</a>. All of Talmudic literature assumes that the legal system imagined for late Hasmonean rule was that of the Davidic kingdom.</i>. Rebbi Levi said, they killed them, as is written, <i>if a corpse is found</i><sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 21:1" href="/Deuteronomy.21.1"><i>Deut</i>. 21:1</a>. This sermon derives הֶחֱלוּ in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Hosea 4:5" href="/Hosea.4.5"><i>Hos</i>. 4:5</a> not from חלה “to be sick” but from חלל “to be perforated”.</i>. Rebbi La said, they deposed them; <i>The day of our king, the princes are profaned from wine’s heat</i><sup class="footnote-marker">14,</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Hosea 4:5" href="/Hosea.4.5"><i>Hos</i>. 4:5</a>.</i><sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">Deriving הֶחֱלוּ in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Hosea 4:5" href="/Hosea.4.5"><i>Hos</i>. 4:5</a> from חול “to be profane”.</i>; the day when princes were profaned. <i>From wine’s heat</i>, they were addicted to wine. <i>He draws the mockers by his hand</i>: When he saw a serious person he placed two scoffers next to him who asked him, which generation was preferred over all generations? He told them, the generation of the Exodus. But did they not practice pagan worship? He answered them, because they were beloved they were not punished. But they told him, be quiet for the king wants to do the same. Not only that, but they made one and this one made two. <i>He put the one up at Bethel; the other he gave to Dan</i><sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="I Kings 12:29" href="/I_Kings.12.29">1K. 12:29.</a></i>.
The haughtiness of Jeroboam damned him absolutely<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">Jeroboam is the only person of those mentioned in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sanhedrin 11:5:5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sanhedrin.11.5.5">Mishnah <i>Sanhedrin</i>11:2</a> as having no part in the Future World who is not rehabilitated in the Yerushalmi Halakhah. The following argument is reproduced in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sanhedrin 101b" href="/Sanhedrin.101b">Babli <i>Sanhedrin</i>101b</a>.</i>. Rebbi Yose ben Jacob said, Jeroboam became king at the end of a Sabbatical year. This refers to what is written<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 31:11" href="/Deuteronomy.31.11"><i>Deut</i>. 31:11</a>.</i>, <i>at the end of seven years, at the time of the Sabbatical year, on the festival of Tabernacles, when all of Israel comes to appear before the Eternal, your God, at the place which He shall choose, you shall read this Torah in the presence of all of Israel to their ears.</i> He said, if I am asked to read<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">By tradition, on this occasion it is the king who has to read selected portions (Mishnah <i>Sotah</i>6:7). It is presumed that the same was true for the First Temple. There is no provision that two kings could read alternate portions, or if they did, the local king certainly would have precedence. The assertion of the book of Kings that there was a perpetual state of war between the Northern and the Southern kingdoms in the time of Jeroboam (<a class="refLink" data-ref="I Kings 15:6" href="/I_Kings.15.6"><i>1K</i>. 15:6</a>) is interpreted as a war of words conducted in the House of Study.<br>Another tradition (mentioned frequently but not undisputed) holds that nobody is allowed to sit in the Temple but a Davidic king (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 5:10:2-4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.5.10.2-4"><i>Pesaḥim</i>5:10</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yoma 3:2:2-7" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yoma.3.2.2-7"><i>Yoma</i>3:2</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 7:7:2-3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.7.7.2-3"><i>Soṭah</i>7:7</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sanhedrin 101b" href="/Sanhedrin.101b">Babli <i>Sanhedrin</i>101b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 40a" href="/Sotah.40a"><i>Soṭah</i>40a</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 41a" href="/Sotah.41a">41a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Yoma 25a" href="/Yoma.25a"><i>Yoma</i>25a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Kiddushin 78a" href="/Kiddushin.78a"><i>Qiddušin</i>78a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tamid 27a" href="/Tamid.27a"><i>Tamid</i>27a</a>). If Yeroboam would have to stand while Rehabeam was sitting, an impossible situation would be created.</i>, I have to stand. They will say, the local king has precedence. But if I am reading as second, it is a shame for me. If I do not read, it is a disgrace for me. And if I let them go<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">Letting the people go to Jerusalem while he remains at Sichem would emphasize the legitimacy of Rehabeam’s rule.</i>, they will ascend and desert me. They will go to Rehabeam, Solomon’s son. This is what is written<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="I Kings 12:26" href="/I_Kings.12.26"><i>1K</i>. 12:26</a>.</i>: <i>If this people would go up to sacrifice in the Temple in Jerusalem will this people’s heart return to their master, to Rehabeam</i>. What did he do? He made two golden calves and wrote on their hearts, “they will kill you.” He said, any king who will succeed me will look at them<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">The king would see the words engraved on the calves and understand that their purpose was to prevent people from observing the assembly in Jerusalem which would undermine his rule.</i>. Rebbi Ḥuna said, <i>companions of ingenious conjurations</i><sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Psalms 58:6" href="/Psalms.58.6"><i>Ps</i>. 58:6</a>.</i>. Anybody who joined with him he will make a coconspirator<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">Correctly reading the first חבר in the verse from the root meaning “to connect” but the second from the homonym meaning “to conjure, to practice sorcery.” Since all kings of Israel adopted his institution of official worship at Bethel as long as that place existed, they became his co-conspirators.</i>. Rebbi Ḥuna said, <i>a slaughtering place deepened by seducers</i><sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Hosea 5:2" href="/Hosea.5.2"><i>Hos</i>. 5:2</a>.</i>, for he deepened in crime. He said, anybody, I shall kill anybody who makes this public<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">The reason for the worship of the calves was never made public. Therefore, the sin was the king’s only.</i>.
Rebbi Abin bar Cahana said, we find that Jeroboam also invented Sabbaths and holidays. That is what is written<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="I Kings 12:32-33" href="/I_Kings.12.32-33"><i>1K</i>. 12:32–33</a>.</i>: <i>Jeroboam made the holiday in the eighth month, on the fifteenth of the month, like the holiday in Jehudah, and went on the altar; so did he at Bethel to sacrifice … in the month which he invented</i>. “In addition to” is written<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Qere</i> is מִלִּיבּוּ “by his invention”; the <i>Ketib</i> is מִלְּבַד “in addition” (or “except”). This is read to mean that in addition to the holiday month which he invented, he invented something else. By comparing the expression used in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 23:38" href="/Leviticus.23.38"><i>Lev</i>. 23:38</a>, the “else” is found to be the Sabbath, justifying R. Abin bar Cahana’s statement. (Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bamidbar Rabbah 21:23" href="/Bamidbar_Rabbah.21.23"><i>Num. r</i>. 21:23</a>.)</i>, as you say, <i>in addition to the Sabbaths of the Eternal</i>.
It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 6b" href="/Avodah_Zarah.6b">Babli 6b</a>. While trade is forbidden, the proceeds of an illegal trade are not.</i>: If one transgressed and traded with him, it is permitted. Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa, Rebbi Yose, in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: Even on his holiday<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">Disputed by the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 6b" href="/Avodah_Zarah.6b">Babli 6b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Avodah Zarah 1:3" href="/Tosefta_Avodah_Zarah.1.3">Tosephta 1:9</a>.</i>. It was stated so:<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">Quoted in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosafot on Avodah Zarah 2a:1:3" href="/Tosafot_on_Avodah_Zarah.2a.1.3">Tosaphot 2a, <i>s.v</i>. אסור</a>.</i> When was this said? About a Gentile whom he does not know. But with a Gentile of his acquaintance it is permitted since he is flattering him. It was stated: If one enters a town and finds them celebrating, he celebrates with them because he only is flattering them.
A <i>ducenarius</i><sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">Latin, “referring to 200”. If this describes an administrator, the reference is to an official such a prefect, procurator, receiving a salary of 200 <i>sestertia</i>(2’000 gold <i>denarii</i>), if a soldier, a commander of 200 men.</i> honored Rebbi Jehudah the Prince<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">The grandson of Rebbi.</i> with a plate full of <i>denarii</i>. He took one of them and sent the remainder to him. He asked Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish who said, he shall bring the value to the Dead Sea<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">“Bringing something to the Dead Sea” means destroying it, since the Dead Sea corrodes and destroys all metal thrown into it. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 6b" href="/Avodah_Zarah.6b">Babli 6b</a>.</i>. But was he not an acquaintance and it was in the past? Rebbi Abbahu said, did not Rabban Gamliel ben Rebbi asked me, may one go to a fair<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">This refers only to fairs which are in honor of some divinity. There is no limit to visits to government-organized fairs (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Avodah Zarah 1:3" href="/Tosefta_Avodah_Zarah.1.3">Tosephta 1:7</a>).</i>, and I forbade it to him, although it was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Avodah Zarah 1:3" href="/Tosefta_Avodah_Zarah.1.3">Tosephta 1:8</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 13a" href="/Avodah_Zarah.13a">Babli 13a</a>.</i>: “one goes to fairs and buys there male and female slaves.” Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish said, not only Jewish slaves<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">Circumcised slaves who were property of another Jew. This cannot refer to Jews sold as slaves since it is very meritorious to redeem Jews from slavery and does not need special permission.</i> but even Gentiles, because he brings them under the wings of the <i>Shekhina</i><sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">The Divine Presence. Since the slave becomes a semi-Jew, in the words of the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 13b" href="/Avodah_Zarah.13b">Babli (13b</a>) buying Gentile slaves “diminishes the number of idolators in the world.”</i>. What about this? Rabban Gamliel was a minor personality and Rebbi Abbahu desired to confine him<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">R. Abbahu did not want to abrogate the general permission for a meritorious intent to visit fairs dedicated to pagan divinities; he only thought that a public personality who probably would be unable to define the limits between per- mitted and forbidden intent should not give a bad example to the people.</i>. But Rebbi Judan the Prince was an important person and Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish wanted to limit the matter<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">While trade is permitted, accepting gifts distributed by a pagan on his holiday essentially is forbidden even though the gift should be accepted to improve intercommunal peace.</i>.
One understands “to lend;” “to borrow” from them? Because he increases his reputation. One understands “to give them loans;” “to ask them for loans”? Because he increases his reputation. One understands “to pay debts to them;” “to accept payment from them”? That he should not say, his pagan worship had helped him<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 6b" href="/Avodah_Zarah.6b">Babli 6b</a>.</i>. Rebbi Abba bar Tevelai in the name of Rav: If it was a loan in danger of being lost it is permitted. It was stated thus: A loan in danger of being lost, by witnesses. A loan not in danger of being lost, by document<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 102a" href="/Bava_Kamma.102a">Babli <i>Bava qamma</i>102a</a>.</i>. Even a loan by document is in danger of being lost, for not at all times does a person succeed in liquidating his debt<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">Quoted in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosafot on Avodah Zarah 2a:1:5" href="/Tosafot_on_Avodah_Zarah.2a.1.5">Tosaphot 2a, <i>s.v.</i> ולפרוע</a>.</i>. How is this? A loan not in danger of being lost, by pledge. A loan in danger of being lost, not by pledge. It was found following the former [opinion]: A loan in danger of being lost, by witnesses. A loan not in danger of being lost, by document.
There, we have stated<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah <i>Mo`ed qaṭan</i>1:5 (in the Mishnah editions, 1:7.) Babli <i>Mo`ed qaṭan</i>9b.</i>: “Rebbi Jehudah says, a woman should not apply lime because it disfigures her<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">On the semi-holidays which are the intermediate days of the Passover and Tabernacles holidays, one should not do what one can do before the holiday. Therefore one cannot get a haircut on the holidays and men cannot trim their beards. Rebbi Jehudah holds that a woman cannot get a beauty treatment in the form of a face mask of lime which will remove all facial hair and will make her cheeks more red by increased blood circulation. Since his name is attached to this prohibition while the others are stated anonymously it follows that the majority of the Sages hold that a woman can get such a treatment at any time.<br>It would seem that one should write נִיבּוּל as <i>pi`el</i> but since the Babli consistently has ניוול they must have heard the Galileans pronounce the word with a soft ב (which does not exclude that Galilean בּ also was pronounced <i>v</i>; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Berakhot 2:4:5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Berakhot.2.4.5"><i>Berakhot</i>2:4 Note 167</a>.)</i>.” Rebbi Ḥanina and Rebbi Mana. One said, they disagreed about lime which she removed on the holiday, but lime which she removes after the holiday is forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">Even for the Sages. While it is forbidden to marry on a holiday since the joy of holidays should not be mixed with the joy of marriage, a girl certainly can get engaged to be married on a holiday. The beauty treatment described was applied mainly to single girls to make them more marriageable. A treatment which terminates only after the holiday is a misuse of holiday time.</i>. But the other says, they disagreed about lime which she removes after the holiday. But lime which she removes during the holiday is permitted. We did not know who said what. From what R. Ḥanina, Rebbi Yose said in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan, Rebbi Jehudah is consistent. Just as Rebbi Jehudah said there, temporary disfiguration is disfiguration, so he says here<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">In our Mishnah where he holds that the temporary pain the Gentile feels when repaying his debt is sufficient to wave the prohibition of commerce.</i>, temporary pain is pain. This implies that they disagreed about lime which she removed on the holiday, but lime which she removes after the holiday is forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">This therefore is not only R. Ḥanina’s opinion but stated practice.</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">A similar text in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Avodah Zarah 1:2" href="/Tosefta_Avodah_Zarah.1.2">Tosephta 1:3</a>.</i> Jewish craftsmen working for a Gentile at that time, in his house it is forbidden, in their houses permitted. Rebbi Simeon ben Eleazar says, when has this been said? When it was cut from the ground, but as long as it is standing on the ground it is forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">The Jewish agricultural worker cannot harvest for the Gentile’s celebration of his holiday. The Tosephta text reads differently: The Jewish worker cannot work if he is paid by the day but the craftsman who is paid as contractor can do anything he wants in his own house. In a similar statement (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Shabbat 4a:1" href="/Shabbat.4a.1"><i>Šabbat</i>1, 4a</a> l. 58) R. Simeon ben Eleazar holds that a Gentile contractor can work on a Jew’s contract in his house on the Sabbath, but not in the Jew’s house nor if he is paid for his time on the Sabbath.</i>. In another town in any case it is permitted. What means “in any case it is permitted”? Whether cut or standing, whether hired or contracting. Rebbi La said, Whether cut or standing, but only in contracting. Rebbi Simeon ben Karsana in the name of Rebbi Aḥa, concerning Sabbath<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">The Jewish agricultural worker cannot harvest for the Gentile’s celebration of his holiday. The Tosephta text reads differently: The Jewish worker cannot work if he is paid by the day but the craftsman who is paid as contractor can do anything he wants in his own house. In a similar statement (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Shabbat 4a:1" href="/Shabbat.4a.1"><i>Šabbat</i>1, 4a</a> l. 58) R. Simeon ben Eleazar holds that a Gentile contractor can work on a Jew’s contract in his house on the Sabbath, but not in the Jew’s house nor if he is paid for his time on the Sabbath.</i>, mourning<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">There is no statement of R. Simeon ben Eleazar in the relevant Chapters of Yerushalmi <i>Mo`ed qaṭan</i>. According to Nachmanides (<i>Milḥamot Hashem ad Alfasi Mo`ed qaṭan</i>§1232) it refers to the <i>baraita</i> in the Babli <i>Mo`ed qaṭan</i>26b where R. Simeon ben Eleazar forbids invisible mending of garments torn at the news of the death of a close relative.</i>, and pagan worship practice follows Rebbi Simeon ben Eleazar.
The colleagues say, the reason of Rebbi Ismael is because of the holiday’s son<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">The day after the holiday which in Jewish custom is a day where mourning and fasting are forbidden. It is presumed that Gentiles follow a similar custom.</i>. Rebbi Abba said, because he knows that it is forbidden to you to trade with him, he reduces the enjoyment of his holiday<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">This assumes that Jews are dominant in trade. If the Gentile cannot replenish his stock after the holiday, he will use it sparingly and thereby refrain from any exuberant celebration.</i>. What is the difference between them? To sell them things which do not keep. In the opinion of the colleagues it is forbidden. In the opinion of Rebbi Abba it is allowed<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote">In the opinion of the colleagues all commerce is forbidden on the day following a holiday. In the opinion of R. Abba (in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 6b" href="/Avodah_Zarah.6b">Babli 6b</a> R. Simeon ben Laqish supported by a <i>baraita</i>) one may sell perishable items on that day since the Gentile could not have bought them before and their availability will not influence the Gentile’s behavior on his holiday.</i>. Rebbi Yudan said, a verse supports what the colleagues said. <i>On the twenty-fourth day of the <u>seventh</u> month the sons of Israel assembled in fasting, <u>and crying</u>, and in sackcloth, and dirt on them</i><sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Nehemiah 9:1" href="/Nehemiah.9.1"><i>Neh</i>. 9:1</a>. Words not appearing in MT are <u>underlined</u>.</i>. Why does it not say, on the twenty-third? Because of the holiday’s son. If you want to say because that was on a Sabbath, you cannot do this, because then the Great Fast would have been on a Sunday<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote">In the Seventh month, the first is New Year’s Day, the tenth is the Day of Atonement, and days 15–22 are Tabernacles. Nehemiah refrained from calling a fast day on the day after the holiday; this dates the observance of the “holiday’s son” at least to Nehemiah, unless it was impossible to call a fast day because the 23<sup class="footnote-marker">rd</sup> was a Sabbath. But then the 15<sup class="footnote-marker">th</sup> and the 1<sup class="footnote-marker">st</sup> would have been Fridays and the day of Atonement a Sunday. Since it is assumed that Nehemiah followed rabbinic rules one also assumes that as head of the rabbinic establishment of his time he would have manipulated the calendar to avoid this situation when one could not cook for a big meal before the fast (as the rabbinic calendar also is manipulated to avoid the fast on a Friday when one could not cook for breaking the fast.) (A similar argument is rejected by <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosafot on Rosh Hashanah 19b:5:1" href="/Tosafot_on_Rosh_Hashanah.19b.5.1">Tosafot in <i>Roš Haššanah</i>19b <i>s. v</i>. מימות</a>; one must assume that the author was ignorant of this paragraph in the Yerushalmi.)</i>. And what about this? Does not Rebbi Onias make light of him who would move it from its place<sup>61</sup><i class="footnote">An Amora of the first generation; in Halakhah 3:1 he is called R. Ḥanina from Hauran. He was a member of the committee of the Academy of Tiberias which fixed the calendar after Rebbi’s death and opposed all manipulations not justified by astronomical facts.</i>? Rebbi Joḥanan ben Madia said, I computed it and it was not on a Sabbath.

Halakhah 2

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> The following are the holidays of the Gentiles: Calendae<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote">The first day of the month; used here only for the first day of the year, the first of January.</i>, Saturnalia<sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote">The year-end licentious festivities in Rome.</i>, and Cratesis<sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote">Greek κράτησις, “might, power, accession to Empire”. Observed on the first of August, the day of capture of Alexandria by Augustus.</i>, and the king’s birthday<sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote">This is the Yerushalmi explanation. In the Babli, the day of accession.</i>, and birthday<sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote">Personal holidays of private persons.</i>, and the day of death<sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote">Of ancestors.</i>, the words of Rebbi Meïr. But the Sages say, any death for which there is burning<sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote">Burning the personal belongings of the deceased.</i> is idolatrous; if there is no burning it is not idolatrous.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “The following are the holidays of the Gentiles,” etc. <sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote">A reformulated parallel is in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Berakhot 8:6:10" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Berakhot.8.6.10"><i>Berakhot</i>8:7, Notes 157–160</a>.</i> Rav said <i>‘edehen</i> and Samuel said <i>`edehen</i>. He who said ‘<i>edehen,</i> <i>they are their witnesses</i><sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Isaiah 44:9" href="/Isaiah.44.9"><i>Is.</i>44:9</a>.</i>. He who said <i>`edehen,</i> <i>for the day of their misfortune is near</i><sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 32:35" href="/Deuteronomy.32.35"><i>Deut</i>. 32:35</a>.</i>. <i>Rav said me`abberim,</i> and Samuel said <i>me‘abberin</i><sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote">The technical term for intercalation in the calendar, adding a month.</i>. He who said <i>me`abberin</i>, one adds a limb to it. He who said <i>me‘abberin</i>, like a pregnant woman. Rav said <i>ye`utu</i>, and Samuel said, <i>ye‘utu</i><sup class="footnote-marker">73</sup><i class="footnote">The condition for a benediction over fire at the end of the Sabbath is that one have use of it.</i><i>.</i> He who said <i>ye`utu</i>, <i>but with this we shall be agreeable to you</i><sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Genesis 34:15" href="/Genesis.34.15"><i>Gen</i>. 34:15</a>.</i>. He who said <i>ye‘utu,</i> <i>to know, to inform the weary of wisdom</i><sup class="footnote-marker">75</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Isaiah 50:4" href="/Isaiah.50.4"><i>Is</i>. 50:4</a>.</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">76</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 8a" href="/Avodah_Zarah.8a">Babli 8a</a>.</i> Rav said, Adam, the first man, instituted Calendas. When he saw that the night was getting longer, he said, woe to me, maybe this is what is written for, <i>he shall smite your head, you will sneak to his heel</i><sup class="footnote-marker">77</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Genesis 3:15" href="/Genesis.3.15"><i>Gen.</i>3:15</a>.</i>, maybe it will come to bite me. <i>I said, but darkness will smite me</i><sup class="footnote-marker">78</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Psalms 139:11" href="/Psalms.139.11"><i>Ps.</i>139.11</a>. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Psalms 139" href="/Psalms.139">Psalm 139</a>, ascribed in the book of Psalms to David, is in aggadic tradition at least partially ascribed to Adam (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Psalms 139" href="/Psalms.139"><i>Midrash Tehillim</i>, <i>Ps</i>. 139</a>, the suthor’s <i>The Scholar’s Haggadah</i> p. 220).</i>. When he saw that days were getting longer, he said <i>calendas</i>, καλόν<i>dies</i><sup class="footnote-marker">79</sup><i class="footnote">“A beautiful day,” mixed Greek (καλόν “beautiful”, accusative) and Latin (<i>dies</i>“day”, nominative) perhaps to indicate the popular language of the unlettered.</i>. This follows him who said that the world was created in Tishre<sup class="footnote-marker">80</sup><i class="footnote">This is the opinion of R. Eliezer. R. Joshua holds that the world was created on the 15th of Nisan. Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Rosh Hashanah 27a" href="/Rosh_Hashanah.27a">Babli <i>Roš Haššanah</i>27a</a> and the author’s <i>Seder Olam</i>(Northvale NJ 1998) pp. 47–48 (Note 3).</i>. But following him who said that the world was created in Nisan, he would have known. Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun said, who thinks that the world was created in Tishre? Rav! As we have stated in the composition for <i>shofar</i> blowing from the House of Rav<sup class="footnote-marker">81</sup><i class="footnote">Rav’s composition for the extended <i>musaf</i> prayer on New Year’s Day. The sentence is found in the middle section.</i>: “This is the day of the beginning of Your works, a remembrance of the first day.” This implies that the world was created on New Year’s Day.
Rebbi Joḥanan does not say so, but the empire of Egypt and the empire of Rome were fighting with one another. They said, how long are we killing one another in this war<sup class="footnote-marker">82</sup><i class="footnote">Greek πόλεμος.</i>? Let us institute the rule that the empire should be preeminent which will tell its army commander, fall on your sword, and he will obey. The Egyptian did not obey. In Rome there was an old man by the name of Januarius who had twelve sons. They told him, if you obey us we shall make your sons dukes<sup class="footnote-marker">83</sup><i class="footnote">Latin <i>dux</i>, pl. <i>duces</i>.</i>, eparchoi<sup class="footnote-marker">84</sup><i class="footnote">Greek ἔπαρχος “governor, prefect.”</i>, and generals<sup class="footnote-marker">85</sup><i class="footnote">Greek στρατηλάτης.</i>. He listened to them. Therefore, it is called <i>Calendae Januarii</i>. The next day they mourn him, a black day<sup class="footnote-marker">86</sup><i class="footnote">Greek μέλαινα ἡμέρα, translation of Latin <i>dies ater</i>, a black (unlucky) day. <i>Dies postridiani</i>, following calendae, ides, or nonae, were considered inauspicious.</i>. Rebbi Yudan from Antodra<sup class="footnote-marker">87</sup><i class="footnote">Or Antondra, Antorda; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Berakhot 4:1:23" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Berakhot.4.1.23"><i>Berakhot</i>4:1 Note 72</a>.</i> said, if one sows lentils<sup class="footnote-marker">87a</sup><i class="footnote">A symbol of mourning, <i>Gen. rabba</i>63(19).</i> on that day, they will not succeed.
Rav said, Calendae are forbidden for everybody<sup class="footnote-marker">88</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 8a" href="/Avodah_Zarah.8a">Babli, 8a</a>, the opinions attributed here to the Babylonian Rav are quoted in the name of the Galilean R. Joshua ben Levi.</i>. Rebbi Joḥanan said, it is forbidden only for those worshipping on them. Saturnalia are forbidden for everybody<sup class="footnote-marker">89</sup><i class="footnote">This also is opinion of Rav/R. Joshua ben Levi.</i>. Rebbi Joḥanan said, both Calendae and Saturnalia are forbidden only for those worshipping on them. The colleagues asked: are wives of worshippers like worshippers? Rebbi Abbahu asked: is the parade<sup class="footnote-marker">90</sup><i class="footnote">This seems to have been a military parade which may have involved the display of the legion’s eagles, considered an idolatrous practice. The participation of Samaritans may have removed the pagan aspect.</i> of Caesarea like worship, since it is full of Samaritans? The parade of Duqim<sup class="footnote-marker">90a</sup><i class="footnote">An unidentified place.</i> is questionable. Rebbi Ze‘ira sent Rebbi Bevai that he should buy him a small web from the Saturnalia of Bet-Shean. He came to Rebbi Yose<sup class="footnote-marker">91</sup><i class="footnote">One has to read Yasa (Assi), the teacher of R. Ze‘ira, not Yose, his student’s student.</i>. He thought that he would instruct him following Rebbi Joshua ben Levi who permitted it. But he instructed him following Rebbi Joḥanan that it was forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">92</sup><i class="footnote">Clearly, one has to switch “permitted” and “forbidden” since the preceding (and <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Avodah Zarah 1:2:5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Avodah_Zarah.1.2.5">Note 88</a>) shows that R. Joshua ben Levi forbade what R. Joḥanan allowed.</i>. Rav said, Calends are eight days before the solstice, Saturnalia eight days after the solstice<sup class="footnote-marker">93</sup><i class="footnote">Here “before” and “after” have to be switched; the statement is correct in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 8a" href="/Avodah_Zarah.8a">Babli, 8a</a>. The Calends of January are 9 to 10 days after the winter solstice.</i>. Rebbi Joḥanan said, <i>prwqṭw</i> is the head of the turning point<sup class="footnote-marker">94</sup><i class="footnote">The dictionaries follow Musaphia in reading <i>tropic</i> for <i>procto,</i> meaning that when the sun is standing over the tropic this is the time of the solstice. It is difficult to accept a double metathesis, and the sun being over one of the tropics is not a beginning of the solstice; it is the solstice. Also תְּקוּפָה “turning point” denotes any one of the four cardinal points of the ecliptic, both solstices and both equinoxes. S. Krauss is correct in rejecting Musaphia’s explanation but his own, by a non-existing Greek word, is no better. Cf. Latin <i>procitat</i>“it drew near”, said e. g. of the orbits of two stars. The earth is nearest to the sun at the summer solstice (E. G.). One would vocalize פְּרוֹקִטוֹ.</i>.
Rebbi Abba in the name of Rav: There are three times<sup class="footnote-marker">95</sup><i class="footnote">Where one has to observe the Mishnah’s restrictions on commerce with Gentiles.</i> in Babylonia, three times in Media. Three times in Babylonia, מהורי וכנוני וכוונתה<sup class="footnote-marker">96</sup><i class="footnote">These days have not been identified except for מהירקנה which is <i>mehragan</i>, Arabic مهرجان, a fall festival. In <i>Responsen der Geonim</i>(Harkavy), Berlin 1887, No. 46 (p. 22) the Gaon describes this as the solstices, still celebrated as a holiday in his time. Also in מהורי one recognizes the root <i>mehr</i>“Mithra”, the sun-god. In כנוני one recognizes <i>kanun</i>, the Accadic and Syriac name of the Month of <i>Ṭevet</i>(or <i>Kislev</i> and <i>Ṭevet</i>) which leads one also to identify the day as connected to the winter solstice. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 11b" href="/Avodah_Zarah.11b">Babli (11b</a>) the readings are for Persian festivals מוטרדי וטוריסקי מוהרנקי ומוהרין, ms. readings מוטירדי וטירייסקי מוהרסנקי ומוהרין, מוטרדי ומורייסקי מוהרקני ומוחרנן, for the Babylonians מוהרנקי ואקניתי׳ בחנוני ועשר באדר, מוהרנקי ואקניתיה ,ואקניית׳ במני ואסר באדר בחנוני ועשרים באדר. In the ms. readings the first series is from the Munich ms. (<i>Diqduqe Soferim</i>), the second from the NY ms. published by Shraga Abramson (New York 1957). The readings do not help much but it is clear that on base of the next sentence in the Yerushalmi in the list of Babylonian festivals one must read with the NY ms. “20 Adar” as Median New Year’s Day. (In his Notes, p. 150a, Abramson incorrectly reads “10 Adar” with the other Babli sources.)</i>. Three in Media, <sup class="footnote-marker">96</sup><i class="footnote">These days have not been identified except for מהירקנה which is <i>mehragan</i>, Arabic مهرجان, a fall festival. In <i>Responsen der Geonim</i>(Harkavy), Berlin 1887, No. 46 (p. 22) the Gaon describes this as the solstices, still celebrated as a holiday in his time. Also in מהורי one recognizes the root <i>mehr</i>“Mithra”, the sun-god. In כנוני one recognizes <i>kanun</i>, the Accadic and Syriac name of the Month of <i>Ṭevet</i>(or <i>Kislev</i> and <i>Ṭevet</i>) which leads one also to identify the day as connected to the winter solstice. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 11b" href="/Avodah_Zarah.11b">Babli (11b</a>) the readings are for Persian festivals מוטרדי וטוריסקי מוהרנקי ומוהרין, ms. readings מוטירדי וטירייסקי מוהרסנקי ומוהרין, מוטרדי ומורייסקי מוהרקני ומוחרנן, for the Babylonians מוהרנקי ואקניתי׳ בחנוני ועשר באדר, מוהרנקי ואקניתיה ,ואקניית׳ במני ואסר באדר בחנוני ועשרים באדר. In the ms. readings the first series is from the Munich ms. (<i>Diqduqe Soferim</i>), the second from the NY ms. published by Shraga Abramson (New York 1957). The readings do not help much but it is clear that on base of the next sentence in the Yerushalmi in the list of Babylonian festivals one must read with the NY ms. “20 Adar” as Median New Year’s Day. (In his Notes, p. 150a, Abramson incorrectly reads “10 Adar” with the other Babli sources.)</i>נוסרדי ותירייסקי ומהירקנה. Rebbi Huna in the name of Rab Naḥman bar Jacob: New Year’s Day<sup class="footnote-marker">97</sup><i class="footnote">Persian نوروز. “Adar” here has the meaning of “March” as in the Syrian Christian calendar. Today’s Persian New Year is what is characterized here as Median.</i> is on the second of March in Persia, on the 20<sup>th</sup> of March in Media.
“Saturnalia,” “hidden hatred; hating, taking revenge, preserving [hatred].<sup class="footnote-marker">98</sup><i class="footnote">A proposed Hebrew etymology of “Saturnalia.”</i>” This is what you say, <i>Esau hated Jacob</i><sup class="footnote-marker">99</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Genesis 27:41" href="/Genesis.27.41"><i>Gen</i>. 27:41</a>.</i>. Rebbi Isaac ben Rebbi Eleazar said, in Rome they call it “senate of Esau<sup class="footnote-marker">100</sup><i class="footnote">In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bereishit Rabbah 66:7" href="/Bereishit_Rabbah.66.7"><i>Gen. rabba</i>66(7</a>) there is a different, more intelligible version: <i>Esau hated Jacob</i>; R. Eleazar ben R. Yose said, he was his steward (סַנְטֻרוי) but became hating, taking revenge, preserving [hatred]; for this one calls the Senators (סֶנָטֹרוי) of Rome. For סנטר <i>saltuarius</i> cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Metzia 5:5:9" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Metzia.5.5.9"><i>Bava mesi‘a</i>5:7 Note 138</a>.</i>.”
“Cratesis.” The day when Rome took hold of the Empire. Was this not stated already<sup class="footnote-marker">101</sup><i class="footnote">In the second interpretation of <i>Calendae.</i></i>? Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun said, for the second time<sup class="footnote-marker">102</sup><i class="footnote">When Augustus became Emperor to end the civil wars.</i>. Rebbi Levi said, on the day when Solomon married the daughter of Pharao Necho, the king of Egypt, Michael descended and put a stick into the Sea which attracted debris and formed a forest; that is the great fortification in Rome. On the day when Jeroboam put up two golden calves, Remus and Romulus built two sheds in Rome. On the day Eliahu disappeared a king was installed at Rome; <i>a king, there is no king appointed in Edom</i><sup class="footnote-marker">103</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="I Kings 22:48" href="/I_Kings.22.48"><i>1K</i>. 22:48</a>. The verse is quoted in incomplete form; in the biblical text נִצָּב is not a verb but a noun: <i>There was no king in Edom; a governor was king</i>. A shortened version is found in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sanhedrin 21b" href="/Sanhedrin.21b">Babli, <i>Sanhedrin</i>21b</a>.</i>.
“The king’s birthday.” <i>It was on the third day, Pharao’s birthday</i><sup class="footnote-marker">104</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Genesis 40:20" href="/Genesis.40.20"><i>Gen</i>. 40:20</a>.</i>.
“Birthday, and the day of death.” The preceding was for the public, from here on private. But it is written<sup class="footnote-marker">104a</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jeremiah 34:5" href="/Jeremiah.34.5"><i>Jer.</i>34:5</a>. Since God promises Sedekia that one will burn things at his funeral, the practice cannot be prohibited.</i>: <i>You shall die in peace, and what was burned for your earlier forefathers …</i> So is the Mishnah: “Any death where there is smoke and burning<sup class="footnote-marker">105</sup><i class="footnote">One burns not only the deceased’s utensils but adds some fuel which creates thick smoke. This is superstitious or idolatrous. The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 11a" href="/Avodah_Zarah.11a">Babli, 11a</a>, accepts burning as practice only for kings and Patriarchs, not for commoners.</i> is idolatrous; if there is not smoke and burning it is not idolatrous.

Halakhah 3

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> The day of the first shaving of his beard and his locks<sup class="footnote-marker">105a</sup><i class="footnote">The religious shaving of the first beard is known from Roman sources (Petronius, <i>Satyricon</i> §29-8, where a golden box with the shavings of the master’s first beard stands in a niche with the <i>lares</i> and a statue of Venus.) According to Ravad [Commentary to Sifra Ahare Pereq 13(9)] this is the sacrifice of locks that one grows for a year or two and then cuts as a religious rite.</i>, the day he arrived from a trip at sea<sup class="footnote-marker">106</sup><i class="footnote">Which always involved mortal danger.</i>, the day he was freed from jail, or a Gentile wedding feast for his son, are forbidden only on the day itself and for this man.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “The day of the first shaving of his beard,” etc. What means “only on the day itself”; maybe the same day every year? Did we not state, his haircut and his son’s haircut is<sup class="footnote-marker">107</sup><i class="footnote">The singular “it is forbidden” implies that this is a double festivity on the same day. Since it is quite impossible that a man and his son should shave their beards for the first time on the same day, it seems that the <i>baraita</i> implies that he celebrates the anniversary of his first shave every year and then shaves his son on that same date. The answer is that nothing in the <i>baraita</i> implies that one speaks of the first shaving of the beard; it could well be that the father lets his hair grow three years from the date of birth of his son and then cuts his and his son's hair on the same day in one pagan rite.</i> forbidden? Explain it that they had a haircut together. Did we not state, his wedding feast and his son’s wedding feast are forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">108</sup><i class="footnote">A son and his widowed father may well marry on the same day.</i>? Explain it that they together had a haircut and married women. Did we not state, his birth and his son’s birth is forbidden? Could you say that they were born together? Explain it that he did not do it on his day, but after the son was born he did it with him<sup class="footnote-marker">109</sup><i class="footnote">Here it seems more reasonable to assume that a father celebrates his birthday every year and his son has the same birthday. But as with the other statements, it is not necessarily so. It either may be that the <i>baraita</i> does not refer to the birthdays of the persons but to birth of children to both of them; nor does it imply that the birthdates are identical; only the celebrations are. The problem is undecided; for practical purposes only the day itself is forbidden, not its date every following year.</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">110</sup><i class="footnote">A thoroughly corrupt paragraph; partially but defectively copied from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Demai 4:3:6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Demai.4.3.6"><i>Demay</i>4:6, Notes 73–74</a>. The basic reference is Tosephta <i>Baba qamma</i>7:8 which declares creating a false impression to obtain goodwill as “stealing minds” and forbidden. The example in the Tosephta reads: “he should not open for him amphoras sold to a grocer,” i. e., one should not give the guest the impression that one goes to great expense for him when in fact the amphora had to be opened anyhow to fulfill an existing sales contract. The text here does not make much sense. The next sentence is also in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Chullin 94a" href="/Chullin.94a"><i>Demay</i>(Babli <i>Ḥulin</i>94a</a>) and makes perfect sense.</i> He should not send him amphoras sold to the grocer because that is like claiming an unnecessary claim. One should not invite another knowing that he will not accept. He should not offer many gifts of closeness knowing that he does not want [them]. What is “of closeness”? He trusts another corner<sup class="footnote-marker">111</sup><i class="footnote">In <i>Demay</i>: “He knows that the other person expects that he would exert himself for him.”</i>. And in Jerusalem he was switching his <i>‘ikhla</i> from right to left<sup class="footnote-marker">112</sup><i class="footnote">The word עִיכְלָא is otherwise unknown; cf. <i>Demay loc. cit.</i> Note 74. Compare Arabic عٍكال “cord (of a curtain)”.</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">113</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Demai 4:3:8-9" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Demai.4.3.8-9"><i>Demay</i>4:7, Notes 76–80</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Gittin (Lieberman) 3:13-14" href="/Tosefta_Gittin_(Lieberman).3.13-14">Tosephta <i>Giṭtin</i>3:13–14</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 61a" href="/Gittin.61a">Babli <i>Giṭtin</i>61a</a>.</i> It was stated: In a town where Gentiles and Jews live together, if the Gentiles contribute the overseers of charity collect from them and from Jews, and provide for Gentile and Jewish poor, visit Gentile and Jewish sick, console Gentile and Jewish mourners, and bury Gentile and Jewish dead. Also one takes in vessels from Gentiles and Jews for the sake of peaceful coexistence. The weavers asked Rebbi Immi, what about a Gentile wedding feast? He wanted to permit it to them from this: “for the sake of peaceful coexistence.” Rebbi Abba told him, did not Rebbi Ḥiyya state that a Gentile wedding feast is forbidden? Rebbi Immi said, if it had not been for Rebbi Abba we would have come to permit their pagan worship; praise be the Omnipresent Who distanced us from them.

Halakhah 4

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> Outside a town with pagan worship it is permitted<sup class="footnote-marker">114</sup><i class="footnote">In a village or small town, if there is a pagan festival, or a fair dedicated to a pagan deity, which is celebrated by everybody there, the restrictions of trade spelled out in Mishnah 1 do apply in the town but not in its surroundings.</i>. If the pagan worship was outside, inside is permitted. May one go there? If the road was dedicated to this place<sup class="footnote-marker">115</sup><i class="footnote">The place where trade with the Gentiles would be forbidden.</i> it is forbidden, but if one could go there also to another place it is permitted. If a town has pagan worship and there were stores decorated and not decorated; there was a case in Bet Shean and the Sages said that the decorated ones are forbidden, the not decorated ones are permitted<sup class="footnote-marker">116</sup><i class="footnote">To trade there since they do not participate in the festivities.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “Outside a town with pagan worship,” etc. Therefore inside it is forbidden. Because in it there is one statue all of it should be forbidden? Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish says, they taught this about a fair<sup class="footnote-marker">117</sup><i class="footnote">The fair is a once yearly affair in honor of some divinity and its temple receives a fee from every transaction at the fair. Trading at the fair is directly subsidizing pagan worship. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 11b" href="/Avodah_Zarah.11b">Babli 11b</a>.</i>. What is the difference between inside and the surroundings? Inside where it profits from the toll it is forbidden. Outside it is permitted because it does not profit from the toll, but if outside it also profits from the toll it is forbidden. But was it not stated<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Avodah Zarah 1:3" href="/Tosefta_Avodah_Zarah.1.3">Tosephta 1:8</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 13a" href="/Avodah_Zarah.13a">Babli 13a</a>.</i>: “One goes to fairs and buys there slaves, slave girls, and animals.” Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish said, not only Jewish slaves<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">Circumcised slaves who were property of another Jew. This cannot refer to Jews sold as slaves since it is very meritorious to redeem Jews from slavery and does not need special permission.</i> but even Gentiles, because he brings them under the wings of the <i>Shekhina</i><sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">The Divine Presence. Since the slave becomes a semi-Jew, in the words of the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 13b" href="/Avodah_Zarah.13b">Babli (13b</a>) buying Gentile slaves “diminishes the number of idolators in the world.”</i>. But was it not stated<sup class="footnote-marker">118</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 13a" href="/Avodah_Zarah.13a">Babli 13a</a>.</i>: If he bought there a garment, it should be burned, an animal should be castrated, coins should be brought to the Dead Sea? One understands that a garment should be burned, coins should be brought to the Dead Sea. An animal should be castrated<sup class="footnote-marker">119</sup><i class="footnote">The statement is unacceptable first because it contradicts the Tosephta and second because there is a biblical prohibition to castrate any animal (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 22:24" href="/Leviticus.22.24"><i>Lev</i>. 22:24</a>). The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 13a" href="/Avodah_Zarah.13a">Babli 13a</a> explains “castrating” as “making unfit for agricultural work” similar to the meaning of the root عقر “to starve an animal” in Arabic.</i>? But was it not stated<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Avodah Zarah 1:3" href="/Tosefta_Avodah_Zarah.1.3">Tosephta 1:8</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 13a" href="/Avodah_Zarah.13a">Babli 13a</a>.</i>: One goes to fairs and buys there slaves, slave girls, and animals.” Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish said, not only Jewish slaves<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">Circumcised slaves who were property of another Jew. This cannot refer to Jews sold as slaves since it is very meritorious to redeem Jews from slavery and does not need special permission.</i> but even Gentiles, because he brings them under the wings of Heaven? Explain it, Israel, Israel<sup class="footnote-marker">120</sup><i class="footnote">He bought from a Jew who was a vendor at the fair. This shows that only the strictly religious persons followed the rules on trade with Gentiles.</i>. But was it not stated: If somebody sells his slave to Gentiles the proceeds are forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">121</sup><i class="footnote">The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 45a" href="/Gittin.45a">Babli (<i>Giṭtin</i>45a</a>) requires the communal court to force the seller to buy the slave back for up to 10 times the sale price. It is forbidden to sell a circumcised slave, who has to keep the Sabbath and eat kosher, to a Gentile. On the other hand, a slave who refuses to be circumcised and become a semi-Jew may be sold to Gentiles without penalty.</i>. Again explain it, Israel, Israel. One buys from an Israel who goes to a fair because he is like one salvaging from their hands. On his return it is forbidden since he profits from pagan worship<sup class="footnote-marker">122</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 33a" href="/Avodah_Zarah.33a">Babli 33a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Avodah Zarah 1:3" href="/Tosefta_Avodah_Zarah.1.3">Tosephta 1:15</a>.</i>. But from Gentiles it is permitted whether they are going or coming. Rebbi Abba the son of Rebbi Ḥiyya bar Abba in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: If it was a hostelry<sup class="footnote-marker">123</sup><i class="footnote">Greek πανδοκεῖον, πανδόκιον. Since the hostelry is a place for strangers, even at the time of a fair it is exempt of the rules restricting participation of Jews.</i> it is permitted. Rebbi Ze‘ira asked: at a fair it is forbidden, in a hostelry it is permitted? Maybe Rebbi Joḥanan said this about merchandise<sup class="footnote-marker">124</sup><i class="footnote">Greek πραγματεία.</i>. Rebbi Abba the son of Rebbi Ḥiyya bar Abba came in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: If it was a hostelry, merchandise is permitted<sup class="footnote-marker">125</sup><i class="footnote">The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 13a" href="/Avodah_Zarah.13a">Babli 13a</a>, b explains that any kind of merchandise one may buy there if no fee is paid to the organizers of the fair.</i>, said Rebbi Joḥanan<sup class="footnote-marker">126</sup><i class="footnote">This clause is superfluous but this is not sufficient reason to delete it from the text.</i>.
Rebbi Abbahu in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: If one bartered an animal for an idol it becomes forbidden. Rav Ḥisda asked: If he had worshipped it he would not have made it forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">127</sup><i class="footnote">Animals used in pagan worship are forbidden as sacrifices (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Temurah 6:1" href="/Mishnah_Temurah.6.1">Mishnah <i>Temurah</i>6:1</a>), but living things cannot become forbidden for all use. Why should barter make the living animal forbidden?</i>. Because he bartered an animal for an idol he made it forbidden? Maybe it was said about merchandise assembled before the fair<sup class="footnote-marker">128</sup><i class="footnote">It is forbidden to give animals (or any other article which might be used in pagan worship) specifically for the purpose of assembling merchandise for sale at the pagan fair. The proceeds would be forbidden as the money of a Jew returning from dealing at the fair.</i>. There came Rebbi Ze‘ira, Rebbi Abbahu in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: If one bartered an animal for an idol it becomes forbidden. This was said about merchandise assembled before the fair. Rebbi Abbahu said, it is forbidden to make a group visit to a fair<sup class="footnote-marker">129</sup><i class="footnote">As stated in the following Tosephta, it is forbidden to greet a celebrant of a pagan rite in public where it increases his prestige on the day of his celebration. Since a group constitutes a public, a person meeting a Gentile of his acquaintance would automatically violate this prohibition.</i>, as we have stated<sup class="footnote-marker">130</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Avodah Zarah 1:2" href="/Tosefta_Avodah_Zarah.1.2">Tosephta 1:2</a>.</i>: “One should not greet him at a place where it gives him prestige. If he meets him on his way, he greets him gravely.”
Rebbi Ḥiyya bar Abba sent to buy sandals for himself at the fair of Tyre. Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa said to him, are you a buyer at fairs<sup class="footnote-marker">131</sup><i class="footnote">The head of the Academy should not be violating rabbinic decrees; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 13b" href="/Avodah_Zarah.13b">Babli 13b</a>.</i>? He answered him, did you never buy a loaf of bread<sup class="footnote-marker">132</sup><i class="footnote">Greek κόλλιξ.</i>? He said to him, there is a difference, since Rebbi Joḥanan said, they did not forbid the necessities of life. Rebbi Simeon ben Joḥanan sent to ask Rebbi Simeon ben Josadaq, did you not check out the nature of the fair at Tyre? He told him, yes. He gave two pounds<sup class="footnote-marker">133</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shevuot 6:2:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shevuot.6.2.2"><i>Ševuot</i>6:3, Note 34</a>.</i> of peppers to an <sup class="footnote-marker">134</sup><i class="footnote">An unexplained word. The proposed etymologies by Perles (<i>scrinarius</i>, a document keeper), Levy (a small oven), and Kohut (Persian <i>eskodār</i>, a courier) are unsatisfactory.</i>איסקרנדה. He went and found an inscription there, “I, King Diocletian, settled this fair at Tyre to the genius of my brother Heraklius<sup class="footnote-marker">135</sup><i class="footnote">Title given by Diocletian Jovius to his co-emperor Maximian. As a government sponsored fair, it should be permitted (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Avodah Zarah 1:3" href="/Tosefta_Avodah_Zarah.1.3">Tosephta 1:7</a>); the dedication to the genie of an Emperor would not make it prohibited since no pagan temple would profit from the sales.</i>, eight days.” Rebbi Isaac bar Naḥman asked Rebbi Ḥanina: What is the nature of the fair at Gaza? He said to him, did you ever go to Tyre and see a Jew and a Gentile sharing a pot, and he does not worry that the Gentile pour something into the pot? This is difficult. He asked him; what did he answer him? But since Rebbi Ḥanina never said something that he had not heard, for that reason when he asked him this he responded with that<sup class="footnote-marker">136</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 11b-12a" href="/Avodah_Zarah.11b-12a">Babli 11b/12a</a>.</i>. Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun came, Abba bar bar Ḥana in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: They forbade only the fair at Botnah<sup class="footnote-marker">137</sup><i class="footnote">Identified by Eusebius as the biblical town of Betonim in the territory of the tribe of Gad.</i>, as it was stated thus: There are three fairs, the fair at Gaza, the fair at Acco, the fair at Botnah. The clearest of them<sup class="footnote-marker">138</sup><i class="footnote">As unquestionably pagan, organized for the benefit of pagan worship.</i> is the fair at Botnah.
“May one go there?” If he was a guest<sup class="footnote-marker">139</sup><i class="footnote">An Aramaic word formed from Greek ξένος “stranger.” The rule of the Mishnah was made for visitors.</i>, it is forbidden; a town dweller is permitted<sup class="footnote-marker">140</sup><i class="footnote">As explained in Mishnah 3:6, real estate cannot become forbidden by being used for worship. A taxpayer of the place cannot be deprived by pagan worship of his right to use public roads.</i>. A caravan is permitted since caravans go everywhere<sup class="footnote-marker">141</sup><i class="footnote">Long distance travellers cannot be expected to know all local happenings; they may continue on their road.</i>.
How are they decorated? Rebbi Joḥanan said, with myrrh; Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish said, with any kind<sup class="footnote-marker">142</sup><i class="footnote">Not necessarily with flowers.</i>. In the opinion of Rebbi Joḥanan, all is forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">143</sup><i class="footnote">Since only myrrh is used, any myrrh used as decoration of any store in town is forbidden as pagan offering, whether or not it was intended as such.</i>; in the opinion of Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish only the addition is forbiddden. How is this done? If he usually takes five boxes outside and he took ten. If you say as a decoration, it is forbidden. If you say as sales practice<sup class="footnote-marker">144</sup><i class="footnote">Greek πρᾶξις, read פרקסיס for פרקסים. If the store expects to do more business because of the fair but outside the fairgrounds, this is not pagan worship.</i>, it is permitted.

Halakhah 5

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> The following are forbidden to be sold to them: Pine cones<sup class="footnote-marker">145</sup><i class="footnote">Greek στρόβιλος “cone”.</i>, and white figs<sup class="footnote-marker">146</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Demai 1:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Demai.1.1.1"><i>Demay</i>1:1, Note 5</a>.</i> with their stalks, and incense, and white cocks. Rebbi Jehudah says, it is permitted to sell a white cock among chickens; when it is alone he cuts one claw and sells it since one does not sacrifice defective ones in pagan worship. All other things are permitted if unspecified<sup class="footnote-marker">147</sup><i class="footnote">If the Gentile does not specify that he needs the objects for pagan worship.</i> but forbidden if specified. Rebbi Meïr says, it is also forbidden to sell good dates, and curved dates<sup class="footnote-marker">148</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Avodah Zarah 1:5:6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Avodah_Zarah.1.5.6">Note 155</a>.</i>, and Nicolaos dates<sup class="footnote-marker">149</sup><i class="footnote">A kind of large Syrian dates, cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Berakhot 6:5:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Berakhot.6.5.4"><i>Berakhot</i>6:5, Note 162</a>.</i> to Gentiles.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “The following are forbidden.” Simeon bar Abba in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: White figs with their stalks and pine cones with their attachments<sup class="footnote-marker">150</sup><i class="footnote">Which are needed to decorate the thyrsus in Dionysian rites. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 14a" href="/Avodah_Zarah.14a">Babli 14a</a>.</i>.
“And incense.” It was stated, if it was a bundle it is permitted. How many are a bundle? “Rebbi Jehudah ben Bathyra said, there are no less than five kinds in a bundle of incense<sup class="footnote-marker">151</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 14a" href="/Avodah_Zarah.14a">Babli 14a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Avodah Zarah 1:4" href="/Tosefta_Avodah_Zarah.1.4">Tosephta 1:21</a>.</i>. If he was a pagan priest it is forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">152</sup><i class="footnote">A Jew may not sell him any of the items specified in the Mishnah even if he does not specify that he needs it for pagan worship.</i>, a medical man is permitted, a trader is permitted. A suspect trader is forbidden.”
We have stated, “a white cock.” Rebbi Ḥiyya stated unspecified “a cock.” Our Mishnah needs the <i>baraita</i> of Rebbi Ḥiyya and the <i>baraita</i> of Rebbi Ḥiyya needs our Mishnah. If we had stated but Rebbi Ḥiyya had not stated, we would have said, only a white cock but an unspecified cock would be permitted even alone. Therefore the <i>baraita</i> of Rebbi Ḥiyya is needed. Or if Rebbi Ḥiyya had stated but we had not stated, we would have said, this is only for an unspecified cock, but a white cock even among chickens is forbidden. Therefore our Mishnah is necessary and the <i>baraita</i> of Rebbi Ḥiyya is necessary. “A chicken to sell,” one may sell him a white cock. “A white cock to sell,” we say by itself it is forbidden, among chickens it is permitted. Rebbi Abun bar Ḥiyya asked: If one saw chickens grazing on a garbage heap and a white cock among them and said, whose is it? Is it as if he specified<sup class="footnote-marker">153</sup><i class="footnote">Since he showed interest in the white cock alone, even if later in negotiating for the sale he does not mention it. Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 14a" href="/Avodah_Zarah.14a">Babli 14a</a>.</i>?
If it had a defect. The verse says that they sacrifice defectives in pagan worship. <i>If you present a blind one to be sacrificed, is there nothing bad</i><sup class="footnote-marker">154</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Malachi 1:8" href="/Malachi.1.8"><i>Malachi</i>1:8</a>. If it had not been acceptable for the Gentiles, the Jews would not have thought of doing this.</i>? This says that they sacrifice defectives in pagan worship.
“Rebbi Meïr says, it is also forbidden to sell good dates, and curved dates, and Nicolaos dates to Gentiles.” Rebbi Ḥama bar Uqba said, Caryotis<sup class="footnote-marker">155</sup><i class="footnote">Greek καρυῶτις “nut shaped kind of dates,” <a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 14b" href="/Avodah_Zarah.14b">Babli 14b</a>. The word חצד may be Arabic خضد “curved, bent”. The reading of the Yerushalmi is preferable to that of the Babli and the independent Mishnah mss., חָצָב, which designates a plant or bush having nothing to do with date palms (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Peah 2:1:6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Peah.2.1.6"><i>Peah</i>2:1 Note 24</a>).</i>. Rebbi Eleazar ben Rebbi Yose said, it is a kind known as <i>ḥaṣda</i>. If somebody transgressed and sold? It will come similar to what Rebbi Eleazar said<sup class="footnote-marker">156</sup><i class="footnote">Halakhah 7, end.</i>, if he transgressed and built it is permitted, and here if he transgressed and sold it is permitted.

Halakhah 6

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> At a place where they used to sell small cattle to Gentiles one sells<sup class="footnote-marker">157</sup><i class="footnote">At places where sheep and goats are not sacrificed in pagan rites.</i>; at a place where they used not to sell one does not sell; nobody should change this because of controversy<sup class="footnote-marker">158</sup><i class="footnote">Even if the situation changes one should not change old usage since this will destroy communal peace.</i>. Nowhere does one sell to them large animals, calves, and donkey foals, whole or damaged<sup class="footnote-marker">159</sup><i class="footnote">Cattle and donkeys were used as animals for work and beasts of burden. Belonging to Jews, these animals have a right to rest on the Sabbath. If they are sold to Gentiles, the seller deprives them of this right; depending on circumstances this might be counted as violation of the Sabbath by the seller. Calves and foals do not work but are raised for work.</i>. Rebbi Jehudah permits damaged ones; Ben Bathyra permits horses<sup class="footnote-marker">160</sup><i class="footnote">In antiquity horses were used only for riding, not for work. Even if the horse is used for hunting, he holds that transporting live animals or birds is not a breach of biblical Sabbath law.<br>Mishnah and Halakhah are also in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 4:3:2-7" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.4.3.2-7"><i>Pesaḥim</i> 4:3</a> (פ) which partially survives in a Genizah text (ג) edited by L. Ginzberg, New York 1909. It is difficult to determine which text is original.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “At a place where they used to sell small cattle,” etc. But is it permitted to raise them<sup class="footnote-marker">161</sup><i class="footnote">Since it is forbidden to raise sheep and goats in the Land of Israel (Mishnah <i>Bava qamma</i>7:10, Note 97), from where does one get them to sell them to a Gentile?</i>? Rebbi Abba said, for example Mahir which is sixteen by sixteen <i>mil</i><sup class="footnote-marker">162</sup><i class="footnote">The Mishnah permits to raise goats and sheep in places unfit for agriculture. <i>Mahir</i> is described in <i>Eccl. rabba</i>1(34) as situated in the domain of the tribe of Reuben in Transjordan, a country of sheep and goats (<i>Num</i>. 32:4). Mentioned also in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 5:3:2-9" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.5.3.2-9"><i>Pesaḥim</i>5:3</a>.</i>. They wanted to say, he who said it is permitted to sell [says] it is permitted to raise<sup class="footnote-marker">163</sup><i class="footnote">Read with <i>Pesaḥim</i>: “to leave alone”.</i>. But he who says, it is forbidden to sell [says] it is forbidden to leave it alone<sup class="footnote-marker">164</sup><i class="footnote">Gentiles are suspected of bestiality (Mishnah 2:1).</i>. Rebbi Jonah, Rebbi Eleazar in the name of Rav<sup class="footnote-marker">165</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 14b-15a" href="/Avodah_Zarah.14b-15a">Babli, 14b/15a</a>, this is R. Eleazar’s statement opposing Rav.</i>: Even one who says it is permitted to sell it [says] it is forbidden to leave it alone. What is the difference between selling and leaving alone? When he sells it to him it becomes the Gentile’s animal. If he lets it be alone with him, it is the Jew’s animal and he is suspected about it.
“At a place where they used not to sell one does not sell.” Why? Because he eliminates the duty of shearing<sup class="footnote-marker">166</sup><i class="footnote">The first shearing of sheep should be given to a Cohen, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 18:4" href="/Deuteronomy.18.4"><i>Deut</i>. 18:4</a>. But goats are not raised for wool and are included in the prohibition.</i>. Think of it, if it was a goat! Because he eliminates the duty of the first-born<sup class="footnote-marker">167</sup><i class="footnote">Which must be given to a Cohen, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 15:19" href="/Deuteronomy.15.19"><i>Deut</i>. 15:19</a>. But a male animal does not bear lambs and also is included in the prohibition.</i>. Think of it, if it was a male! Because he eliminates the gifts<sup class="footnote-marker">168</sup><i class="footnote">The parts of the animal to be given to a Cohen, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 18:3" href="/Deuteronomy.18.3"><i>Deut</i>. 18:3</a>.</i>. Then one should not sell wheat to them because he eliminates the duty of <i>ḥallah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">169</sup><i class="footnote">The gift to the Cohen from bread dough, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 15:20" href="/Numbers.15.20"><i>Num.</i>15:20</a>.</i>; then one should not sell wine and oil to them because he eliminates the duty of benedictions<sup class="footnote-marker">170</sup><i class="footnote">Which are pronounced before and after food, as described in the later Chapters of Tractate <i>Berakhot.</i><br>The problem treated here is that in the next paragraph it is stated that one does not sell large animals to Gentiles because of problems with the laws of the Sabbath. Therefore one is inclined to say that the prohibitions of Mishnah 5 are because of pagan worship and those of Mishnah 6 because of Jewish worship. It is shown that this does not hold; the first part of Mishnah 6 is about pagan worship, about places where sheep or goats are sacrificed by pagans; only the second part is about Jewish matters.</i>.
Nowhere does one sell large animals to them.” Why? For a large animal there might be an obligation for a purification offering<sup class="footnote-marker">172</sup><i class="footnote">If the Gentile does work with the Jew’s animal on the Sabbath, it is a violation of the Sabbath by biblical standards.</i>; for a small animal there can be no obligation for a purification offering. But does he not shear, does he not milk? One says, there it is liable, here he is liable<sup class="footnote-marker">173</sup><i class="footnote">It is clear that shearing a sheep on the Sabbath is a violation of biblical law (Mishnah <i>Šabbat</i>7:2). The Yerushalmi holds that milking a goat also is a biblical prohibition. But giving the animal rest on the Sabbath is an obligation of the owner; he is liable even if another person does the work with his consent. But the Gentile who shears or milks the animal violates no prohibition; since the animal is passive the owner also does not violate any biblical statute.</i>. But if it is sold, is it not the Gentile’s animal? The Babylonian Rebbi Immi<sup class="footnote-marker">174</sup><i class="footnote">A Babylonian who immigrated to Galilee in the generation after R. Immi the Galilean. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 15a" href="/Avodah_Zarah.15a">Babli 15a</a> he is called Rami ben Rebbi Yeva.</i> in the name of the rabbis there: Sometimes he sells it on trial and returns it after three days, then it turns out that he did do work with the Jew’s animal<sup class="footnote-marker">175</sup><i class="footnote">If the Gentile takes the animal on Friday and returns it on Sunday he will have worked with it on the Sabbath with the agreement of the Jew who it turns out still is the owner.</i>. Then on trial it should be forbidden, not on trial permitted. One is because of the other<sup class="footnote-marker">176</sup><i class="footnote">This is a rabbinic “fence around the law,” far from biblical prohibitions.</i>.
If he transgressed and sold one fines him. Just as one fines for practice so one fines for custom. From where that one fines for custom? A person sold his camel to an Aramean<sup class="footnote-marker">177</sup><i class="footnote">A Gentile. While “Gentile” is used to emphasize the pagan character of a person, “Aramean” simply characterizes him as Non-Jew.</i>. The case came before Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish who fined him double<sup class="footnote-marker">178</sup><i class="footnote">Greek διπλῇ. In פ the Hebrew/ Aramaic translation is used.
