In פ and the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bekhorot 3a" href="/Bekhorot.3a">Babli, <i>Bekhorot</i>3a</a>, he authorizes a fine up to ten times its price.</i> to make him take back the camel. Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun said, they fined the broker and called him son who brokers to Arameans<sup class="footnote-marker">179</sup><i class="footnote">Translated following פ.</i>. Does Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish follow Rebbi Jehudah<sup class="footnote-marker">180</sup><i class="footnote">Could R. Simeon ben Laqish act against accepted practice in this case?</i>? As it was stated in the name of Rebbi Jehuda<sup class="footnote-marker">181</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bekhorot 2:1" href="/Tosefta_Bekhorot.2.1">Tosephta <i>Bekhorot</i>2:1</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bekhorot 2b" href="/Bekhorot.2b">Babli <i>Bekhorot</i>2b</a>.</i>: “If somebody buys an animal from a Gentile and it gave birth to a firstling, he buys it up to its worth and gives half of its worth to a Cohen. If it was given to him as contractor, he has to pay for up to ten times its worth and gives all of its worth to the Cohen. But the Sages say, since the finger of the Gentile in involved it is no longer liable as firstling<sup class="footnote-marker">182</sup><i class="footnote">Undisputed practice follows the Sages, cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Avodah Zarah 1:6:6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Avodah_Zarah.1.6.6">Note 187</a>.</i>.” Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish is more than Rebbi Jehudah. What Rebbi Jehudah said because of the practice of firstlings; but what Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish said because of practice regarding a large animal<sup class="footnote-marker">183</sup><i class="footnote">The two cases have nothing in common.</i>.
“Rebbi Jehudah permits damaged ones.” Rebbi Jehudah said this only for a damaged one which cannot be healed<sup class="footnote-marker">184</sup><i class="footnote">To ever work again.</i>. They told him, may he not bring a male to it, he fertilizes it and it gives birth? He said to them, I also said this only about a male (horse) [damaged one]<sup class="footnote-marker">185</sup><i class="footnote">The text in parentheses is from the text here, the (correct) one in brackets from <i>Pesaḥim</i>.</i> which cannot be healed. They told him, may he not bring a female to him, she is fertilized by him and gives birth? Rav Abin in the name of the rabbis there: This implies that one is forbidden to provide them with fetuses<sup class="footnote-marker">186</sup><i class="footnote">Since the argument of the rabbis has nothing to do with Sabbath prohibitions. The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 16a" href="/Avodah_Zarah.16a">Babli, 16a</a> and <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bekhorot 2b" href="/Bekhorot.2b"><i>Bekhorot</i>2b</a>, disagrees and reports that R. Jehudah denies that a disabled cow will accept a male.</i>. There<sup class="footnote-marker">187</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Bekhorot 1:1" href="/Mishnah_Bekhorot.1.1">Mishnah <i>Bekhorot</i>1:1</a>.</i>, we have stated: “If somebody buys a Non-Jew’s donkey fetus or who sells one to him even though he is not authorized, or one who enters into partnership, or accepts from him as contractor, or lets it in contract, is not liable for firstling.” Rebbi Ḥaggai asked before Rebbi Yose, does this<sup class="footnote-marker">188</sup><i class="footnote">The note that one is not authorized to sell a pregnant animal to a Gentile.</i> not imply that one is forbidden to provide them with fetuses? He said to them, Rebbi Abin in the name of the rabbis there already preceded you: This implies that one is forbidden to provide them with fetuses.
“Ben Bathyra permits horses.” Ben Bathyra said this only about a male horse because it kills its owner in war. Some say, because it runs after females, and some say, because it stands still to urinate<sup class="footnote-marker">189</sup><i class="footnote">At this moment the horse will not obey its master and therefore be dangerous.</i>. What is between them? A gelding. He who says because it runs after females, it does not run. He who says because it stands still to urinate, this one also stands still to urinate. [Rebbi Aḥa in the name of]<sup class="footnote-marker">190</sup><i class="footnote">Added from the <i>Pesaḥim</i> texts.</i> Rebbi Tanḥum bar Ḥiyya: If it gets old he binds it to the grindstone<sup class="footnote-marker">191</sup><i class="footnote">Then it is used like a beast of burden and the same restriction may apply as to the sale of cattle. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 16a" href="/Avodah_Zarah.16a">Babli 16a</a>.</i>. Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun in the name of Rebbi Huna: Ben Bathyra and Rebbi Nathan both said the same<sup class="footnote-marker">192</sup><i class="footnote">For the laws of Sabbath. Everybody agrees that “a living person carries himself”; it is permitted to carry a human baby from private to public domains. The rabbis restrict this to humans; R. Nathan explicitly and Ben Bathyra implicitly (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Avodah Zarah 1:6:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Avodah_Zarah.1.6.1">Note 160</a>) extend the rule to animals. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shabbat 94a" href="/Shabbat.94a">Babli <i>Šabbat</i>94a</a>.</i>, as it was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">193</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Shabbat (Lieberman) 8:34" href="/Tosefta_Shabbat_(Lieberman).8.34">Tosephta <i>Šabbat</i>8:34</a> <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shabbat 94a" href="/Shabbat.94a">Babli <i>Šabbat</i>94a</a>.</i>: “If he carried out domestic animals, wild animals, or birds, whether alive or dead, he is liable. Rebbi Nathan says, dead he is liable, alive he is not liable.” The rabbis hold that he is liable for a purification sacrifice and they answer him so<sup class="footnote-marker">194</sup><i class="footnote">Why do they object because of the use of old horses which only implies a violation of rabbinic rules but not to the possible use of a younger horse in hunting which would violate biblical rules?</i>? They answer following his own argument. Following your argument, since you are saying because of rabbinic Sabbath prohibition, also we hold that if it gets old he binds it to the grindstone. Rebbi says, I am saying that it is forbidden for two reasons, as a weapon<sup class="footnote-marker">195</sup><i class="footnote">Which makes the seller of the weapon an accessory to murder before the fact. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 16a" href="/Avodah_Zarah.16a">Babli 16a</a>.</i> and as a large animal. It was stated so: A large wild animal is like a (small) [large]<sup class="footnote-marker">196</sup><i class="footnote">The correct text in brackets is from <i>Pesaḥim</i>, the text in parentheses is from <i>Avodah zarah</i>. In the last sentence, the Genizah text follows the one in <i>Avodah zarah</i>; this clearly is impossible.</i> domestic animal<sup class="footnote-marker">197</sup><i class="footnote">A horse is considered a tamed wild animal, not domesticated by nature.</i>. Who stated this? Rebbi. The words of the Sages: Rebbi Bisna, Rebbi Ḥanan bar Abba in the name of Rav<sup class="footnote-marker">198</sup><i class="footnote">It should be Rav Ḥanin, not Rebbi. The Genizah text has the statement in the name of Rebbi Ḥiyya, Rav’s uncle and foremost teacher.</i>: A wild animal is like a (large) [small]<sup class="footnote-marker">196</sup><i class="footnote">The correct text in brackets is from <i>Pesaḥim</i>, the text in parentheses is from <i>Avodah zarah</i>. In the last sentence, the Genizah text follows the one in <i>Avodah zarah</i>; this clearly is impossible.</i> domestic animal.

Halakhah 7

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> One does not sell them bears and lions, or anything damaging to the public; nor does one build with them a basilica<sup class="footnote-marker">199</sup><i class="footnote">Greek βασιλική “king’s palace”, a government building in the provinces, automatically dedicated to the worship of Rome and the Emperor.</i>, a gallows<sup class="footnote-marker">200</sup><i class="footnote">Since one may assume that most of death sentences passed by the Roman government are unjust.</i>, a stadion<sup class="footnote-marker">201</sup><i class="footnote">Greek στάδιον, the place of gladiator’s combats, race courses, etc.</i>, or a rostrum<sup class="footnote-marker">202</sup><i class="footnote">Podiums for public speeches or trials.</i>. But one builds with them public<sup class="footnote-marker">203</sup><i class="footnote">Reading with Maimonides and the ‘<i>Arukh</i>דימוסיות from Greek δημόσια.</i> and private baths; when they come to the cupola where an idol will be placed it is forbidden to build it.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “One does not sell them bears and lions,” etc. Therefore something which does not present a danger to the public one may sell to them. Our Mishnah follows Rebbi<sup class="footnote-marker">204</sup><i class="footnote">It is not known what this refers to.</i>. As it was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">205</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 18b" href="/Avodah_Zarah.18b">Babli 18b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Avodah Zarah 2:2" href="/Tosefta_Avodah_Zarah.2.2">Tosephta 2:6</a>, <i>Yalquṭ Ps.</i>1:1 (#613).</i>: “One who sees snake charmers and conjurers, <i>muqion, mupion, mulion, milarin, milaria, sigillarin, sigillaria</i><sup class="footnote-marker">206</sup><i class="footnote">The different sources give different readings for the names of the performers.<br>Yerushalimi:<br>מוקיון מופיון מוליון מילרין מילריה סגילרין סגילרה<br>Tosephta ed. Zuckermandel<br>בוקיון מוקיון מוליון סגלרין סגלריא<br>Tosephta ed. princeps<br>בוקיון מוקיון מוליון סגלאדין סגלאדא<br>Babli ed. princeps<br>בוקיון מוקיון מוליון לוליון בלורין סלגורין<br>Babli Ms. Munich<br>בוקיון מוקיון מוליון לוליון סגליון סגלקיין בלרין<br>בלריון מלרין מלריון סלגלוריון<br>Yalquṭ<br>בוקיון מוקיון מוליון לולין סגלין סגלדין כלרין בלרין<br>מלרין מלריון<br>For the words appearing in the Yerushalmi there seems to be unanimity that the first three are <i>maccus</i>, the buffoon, corresponding to the Babli’s <i>bucco, μώκος</i> the comedian, <i>mulio</i> the donkey trainer. <i>Sigillaria</i> is the holiday which concludes the <i>saturnalia</i>; Kohut notes a meaning “puppeteer” for <i>sigillarius</i> (סגילרין) quoted in DuCange’s Medieval Latin dictionary. Compare also <i>sicinnista</i>, “dancer (<i>m</i>.) of the <i>sicinnis</i>” a dance of satyrs (E. G.) The remaining two words מילרין מילריה seem to remain without reasonable identification.</i>, this is forbidden because of ‘seat of the scoffers,’ as it is said,<sup class="footnote-marker">207</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Psalms 1:1" href="/Psalms.1.1"><i>Ps.</i> 1:1</a>.</i> <i>in the seat of scoffers he did not sit.</i> All this leads to neglect of the Torah, as it is said<sup class="footnote-marker">208</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Psalms 1:2" href="/Psalms.1.2"><i>Ps.</i>1:2</a>.</i>, <i>but in the teachings of the Eternal is his desire.</i> Going to the theater<sup class="footnote-marker">209</sup><i class="footnote">Probably meaning: amphitheater (E. G.). Both Greek tragedy and Comedy were religious ceremonies.</i> is forbidden as pagan worship, the words of Rebbi Meïr, but the Sages say, when they present offerings it is forbidden because of pagan worship, otherwise it only is forbidden because of ‘seat of scoffers.’ One who goes to the theater to shout<sup class="footnote-marker">210</sup><i class="footnote">In the theater it was to vote with one of the parties. In the Babli sources (Babli and Tosephta), instead of מִתְחֵַשֵּׂד “to ingratiate oneself” one reads מִתְחַשֵּׁד “to be counted as one of them”. The meaning is the same.</i>; if it is for a public need it is permitted, if to ingratiate himself it is forbidden. One who sits in the stadium is a spiller of blood<sup class="footnote-marker">211</sup><i class="footnote">If there were no public, neither fights of gladiators nor fights of men with wild beasts would be staged.</i>. Rebbi Nathan permits because of two reasons, because he can shout and save lives<sup class="footnote-marker">212</sup><i class="footnote">If a wounded gladiator begs for his life, he can vote to let him live.</i>, and he can testify for a woman that she can remarry<sup class="footnote-marker">213</sup><i class="footnote">If a Jew is killed in one of these performances the spectator can testify that his wife is a widow and can remarry.</i>.”
If he transgressed and built. Rebbi Eleazar said, it remains permitted<sup class="footnote-marker">214</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Avodah Zarah 1:5:6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Avodah_Zarah.1.5.6">Halakhah 1:5, Note 156</a>. The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 19b" href="/Avodah_Zarah.19b">Babli, 19b</a>, knows only of R. Eleazar’s ruling.</i>. Rebbi Mana said, it rather seems reasonable following the opinion that it is forbidden because he would have prevented<sup class="footnote-marker">215</sup><i class="footnote">From the verb حيد “to deviate”. Maybe if the Jew had refused to build the cupola it would not have been built.</i> the entire cupola.

Halakhah 8

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> One does not sell to them anything connected to the ground, but one may sell for when it will be cut<sup class="footnote-marker">216</sup><i class="footnote">This is a rule particular to the Land of Israel, to preserve the Jewish character of the land, as explained in Halakha 9. It does not apply to the diaspora.</i>. Rebbi Jehudah says, he may sell for the purpose of cutting.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “One does not sell to them anything connected to the ground,” etc. Rebbi Abun bar Ḥiyya asked: Is there also a disagreement about large cattle? May one sell for the purpose of slaughter? It was found stated: Also large cattle is in dispute; Rebbi Jehudah says, he may sell for the purpose of slaughtering<sup class="footnote-marker">217</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 20a" href="/Avodah_Zarah.20a">Babli 20a</a>. This is not dependent on the Land; it applies everywhere. The Babli defines R. Jehudah’s adversary as R. Meïr.</i>.

Halakhah 9

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> In the Land of Israel<sup class="footnote-marker">218</sup><i class="footnote">As defined in <i>Ševi‘it</i>6:1.</i> one does not lease houses to them and certainly not fields. In Syria<sup class="footnote-marker">219</sup><i class="footnote">All territory ruled over by David but not included in the Land proper; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Peah 7:5:6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Peah.7.5.6"><i>Peah</i>7:6 Note 119</a>.</i> one leases houses to them but not fields. Outside the Land one sells houses to them and leases fields, the words of Rebbi Meïr. Rebbi Yose says, also in the Land of Israel one leases houses to them but not fields; in Syria one sells houses and leases fields; outside the Land one sells both<sup class="footnote-marker">220</sup><i class="footnote">The opinion of R. Yose is preeminent.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “One does not lease houses to them,” etc. <sup class="footnote-marker">221</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 19b-20a" href="/Avodah_Zarah.19b-20a">Babli 19b/20a</a>. The entire theory built on <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 7:2" href="/Deuteronomy.7.2"><i>Deut</i>. 7:2</a> is Amoraic even though in the Babli it is supported by a <i>baraita</i>.</i> Rebbi Ze‘ira in the name of Rebbi Yose ben Ḥanina, Rebbi Abba, Rebbi Ḥiyya<sup class="footnote-marker">222</sup><i class="footnote">These are two parallel traditions, one in the name of R. Yose bar Ḥanina, the other in the name of R. Joḥanan.</i> in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: לֹא תְחָנֵּֽם<sup class="footnote-marker">223</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 7:2" href="/Deuteronomy.7.2"><i>Deut</i>. 7:2</a>. The verse refers only to the original Canaanite inhabitants.</i> do credit them with charm; לֹא תְחָנֵּֽם do not give them free gifts; לֹא תְחָנֵּֽם do not give them encampment in the Land<sup class="footnote-marker">224</sup><i class="footnote">The different interpretations are based on reading the word as derived from the roots חנן, חנם, חנה. They will be discussed in the inverse order of the first quote.</i>. But did we not state, “Rebbi Yose says, also in the Land of Israel one leases houses to them”? A house is not a source of blessing; a field is a source of blessing<sup class="footnote-marker">225</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Menachot 66b" href="/Menachot.66b">Babli <i>Menaḥot</i>66b Tosaphot <i>s. v.</i>מירוח</a>.</i>. Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun instructed that it is forbidden to let them lease a grave in the Land of Israel because of “do not give them encampment in the Land.<sup class="footnote-marker">226</sup><i class="footnote">Since a permanent irrevocable lease is the equivalent of a sale.</i>”
לֹא תְחָנֵּֽם do not give them free gifts. But was it not stated<sup class="footnote-marker">227</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Pesachim (Lieberman) 2:15" href="/Tosefta_Pesachim_(Lieberman).2.15">Tosephta <i>Pesaḥim</i>2:15</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 64b" href="/Eruvin.64b">Babli <i>Eruvin</i>64b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Vayikra Rabbah 37:3" href="/Vayikra_Rabbah.37.3"><i>Lev. rabba</i>37(3</a>). There it is stated that he went from Acco to Akzib near the Lebanese border riding on a donkey and implied that it was the day after the end of Passover.</i>: “It happened that Rabban Gamliel was walking on the road and saw a loaf of bread<sup class="footnote-marker">132</sup><i class="footnote">Greek κόλλιξ.</i> thrown on the road. He said to his slave Tabi, pick up this loaf. He saw a Gentile coming towards him and said to him, Mabgai<sup class="footnote-marker">228</sup><i class="footnote">Levy reads the name as derived from <i>paganus</i>, “village dweller”. It is possible to see here the root بجّ “to fatten animals” said of pasture.</i>, take this loaf. Rebbi Illai ran after him and asked him, what is your name? He told him, Mabgai. From where are you? He told him, from the guard-tower<sup class="footnote-marker">229</sup><i class="footnote">From Latin <i>burgus</i>, watch lower at a <i>limes</i>, Greek πυργός “tower”.</i> villages. Did Rabban Gamliel ever come to know you?, He told him, no. Rabban Gamliel had it right by the Holy Spirit. We learned from him three things. We learned that one does not pass over food. And that leavened matter of a Gentile is permitted immediately after Passover. And that one follows the majority of travelers<sup class="footnote-marker">230</sup><i class="footnote">Since Tabi was circumcised and the time was just after the end of Passover, Rabban Gamliel riding on a donkey could not have asked his slave walking beside him to pick up a loaf of bread, and therefore acquiring it as ownerless, if he could not assume that it was lost by a Gentile since most of the travelers on that road were Gentiles. The road itself is described in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sheviit 6:1:6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sheviit.6.1.6"><i>Ševi‘it</i>(6:1 Note 21</a>) as being the border between Jewish Galilee to the East and pagan Phoenicia to the West. If the leavened bread had been the property of a Jew during Passover its usufruct would be permanently forbidden to Jews.</i>.” Rebbi Jacob bar Zavdi in the name of Rebbi Abbahu: That is, in earlier times. But today we pass over edibles because of sorcery<sup class="footnote-marker">231</sup><i class="footnote">Since abandoned food may be a health hazard, one is justified not to touch it.</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">232</sup><i class="footnote">Found in the same sources as the preceding paragraph, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Avodah Zarah 1:9:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Avodah_Zarah.1.9.3">Note 227</a>.</i>“When he was leaving Akzib there came a person to ask about his vow<sup class="footnote-marker">233</sup><i class="footnote">Which according to Pharisaic tradition can be dissolved by an ordained rabbi; cf. <i>Introduction to Tractate Nedarim</i>, p. 422; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 3:1:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.3.1.2"><i>Nedarim</i>3:1 Note 7</a>.</i>. He asked his travel companion, would you say that we drank a <i>quartarius</i><sup class="footnote-marker">234</sup><i class="footnote">About 135 ml.</i> of Italian wine? He said to him, yes. He told the person who asked, walk with us until we dissipate our wine. When they came to the Tyrian Ladder<sup class="footnote-marker">235</sup><i class="footnote">Modern <i>Rosh Hanniqra</i> on the Lebanese border.</i> Rabban Gamliel dismounted, covered himself<sup class="footnote-marker">236</sup><i class="footnote">Wrapped himself in his toga.</i>, sat down, and dissolved his vow. From his words we learned that a <i>quartarius</i> of wine intoxicates and that travelling dissipates the wine. Also that one does not ask about vows and gives no instruction after drinking wine<sup class="footnote-marker">237</sup><i class="footnote">For priests officiating in the Temple this is a biblical prohibition, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 10:9-10" href="/Leviticus.10.9-10"><i>Lev</i>. 10:9–10</a>.</i>. Also one does not dissolve vows while walking but covered and sitting.” Rebbi Joḥanan said, so he opened for him: <i>Some blurt out unthinkingly like sword piercing</i><sup class="footnote-marker">238</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Proverbs 12:18" href="/Proverbs.12.18"><i>Prov</i>. 12:18</a>.</i> etc. For example, one who made a vow not [to eat] a loaf. Woe if he eats, woe if he does not eat. If he eats he violates his vow. If he does not eat he sins against himself<sup class="footnote-marker">239</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>nazir</i> who vows to abstain from wine at the end of his period of abstinence has to bring a purification offering usually reserved for sinners. His sin is that he voluntarily deprived himself of permitted enjoyments; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 1:1:14" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.1.1.14"><i>Nedarim</i>1:1 Note 95</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 4:12:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.4.12.3"><i>Qiddušin</i>4:12 Note 272</a>.</i>. What should he do? He should go to the Sages who will dissolve his vow as is written, <i>but the sayings of the Sages are healing</i>.
לֹא תְחָנֵּֽם do not credit them with charm. <sup class="footnote-marker">240</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 20" href="/Avodah_Zarah.20">Babli 20a/b</a>. A parallel (not a copy) is in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Berakhot 9:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Berakhot.9.2"><i>Berakhot</i>9:2, Notes 79–81</a>.</i> But was it not stated: It happened that Rabban Gamliel while walking on the Temple Mount saw a beautiful Gentile woman and recited a blessing over her. Does Rabban Gamliel usually look at women? It was a curved road, like a <i>pswros</i><sup class="footnote-marker">241</sup><i class="footnote">The word is unexplained; perhaps ἐπίσυρμα “trail or track made by something dragged.” (E. G.)</i>, he looked at her because he could not avoid her. But did not Rebbi Ze‘ura in the name of Rebbi Yose ben Ḥanina, Rebbi Abba, Rebbi Ḥiyya in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan say: לֹא תְחָנֵּֽם do not credit them with charm? What did he say? He did not say ἀβάσκαντα<sup class="footnote-marker">242</sup><i class="footnote">Greek “free from harm” <i>Berakhot</i>9:2, Note 79.</i> but “praised be He Who has beautiful creatures in His world,” because even if one saw a beautiful donkey, a beautiful camel, a beautiful horse, one says “praised be He Who has such beautiful creatures in His world.”
Rebbi Simeon, Rebbi Abbahu in the name of Rebbi Yose bar Ḥanina: a Jewish braider should not braid a Gentile because of “do not give on them charm.” Also a Jew should not be a Gentile’s best man because of <i>do not contract marriage with them</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">243</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 7:3" href="/Deuteronomy.7.3"><i>Deut</i>. 7:3</a>.</i> Rebbi Isaac from Gufta asked before Rebbi Mana: It is forbidden to go to their wedding feasts, not <i>a fortiori</i> to become his best man? He told him, to put a biblical prohibition on it<sup class="footnote-marker">245</sup><i class="footnote">However the verse refers only to Canaanites, not Gentiles in general. The prohibition remains rabbinic.</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">246</sup><i class="footnote">From here intermittently through fol. 43a line 7 there is a Geniza (G) text, in many places very fragmentary, edited by L. Ginzberg (New York 1909). From fol. 41d line 42 there exists a second Geniza text from the Antonin collection edited by J.N. Epstein (E), <i>Tarbiz</i>3 (1932) pp. 15–26, in addition to the Leiden ms. (L).<br>The present paragraph is a copy from <i>Demay</i>6:1 (<b>ד</b>) Notes 15–18. In the readings, a reference (G) means that the word is not completely preserved in G.</i> Rabbi Simon had vineyard planters on King’s Mountain<sup class="footnote-marker">247</sup><i class="footnote">A region conquered by King Yannai and part of the later Hasmonean kingdom, not settled by the earlier returnees from Babylonia. Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Demai 5:8:6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Demai.5.8.6"><i>Demay</i>5 Note 115</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sheviit 9:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sheviit.9.2.1"><i>Ševi‘it</i>9 Note 63</a>. In Amoraic times it was settled only by Gentiles.<br>The text of the other two sources, “fields” is preferable over “vineyard planters”.</i>. He asked Rebbi Joḥanan who told him, let them lie fallow and do not lease them to a Gentile. He asked Rebbi Joshua [ben Levi]<sup class="footnote-marker">248</sup><i class="footnote">Added from <b>ד</b> and G, missing in L, required by the context.</i>, may one lease them to a Gentile? Rebbi Joshua [ben Levi]<sup class="footnote-marker">248</sup><i class="footnote">Added from <b>ד</b> and G, missing in L, required by the context.</i> permitted it at a place where no Jews could be found as for example Syria. About Syria we can understand from the following: Rebbi Ḥaggai went to Homs. Those of Bar Ashtor came and asked him: Since we cannot find Jews we lease to Gentiles;l do we have to tithe through them<sup class="footnote-marker">249</sup><i class="footnote">The Gentile is not required to tithe. If he pays his rent in produce, is the landlord required to tithe? Produce in Syria has to be tithed only by rabbinic decree, twice removed from biblical duty. Since the Gentile acts on his own, not on behalf of the owner of the land, what he pays as rent is exempt Gentile produce.</i>? He sent and asked Rebbi Ze‘ira. Rebbi Ze‘ira asked Rebbi Immi (and said to him, rabbi, does one have to tithe?)<sup class="footnote-marker">250</sup><i class="footnote">Delete with <b>ד</b> and G.</i> He told him, one does not have to tithe because of them. From this you (Rebbi Simeon)<sup class="footnote-marker">250</sup><i class="footnote">Delete with <b>ד</b> and G.</i> [understand]<sup class="footnote-marker">251</sup><i class="footnote">Add as reading of <b>ד</b> and G.</i> to lease following Rebbi Yose. In addition, from what Rebbi Ḥanina (Rebbi Abbahu)<sup class="footnote-marker">250</sup><i class="footnote">Delete with <b>ד</b> and G.</i> the son of Rebbi Abbahu said, my father had a case. He sent and asked Rebbi Ḥiyya, Rebbi Yasa<sup class="footnote-marker">252</sup><i class="footnote">Missing in G.</i>, Rebbi Immi, and they instructed him to lease following Rebbi Yose. Therefore one does not tithe through them<sup class="footnote-marker">253</sup><i class="footnote">As explained in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Avodah Zarah 1:9:7" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Avodah_Zarah.1.9.7">Note 249</a> there is no need to tithe. The only question is whether one should require them to tithe anyhow as a fine for leasing land to Gentiles, but since the lease is legitimate following R. Yose there is no reason for such a fine. The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 21a" href="/Avodah_Zarah.21a">Babli agrees, 21a</a>.</i>.

Halakhah 10

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> Also in a case where they said to lease they did not mean a dwelling because he will introduce idols as it is said, <i>do not bring an abomination into your house for you would be anathema like it</i><sup class="footnote-marker">255</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 7:26" href="/Deuteronomy.7.26"><i>Deut</i>. 7:26</a>.</i>, etc. In any case he should not lease to him a bath house because it is called by his name<sup class="footnote-marker">256</sup><i class="footnote">If the bath house (or any other public convenience) is known as the Jew’s property it cannot be operated on the Sabbath since people would think that the Jew operates it.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “Also in a case where they said to lease,” etc. Therefore, at a place where one is used to sell<sup class="footnote-marker">257</sup><i class="footnote">Outside the Land for R. Meïr and in Syria for R. Yose.</i>, he may sell even a dwelling and lease a dwelling<sup class="footnote-marker">258</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 7:26" href="/Deuteronomy.7.26"><i>Deut</i>. 7:26</a> applies only to the Land. In the theory of the Babli, Gentiles outside the Land are no real idolators but only followers of inherited superstitions. Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Avodah Zarah 2:3" href="/Tosefta_Avodah_Zarah.2.3">Tosephta 2:8</a>.</i>. Rebbi Aḥa, Rebbi Tanḥum bar Ḥiyya in the name of Rebbi Eleazar ben Rebbi Yose: Even a small room as in the tannery of Sidon<sup class="footnote-marker">259</sup><i class="footnote">If a small room in an industrial complex cannot be leased to Gentiles as living space. A similar text in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 21b" href="/Avodah_Zarah.21b">Babli 21b</a>.</i>. Not only all of it, but even one room. “If there were two, one inside the other, the inner one is liable, the outer one is not liable.<sup class="footnote-marker">260</sup><i class="footnote">This is Mishnah <i>Ma`serot</i>3:5 (Note 92). Harvested grain becomes liable for tithes only when it is being prepared for storage. An example of this is grain transported from the field to the courtyard attached to the farmer’s dwelling. R. Jehudah holds that if there is a courtyard inside another, the inner one only counts in this respect.</i>” If there was one and it was subdivided into two, two and combined into one<sup class="footnote-marker">261</sup><i class="footnote">How do the rules of tithing apply? The question is inappropriate here and not answered.</i>? Rebbi Abin in the name of the rabbis there:<sup class="footnote-marker">262</sup><i class="footnote">Of Babylonia.</i> This<sup class="footnote-marker">263</sup><i class="footnote">The argument of the Mishnah about leasing a bathhouse. In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Avodah Zarah 2:3" href="/Tosefta_Avodah_Zarah.2.3">Tosephta 2:9</a>, R. Simeon forbids leasing a field anywhere for the same reason.</i> implies that if there was a field adjacent to a road it is forbidden to lease it to Gentiles because it is known by the name of the Jew and they plough there on Sabbaths and holidays.

Chapter 2



Halakhah 1

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> One does not put up an animal in hostelries<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Avodah Zarah 1:4:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Avodah_Zarah.1.4.2">Chapter 1, Note 123</a>.</i> of Gentiles since they are suspected of bestiality. A woman should not be alone with them<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">A single woman with any number of Gentile men.</i> because they are suspected of forbidden sexual relations; a man should not stay alone with them since they are suspected of shedding blood. A Jewish woman should not be a midwife for a Non-Jew<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">Since she helps to increase the number of pagan worshippers. This reason given in the Halakhah is (incorrectly) part of the Mishnah in some Babli and Mishnah texts.</i> but a Non-Jew may be a midwife for a Jewish woman. A Jewish woman should not nurse the child of a Non-Jewish woman but a Non-Jewish woman may nurse the child of a Jewish woman at her home<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">The wet-nurse should not be allowed to remove the baby from his mother’s house since she might kill it.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “One does not put up animals in hostelries of Gentiles,” etc. Rebbi Ze‘ira, Rebbi Abbahu in the name of Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Ḥanina; Rebbi Abba, Rebbi Jonah<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">Unfortunately G is very lacunary here; therefore its readings are not given after the Hebrew text. The Halakhah starts: “R. Ze‘urah, R. Abbahu in the name of R. Yose ben Ḥanina, R. ..… R. Abbahu in the name of R. Yose ben Ḥanina, explain it …” There is no reason to mention the sixth generation R. Abba III and the fifth generation R. Jonah who later disagrees with the statement if already it was attributed to the second generation R. Yose ben Ḥanina.</i>: explain it<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">The Mishnah which forbids to put up unsupervised animals in the stable of a Gentile hostelry makes travel outside Jewish settlements practically impossible. An attribution to R. Eliezer removes the first part of the Mishnah from practice.</i> following Rebbi Eliezer, since Rebbi Eliezer said “it may not be bought from Gentiles.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Parah 2:1" href="/Mishnah_Parah.2.1">Mishnah <i>Parah</i>2:1</a>. This refers to the Red Cow (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 19" href="/Numbers.19"><i>Num</i>. 19</a>) whose ashes are needed to cleanse people from the impurity of the dead. The Cow is not a Temple sacrifice; how far the restrictions imposed on sacrificial animals and the officiating priests apply to the Cow and its officiants are old matters of dispute between Sadducees and Pharisees (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Parah 3:3" href="/Mishnah_Parah.3.3">Mishnah <i>Parah</i>3:3</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Parah 3:7" href="/Mishnah_Parah.3.7">7</a>) and among the rabbis themselves. One of the biblical requirements is that the Red Cow never carried a yoke (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 19:2" href="/Numbers.19.2"><i>Num</i>. 19:2</a>). In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Parah 2:1" href="/Mishnah_Parah.2.1">Mishnah <i>Parah</i>2:1</a>, a first statement of R. Eliezer validates a pregnant Red Cow for the ceremonies; the reason is explained in Mishnah 4 by his student’s R. Illai’s son R. Jehudah, that a bull mounting the Cow on his own cannot be considered making her carrying anything; only copulation by human intervention makes the ox the equivalent of a yoke. The majority rejects the ruling; it considers the Cow and its fetus as two separate beings but v. 19:3 requires the Cow alone to be taken. These restrictions do not apply to ordinary sacrifices. R. Eliezer then forbids buying a Red Cow from Gentiles since a sodomized animal is unfit for the altar; the Sages disagree since without reason one does not suspect that such a thing happened; this would make the Mishnah here unreasonable. (The last generation R. Yose bar Abun even introduces buying a Red Cow from a Gentile into a story about R. Eliezer; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Peah 1:1:22" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Peah.1.1.22"><i>Peah</i>1:1 Notes 99</a> ff., <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 1:7:5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.1.7.5"><i>Qiddušin</i>1:7 Notes 607</a> ff.) <a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 23a" href="/Avodah_Zarah.23a">Babli 23a</a>.</i>” Rebbi Jonah asked, why do we not explain it according to everybody, following what Rebbi Eleazar said in the name of Rav: Even one who says it is permitted to sell [says] it is forbidden to leave alone<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">One may not leave the animal alone with a Gentile. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Avodah Zarah 1:6:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Avodah_Zarah.1.6.2">Chapter 1:6, Note 165</a>. There the first tradent is R. Jonah himself.</i>. If he transgressed and left it alone by everybody’s opinion<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">It is not said what everybody’s opinion is; the corresponding passage in G is in the name of “the rabbis of Caesarea, Giddul bar Benjamin in the name of Rav” but then is missing the conclusion. One has to assume that it was “if he transgressed and left it alone by everybody’s opinion the animal remains permitted.”</i>. Rebbi Jeremiah said, let us hear from the following<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 2:8:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.2.8.1">Mishnah <i>Ketubot</i>2:9</a>. If she is jailed because of money matters one must assume that she was not raped; if she was condemned to death one must assume that she was raped or consented to sex with her jailers. If a woman can be alone in the custody of Gentiles without being raped, may the same not be assumed of female animals?</i>: “A woman who was jailed by Gentiles.” Rebbi Yose said, it is different for a woman because she usually cries. Think of it if she was mute! She uses sign language. What about it<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">The argument appears to be cogent.</i>? Explain it following Rebbi Eliezer, since Rebbi Eliezer said “it may not be bought from Gentiles.” They wanted to say, where do Rebbi Eliezer and the rabbis disagree? About the cow, because of “eminence was given to the cow, an adornment was made for the cow.<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">In the Mishnah, not only do the rabbis permit to buy a Red Cow from a Gentile owner, they also allow to buy sacrificial animals from him. The question arises whether R. Eliezer will agree with this or not. The assumption here is that R. Eliezer will agree that sacrifices can be bought from Gentiles but not the Red Cow, whose ceremonies in pharisaic theory were made with many non-scriptural restrictions because of an important difference with Sadducees in matters of ritual purity (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Parah 3:7" href="/Mishnah_Parah.3.7">Mishnah <i>Parah</i>3:7</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Parah 3:8" href="/Mishnah_Parah.3.8">8</a>).</i>” But since the rabbis answer to Rebbi Eliezer <i>all sheep of Qedar will be assembled for you</i><sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Isaiah 60:7" href="/Isaiah.60.7"><i>Is.</i>60:7</a>. The verse ends: <i>they will be brought on My altar for pleasure</i>. This gives divine sanction for using animals raised by Gentiles for the altar. Since the verse speaks of sacrifices, not of the Red C ow, R. Eliezer must hold that sacrificial animals cannot be bought from Gentiles. The same argument is quoted in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 24a" href="/Avodah_Zarah.24a">Babli 24a</a>.</i>, this says that Rebbi Eliezer disagrees about everything. Rebbi Hoshaia asked, does one reply with an argument about the future against one about the past<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">The preceding argument does not prove anything in practice since in the next paragraph it will be shown that in messianic times the Gentiles will accept the Torah. Their animals may well be acceptable then but not now.</i>? Rebbi Abin asked, does one reply with an argument when evil inclinations will have disappeared against one when evil inclinations exist<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">This is the same argument as before.</i>?
Rebbi Hoshaia said<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">In G: asked. This paragraph interrupts the discussion of the Mishnah and turns to explaining the verse <a class="refLink" data-ref="Isaiah 60:7" href="/Isaiah.60.7"><i>Is.</i>60:7</a> quoted earlier.</i>, why do we not explain it according to everybody, as Rav Huna said in the name of Rav: <i>They weighed for me my (sheqels) [wages]</i><sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Zechariah 11:12" href="/Zechariah.11.12"><i>Sach.</i>11:12</a>. The quote is wrong in L (in parentheses), correct in G [in brackets]. In v. 13 the pieces of silver are declared to be “the Eternal’s treasure;” this explains the identification as saints.</i><i>, thirty pieces of silver</i>. These are the thirty commandments which the descendants of Noah will take upon themselves in the future<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">“In the future”, i. e, in the time of the Messiah. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bereishit Rabbah 98:14" href="/Bereishit_Rabbah.98.14"><i>Gen. rabba</i>98(14</a>) as opinion of R. Joḥanan. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Chullin 92a" href="/Chullin.92a">Babli, <i>Ḥulin</i>92a</a>, in one opinion 30 commandments which the Gentiles already took upon themselves, of which they keep only three; one of them being not to allow homosexual marriages.</i>. But the rabbis say, there are the 30 just persons which the world never will be lacking<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Bereishit Rabbah 35:2" href="/Bereishit_Rabbah.35.2"><i>Gen</i>. <i>rabba</i>35(2</a>) in the name of R. Simeon ben Ioḥai. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Chullin 92a" href="/Chullin.92a">Babli, <i>Ḥulin</i>92a</a>, in another opinion the 30 just Gentiles on whose merit the Gentile world exists. The Jewish world exists for the Babli on the merit of 36 just men (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Sukkah 45b" href="/Sukkah.45b"><i>Sukkah</i>45b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sanhedrin 97b" href="/Sanhedrin.97b"><i>Sanhedrin</i>97b</a>).</i>, as Rebbi Naḥman said in the name of Rebbi Mana, the world cannot be with less than 30 just men like our father Abraham. What is the reason? <i>Abraham will certainly be</i><sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Genesis 18:18" href="/Genesis.18.18"><i>Gen</i>. 18:18</a>.</i>, the numerical value of יהיה is 30<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">5+10+5+10=30.</i>. Sometimes the majority are in Babylonia and the minority in the Land of Israel; sometimes the majority are in the Land of Israel and the minority in Babylonia. It is a good sign for the world if most of them are in the Land<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Bereishit Rabbah 98:14" href="/Bereishit_Rabbah.98.14"><i>Gen. rabba</i>98(14</a>) as opinion of Rav.</i>. Rebbi Ḥiyya bar Julianus in the name of Rebbi Hoshaia: In the future the descendants of Noah will take all commandments upon themselves. What is the reason? <i>Then I shall transform Gentiles’ purified lips</i><sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Zephaniah 3:9" href="/Zephaniah.3.9"><i>Zeph.</i>3:9</a>. The verse ends: <i>They all are invoking the Eternal’s Name, to serve Him with united shoulder</i>, implying that the Gentiles will accept the Jewish style of worship. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Berakhot 57b" href="/Berakhot.57b">Babli <i>Berakhot</i>57b</a>, in the name of R. Simeon ben Ioḥai.</i>. But at the end they will renege on it. What is the reason? <i>Let us break their chains, and throw from us their ropes</i><sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Psalms 2:3" href="/Psalms.2.3"><i>Ps</i>. 2:3</a>; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 3b" href="/Avodah_Zarah.3b">Babli 3b</a>.</i>! That is the commandment of phylacteries<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Psalms 2:8" href="/Psalms.2.8"><i>Midrash Psalms</i>2(8</a>). The chains are the straps of the phylactery on the arm.</i>; that is the commandment of <i>ṣiṣit</i><sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">These are the “ropes”, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosafot on Avodah Zarah 3b:4:1" href="/Tosafot_on_Avodah_Zarah.3b.4.1"><i>Tosaphot Avodah zarah</i>3b <i>s. v.</i> ומניחין</a>.</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">One returns to the prior topic, to try to understand R. Eliezer’s position in view of biblical verses which seem to indicate that Gentile animals are perfectly acceptable as sacrifices.</i> Rebbi Isaac and Rebbi Immi were sitting and asking, is it not written<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="II Chronicles 15:11" href="/II_Chronicles.15.11"><i>2Chr.</i>15:11</a>. At first glance this refers to the booty which they took from the Nubians as described in Chapter 14 since no other war of Asa was mentioned.</i>: <i>On that day they slaughtered of the booty for the Eternal</i>? They explained it, but we do not know whether the colleagues explained it or Rebbi Immi explained it, from previous ones, from booty which was in their hands they sacrificed<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">Since Chapter 15 does not refer to the war described in Chapter 14, it is not necessary to assume that “the booty” was taken from the Nubians.</i>. But is it not written<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="I Samuel 6:15" href="/I_Samuel.6.15"><i>1S</i>. 6:15</a>, one the day the Philistines returned the Ark on a carriage drawn by cows.</i>: <i>The men of Bet Shemesh brought an elevation offering and slaughtered family offerings to the Eternal, oxen</i>? Can we learn anything from the rulers of the Philistines? Did not Rebbi Abbahu say in the name of Rebbi Yose ben Ḥanina, they even sacrificed females, <i>the cows they offered as elevation offering to the Eternal</i><sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="I Samuel 6:14" href="/I_Samuel.6.14"><i>1S</i>. 6:14</a>. In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 1:3" href="/Leviticus.1.3"><i>Lev</i>. 1:3</a> it is spelled out that an elevation offering must be a male animal. Since the entire proceedings were irregular, one cannot infer anything about the rules to follow.</i>. But is it not written<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="I Samuel 15:15" href="/I_Samuel.15.15"><i>1S</i>. 15:15</a>. As explained in <a class="refLink" data-ref="I Samuel 15:21" href="/I_Samuel.15.21">v. 21</a>, the animals taken from the Gentile tribe were to be slaughtered as sacrifices. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 24b" href="/Avodah_Zarah.24b">Babli 24b</a>.</i>, <i>Saul said, they brought them from the Amalekite</i>? One does not learn from Saul; as Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish said, Saul was a sycamore shoot<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">Something inedible. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 53" href="/Eruvin.53">Babli, <i>Eruvin</i>53a/b</a>, the verse <a class="refLink" data-ref="I Samuel 14:47" href="/I_Samuel.14.47"><i>1S</i>. 14:47</a> in interpreted that religious practice never follows Saul. The same is intended here.</i>. But it is not written<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="II Samuel 24:24" href="/II_Samuel.24.24"><i>2S</i>. 24:24</a>.</i>, <i>David bought the threshing floor</i>? He bought but did not sacrifice. But is it not written<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="II Samuel 24:23" href="/II_Samuel.24.23"><i>2S</i>. 24:23</a>. To give pleasure before the Eternal usually is asserted of sacrifices (e. g., <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 28:38" href="/Exodus.28.38"><i>Ex</i>. 28:38</a>). <a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 24b" href="/Avodah_Zarah.24b">Babli 24b</a>.</i>, <i>Arawna said to the king, may the Eternal, your God, have pleasure with you.</i> May he find pleasure with you in prayer. But is it not written<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 22:25" href="/Leviticus.22.25"><i>Lev</i>. 22:25</a>.</i>, <i>Also from the hand of a stranger you should not bring your God’s bread from any of these</i>? From any of these you do not sacrifice; you buy unblemished ones and sacrifice. What does Rebbi Eliezer do with this? You are buying for money and sacrifice? The one who stated “for money” cannot follow Rebbi Eliezer<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">Since R. Eliezer considers all animals of a Gentile unfit for the altar. But he must read the verse as absolute prohibition of accepting any sacrifice from a Gentile, similar to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sifra, Emor, Chapter 7 12" href="/Sifra,_Emor,_Chapter_7.12"><i>Sifra Emor Pereq</i>7(12</a>) where the verse is read to prohibit accepting money of the Temple tax from Gentiles.</i>.
It was stated:<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 14a" href="/Avodah_Zarah.14a">Babli 14a</a>, Tosephta 3:2.</i> “One does not put up animals in hostelries, even males next to males and females next to females; it is not necessary to say males next to females and females next to males.” Males next to males, what do you have? His beloved comes and she does not find him, then she is impregnated by it<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">By the animal. Since Gentiles are used to have pre- and extramarital sex, if one of the partners feels the need for sex but does not find his/her partner, he/she will take an animal as replacement to satisfy him/her. The Tosephta holds otherwise, <i>viz.,</i> that male animals attract homosexuals and females heterosexuals. If males are put next to females, animal copulation will stimulate Gentile voyeurs.</i>. Females next to females, what do you have? Her lover comes and does not find her and impregnates it. Rebbi Ḥaggai in the name of Rebbi Ze‘ira: Not only the Jew’s animal at a Gentile’s but even a Gentile’s animal at a Jew’s should not be left alone with another Gentile acquaintance, lest it cause him a mishap<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">The Jew would be the instrument of the second Gentile’s copulation with the first Gentile’s animal.</i>. But then even if he leased his animal it would not be nice to return it to be alone with him, lest it cause him a mishap? He is suspected of cohabiting with another’s animal; he is not suspected to have sexual relations with his own animal. For he knows that if he cohabits with her he makes her sterile; therefore he will not cohabit with her<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 15a" href="/Avodah_Zarah.15a">Babli 15a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 22b" href="/Avodah_Zarah.22b">22b</a>.</i>.
“But one may put up an animal in a Samaritan hostelry,”<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">Tosephta 3:1. The Note “etc.” implies that the entire Tosephta (or an equivalent Galilean <i>baraita</i>) should have been quoted.</i> etc. This implies that Samaritans are not suspected of forbidden relations. And it was stated thus<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Kiddushin (Lieberman) 5:9" href="/Tosefta_Kiddushin_(Lieberman).5.9">Tosephta <i>Qiddušin</i>5:9</a>.</i>: “A woman may be alone with two men<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">Two Jews. This excludes being alone with husband, father, son, and possibly brother (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 4:11:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.4.11.3"><i>Qiddušin</i>4:11, Notes 246–254</a>). <a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 25b" href="/Avodah_Zarah.25b">Babli 25b</a>.</i>, even if both are slaves<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">Converted and circumcised who belong to a Jewish <i>familia</i>.</i> or one a Samaritan and one a slave. But she should not be alone with a Gentile<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">This statement is obviously incomplete since a Jewish woman is not allowed to be alone with a single Jewish man, so certainly not with a single Gentile. One must read with the Tosephta and G, of which a new leaf starts here: “not even with a hundred Gentiles.”</i>. His mother-in-law, and his wife’s sister, and all other forbidden relations should be alone with him but require the presence of two [others]<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Kiddushin (Lieberman) 5:10" href="/Tosefta_Kiddushin_(Lieberman).5.10">Tosephta <i>Qiddušin</i>5:10</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Kiddushin 81b" href="/Kiddushin.81b">Babli <i>Qiddušin</i>81b</a>. These Babli sources have “sister” instead of “wife’s sister” but the Yerushalmi text is confirmed by G. In contrast to the Babli, the Yerushalmi both here and in <i>Qiddušin</i> unquestionably permits a man to be alone with his sister (but not to share a bed with her.)</i>.”
It was stated:<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Avodah Zarah 2:1-2" href="/Tosefta_Avodah_Zarah.2.1-2">Tosephta 2:4</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 15b" href="/Avodah_Zarah.15b">Babli 15b</a>.</i> “One sells them neither weapons nor accessories to weapons<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">Such as a sheath of a sword.</i>. One does not sharpen weapons for them.” Explain it in a town of only Gentiles<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">But in a town with Jewish inhabitants one may provide weapons for the security forces.</i>. Is a woman not in danger to be killed<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to the Mishnah which states that a woman is in danger to be raped but a man to be killed.</i>? Rebbi Immi<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">In G: R. Mana, the same in a quote in <i>Or zarua</i>. In the latter’s interpretation, a strong woman is one who would survive a gang rape. In a quote in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosafot on Avodah Zarah 25b:4:1" href="/Tosafot_on_Avodah_Zarah.25b.4.1">Tosaphot 25b s. v. איכא</a> R. Jeremiah.</i> said, explain it about a muscular one. Rebbi Abin said, even you may say [about a weak one.<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">Added from G. (The vocalization is from G.)</i>] A woman may hide herself and say, I am a Gentile, but a man cannot hide himself and say, he is a Gentile<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">Since he is circumcized.</i>.
It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">Tosephta 3:4, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 25b" href="/Avodah_Zarah.25b">Babli 25b</a>.</i>: “If a Gentile is paired with a Jew, he puts him on his right hand side. Rebbi Ismael ben Rebbi Yose says, with a sword, at his right; with a staff, at his left<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">Since the sword is girded at his right side and the staff held in his left hand. The Gentile should not have space to swing his weapon.</i>. If he climbs with him to an upper floor or descends to the exit, the Jew should be higher and the Gentile below. He should not lie down before him lest he smite his skull. And he should extend the way to him, that if he asks him where he goes he should indicate a farther distance, as our father Jacob did with Esau, <i>until I shall come to my Lord at Seir</i><sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Genesis 33:14" href="/Genesis.33.14"><i>Gen</i>. 33:14</a>.</i>. But he went to Sukkot<i>.</i> Rebbi Huna said, we do not find that our father Jacob went to Seir. Rebbi Yudan, son of Rebbi [Aivo]<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">Added from G, missing in L.</i>, he spoke to him of the future world, <i>when saviors will climb on Mount Sion to judge Mount Esau.</i><sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ob</i>. 21.</i>”
<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote">Tosephta 3:3, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 26a" href="/Avodah_Zarah.26a">Babli 26a</a>.</i>“A Jewish woman should not be a midwife for a Non-Jew, for she brings a son<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote">This is the reading in all sources except G which reads גוי “Gentile” instead of בן “son”. Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Avodah Zarah 1:6:5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Avodah_Zarah.1.6.5">Chapter 1, Note 177</a>.</i> to pagan worship; but a Non-Jew may be a midwife for a Jewish woman.” It was stated so: From the outside but not the inside. She should not put her hand inside not to squish the fetus in her innards, and she should not give her a cup of root-extract to drink<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote">A concoction used to prevent pregnancies. The obligation of having children uniquely is on the male (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yevamot 6:6:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yevamot.6.6.1">Mishnah <i>Yebamot</i>6:6</a>); therefore a woman is permitted to use chemical birth control methods which do not change the sex act. Since Gentiles are suspect as potential murderers such a potion may be taken only from a Jewish source or, as indicated later in this paragraph, also from a duly qualified Gentile professional.</i>. If she was a medical doctor? This will come as what Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa said in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: If he was a professional healer it is permitted<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 27a" href="/Avodah_Zarah.27a">Babli 27a</a>, also in the name of R. Joḥanan. He has to worry about his professional reputation.</i>. And here, if she was a medical doctor it is permitted. [Rebbi Yose bar Abun, these questions … [doc]tor permitted.<sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote">Fragment in G of a sentence missing in L and the <i>editio princeps</i>; probably a confirmation of the permission to use Gentile certified medical professionals.</i>]
“A Jewish woman should not nurse the child of a Non-Jewish woman” because she gives it life<sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote">In Tosephta 3:3 “because she raises a son for pagan worship” as in the preceding paragraph. G is defective at this point.</i>. Rebbi Yose said, this implies that one may not teach him a trade<sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote">In order to live one has to make a living. Therefore it is a religious duty for a man to see to it that his son learn a trade; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 4:12:2-3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.4.12.2-3"><i>Qiddušin</i>4:12</a>.</i>. As the following: Two trades, glaziers and carpenters<sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote">A hybrid of Greek κιβωτός “box, chest” (in LXX used to translate תֵּיבָה) and the Latin suffix -<i>arius</i>“craftsman of …” There exists a Greek word κιβωτάριον but this is a diminutive, “small box or chamber” and does not designate a profession.<br>Jastrow explains as “maker of pickles” but this is a gross misreading of κιβώτιον in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Terumot 10:2:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Terumot.10.2.4"><i>Terumot</i>10:3, Note 36</a>.</i> were at (Giro) [Acco]<sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote">No place Giro (ms. L) is known. (If one would read גידו the place would be Jedda in Arabia.) Acco (reading of G) was known as a city divided between Jews and Gentiles, only partially within the borders of the Land; cf. Ševi‘it 6:1 Note. 30.</i>. The glaziers did not teach<sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote">They did not accept Gentile apprentices.</i> and continued, the carpenters taught and were pushed out.
“But a Non-Jewish woman may nurse the child of a Jewish woman” as it is written, <i>their kings will be your pedagogues and their princesses your wet nurses</i><sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Isaiah 49:23" href="/Isaiah.49.23"><i>Is</i>. 49:23</a>, giving divine sanction to Gentile wet nurses.</i><i>.</i> It was stated: A baby nurses continuously from a Non-Jewish woman or from a non-kosher animal and one brings him milk from anywhere and does not worry either about abomination or because of impurity<sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote">The Babylonian attitude is expressed in Tosephta <i>Šabbat</i>9:22: One does not nurse from a Non-Jewish woman or from a non-kosher animal but if it was a matter of survival nothing stands before saving lives.</i>.

Halakhah 2

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> One may be healed from the healing of property but not healing of persons. One may not be shaved from them anywhere<sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote">Since the barber easily may cut the client’s throat with his knife.</i>, the words of Rebbi Meïr; but the Sages say it is permitted in the public domain but not in private.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “One may be healed from the healing of property,” etc. Rebbi Jacob bar Zavdi in the name of Rebbi Abbahu: “healing of property,” his animal. “Healing of persons,” his body<sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 27a" href="/Avodah_Zarah.27a">Babli 27a</a>.</i>. Rebbi Abba in the name of Rav Jehudah: If it was scratched it is forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">73</sup><i class="footnote">Babli <i>loc. cit</i>. He does not permit any Gentile doctor, even qualified, to do any surgical procedure on the body of a Jew; only medication can be accepted.<br>The translation reads here נחמס as in the sequel, not נחמם “developed a fever.” G is missing at this passage.</i>. As in the following case: Rebbi Immi went with Rebbi Jehudah the Prince to the hot springs of Gader<sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote">Where usually the intercalation of a month was announced.</i>. He hurt his finger and put a bandage<sup class="footnote-marker">75</sup><i class="footnote">Latin <i>splenium</i>“pad, wound dressing”; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Orlah 3:1:5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Orlah.3.1.5"><i>Orlah</i>3:1 Note 20</a>.</i> on it. He saw it penetrate and cut it off<sup class="footnote-marker">76</sup><i class="footnote">Arabic قلم “to cut”. The feminine suffix shows that the bandage (f.) was cut off, not the finger (m.).</i>. Did he not hear what Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa said in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: If he was a professional healer it is permitted<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 27a" href="/Avodah_Zarah.27a">Babli 27a</a>, also in the name of R. Joḥanan. He has to worry about his professional reputation.</i>? This is not comparable. There if it was scratched it is permitted, but here it was scratched and is forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">77</sup><i class="footnote">Deriving חמס from a root like Arabic خمش “to scratch”. He could not go to the local Gentile doctor because he needed surgical intervention which according to Rav Jehudah is forbidden; R. Joḥanan permits only non-surgical interventions.</i>.
May one have one’s eyelids painted by them<sup class="footnote-marker">78</sup><i class="footnote">Applying <i>koḥl</i>, originally the Aramaic/Arabic name for antimony, then used for any paste applied in ophthalmology.</i>? Rav said, anybody who wants to be blinded may be blinded. Levi said, anybody who wants to die may die<sup class="footnote-marker">79</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Niddah 55b" href="/Niddah.55b">Babli <i>Niddah</i>55b</a>.</i>. Rav never had his eye painted; Levi had his eye painted. Rebbi Abba said, but do we not see that they taste this <i>collyrium</i><sup class="footnote-marker">80</sup><i class="footnote">Greek κολλύρα “eye salve”.</i>? We may say that they taste it if it is good. If it is no good it keeps and blinds. Opium<sup class="footnote-marker">81</sup><i class="footnote">Greek ὄπιον.</i> is dangerous. Treacle<sup class="footnote-marker">82</sup><i class="footnote">Greek θηριακή, <i>scil.</i> ἀντίδοτος, “antidote against a poisonous bite.”</i>, Rebbi Simon says it is forbidden; Rebbi Joḥanan<sup class="footnote-marker">83</sup><i class="footnote">In G: E. Immi. This seems a more trustworthy reading.</i> says it is permitted.
<sup class="footnote-marker">83a</sup><i class="footnote">From here to almost the end of the Halakhah there is a parallel in Šabbat 14:4 (ש); in addition the present paragraph still is available in a Genizah text (G). If these two sources present a common reading different from the text here, one may assume that the text here is in error, either by the scribe or in the text from which he copied.</i> The colleagues in the name of Rebbi Abba bar Zavda: Anything inside of the lips one heals on the Sabbath<sup class="footnote-marker">84</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 28a" href="/Avodah_Zarah.28a">Babli, 28a</a>, this is the position of Rebbi Immi. Since medical practice usually involves activities biblically forbidden on the Sabbath, such as compounding medicines and ointments or surgical interventions, also healing activities which do not involve these prohibitions are rabbinically forbidden on the Sabbath. However, if a condition is life-threatening, Sabbath prohibitions do not apply. A declaration that “one heals such-and-such a condition on the Sabbath” is the equivalent of declaring the condition as life-threatening.</i>. Rebbi Ze‘ira objected, did we not state<sup class="footnote-marker">85</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shabbat 14:1:2-4:14" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shabbat.14.1.2-4.14">Mishnah <i>Šabbat</i>14:4</a>. While one is permitted to drink (diluted) vinegar on the Sabbath, one may not use it as a pain killer since this is medical treatment of a situation which in general is not life-threatening.</i>: “A person having a tooth ache should not suck vinegar with them”? Is this not inside from the lips? Rebbi Ze‘ira did not say so, but Rebbi Ze‘ira in the name of Rebbi Abba bar Zavda: Anything inside a body cavity one heals on the Sabbath<sup class="footnote-marker">86</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 27b-28a" href="/Avodah_Zarah.27b-28a">Babli 27b/28a</a>, in the name of R. Joḥanan.</i>. Rebbi Ze‘ira, Rebbi Abba bar Zuṭra, Rebbi Ḥanina in the name of Rebbi: One treats the bone of the skull on the Sabbath<sup class="footnote-marker">87</sup><i class="footnote">Any injury to the skull bones.</i>. Rebbi Ḥiyya the Mede, Rebbi Jona, Rebbi Ze‘ira, Rebbi Abba bar Zuṭra, Rebbi Ḥanina in the name of Rebbi: One treats glands of the throat<sup class="footnote-marker">88</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 28b" href="/Avodah_Zarah.28b">Babli 28b</a>. The translation used here of “daughters of the ear” is an interpretation of Rashi’s explanation in the Babli: “Sinews of the ear which sometimes are lowered and keep the jaws open; then it is necessary to lift them and this is dangerous.”</i> on the Sabbath. Rebbi Abbahu in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: One treats an infected eye<sup class="footnote-marker">89</sup><i class="footnote">“An eye which rebelled”: it feels as if the eye would leave its place. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 28b" href="/Avodah_Zarah.28b">Babli 28b</a>.</i> on the Sabbath. There, they say in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: Tops of hands and feet are a danger<sup class="footnote-marker">90</sup><i class="footnote">Any wounds at these places are considered life-threatening. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 28a" href="/Avodah_Zarah.28a">Babli 28a</a>.</i>. Rebbi Abbahu in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: Red color is dangerous<sup class="footnote-marker">91</sup><i class="footnote">Dark red color of a wound is indication of a life-threatening infection.</i>. Rebbi Abin said, one removes the sting of a scorpion on the Sabbath. Rav said, wine for exterior treatment of the eye is permitted, inside the eye it is forbidden. Samuel said, tasteless spit is forbidden for the eye on the Sabbath<sup class="footnote-marker">92</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Shabbat 108b" href="/Shabbat.108b">Babli <i>Šabbat</i>108b</a>, with the names of Rav and Samuel switched. Since people sometimes put wine on their eyelids for non-medical reasons, it is permitted to do the same for minor aches for which medical intervention would be forbidden on the Sabbath.</i> From this you infer for lichen<sup class="footnote-marker">93</sup><i class="footnote">A skin disease, purely external.</i>. The rabbis of Caesarea said, <i>ranula</i><sup class="footnote-marker">94</sup><i class="footnote">“Frog” (Aramaic), Latin <i>ranula,</i> an infection of the mouth.</i> is dangerous. Rebbi Ḥizqiah (said) [from Acco]<sup class="footnote-marker">95</sup><i class="footnote">The text in parentheses is from the Leiden ms., the one in brackets from G and <b>ש</b>.</i> in the name of the rabbis of Caesarea: spider sickness<sup class="footnote-marker">96</sup><i class="footnote">This may either describe the bite of a spider or more likely a cancerous growth looking like a spider.</i> is dangerous. Rebbi Samuel bar Rav Isaac: gangrene is dangerous. Rebbi Jeremiah said, one can put sour dough on it on Passover<sup class="footnote-marker">97</sup><i class="footnote">Using leavened matter on Passover is a deadly sin but for medical purposes in life threatening situations it may be used without hesitation.</i>. A boil is permitted.<sup class="footnote-marker">98</sup><i class="footnote">A boil filled with pus may be opened on the Sabbath.</i> Rebbi Yose said, the Mishnah says this<sup class="footnote-marker">99</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shabbat 17:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shabbat.17.2.1">Mishnah <i>Šabbat</i>17:2</a>. As a matter of principle one may move a vessel on the Sabbath only if it is of any use on this day. A sewing needle cannot be used for its usual function on the Sabbath but may be used to remove a thorn in one’s foot or to open a boil.</i>: “A small needle to remove a thorn.” Otherwise, what is the difference between a thorn and a boil? Darkening of the eye<sup class="footnote-marker">100</sup><i class="footnote">This may describe a cataract or glaucoma.</i>, they asked Rebbi Jeremiah. He told them, is not Rebbi Abba available for you? They asked Rebbi Abba who permitted it to them. He<sup class="footnote-marker">101</sup><i class="footnote">R. Jeremiah. G ends here.</i> told them, also I am permitting it.
<sup class="footnote-marker">102</sup><i class="footnote">It seems that the original of this paragraph is in <i>Šabbat</i>14:4. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 28a" href="/Avodah_Zarah.28a">Babli 28a</a>.</i> Rebbi Abbahu in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan. Scurvy<sup class="footnote-marker">103</sup><i class="footnote">This is the meaning of צַפְדִּינָה in modern Hebrew. The different vocalization here is based on the text in <i>Šabbat</i>. Rashi in the Babli explains the word as French <i>muguet</i>, English thrush, a sickness producing a musk like smell of the mouth.</i> is dangerous. Rebbi Joḥanan had it; he was treated by the daughter of Domitianus<sup class="footnote-marker">104</sup><i class="footnote">Translation of the text in <i>Šabbat.</i> Here “Domitianus’s”. If they are professionals, female doctors are as acceptable as male.</i> of Tiberias. Friday evening he went down to her<sup class="footnote-marker">105</sup><i class="footnote">In <i>Šabbat</i>14:4, “went up.” In Tiberias, one goes up or down, almost never level.</i> and asked her, will I need anything tomorrow? She said no, but if you need anything take date pits, some say of Nicolaus dates, split and heated, skin<sup class="footnote-marker">106</sup><i class="footnote">Bran.</i> of barley grain, and dry excrement of a baby, grind it and apply it; do not tell this to anybody. The next day he went up and preached it in the House of Study<sup class="footnote-marker">107</sup><i class="footnote">In <i>Šabbat</i>14:4 “in public”, which is the better version.</i>. She heard it and strangled herself; but some say that she converted<sup class="footnote-marker">108</sup><i class="footnote">Either she committed suicide because she lost her trade secret or she converted because she admired R. Joḥanan who made the recipe public and did not try to make money through his knowledge.</i>. You understand from this three things. You understand that scurvy is dangerous. You understand that anything inside of the lips one heals on the Sabbath<sup class="footnote-marker">84,</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 28a" href="/Avodah_Zarah.28a">Babli, 28a</a>, this is the position of Rebbi Immi. Since medical practice usually involves activities biblically forbidden on the Sabbath, such as compounding medicines and ointments or surgical interventions, also healing activities which do not involve these prohibitions are rabbinically forbidden on the Sabbath. However, if a condition is life-threatening, Sabbath prohibitions do not apply. A declaration that “one heals such-and-such a condition on the Sabbath” is the equivalent of declaring the condition as life-threatening.</i><sup class="footnote-marker">109</sup><i class="footnote">Since R. Joḥanan acted on this, it is practice notwithstanding R. Ze‘ira’s criticism.</i>. You understand what Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa said in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: If he was a professional healer it is permitted.
Rebbi Joshua ben Levy was suffering from colic<sup class="footnote-marker">110</sup><i class="footnote">Greek κῶλον, “gut, disease of the colon.”</i>; Rebbi Ḥanina and Rebbi Jonathan instructed him to grind <i>taḥlusin</i><sup class="footnote-marker">111</sup><i class="footnote">This probably is a plural of תּוּכְלָא “hard unripe date” which can be ground rather than תִּחְלֵי “cress, nasturtium” which could be cut but not ground.</i>, put it in old wine and drink it to avoid becoming endangered<sup class="footnote-marker">112</sup><i class="footnote">Since this is not a usual drink; its use on the Sabbath is rabbinically prohibited but to avoid future danger this prohibition can be waived. R. Joshua ben Levy was himself at least as qualified as the other two authorities to decide but he did not want to apply a leniency to himself on his own word.</i>.
His<sup class="footnote-marker">113</sup><i class="footnote">R. Joshua ben Levi’s grandson.</i> grandson suffered from choking; there came one, whispered something in the name of Jesus ben Pandera<sup class="footnote-marker">114</sup><i class="footnote">This name (or <i>Panṭera</i>) is unexplained. [Perhaps a distortion of Greek πανταρκής, Latin <i>Pantarces</i>, “all-helping”, a surname of Jupiter (E. G.)].</i> and he could breathe. When he left, he asked him<sup class="footnote-marker">115</sup><i class="footnote">R. Joshua ban Levi asked the Christian missionary.</i>, what did you say over him? He answered, such and such words. He said, it would have been better for him had he died and not heard these words. It happened to him<sup class="footnote-marker">116</sup><i class="footnote">The grandson died.</i>, like an erroneous order from a ruler.
Rebbi Jacob [bar Idi]<sup class="footnote-marker">117</sup><i class="footnote">From the text in <i>Šabbat</i>, needed for chronological reasons. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 25a" href="/Pesachim.25a">Babli, <i>Pesaḥim</i>25a</a>, the tradition is by R. Abin (Abun) in the name of R. Joḥanan.</i> in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: One heals with anything except pagan worship, uncovering nakednesses, and spilling blood<sup class="footnote-marker">118</sup><i class="footnote">The three sins which one is not permitted to commit even in order to save one’s life. Since Christian faith healing is rejected before pagan healing is discussed it is clear that it is not rejected as pagan but as falsely claiming to be Jewish.</i>. Rebbi Phineas asked: So far if he said, bring me leaves from pagan worship, and he brought him. If he said to him bring me leaves (unspecified) and he brought him from pagan worship? Let us hear from the following: Rebbi Aḥa had a fever attack. They brought him from the penis of Dohi<sup class="footnote-marker">119</sup><i class="footnote">A pagan statue with a spout in form of a penis, like Brussel’s Manneken Piss.</i> but he did not drink. They brought to Rebbi Jonah and he drank. Rebbi Mana said, if my father Rebbi Jonah had known from where it was, he would not have drunk<sup class="footnote-marker">120</sup><i class="footnote">Therefore healing by idolatrous materials is forbidden even if not specifically asked for.</i>.
Rebbi Huna said, (this means) [a <i>baraita</i> implies]<sup class="footnote-marker">121</sup><i class="footnote">Since the proof follows the statement, the text in brackets, from <i>Šabbat</i>, has to be preferred over the text here (in parentheses).</i> that one does not heal through uncovering nakedness<sup class="footnote-marker">122</sup><i class="footnote">The technical term for criminal acts of a sexual nature, including but not restricted to incest and adultery.</i>, as it was stated: The Sabbath was permitted in exceptional cases; the betrothed maiden was never permitted in exceptional cases<sup class="footnote-marker">123</sup><i class="footnote">The laws of the Sabbath are suspended in the Temple where the prescribed sacrifices are slaughtered and burned, actions which outside would be capital crimes. But a preliminarily married woman is absolutely forbidden for any man without exception.</i>. Was not the Sabbath permitted in exceptional cases for healing? Therefore the betrothed maiden was never permitted, not even for healing. Not only if one said to another, bring me a married woman, but even to hear her voice, as the following<sup class="footnote-marker">124</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Sanhedrin 75a" href="/Sanhedrin.75a">Babli <i>Sanhedrin</i>75a</a>. The text in the Babli makes it clear that R. Eleazar here is the Tanna, ben Shamua.</i>: In the days of Rebbi Eleazar a man loved a woman and fell dangerously ill. They came and asked Rebbi Eleazar, should she parade before him that he may live? He said to them, he should die but not this. May he hear her voice and live? He said to them, he should die but not this. How was it? Rebbi Jacob bar Idi and Rebbi Isaac bar Naḥman, one said, she was a married woman, but one said, she was single. One who said that she was a married woman we understand. But the one who said that she was single?
