Did not Bar Koḥa the carpenter<sup class="footnote-marker">125</sup><i class="footnote">It is possible that Ben Koḥa is not a proper name but means “strongman”.</i> love a woman in the days of Rebbi Eleazar and (became dangerously sick) [and he permitted him]<sup class="footnote-marker">126</sup><i class="footnote">The text in brackets is from <i>Šabbat</i>, to be preferred over the text here in parentheses. The main point is that R. Eleazar permitted the man to date the woman and to contract a marriage which was neither pre-arranged nor brokered.</i>? One case about a married woman and the other about a single one. Even you may say, here and here about a single one, here and here about a married woman. Explain it that he became infatuated with her when she still was married. Some want to say, she was a woman of substance (and did not listen to him) [and did not want to marry.]<sup class="footnote-marker">127</sup><i class="footnote">The text in brackets is from <i>Šabbat</i>, to be preferred over the text here in parentheses. In the Babli they hold that the man was not interested in marriage at all, only in sex.</i> Everything which he did was forbidden; therefore he did permit nothing to him.
Rebbi Ḥanina said, (this means) [a <i>baraita</i> implies]<sup class="footnote-marker">121</sup><i class="footnote">Since the proof follows the statement, the text in brackets, from <i>Šabbat</i>, has to be preferred over the text here (in parentheses).</i> that one does not heal through spilling blood, as we have stated there<sup class="footnote-marker">128</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Oholot 7:6" href="/Mishnah_Oholot.7.6">Mishnah <i>Ahilut</i>7:6</a>. The Mishnah states that if a child during childbirth endangers the life of the mother one saves the mother’s life by cutting the fetus into pieces. But if head and part of the body are already born so that the baby breathes on his own one may not harm him. Maimonides in his Mishnah Commentary notes “all this is obvious and does not need commentary.”</i>: “If most of his body was outside one does not touch him,” we suspect that he will live<sup class="footnote-marker">129</sup><i class="footnote">Even though the baby might be stillborn (this includes the possibility that he might not live for a full thirty days after birth, in which case a person killing the baby could not be prosecuted for murder), if actually he is breathing on his own he must be treated as certainly being alive.</i>, “for one does not push aside one life before another life.” Not only if one would say, kill this person, but even if he tell him, injure that person. A Gentile against a Gentile, a Gentile against a Jew, is liable<sup class="footnote-marker">130</sup><i class="footnote">Is prosecutable in a Jewish court.</i>. A Jew against a Gentile is not liable<sup class="footnote-marker">131</sup><i class="footnote">Is not prosecutable by biblical rules, only by the king’s police powers (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sanhedrin 6:3:6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sanhedrin.6.3.6"><i>Sanhedrin</i>6:5 Note 75</a>).</i>. Rav Ḥisda asked, may one save the life of an adult with the life of a minor? Rebbi Jeremiah objected, did we not state, “If most of his body was outside one does not touch him, for one does not push aside one life before another life”? Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun in the name of Rebbi Levi:<sup class="footnote-marker">132</sup><i class="footnote">In <i>Šabbat</i>: Rav Ḥisda.</i> There it is different for it is not known who is endangering whom.
<sup class="footnote-marker">133</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 27b" href="/Avodah_Zarah.27b">Babli 27b</a>. According to this source, ben Dama was the son of R. Ismael’s sister.</i> It happened that Eleazar ben Dama was bitten by a snake and Jacob from Kefar-Sama came to heal him (,and told him, I shall speak to you)<sup class="footnote-marker">134</sup><i class="footnote">Addition in the text here.</i> in the name of Jesus ben Pandera. Rebbi Ismael said to him, Ben Dama you are not allowed. He told him, I shall bring a proof that he can heal me. He could not bring proof before he died. Rebbi Ismael said to him, you are blessed, ben Dama, that you left this world in peace and did not tear down the fences of the Sages, (to confirm)<sup class="footnote-marker">134</sup><i class="footnote">Addition in the text here.</i> as it is written<sup class="footnote-marker">135</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Ecclesiastes 10:8" href="/Ecclesiastes.10.8"><i>Eccl</i>. 10:8</a>. The fence is a stone wall without mortar with holes in which a snake may hide.</i>, <i>he who tears down a fence will be bitten by a snake</i>. But did not a snake bite him? But that it will not bite him in the Future World<sup class="footnote-marker">136</sup><i class="footnote">The mythical snake which seduced Eve.</i>. What could he have said? <i>Which a person should do and live by them</i><sup class="footnote-marker">137</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 18:5" href="/Leviticus.18.5"><i>Lev</i>. 18:5</a>.</i>.
“One who cuts a Gentile’s hair cuts it until he comes to the lock<sup class="footnote-marker">138</sup><i class="footnote">A word of undetermined etymology which R. Ḥananel in his <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sanhedrin 21a" href="/Sanhedrin.21a">Commentary to <i>Sanhedrin</i>21a</a> explains by Arabic دوابة which means both a toupee and a mane; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Avodah Zarah 1:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Avodah_Zarah.1.3.1">Chapter 1, Note 105</a>. Levy suggests Latin <i>vellera crinis</i>.</i>. Once he comes to the lock he desists. One who gets a haircut from a Gentile looks in the mirror<sup class="footnote-marker">139</sup><i class="footnote">To make sure that the Gentile will not cut his throat. In general, for a man to look in a mirror to check on his grooming is rabbinically forbidden as “woman’s garb” (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 22:5" href="/Deuteronomy.22.5"><i>Deut</i>. 22:5</a>). <a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 29a" href="/Avodah_Zarah.29a">Babli 29a</a>.</i>, from a Samaritan he does not look into the mirror<sup class="footnote-marker">140</sup><i class="footnote">Since they are (Sadducee) Jews, there is no reason to allow looking in a mirror.</i>. They permitted the family of Rebbi<sup class="footnote-marker">141</sup><i class="footnote">The Patriarch’s family, the official representative of the Jews vis-a-vis the Roman Government from the Severan Emperors to Theodosius II.</i> that they might look, because they were in need of Roman government connections.<sup class="footnote-marker">142</sup><i class="footnote">Tosephta 3:4.</i>” Three things they permitted the House of Rebbi, that they might look into a mirror, that they got a haircut with a lock<sup class="footnote-marker">143</sup><i class="footnote">Greek κόμη.</i>, and that they taught their children Greek<sup class="footnote-marker">144</sup><i class="footnote">Not simply the Greek language which every trader had to master but Greek education, literature, and philosophy, which are intrinsically pagan. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 49b" href="/Sotah.49b">Babli <i>Soṭah</i> 49b</a>.</i>, because they were in need of Roman government connections.
A convert was a barber and astrologer<sup class="footnote-marker">145</sup><i class="footnote">Greek ἀστρόλογος, ἀστρολογία.</i>. He saw in his astrology<sup class="footnote-marker">145</sup><i class="footnote">Greek ἀστρόλογος, ἀστρολογία.</i> that Jews were spilling his blood. This only referred to his circumcision but when a Jew came to him to get a haircut, he killed him. How many did he kill? Rebbi Eleazar ben Rebbi Yose said eighty. Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun said three hundred. At the end they prayed about him and he returned to his origin<sup class="footnote-marker">146</sup><i class="footnote">Then Jews who came to his place had to watch in a mirror what he was doing.</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">147</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 4:6:5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.4.6.5"><i>Giṭtin</i> 4:6, Notes 170–172</a>.</i> It was stated: One does not buy from a Gentile who sells Torah scrolls, phylacteries, and <i>mezuzzot</i><sup class="footnote-marker">148</sup><i class="footnote">This opposes a <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Avodah Zarah 3:2" href="/Tosefta_Avodah_Zarah.3.2">Tosephta (3:6</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 45b" href="/Gittin.45b">Babli <i>Giṭtin</i> 45b</a>) which permits to buy these from a Gentile trader if one is able to check that they are written according to the special rules. The problem is that one may be able to verify that all letters were written correctly but it is impossible to verify that the leather (or in later technology the parchment) was prepared correctly and that they were written by a Jew obligated to have one of these. The latter requirement is indispensable.</i>. <sup class="footnote-marker">149</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Avodah Zarah 3:2" href="/Tosefta_Avodah_Zarah.3.2">Tosephta 3:7</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 45b" href="/Gittin.45b">Babli <i>Giṭtin</i> 45b</a>.</i>“It happened that a Gentile in Sidon was selling phylacteries, Torah scrolls, and <i>mezuzzot</i>. The case came before the Sages who ruled that one might buy from him.” Rebbi Samuel ben Naḥman in the name of Rebbi Ḥama bar Ḥanina: This was a convert who returned to his origin<sup class="footnote-marker">150</sup><i class="footnote">Since conversion to Judaism is irreversible, even if he starts following pagan rites again he remains obligated to wear phylacteries, to affix a <i>mezuzzah</i> to his doorpost, and to study Torah, i. e., also empowered to write it.</i>.

Halakhah 3

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> The following Gentile properties are forbidden and the prohibition is of usufruct: Wine<sup class="footnote-marker">151</sup><i class="footnote">One may assume that the Gentile before drinking the wine made a libation to the gods. If done for a full barrel, it makes all the wine in the barrel forbidden as חֶרֶם (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 13:18" href="/Deuteronomy.13.18"><i>Deut</i>. 13:18</a>) which biblically is forbidden for all usufruct.</i>, and vinegar which originally was wine<sup class="footnote-marker">152</sup><i class="footnote">Even though it is no longer wine and cannot be used for libations, since it remains usable the original prohibition is not removed.</i>, and Hadrianic pottery,<sup class="footnote-marker">153</sup><i class="footnote">Very porous earthenware which is soaked in wine to absorb it. It is taken on caravan trips to give a taste of wine to water taken on the trip.</i> and heart-removed skins<sup class="footnote-marker">154</sup><i class="footnote">Skins of the belly which show that the heart was removed before the animal was skinned. This is done only in pagan rituals; the animal including any parts not used in pagan worship is חֶרֶם.</i>. Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel says, if the tear is circular they are forbidden, oblong permitted. Meat brought into a pagan Temple is permitted but what leaves is forbidden because it is sacrifice to the dead, the words of Rebbi Aqiba<sup class="footnote-marker">155</sup><i class="footnote">The expression “sacrifice to the dead” is from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Psalms 106:26" href="/Psalms.106.26"><i>Ps</i>. 106:26</a>; it describes all pagan sacrifices.</i>. It is forbidden to trade with those who go to ugliness<sup class="footnote-marker">156</sup><i class="footnote">This is the meaning of the word in other contexts. The meaning here is not uncontested, cf. Notes 220 ff.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “The following Gentile properties are forbidden,” etc. <sup class="footnote-marker">157</sup><i class="footnote">This paragraph and many of the following have a parallel in <i>Terumot</i>8:5 (Notes 82–111). At places the text here is a reformulation, not a direct copy, of the text there; though it is not excluded that both might represent the same original by two very different paths of copying.</i> Rebbi Isaac bar Naḥman in the name of Rebbi Joshua ben Levi: Bitter, sweet, and hot are not under the rules of being uncovered<sup class="footnote-marker">158</sup><i class="footnote">In both Talmudim it is assumed that a snake will drink from uncovered unattended fluids and in the process poison the drink for humans. Then the drink cannot be used, neither can it be sold since this would be “putting a stone in the path of a blind person.” This makes uncovered unattended wine almost as forbidden as wine used for pagan practices.</i>. Rebbi Simon in the name of Rebbi Joshua ben Levi: The hot, the bitter, and the sweet are not under the rules of being uncovered or under those of libation wine<sup class="footnote-marker">159</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 30a" href="/Avodah_Zarah.30a">Babli 30a</a>. “Libation wine” is the technical term for Gentile wine which was used for pagan libations or other pagan ceremonies and therefore is forbidden for all usufruct.</i>. Rebbi Simon explains: The hot, spice wine<sup class="footnote-marker">160</sup><i class="footnote">Latin (<i>vinum</i>) <i>conditum</i>.</i>. The bitter, vermouth wine<sup class="footnote-marker">161</sup><i class="footnote">Greek ἀψίνθιον, “wormwood, <i>Artemisia Absinthium.”</i></i>. And the sweet, cooked wine. Rebbi Joshua ben Zeidal had cooked wine which was left unsupervised in the custudy of a Gentile. He asked Rebbi Yannai ben Rebbi Ismael who said to him, so says Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish: The sweet is not under the rules of being uncovered or under those of libation wine. Rebbi Yannai ben Rebbi Ismael fell ill. Rebbi Ze`ira, Rebbi (Joshua) [Hoshaia]<sup class="footnote-marker">162</sup><i class="footnote">The text in brackets is the reading of the text in <i>Terumot</i>; it is preferred since no Amora “R. Joshua” without patronymic is known.</i>, Rebbi Abun bar Cahana, and Rebbi Ḥanianiah the colleague of the rabbis, went to visit him. They saw Rebbi Joshua ben Zeidal sitting there. They said, there is the master of the decision and the person of the happening, let us ask him. He said to them, so says Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish, the sweet is not under the rules of being uncovered or under those of libation wine. One said to him, maybe that of Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish is for argument’s sake? He told them, it is for action and you can rely on it. When (they got up) [returned]<sup class="footnote-marker">163</sup><i class="footnote">The text in brackets is the reading of the text in <i>Terumot</i>, the one in parenthesis is the text here.</i>, Rebbi Ila was standing with Rebbi Abun bar Cahana and told him, if you did not prefer decisions, is this not a <i>baraita</i>? As Rebbi Ḥiyya stated, “why is cooked wine of Gentiles forbidden? Because it started out as wine.<sup class="footnote-marker">164</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 29b" href="/Avodah_Zarah.29b">Babli 29b</a>. The statement implies that Jewish cooked wine (pasteurized grape juice) cannot become forbidden after being handled by a Gentile.</i>” Rebbi Yose said, the Mishnah says so, “wine and vinegar which originally was wine<sup class="footnote-marker">165</sup><i class="footnote">But anything manufactured from wine that was not originally forbidden does not become forbidden; same consequence as in the preceding Note.</i>.”
<sup class="footnote-marker">166</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Terumot</i> 8:5 Notes 91–94.</i> Rebbi Immi had guests. He said to them, if my cooked wine had not been left uncovered, would I not have served you? Rebbi Vivian said, bring it and we shall drink. He answered, he who wants to die should go and die in his own house.
Bar Yudani had uncovered spice wine. He went and asked the rabbis who forbade it. But did not Rebbi Isaac bar Naḥman say in the name of Rebbi Joshua ben Levi: The hot, the bitter, and the sweet, are not under the rules of being uncovered. The rabbis of Caesarea in the name of Rebbi Judah bar Titus: About one which consists of one to three parts ground [spices]<sup class="footnote-marker">167</sup><i class="footnote">One part spices for every three parts wine (one fourth of the volume) is the minimal concentration for which R. Joshua ben Levi’s rule may be applied.</i>.
They asked before Rebbi Abbahu, what is the rule for uncovered cooked [wine]? He told them, Rebbi Joḥanan had a problem with <i>carenum</i><sup class="footnote-marker">168</sup><i class="footnote">Latin <i>carenum</i>, Greek κάροινον, wine which by cooking has lost one third of its volume. Since the alcohol-producing bacteria die at 13% alcohol by volume, if the volume has shrunk by one third certainly all alcohol had evaporated and no alcohol was left in the fluid.</i> and you are asking me this? They asked Rebbi Isaac and he forbade it to them. Then Rebbi Abbahu remembered that Rebbi Joḥanan had said, it is forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">169</sup><i class="footnote">R. Simeon ben Laqish’s statement is rejected in practice.</i>.
(Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa, Rebbi Immi, in the name of Rebbi Eleazar.)<sup class="footnote-marker">170</sup><i class="footnote">In the Leiden ms. this sentence was deleted by the scribe; it does not appear in the prints. In <i>Terumot</i>, the corresponding sentence reads: Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa, Rebbi Immi, in the name of Rebbi Eleazar: if he was entering and leaving it is permitted. This means that one may assume that no snake came out of its hole to eat from the food or drink the fluid if there was constant traffic in the room. The sentence really is needed to justify R. Abba bar Mamal’s ruling in the sentence after the next. Possibly it was deleted to indicate that the passage here should not be considered an exact copy of the text in <i>Terumot</i>. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Terumot 8:3:8" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Terumot.8.3.8"><i>Terumot</i> 8:5 Note 95</a>.</i> The barrel of Ben Neṭoza became uncovered. He went and asked Rebbi Abba bar Mamal, who told him, it is permitted. Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa, Rebbi Immi, in the name of Rebbi Eleazar: if he was sleeping it is permitted<sup class="footnote-marker">171</sup><i class="footnote">Also here one does not assume that a snake would venture into the room, even though the story in the next paragraph disproves the assumption. The statement also is rejected in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 30a" href="/Avodah_Zarah.30a">Babli, 30a</a>.</i>. Rebbi Ḥanina and Rebbi Joshua ben Levi, one said, if he was sleeping it is permitted; the other said, if he was sleeping it is forbidden. In is reasonable [to infer] that Rebbi Ḥanina said, if he was sleeping it is permitted, since Rebbi Eleazar everywhere relies on Rebbi Ḥanina<sup class="footnote-marker">172</sup><i class="footnote">The frequent disagreements of R. Eleazar and R. Joḥanan in both Talmudim really are disagreements between R. Ḥanina and R. Joḥanan.</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">173</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Terumot 8:3:9" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Terumot.8.3.9"><i>Terumot</i>8:5 Notes 96–101</a>; the text is somewhat reformulated.</i> Rebbi Yose ben Shaul understands it from the following occurrence. It happened that a woman loved to do good deeds but her husband hated good deeds. There came a poor man and she gave him to eat. While he was eating she noticed her husband; she made him climb and hid him in the upper floor. She returned to her husband who ate and fell asleep. There came a snake and ate from what was before him; her husband awoke from his sleep and wanted to eat. The one in the upper floor started to talk<sup class="footnote-marker">174</sup><i class="footnote">To warn the husband not to eat from the leftovers.</i>. That means if he was sleeping it is permitted<sup class="footnote-marker">175</sup><i class="footnote">Without warning the husband would have eaten the poisoned food. This proves both that the rule is that one may eat food left while a person is sleeping nearby and that the rule is questionable, at least if there is a house snake familiar with the happenings there.</i>. It was used to it. But is it not forbidden because of being alone? <i>For they committed adultery, blood is on their hands</i><sup class="footnote-marker">176</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Ezekiel 23:37" href="/Ezekiel.23.37"><i>Ez</i>. 23:37</a><i>.</i></i>. Since he is not suspected of one he is not suspected of the other<sup class="footnote-marker">177</sup><i class="footnote">Since the guest saved the husband from danger to his life he is not suspected of wanting him to die; this also clears him of any suspicion of adultery (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Nedarim 81b" href="/Nedarim.81b">Babli <i>Nedarim</i>81b</a>).</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">178</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Terumot 8:3:11" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Terumot.8.3.11"><i>Terumot</i>8:5 Notes 104–105</a>.</i> A pious person happened to be insistent in the matter of uncovered [drinks]; he was smitten with a fever. They saw him preaching on the Day of Atonement with a flask of water in his hand<sup class="footnote-marker">179</sup><i class="footnote">For medical reasons he would be permitted to take small sips of water on the day of the fast but it was extraordinary that he would have wanted to be seen with a flask of water which might lead people to believe that he did not fast. He took that chance rather than to risk leaving the water unattended.</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">180</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Terumot 8:3:10" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Terumot.8.3.10"><i>Terumot</i>8:5 Notes 102–103</a>. Although in the first part the <i>Terumot</i> text appears as the original, from here on the order of texts makes more sense. The only connection the present paragraph has with the others is the mention of the Day of Atonement (missing in <i>Terumot</i>) which was introduced in the preceding one (and continues in the next one with the original theme.)</i> It happened that a butcher in Sepphoris sold Jews carcasses and torn meats. Once on the eve of the day of Atonement before nightfall he drank a great deal of wine and became drunk. He went to the roof, fell down, and died. The dogs started to lick his blood; people came to ask Rebbi Ḥanina how to remove him from before them. He told them, it is written<sup class="footnote-marker">181</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 22:30" href="/Exodus.22.30"><i>Ex.</i>22:30</a>.</i>, <i>holy people you shall be to Me; torn meat on the fields you shall not eat, you must throw it to the dogs</i>. This one robbed the dogs and fed the Jews carcasses and torn meat. Let them alone, they are eating from their own.
<sup class="footnote-marker">182</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Terumot 8:3:13-16" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Terumot.8.3.13-16"><i>Terumot</i>8:5 Notes 106–112</a>. These are the last paragraphs having a parallel there.</i> A person’s amphora of wine became uncovered on the eve of the Great Fast. He went to pour it out; another person said to him, bring it and I shall drink it. He told him, it was uncovered. He answered, does not the Master of the Day exist? He had not finished tasting when he started shaking.
Rebbi Jeremiah in the name of Rebbi Ḥiyya bar Abba: All poisons cause scab but the poison of a snake kills<sup class="footnote-marker">183</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 31b" href="/Avodah_Zarah.31b">Babli 31b</a>: reptile poisons do not kill except that of a snake.</i>. Rebbi Ḥiyya said, one is not asked about uncovered [drinks]<sup class="footnote-marker">184</sup><i class="footnote">One should not ask a rabbi to authorize behavior that will endanger one’s health. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 30a" href="/Avodah_Zarah.30a">Babli 30a</a>.</i>. Rebbi Jeremiah asked before Rebbi Ze`ira; as master of the ruling he asked him<sup class="footnote-marker">185</sup><i class="footnote">How could R. Jeremiah have asked if he is the person who teaches that one does not ask? He asked in order to be able to formulate his rule.</i>.
Rebbi Ze`ira was dozing; he was sitting and eating in the evening. The light went out; he put his hand on the toman<sup class="footnote-marker">186</sup><i class="footnote">A vessel containing half a <i>log</i>(a <i>hemina</i>, half a <i>sextarius</i>) of wine. Sine the room was dark he held that the presence of a human in the room would not deter a snake from drinking the wine. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 30a" href="/Avodah_Zarah.30a">Babli 30a</a>.</i>. They lit the light and found a snakelet like a hair curled around it. He said to it, evil one, was I not careful about you?
Rebbi Immi said, one has to be careful with what the creatures are careful with: It is forbidden to put coins in one’s mouth, a bread under the armpit, a dish under one’s bed, and to stick a knife<sup class="footnote-marker">187</sup><i class="footnote">Store a knife there.</i> in an <i>etrog</i>, or a knife into a radish. Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Bun said, all sweat that exudes from a human is deadly poison except the sweat of the face.
Rebbi Yannai said, if you bit something off, you bit off soot. If you lose, you lose a pearl<sup class="footnote-marker">188</sup><i class="footnote">If you take chances with your health you may lose it.</i>. If anybody asked Rebbi Jonathan he told him: Am I the guarantor of your life? Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish said, if you sold yourself for the games<sup class="footnote-marker">189</sup><i class="footnote">Latin <i>ludi</i>; as a gladiator.</i>, you would sell yourself for an excessive price; but here for a very cheap price.
Rebbi Assi<sup class="footnote-marker">190</sup><i class="footnote">This is the Babli’s name of R. Yasa who later is quoted with his Galilean name. Note also in the first part Babylonian הנאה for Galilean הנייה.</i>, Rebbi Joḥanan in the name of Ben Bathyra: If libation wine fell into a cistern, it should be sold entirely to a Gentile except the value of the libation wine contained in it<sup class="footnote-marker">191</sup><i class="footnote">This is Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel’s opinion in Mishnah 5:13, opposed by the anonymous majority which declares the entire cistern as forbidden for usufruct. The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 74a" href="/Avodah_Zarah.74a">Babli (74a</a>) agrees that practice has to follow Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel.</i>. Rebbi Assi in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: Undetermined wine<sup class="footnote-marker">192</sup><i class="footnote">Wine about whose previous use nothing is known.</i> of a Gentile is forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">193</sup><i class="footnote">In this sentence, “forbidden” means “forbidden for usufruct”.</i> but does not make impure. If he deposited it<sup class="footnote-marker">194</sup><i class="footnote">Wines produced by Jews if handled by Gentiles are permitted to drink only if they are guarded by an inner seal and an outer seal which make it practically impossible for the Gentile to get to the wine without being detected. If there is no double seal, the wine becomes forbidden to drink. The problem then is to determine when Jewish wine handled by a Gentile or in the custody of a Gentile is permitted for usufruct.</i> with him it is forbidden for drinking but permitted for usufruct. Rebbi Ze‘ira asked before Rebbi Yasa, how? If he segregated it? He told him, if he deposited it<sup class="footnote-marker">195</sup><i class="footnote">In the first case, it was specified that the Jew’s closed wine amphora has to be kept at a specified place, with the Jew being able to inspect the place and see that the condition was kept. In the second case the amphora is to be kept by the Gentile in his wine cellar, not separate from his own wine amphoras.</i>. Rebbi Abbahu came in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: There are three kinds of wine. If he saw the Gentile pouring a libation, it is certainly pagan worship and induces severe impurity like a reptile<sup class="footnote-marker">196</sup><i class="footnote">In rabbinic teaching in many aspects pagan worship induces impurity much more severe than that of a dead reptile, cf. Mishnah 3:13, <i>Šabbat</i> 9:1. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 30b-31a" href="/Avodah_Zarah.30b-31a">Babli, 30b/31a</a>, the formulation is more correctly “severe impurity”.</i>.Gentiles’ undetermined wine is forbidden but does not cause impurity. If he deposited with him<sup class="footnote-marker">193</sup><i class="footnote">In this sentence, “forbidden” means “forbidden for usufruct”.</i> with one seal it is forbidden to drink<sup class="footnote-marker">194</sup><i class="footnote">Wines produced by Jews if handled by Gentiles are permitted to drink only if they are guarded by an inner seal and an outer seal which make it practically impossible for the Gentile to get to the wine without being detected. If there is no double seal, the wine becomes forbidden to drink. The problem then is to determine when Jewish wine handled by a Gentile or in the custody of a Gentile is permitted for usufruct.</i> and permitted for usufruct. Rebbi Jeremiah said before Rebbi Ze‘ira: See what he said! He only said with a seal. Therefore without a seal it is forbidden for drinking and usufruct. Rebbi Eleazar instructed: if it was segregated; Rebbi Ze‘ira enjoyed it<sup class="footnote-marker">197</sup><i class="footnote">Since R. Eleazar confirmed that he was correct in his question to R. Yasa, against R. Yasa. Since R. Eleazar died before R. Joḥanan, the entire discussion must be dated to the generation after R. Joḥanan, trying to understand the latter’s teachings.</i>.
There, we have stated<sup class="footnote-marker">198</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah <i>Demay</i> 3:4. <i>Demay</i> is produce bought from a Jew who is suspected of not following rabbinic rules. He is presumed to have given heave, which is a small amount and the violation of whose prohibition is clearly a biblical deadly sin, but is suspected of neglecting all other rules (cf. Introduction to Tractate <i>Demay</i>). The anonymous majority holds that Gentile produce in the Land of Israel is free from the rules of heave and tithes. (For the opinion of R. Simeon, cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Demai 3:4:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Demai.3.4.1"><i>Demay</i> 3:4 Note 91</a>). <i>Ṭevel</i> is Jewish produce from which no heave or tithes have been taken, forbidden for any Jew after storage.</i>: “With a Gentile, it is like his produce. Rebbi Simeon says, it is <i>demay</i>.” Rebbi Ḥananiah asked before Rebbi Mani: Does it mean, really like his produce? Freeing what would be <i>ṭevel</i> at another place? He said to him, for all that is in it. Rebbi Ḥanina was uselessly opposing him<sup class="footnote-marker">199</sup><i class="footnote">The paragraph has a parallel in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Demai 3:4:5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Demai.3.4.5"><i>Demay</i>3:4, Notes 104–107</a>. In the opinion of the anonymous Tanna, since the obligation of <i>ṭevel</i> starts only after storage, initial storage by a Gentile removes the obligation completely.<br>Since R. Mani (Mana I) was an Amora of the first generation, the name has to be Ḥanina in both occurrences unless one reads Mana (II) and Ḥanania in all occurrences here and the next paragraph..<br>The placing of the quote here can only be understood with the help of the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 31a" href="/Avodah_Zarah.31a">Babli (31a</a>). In the preceding paragraph it was established that the Jew’s wine stored with the Gentile under certain conditions remains permitted for usufruct. Then the question is why wine does not follow the rules of produce, that if stored by the Gentile it follows the rules of Gentile produce. While for grain this implies release from the obligations of heave and tithes, for wine it should imply prohibition of all usufruct. The answer is given in the next paragraph (having no parallel in the Babli) that the person who frees grain from heave and tithes forbids wine stored with the Gentile for usufruct.</i>.
If he deposited with him with a seal<sup class="footnote-marker">200</sup><i class="footnote">A barrel or bottle of wine, deposited with a Gentile without being held at a separate place. As noted earlier, it remains permitted as a drink for Jews only if it is secured by a double seal. The question remains about its status if it is secured only by a single seal which makes it unlikely but not impossible for the Gentile to access the wine.</i>. Rebbi Ḥananiah and Rebbi Mana, one says, prohibited<sup class="footnote-marker">201</sup><i class="footnote">R. Mana..</i>, and one says, permitted. Where do they disagree? For usufruct. But for drinking it is forbidden.
Rav said, <i>ḥbyt</i> is forbidden, <i>ḥmpg</i><sup class="footnote-marker">202</sup><i class="footnote">Mnemonic marks composed of initial letters of the items to be remembered; pronounced <i>ḥavit</i> and <i>ḥampag</i>. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 39" href="/Avodah_Zarah.39">Babli 39a/b</a>.</i> is permitted. A piece of fish without distinguishing mark<sup class="footnote-marker">203</sup><i class="footnote">If it is not directly visible that it is a piece of a fish with fins and scales, permitted as food.</i>, meat, wine, and dark blue color<sup class="footnote-marker">204</sup><i class="footnote">Woolen threads for use in the <i>ṣiṣit</i>, which by tradition must be dyed with the blood of a marine snail which can easily be counterfeit by (much cheaper) indigo, and therefore needs certification. (In modern Hebrew תכלת is light blue but classically very dark blue.)</i>, are forbidden with a single seal; assa foetida<sup class="footnote-marker">205</sup><i class="footnote">A medical concoction on vegetal basis but for which (forbidden) wine might have been used.</i>, fish sauce<sup class="footnote-marker">206</sup><i class="footnote">Latin <i>muries</i>; this also might have been made with wine, even though using only salt and water is cheaper.</i>, bread, and cheese, are permitted with a single seal. Rebbi Yudan said, the reason of Rav is that anything which is forbidden in itself is forbidden with a single seal, if because of admixture it is permitted with a single seal. Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa, Rebbi Simeon bar Abba, Rebbi Eleazar in the name of Rebbi Ḥanina; Rebbi Abba, Rebbi Ḥiyya in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan; Rebbi Ze‘ira in the name of Rebbi Joshua ben Levi: Everything is permitted with a single seal except wine<sup class="footnote-marker">207</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 31a" href="/Avodah_Zarah.31a">Babli 31a</a>.</i>.
“And Hadrianic pottery.” <sup class="footnote-marker">208</sup><i class="footnote">The origin of this text is in <i>‘Orlah</i> 3:2, Notes 64–76, since in the text “here” means <i>‘Orlah</i> and “there” <i>Avodah zarah</i>. Nevertheless the order of sentences is too different and the text here cannot be considered as simply a copy of the text there.</i> Rebbi Ze`ira in the name of Rebbi Jeremiah<sup class="footnote-marker">209</sup><i class="footnote">Probably this should read: Ze`iri in the name of Rav Jeremiah.</i>: does practice follow Rebbi Meïr? As it was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">210</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Avodah Zarah 5:3" href="/Tosefta_Avodah_Zarah.5.3">Tosephta 4:8</a>. Since the majority opposes R. Meïr, it is difficult to understand why the Mishnah should support a minority opinion as practice.</i>, “Hadrianic pottery is prohibited and its prohibition is of usufruct, the words of Rebbi Meïr<sup class="footnote-marker">211</sup><i class="footnote">In the <i>editio princeps</i>: R. Eliezer.</i>, but the Sages say, its prohibition is not of usufruct.” Rebbi Jeremiah asked before Rebbi Ze‘ira: what is the status of the cloth about which we stated here<sup class="footnote-marker">212</sup><i class="footnote">This shows that the paragraph belongs to <i>‘Orlah</i>, the treatise about the prohibition to use the fruits of a tree in the first three years of its growth. Mishnah <i>‘Orlah</i>3:1 states: “Cloth dyed with <i>‘orlah</i> shell shall be burned.” R. Jeremiah asks, why do the Sages hold that libation wine absorbed in pottery is not forbidden for usufruct but dye from <i>‘orlah</i> shells (e. g. walnut shells) absorbed in cloth make the entire cloth forbidden for usufruct?</i>? He was offended by this and told him, even for him who permits there, here it is forbidden. There, the prohibition is not visible, here the prohibition is visible. May one use it to support the legs of a couch<sup class="footnote-marker">213</sup><i class="footnote">They used to put the legs of the couch in water containers to shield the sleeper from snakes and other crawling animals.</i>? Rebbi Eleazar says it is permitted, Rebbi Joḥanan says it is forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">214</sup><i class="footnote">In ‘<i>Orlah</i>, the attributions are switched. The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 32a" href="/Avodah_Zarah.32a">Babli 32a</a> cannot decide between the two traditions.</i>.
“And heart-removed skins.” Rebbi Jeremiah in the name of Rav: Practice follows Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel<sup class="footnote-marker">215</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 32b" href="/Avodah_Zarah.32b">Babli 32b</a> this is a tradition of Samuel. R. Jeremiah here is the first generation Babylonian.</i>.
How does he do it? He tears its heart out from the living animal for pagan worship. How does one know<sup class="footnote-marker">216</sup><i class="footnote">Which skins were used in pagan ceremonies and are forbidden?</i>? Rebbi Ḥuna said, if it is torn out from the living animal it rebounds and becomes circular; after slaughter it becomes stretched. Rebbi Yose said, one can infer from this that if he slaughtered one sign, even though one says that nothing living can become prohibited, here it becomes prohibited<sup class="footnote-marker">217</sup><i class="footnote">Even an animal which was worshipped as a divinity is not forbidden for usufruct (it is forbidden as a sacrifice.) If in an act of pagan worship only the windpipe of an animal is cut but not the esophagus and the carotid arteries, the animal is not immediately killed. But since it is for pagan worship it is not less than tearing the heart out from a living animal which makes the entire carcass forbidden for usufruct.</i>.
“Meat brought into a pagan Temple is permitted.” Rebbi Abba, Rebbi Ḥiyya in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan said, to exclude the statement of Rebbi Eliezer, since Rebbi Eliezer says that the thoughts of a Non-Jew are about pagan worship<sup class="footnote-marker">218</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Chullin 2:7" href="/Mishnah_Chullin.2.7">Mishnah <i>Ḥulin</i> 2:7</a>. He holds that even if a Jew correctly slaughters an animal for a Gentile the meat is forbidden since the Gentile owner in his mind dedicates the slaughter to his god. Practice follows R. Yose who holds that only the slaughterer’s intentions have any influence on the process of slaughtering. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 32b" href="/Avodah_Zarah.32b">Babli 32b</a>.</i>.
“But what leaves is forbidden.” Rebbi Abinna in the name of Rav Jeremiah: When he introduced it inside the lattice railing<sup class="footnote-marker">219</sup><i class="footnote">Greek κιγκλίς, mostly used in the plural Greek κιγκλίδες, Latin <i>cancelli</i>, “lattice gates” (of Temples, eic.) <a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 51b" href="/Avodah_Zarah.51b">Babli 51b</a>.</i>. But if he did not introduce it inside the lattice railing it is permitted. That is about a pagan Temple which has a lattice railing. But if a pagan Temple has no lattice railing, the entire building is considered lattice railing.
“Those who go in<sup class="footnote-marker">220</sup><i class="footnote">This text, בתרפות, is otherwise found only in Mishnah texts of the Maimonides tradition. It seems preferable to the common text לתרפות.</i> ugliness.” Rebbi Ḥiyya in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan, θόρυβος<sup class="footnote-marker">221</sup><i class="footnote">“Tumult.” The reference is to pagan processions.</i>. One brings a large idol to a small idol. Some state תרפות.<sup class="footnote-marker">222</sup><i class="footnote">There seems a sentence to be missing, “some state תרבות”. This is a word תַּרְבּוּת both in biblical Hebrew, “brood” (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 32:14" href="/Numbers.32.14"><i>Num</i>. 32:14</a>), as also in rabbinic, “culture”. For תרפות compare Greek τράπεζα “table” including table dedicated to divinities with offerings of food (E. G.).</i> He who says תרפות, idols<sup class="footnote-marker">223</sup><i class="footnote">A word derived from a root תרפ used either for household gods or dolls (<a class="refLink" data-ref="I Samuel 13" href="/I_Samuel.13"><i>1S</i>. 13</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="I Samuel 16" href="/I_Samuel.16">16</a>). Arabic ترف “to live the good life”.</i>. He who says <sup class="footnote-marker">224</sup><i class="footnote">To be vocalized תֹרֻבוֹתֿ.</i>תרבות, θόρυβος.
“But those who return are permitted.<sup class="footnote-marker">225</sup><i class="footnote">A quote from the Mishnah in all versions except the Yerushalmi separate Mishnah.</i>” Rebbi Abinnas in the name of Rav Jeremiah: If their returning is not like their going. But if their returning is like their going, also those returning are forbidden.

Halakhah 4

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> Gentiles’ wine skins and jugs<sup class="footnote-marker">226</sup><i class="footnote">Which were not thoroughly cleansed to remove all traces of use for Gentile wine.</i> containing an Israel’s wine are forbidden and their prohibition is one of usufruct, the words of Rebbi Meïr; but the Sages say that their prohibition is not one of usufruct<sup class="footnote-marker">227</sup><i class="footnote">The wine may be used for trade; it is forbidden for Jews to drink.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “Gentiles’ wine skins and jugs,” etc. <sup class="footnote-marker">228</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Avodah Zarah 5:4" href="/Tosefta_Avodah_Zarah.5.4">Tosephta 4:10</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 33a" href="/Avodah_Zarah.33a">Babli 33a</a>.</i>“Rough Gentile wine skins<sup class="footnote-marker">229</sup><i class="footnote">The interior surface is leather only; it was not smoothened by tar.</i> are permitted; tarred ones are forbidden. If a Gentile made it and insulated<sup class="footnote-marker">230</sup><i class="footnote">It is difficult to see how the scribe could have written דובבן for זופתן “tarred them” in the other sources; it may be better to accept the word as it stands and read it as ذبّب “to protect, defend something”, to protect the skin against being penetrated by the wine.</i> it while a Jew was watching, one may fill it with wine without worry.” Should one not be afraid that possibly he made a libation? He will not usually make a libation in repugnant circumstances<sup class="footnote-marker">231</sup><i class="footnote">Even if the pitch is diluted with vinegar when applied to the skin, the bad smell does not invite making a libation. One assumes that the Jew has provided the vinegar which was made from kosher wine.</i>. Then even if no Jew was watching? I say, maybe he switched<sup class="footnote-marker">232</sup><i class="footnote">Then one has no control over the vinegar which was used.</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">228</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Avodah Zarah 5:4" href="/Tosefta_Avodah_Zarah.5.4">Tosephta 4:10</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 33a" href="/Avodah_Zarah.33a">Babli 33a</a>.</i>“Gentile jugs, new ones are permitted even tarred; old are forbidden even if not tarred.” Here you say tarred are permitted and there you say tarred are prohibited. Rebbi Abbahu said, I investigated tarring of jugs; they do not use vinegar while tarring<sup class="footnote-marker">233</sup><i class="footnote">There is no reason to forbid new jugs.</i>.
Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa in the name of the rabbis: If somebody puts wine which was forbidden to drink but permitted to use into jugs, the jugs become like the wine<sup class="footnote-marker">234</sup><i class="footnote">It cannot be exactly like the wine since one cannot drink the solid jug. But it means that the jug is permitted for usufruct but will make any wine which touches its inside forbidden to drink.</i>. If he removed it and put new wine<sup class="footnote-marker">235</sup><i class="footnote">Kosher wine.</i> into them, the wine becomes like the jugs<sup class="footnote-marker">236</sup><i class="footnote">Forbidden to drink but permitted for usufruct.</i>. If he removed it and put new wine<sup class="footnote-marker">235</sup><i class="footnote">Kosher wine.</i> into them, the wine is forbidden but the jugs permitted<sup class="footnote-marker">237</sup><i class="footnote">The first wine was forbidden. If the jug was filled twice with kosher wine, it has been cleansed from the first contamination; the third time the kosher wine remains kosher.</i>. Why “forbidden to drink but permitted to use”? Because of Rebbi Meïr who said, forbidden to drink and for use<sup class="footnote-marker">238</sup><i class="footnote">He will reject this way of cleansing the jug.</i>.
Rebbi Abba said, when Rebbi Aqiba went to the tar workers, they came and asked him, how does one purify Gentiles’ jugs? From the following I taught them, since they are permitted if not tarred, if they were tarred but the pitch was shaved off not so much more? But when I came to my colleagues they told me that they are absorbing because of the pitch<sup class="footnote-marker">239</sup><i class="footnote">They hold that wine absorbed in earthenware which had been tarred will not be removed even if the tar was removed and the vessel cleansed with water for three days as explained at the end of the Halakhah.</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">228,</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Avodah Zarah 5:4" href="/Tosefta_Avodah_Zarah.5.4">Tosephta 4:10</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 33a" href="/Avodah_Zarah.33a">Babli 33a</a>.</i><sup class="footnote-marker">240</sup><i class="footnote">A very different version in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 33a" href="/Avodah_Zarah.33a">Babli, 33a</a>, bottom.</i>“If the Gentile filled it with water, the Jew fills it with water; then he fills it with wine without worry. It the Gentile filled it with fish fluid<sup class="footnote-marker">241</sup><i class="footnote">צִיר is any fluid obtained by squeezing something, whether fruit, meat, or in this case fish. <i>Muries</i> is a sauce in which pieces of fish are preserved.</i> or fish sauce<sup class="footnote-marker">206</sup><i class="footnote">Latin <i>muries</i>; this also might have been made with wine, even though using only salt and water is cheaper.</i> the Jew may fill it with wine<sup class="footnote-marker">242</sup><i class="footnote">The smell of the fish will obliterate any taste that might have remained of the Gentile’s wine.</i>. If the Gentile filled it with wine, the Jew fills it with fish fluid or fish sauce, then he fills it with wine without worry.”
Rebbi Joḥanan went to meet<sup class="footnote-marker">243</sup><i class="footnote">Greek ἀπαντάω “to meet” with Atamaic prefix ל.</i> Rebbi Yudan the Prince at Acco. They came and asked him, how does one purify jugs? He said, from the following I taught them, since after the Gentile filled it with fish fluid or fish sauce the Jew fills it with wine<sup class="footnote-marker">244</sup><i class="footnote">This is the quote from the previous Tosephta. Of R. Joḥanan’s argument there exist two versions, one by R. Yasa (reported in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 33b" href="/Avodah_Zarah.33b">Babli 33b</a>), the other by R. Ḥiyya bar Abba.</i>. Rebbi Yasa in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan, if he puts them into the fire not so much more? Rebbi Ḥiyya in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan, if he strips off the tarring<sup class="footnote-marker">245</sup><i class="footnote">By burning of the tar.</i> not so much more? Do they stand this? If they stand it, they stand<sup class="footnote-marker">246</sup><i class="footnote">Both R. Yasa and R. Ḥiyya require that the earthenware jug be treated with fire; either fired in its entirety or fire applied to the tar lining. It is agreed that there is a risk that the vessel will burst in the fire, but if it withstands the process it may be used to store Jewish wine.</i>.
Untarred jugs<sup class="footnote-marker">247</sup><i class="footnote">Which had been used by Gentiles.</i>, Rebbi Assi<sup class="footnote-marker">190</sup><i class="footnote">This is the Babli’s name of R. Yasa who later is quoted with his Galilean name. Note also in the first part Babylonian הנאה for Galilean הנייה.</i> said, they are forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">248</sup><i class="footnote">They cannot be cleansed to become usable.</i>, Rebbi Immi said, they are permitted. Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa said, Rebbi Assi questioned: do we say that a tankard does not absorb<sup class="footnote-marker">249</sup><i class="footnote">Both R. Assi and Rav Sheshet question the position of R. Immi.</i>? Rebbi Abba said, Rav Sheshet asked, could we say that a vessel for urine does not absorb?
Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa, Rebbi Simeon bar Abba in the name of Rebbi Ḥanina: One fills Gentiles’ jugs with water three days from time to time<sup class="footnote-marker">250</sup><i class="footnote">One fills the vessel with water, lets it stand for 24 hours, pours the old water out and refills with new, and repeats twice.</i>. Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa said, Rebbi Assi<sup class="footnote-marker">190</sup><i class="footnote">This is the Babli’s name of R. Yasa who later is quoted with his Galilean name. Note also in the first part Babylonian הנאה for Galilean הנייה.</i> asked, does one do this? Rebbi Yose from Malḥa brought a case before Rebbi Mana. He told him, that is what happened; he answered that it is permitted. Is that to start? He told him, it is forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">251</sup><i class="footnote">The method is a short-cut which cannot be used generally; if people used it without asking one cannot prohibit the vessels cleansed in this way.</i>. Rebbi Jeremiah went to Goblana; he instructed about large cups<sup class="footnote-marker">252</sup><i class="footnote">Latin <i>patera</i>“flat dish or saucer; libation bowl” or Greek ποτήριον (cup, receptacle for offerings in temples; cup in the Eucharist) (Kohut).</i> that one fills them with water three days from time to time<sup class="footnote-marker">253</sup><i class="footnote">He sees nothing wrong with the method and recommends it publicly. He is quoted in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 33a" href="/Avodah_Zarah.33a">Babli, 33a</a>, in the same sense and ascribes the ruling to R. Immi.</i>.
An Aramean’s<sup class="footnote-marker">254</sup><i class="footnote">A Non-Jew, not necessarily a pagan; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Avodah Zarah 1:6:5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Avodah_Zarah.1.6.5">Chapter 1, Note 177</a>.</i> leather bag split; the Jew received it into his<sup class="footnote-marker">255</sup><i class="footnote">The Aramean’s wine-skin started leaking; a Jew saved his wine by receiving it into his own wine-skin. The Gentile’s wine certainly is forbidden to the Jew; the question is how the Jew can regain the use of his skin after the wine was transferred to the Gentile’s vessel, without ruining the vessel by using it for fish sauce.</i>. The case came before the rabbis who said, one fills them with water three days from time to time<sup class="footnote-marker">256</sup><i class="footnote">Following R. Jeremiah, against R. Yasa (Assi).</i>.
Rebbi Yasa<sup class="footnote-marker">257</sup><i class="footnote">In the entire Halakhah he opposes cleansing Gentile wine vessels for use by Jews.</i> went to Tyre. He saw them<sup class="footnote-marker">258</sup><i class="footnote">Gentile workers.</i> tarring small leather bags and Jews bought them. He asked, who permitted you? They asked Rebbi Isaac and Rebbi Mani<sup class="footnote-marker">259</sup><i class="footnote">The earlier authorities all forbid refurbishing wine skins and jugs; the later generations do permit it.</i>, who prohibited it.

Halakhah 5

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> <sup class="footnote-marker">260</sup><i class="footnote">In the Mishnah this text follows Mishnah 5 which belongs to Halakhah 6. The sequence of the Halakhah also is the one in the Babli and Maimonides’s Mishnah Commentary.</i> Gentiles’ grape kernels and skins<sup class="footnote-marker">261</sup><i class="footnote">Which remain after the production of wine.</i> are forbidden and the prohibition is of usufruct, the words of Rebbi Meïr. But the Sages say, moist they are forbidden, dry permitted.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “Gentiles’ grape kernels and skins,” etc. Rav Sheshet in the name of Rav: Moist they are forbidden even for usufruct; dry they are permitted even to eat<sup class="footnote-marker">262</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 34a" href="/Avodah_Zarah.34a">Babli, 34a</a>, ascribed to the Galilean R. Joḥanan.</i>. But did we not state<sup class="footnote-marker">263</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah 4:8.</i>, “it does not become libation wine until it descends into the cistern<sup class="footnote-marker">264</sup><i class="footnote">Grape juice pressed out of grapes does not become forbidden before it is collected in a vat below the wine press. Since kernels and skins are kept in the press they should not become forbidden.</i>”? Rebbi Abba in the name of Rebbi Judah: The Mishnah is about one who lowers them into the cistern<sup class="footnote-marker">265</sup><i class="footnote">The only skins forbidden are those put in the fermenting wine to give it red color.</i>.
There we have stated:<sup class="footnote-marker">266</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Kelim 9:5" href="/Mishnah_Kelim.9.5">Mishnah <i>Kelim</i>9:5</a>.</i> “And so it is with new solid matter of olives; but old one is pure.<sup class="footnote-marker">267</sup><i class="footnote">If the solid remnants in the olive press which remain after the production of oil are impure, they impart impurity only as long as they are somewhat moist. Once they are stone dry they do no longer impart impurity to clay vessels.</i>” What is new? Within twelve months. Old, after twelve months<sup class="footnote-marker">268</sup><i class="footnote">The same is true for grape skins used for the production of Gentile wine. Once they are completely dry, having dried out for a full 12 months, they are forbidden no longer; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 34a" href="/Avodah_Zarah.34a">Babli 34a</a> (attributed there to Samuel).</i>.

Halakhah 6

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> Fish sauce<sup class="footnote-marker">206</sup><i class="footnote">Latin <i>muries</i>; this also might have been made with wine, even though using only salt and water is cheaper.</i>, cheese<sup class="footnote-marker">269</sup><i class="footnote">The reasons for prohibiting Gentile cheese will be discussed in the next Halakhot.</i>, and theriac<sup class="footnote-marker">270</sup><i class="footnote">Greek θηριακή, <i>scil</i>., φάρμακα “medicines made from poisonous beasts (θηρίον)”. It is possible however that ותרייקי is an error for בתנייקי “Bithynian”, which in the Mishnah in the Babli and the independent Mishnah mss. characterizes the kind of cheese which if made by Gentiles is forbidden for usufruct; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Avodah Zarah 2:9:6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Avodah_Zarah.2.9.6">Note 431</a>.</i> of Gentiles are forbidden and the prohibition is of usufruct, the words of Rebbi Meïr. But the Sages say, theitr prohibition is not of usufruct.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “Fish sauce, cheese,” etc.<sup class="footnote-marker">271</sup><i class="footnote">From here on, there exists a Genizah text (ג) which differs from the Leiden text mainly in its spelling. The origin of the entire Halakhah is <i>Terumot</i>11:1 (<b>ת</b>, Notes 11–23). However, the first paragraph is a reformulation and explanation of that text as noted by S. Lieberman (<i>Tosefta kiFshutah Terumot</i> p. 454). The second paragraph also belongs to <i>Terumot</i>2:5 (<b>תּ</b>, Notes 118–119).<br>The quote refers to a <i>baraita</i> similar to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Terumot (Lieberman) 9:6" href="/Tosefta_Terumot_(Lieberman).9.6">Tosephta <i>Terumot</i>9:6</a>. According to Rebbi one puts wine into <i>muries</i> to improve its taste. This is a legitimate use of wine as food; therefore the Cohen may use his heave wine, sanctified food which has to be consumed in purity as a religious duty, to improve his <i>muries.</i> On the other hand, since the improved taste is noticeable, the entire <i>muries</i> now must be eaten under the rules of heave.<br>R. Eleazar holds that wine is used in <i>muries</i> only to clean it from contaminations. To use heave wine for this purpose is sinful since it destroys sanctified drink. But if heave wine was used in this illegitimate way, it was destroyed and therefore lost its sanctity. The <i>muries</i> is profane and may be eaten by lay people in impurity.</i>“Wine in <i>muries</i>, Rebbi permits but Rebbi Eleazar ben Rebbi Simeon forbids.” Therefore, if somebody transgressed and put it in, Rebbi forbids it to laymen but Rebbi Eleazar ben Rebbi Simeon permits it to laymen.” Rebbi Mana bar Tanḥum asked: Following him who permits for laymen, why is <i>muries</i> of Gentiles forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">272</sup><i class="footnote">Since the amount of any forbidden wine left in the sauce would be insignificant. If the trace of heave wine is permitted to impure laymen, should not a trace of Gentile wine be permitted to everybody?</i>? Rebbi Jeremiah in the name of Rebbi Ḥiyya ber Abba, it is forbidden as being cooked by Gentiles<sup class="footnote-marker">273</sup><i class="footnote">Whose general prohibition is discussed in Halakhah 9.</i>. But did not [Rebbi Yose]<sup class="footnote-marker">274</sup><i class="footnote">Added from <b>ג</b> and the <i>Terumot</i> texts.</i> state: “From a professional<sup class="footnote-marker">275</sup><i class="footnote">This is the reading of the Leiden ms., <a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 34b" href="/Avodah_Zarah.34b">Babli 34b</a>, and <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Terumot (Lieberman) 4:13" href="/Tosefta_Terumot_(Lieberman).4.13">Tosephta <i>Terumot</i> 4:13</a>. The Leiden ms. in <i>Terumot</i> reads המין “trustworthy”, which in <i>editio princeps</i> became an unintelligible חמין “hot”. The reading of <b>ג</b>, אמון may be read either as אָמוֹן “craftsman” with the Leiden ms. here or אֵמוּן “trustworthiness” with the text in <i>Terumot</i>.</i> it is permitted, from a non-professional it is forbidden.” From a professional it is permitted, is that not if it is not cooked<sup class="footnote-marker">276</sup><i class="footnote">Since otherwise it still would be forbidden as Gentile cooking even if one was sure that no wine was used in its production.</i>? Similarly, from a non-professional it is forbidden even if cooked. What about it? If it is to remove contamination it is forbidden because of pagan worship<sup class="footnote-marker">277</sup><i class="footnote">In industrial production a Gentile might use wine of which a libation was made.</i>. This implies that usufruct of pagan worship is forbidden, usufruct of heave is permitted<sup class="footnote-marker">278</sup><i class="footnote">Since usufruct of pagan wine is forbidden, the rules of declaring an amount as insignificant do not apply; the most minute amount makes the entire product forbidden whereas less than ½% of heave in food leaves it permitted for everybody.</i>. Rebbi Joḥanan bar Madia said, following him who said from a professional it is permitted, only if he knows him<sup class="footnote-marker">279</sup><i class="footnote">In modern terms, if the <i>muries</i> carries a rabbinic certification that no wine was used in its production.</i>.
Rebbi Joḥanan and Rebbi Eleazar. One says because he diminishes its volume, the other says because he reduces the number of drinkers<sup class="footnote-marker">280</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to Mishnah <i>Terumot</i> 11:1: “One may not cook heave wine because he diminishes its volume; R. Jehudah permits since he improves it.” But some people do not want to drink alcohol-free cooked wine. The problem really is whether the vintner is permitted to cook his wine before he gave heave since (a) with the diminution in volume of the wine he diminishes the volume of heave to be taken and (b) not every Cohen may be willing to accept cooked heave wine.</i>. We do not know who said what. Since Rebbi Joḥanan said, Rebbi Jehudah inverted his reasoning but Rebbi Eleazar said, it was not inverted, there for the Cohen, here for the owners, it follows that Rebbi Joḥanan said because he reduces the number of drinkers.

Halakhah 7

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> Rebbi Jehudah said, Rebbi Ismael asked Rebbi Joshua when they were walking on a road, why did they forbid Gentiles’ cheeses<sup class="footnote-marker">281</sup><i class="footnote">They were forbidden in Mishnah 6 for all usufruct without any obvious reason.</i>? He told him, because they make them with stomach of cadaver<sup class="footnote-marker">282</sup><i class="footnote">The rennet is taken from the stomach contents of cattle killed by Gentiles and therefore automatically have the status of cadavers. Since the rennet causes the milk to curdle, its addition cannot be said to be insignificant. Therefore one understands that the cheeses are forbidden as food. Since the rennet is produced from an animal and cheese (including kosher cheese made by Jews) is made by cooking milk with rennet, it could be forbidden for all usufruct as meat cooked in milk following R. Simeon ben Ioḥai (<i>Mekhilta dR. Ismael Masekhta deKaspa</i>20).</i>. He answered him, is not the stomach of an elevation offering<sup class="footnote-marker">283</sup><i class="footnote">An elevation offering is burned completely on the altar. The stomach contents (and the contents of its intestines) are not burned; the innards have to be washed before being put on the altar (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 1:9" href="/Leviticus.1.9"><i>Lev</i>. 1:9</a>). Eating from a cadaver is a simple infraction which requires no sacrifice; eating from an animal dedicated as elevation sacrifice is both an infraction and larceny requiring a sacrifice.</i> more serious than the stomach of a cadaver and they said, a Cohen who is not repulsed may burn it raw<sup class="footnote-marker">284</sup><i class="footnote">The Cohen may eat the stomach contents raw since they are considered excrement; the rennet therefore cannot be considered meat and the Gentile’s cheese should be permitted even as food! For the expression “to burn” for “to slurp” see <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Avodah Zarah 2:7:7" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Avodah_Zarah.2.7.7">Note 321</a>.</i>; they did not agree to this but said one has no usufruct<sup class="footnote-marker">285</sup><i class="footnote">While the previous statement is essentially correct there is a (customary or rabbinic) rule that it would not be decorous to do so; one refrains from using any part of the animal (except the hide given to the priests, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 7:8" href="/Leviticus.7.8"><i>Lev</i>. 7:8</a>) as a practical rule.</i> but does not commit larceny<sup class="footnote-marker">286</sup><i class="footnote">As a rule of biblical law. Therefore Gentiles’ cheese could at most be rabbinically forbidden.</i>.<br>He<sup class="footnote-marker">303</sup><i class="footnote">R. Joshua.</i> gave a second argument and said, because they curd it with stomach content of calves for pagan worship. He<sup class="footnote-marker">304</sup><i class="footnote">R. Ismael.</i> retorted, then why did they not forbid it for usufruct<sup class="footnote-marker">289</sup><i class="footnote">Therefore even R. Ismael must agree that these cheeses are forbidden; but they should be forbidden for usufruct.</i>? He<sup class="footnote-marker">303</sup><i class="footnote">R. Joshua.</i> deflected him to an other subject<sup class="footnote-marker">305</sup><i class="footnote">As the Babli explains, since it was a new purely rabbinic restriction introduced after the destruction of the Temple, he did not want to disclose the reason.</i> and said to him, my brother Ismael, how do you read, <i>for your (m.) friends are better than wine</i>, or <i>for your (f.) friends are better than wine</i><sup class="footnote-marker">306</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Song of Songs 1:2" href="/Song_of_Songs.1.2"><i>Cant</i>. 1:2</a>. Since the Song is read as a dialogue between God (m.) and Israel (f.), the theological interpretation depends on the vocalization which was not directly expressible before the invention of vowel signs. The interpretation given in the Halakhah requires the identification of דּוֹדִים as “friends, lovers” rather than “friendship, love.”</i>? He<sup class="footnote-marker">304</sup><i class="footnote">R. Ismael.</i> told him, <i>for your (f.) friendship is better</i>. He<sup class="footnote-marker">303</sup><i class="footnote">R. Joshua.</i> answered, it is not so since the next verse implies it, <i>by the scent of your (m.) good oils</i><sup class="footnote-marker">307</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Song of Songs 1:3" href="/Song_of_Songs.1.3"><i>Cant</i>. 1:3</a>. As in most cases, the proof is from the part of the verse not quoted. The verse ends: <i>therefore girls love you</i>. Since the Song of Songs clearly celebrates heterosexual love, the speaker must be a female addressing a male.<br>The following interpretation of the Mishnah follows S. Naeh [שלמה נאה, טובים דודיך מיין, מבט חדש על משנת ע״ז ב:ה; מחקרים בתלמוד ובמדרש, ספר זכרון לתירצה ליפשיץ, י-ם 2005 ע' 434-411.]. There are two problems. What is the relationship of the Mishnah 7 and the first part of Mishnah 8 to the second part of Mishnah 8? Also the discussion in Mishnah 8 does not seem to make sense. Since <a class="refLink" data-ref="Song of Songs 1:2" href="/Song_of_Songs.1.2"><i>Cant</i>. 1:2</a> starts: <i>May he kiss me with kisses of his mouth</i>, it should be clear that the speaker is the female. Why should R. Ismael, who everywhere else requires that a verse be interpreted according to its plain sense, suddenly switch speakers in middle sentence? Why does R. Joshua refer to 1:3, when a referral to 1:2 would be more appropriate? The unvocalized text of 1:2–3 has a chiastic structure: It starts clearly with the masculine, has a middle section which could be read in the masculine or the feminine, and ends again with the masculine.<br>R. Ismael proves convincingly that there is no biblical basis for the prohibition of Gentile cheese; it is purely rabbinical. It can be regarded as a “fence around the law” only with regard to the prohibition of intermarriage, since it is designed to make social intercourse between Jews and Gentiles as difficult as possible. Then the question arises as to the status of the much more important prohibitions oil and wine. The prohibition of oil clearly is rabbinical even though it is mentioned as particularly meritorious in <i>Daniel</i>; no reason could be found in dietary laws to prohibit Gentile cold pressed virgin olive oil. While wine actually used for pagan libations is biblically forbidden, the extension of the prohibition to almost any wine moved in any way by a Gentile, even one adhering to a faith not practicing libations (or even prohibiting the drinking of wine), must be considered rabbinical. Now wine in mentioned in v. 1:2 and oil in v. 1:3. The discussion between Rabbis Ismael and Joshua is about the status of the prohibitions of wine and oil.<br>As mentioned in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Avodah Zarah 2:7:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Avodah_Zarah.2.7.1">Note 306</a>, any rabbinic reference to the Song of Songs unquestionably reads sentences put into the mouth of the female as coming from the congregation of Israel (as represented by its rabbinical leaders) and that of the male as referring to God. R. Joshua asks R. Ismael about his opinion about the actual rules referring to Gentile wine. The latter, by putting the reference to wine in the mouth of the male, asserts that the prohibition of Gentile wine essentially is God’s decree, is biblical. R. Joshua, the overriding authority, informs him that the references to wine and oil have equal status; since the prohibition of oil in almost all cases has no pentateuchal basis, the prohibition of wine also in almost all cases is purely rabbinical (Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Avodah Zarah 5:4:6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Avodah_Zarah.5.4.6">Halakhah 5:4, Note 67</a>).</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “Rebbi Jehudah said, Rebbi Ismael asked Rebbi Joshua,” etc. Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa, Rebbi Simeon bar Abba in the name of Rebbi Joshua ben Levi: Because [most]<sup class="footnote-marker">287</sup><i class="footnote">Added from the Genizah text, necessary for syntactic reasons.</i> calves there<sup class="footnote-marker">288</sup><i class="footnote">This shows that the Mishnah underlying this Halakhah is not the one given in the separate Mishnah in the Yerushalmi but the one found in the Babli and Maimonides: R. Meïr declares <i>Bithynian</i> Gentile cheese as forbidden for usufruct. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 34b" href="/Avodah_Zarah.34b">Babli 34b</a>.</i> are slaughtered in the name of pagan worship. Rebbi Joḥanan heard this and said, my teacher taught us well, since if one slaughters for pagan worship even its excrement is forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">289</sup><i class="footnote">Therefore even R. Ismael must agree that these cheeses are forbidden; but they should be forbidden for usufruct.</i>. Rebbi Joḥanan asked, if one found a ring in it? Rebbi Yose said, a ring is recognizable<sup class="footnote-marker">290</sup><i class="footnote">It never was part of the animal and did not become forbidden by its slaughter for idolatry.</i>; excrement is part of its body.
<sup class="footnote-marker">293</sup><i class="footnote">For this paragraph there exist two additional witnesses, the Leiden ms. in <i>Beṣah</i>1:1 (60a ll. 39–54, <b>ב</b>) and a Genizah text (<i>Yerushalmi Fragments</i>, pp. 156–157, <b>בּ</b>) of the same. The source clearly is the <i>Beṣah</i> text even though the argument is from <i>Avodah zarah</i> and the reference to <i>Beṣah</i> is a side remark.<br>“Gentile’s stomach content” naturally means “stomach content of animal slaughtered by a Gentile.”</i> Rebbi Ḥiyya bar Abba in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: At first they were saying, one curdles neither with the stomach content of the carcass<sup class="footnote-marker">294</sup><i class="footnote">An animal which was not slaughtered following the rules of ritual slaughter. whose flesh therefore is forbidden as meat.</i> nor with the stomach contents of the Gentile. They changed to say, one curdles with the stomach content of the carcass but not with the stomach contents of the Gentile. Rebbi Abba bar Zavda, Rebbi Samuel bar Rav Isaac asked: Does this negate the declaration of Rebbi Eliezer, since Rebbi Eliezer said, the thoughts of the Non-Jew are about pagan worship<sup class="footnote-marker">295</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Beṣah</i> text seems better: Does this (i. e., the prohibition of Gentile rennet) follow R. Eliezer (whom practice does not follow in general) since it treats Gentile rennet as more problematic than the one derived from a carcass? Since here practice is said to follow R. Eliezer, the tradition seems questionable; one has to search for parallel traditions either to confirm or to reject.</i>? Rebbi (Assi) [Yasa]<sup class="footnote-marker">296</sup><i class="footnote">The Babylonian form of the name (in parentheses) has to be deleted in favor of the Galilean form used in the three parallel sources [in brackets].</i> came in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: Originally, they said, one does curdle [neither]<sup class="footnote-marker">297</sup><i class="footnote">To be added by the unanimous testimony of the other three sources and the later arguments.</i> with carcass stomach contents nor with Gentile’s stomach contents. They changed to say, one does curdle with carcass stomach contents and with Gentile’s stomach contents. The language of a <i>baraita</i> supports Rebbi Ḥiyya bar Abba: Stomach contents of a Gentile or a carcass is forbidden following the original teaching. (The stomach contents of a torn animal which suckled from a qualified one are permitted following the later teaching; the stomach contents of a qualified animal which suckled from a torn one are forbidden following the later teaching.) [The stomach contents of a qualified animal which suckled from a torn one are forbidden following the former teaching, and the stomach contents of a torn animal which suckled from a qualified one are permitted following the later teaching.]<sup class="footnote-marker">298</sup><i class="footnote">The ms. text (in parentheses) is inconsistent. It has to be replaced by the text of the other three sources [in brackets] which is consistent with R. Joḥanan’s statement in both versions since for dietary laws a “torn” animal has the same status as a carcass.<br>A “torn” animal is one of which a vital organ is impaired or defective, the most common one being tuberculous lesions of the lung. If a cow is known to suffer from a defect which makes it “torn”, its milk is forbidden as drink but as stomach contents it becomes excrement and is no longer forbidden. (Karaites deny the existence of internal <i>ṭerefa</i> and prohibit as torn only the victims of predator attacks as described by the verse, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 22:30" href="/Exodus.22.30"><i>Ex.</i>22:30</a>.)</i> And even if the House of Shammai would argue like the House of Hillel in the first teaching, an egg is a growth of its body; the contents of the stomach come from the outside<sup class="footnote-marker">299</sup><i class="footnote">This now connects the discussion with <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Beitzah 1:1:2-10:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Beitzah.1.1.2-10.3">Mishnah <i>Beṣah</i>1:1</a> which states that the House of Shammai permit the use on the holiday of an egg laid on that day while the House of Hillel forbid it. The problem is to understand the position of the House of Hillel since the preparation of food is permitted on a holiday (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 12:16" href="/Exodus.12.16"><i>Ex</i>. 12:16</a>). According to the Babli and one opinion in the Yerushalmi the prohibition applies only to eggs laid by chickens raised to produce eggs. Then the mother was not food at the start of the holiday. While the egg is food according to everybody, for the House of Hillel it cannot change its character from non-food to food on the holiday. Then the question is raised why milk which is prohibited as food before being ingested by a ruminant becomes permitted by the animal’s stomach. In this question, the revised ruling in matters of rennet would agree with the stance of the House of Shammai. But the answer is that the two cases cannot be compared; the stomach contents of an animal never were part of the animal and do not have the latter’s status.</i>. It parallels what Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun said in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: It happened to the son[s]<sup class="footnote-marker">300</sup><i class="footnote">The plural is attested to in the three parallel sources and required by the context.</i> of (Rebbi) Jehudah ben Shamua<sup class="footnote-marker">301</sup><i class="footnote">The parallels unanimously state the name as Jehudah ben Shemu`ai without a rabbinic title.</i> that wolves tore more than 300 of their flock. The case came before the Sages who permitted their stomach contents<sup class="footnote-marker">302</sup><i class="footnote">The latter teaching which permits rennet from animals prohibited as meat is confirmed by a ruling in an actual case.</i>. They said, an egg is a growth of its body; the contents of the stomach come from the outside.
“Because they curdle it,” etc. <sup class="footnote-marker">309</sup><i class="footnote">This paragraph and the next are from <i>Berakhot</i> 1:7 (Notes 182-191) and <i>Sanhedrin</i> 11:6 (Notes 54-57); Midrash Cant. 1(18). The Geniza fragment ends here on line 2.</i>The colleagues in the name of Rebbi [Joḥanan]: The words of the Sopherim are related to the words of Scripture and are (more pleasant than) [pleasant like] the words of Scripture; <i>your throat is like good wine</i><sup class="footnote-marker">310</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Song of Songs 7:10" href="/Song_of_Songs.7.10"><i>Cant.</i>7:10</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 35a" href="/Avodah_Zarah.35a">Babli 35a</a>.</i>. Simeon bar Abba in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: The words of the Sopherim are related to the words of Scripture and are more pleasant than the words of Scripture, <i>for your friends are better than wine</i><sup class="footnote-marker">311</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Berakhot 1:4:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Berakhot.1.4.1">Mishnah <i>Berakhot</i> 1:7</a>.</i>. Rebbi Abba bar Cohen in the name of Bar Pazi: You may know that the words of the Sopherim are more pleasant than the words of Scripture, because if Rebbi Tarphon<sup class="footnote-marker">312</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Song of Songs 1:2" href="/Song_of_Songs.1.2"><i>Cant</i>. 1:2</a>.</i> did not recite at all he would only have transgressed a positive commandment. But because he transgressed the words of the House of Hillel he should have suffered death since it says, <i>if one breaches a wall he will be bitten by a snake</i><sup class="footnote-marker">313</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Ecclesiastes 10:8" href="/Ecclesiastes.10.8"><i>Eccl</i>. 10:8</a>.</i>.
Rebbi Ismael stated: In the Torah there are forbidden matters and permitted matters. There are easy parts and severe parts. But in the words of the Sopherim all are severe, since we have stated: “He who says that there are no phylacteries, to transgress the words of the Torah, is not prosecutable. Five compartments to add to the words of the Sopherim is punishable.” Rebbi Ḥanina<sup class="footnote-marker">314</sup><i class="footnote">With the other two sources read: Ḥinena.</i> in the name of Rebbi Idi in the name of Rebbi Tanḥum bar Ḥiyya: The words of the Sages carry more weight than those of the prophets since it is written, <i>do not preach, they preach</i><sup class="footnote-marker">315</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Micah 2:6" href="/Micah.2.6"><i>Micha</i> 2:6</a>.</i>.
