And it is written,<i>I shall preach to you for wine and liquor</i><sup class="footnote-marker">316</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Micah 2:11" href="/Micah.2.11"><i>Micha</i> 2:11</a>.</i>. The relation of prophet and scholar can be compared to the case of a king who sent two seals<sup class="footnote-marker">317</sup><i class="footnote">As the text shows this is a scribal error. Read with the other sources פלמנטרין<i>diplomatarii</i>, persons authorized to use the imperial mail.</i> to a province. About one of them he wrote, if he does not show you my seal and σημαντήριον<sup class="footnote-marker">318</sup><i class="footnote">Greek “seal”.</i>, do not believe him. About the other he wrote, even if he does not show you my seal and σημαντήριον, believe him. So about a prophet is written, <i>he gives you a sign or miracle</i><sup class="footnote-marker">319</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 13:2" href="/Deuteronomy.13.2"><i>Deut</i>. 13:2</a>.</i>. But here it is written, <i>according to the teachings that they will teach you</i><sup class="footnote-marker">320</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 17:11" href="/Deuteronomy.17.11"><i>Deut</i>. 17:11</a>.</i>.
Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa, Rebbi Simeon bar Abba in the name of Rebbi Joshua ben Levi: Because most calves there<sup class="footnote-marker">288</sup><i class="footnote">This shows that the Mishnah underlying this Halakhah is not the one given in the separate Mishnah in the Yerushalmi but the one found in the Babli and Maimonides: R. Meïr declares <i>Bithynian</i> Gentile cheese as forbidden for usufruct. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 34b" href="/Avodah_Zarah.34b">Babli 34b</a>.</i> are slaughtered in the name of pagan worship. Rebbi Joḥanan heard this and said, my teacher taught us well, since if one slaughters an animal for pagan worship even its excrement is forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">289</sup><i class="footnote">Therefore even R. Ismael must agree that these cheeses are forbidden; but they should be forbidden for usufruct.</i>. Rebbi Joḥanan asked, if one found a ring in it? Rebbi Yose said, a ring is recognizable<sup class="footnote-marker">290</sup><i class="footnote">It never was part of the animal and did not become forbidden by its slaughter for idolatry.</i>; excrement is part of its body.
What means “burns it”? He slurps it<sup class="footnote-marker">321</sup><i class="footnote">In <i>Midrash Cant</i>. 1(17) גמיע.</i>. Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish said, in a dirty cup. This implies that one who drinks from a dirty cup neither benefits nor commits larceny<sup class="footnote-marker">322</sup><i class="footnote">Since he must slurp the contents of the stomach in the Temple it would be a desecration to use a clean Temple vessel for this purpose. A private vessel may not be introduced into the Temple precinct. The priest may use a dirty vessel which nobody else would use for food to eat the disgusting matter.</i>.
Why did he not tell him? Because they prohibited it recently and Rebbi Ismael was young<sup class="footnote-marker">323</sup><i class="footnote">They had to establish the authority of the newly formed Synhedrion at Jabneh; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 35a" href="/Avodah_Zarah.35a">Babli 35a</a>. The story must be dated to the last 20 years of the first Century, after the death of Rabban Joḥanan ben Zakkai (at an unknown date). R. Ismael never appears as member of the Synhedrion.</i>.
Rebbi Onias said, Rebbi Ḥama bar Uqba raised a difficulty: If he wanted to deflect him to another subject<sup class="footnote-marker">324</sup><i class="footnote">If R. Joshua’s intention only was to deflect R. Ismael’s inquiry there were many other questions to be asked.</i>, he should have removed him to the five indeterminate places in the Torah<sup class="footnote-marker">325</sup><i class="footnote">In the absence of masoretic accents it may be difficult to parse a sentence. In Babli sources, this is called “verses that have no decision,” i. e., where to place the caesura. In our masoretic texts only <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 25:34" href="/Exodus.25.34"><i>Ex</i>. 25:34</a> remains undecided in this sense. Parallel sources are <a class="refLink" data-ref="Yoma 52" href="/Yoma.52">Babli <i>Yoma</i>52a/b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bereishit Rabbah 80:5" href="/Bereishit_Rabbah.80.5"><i>Gen. rabba</i>80(5</a>) (Theodor-Albeck #957/958, Sokoloff <i>Geniza Fragments</i> p. 170), <i>Midrash Cant</i>. 1(18), <i>Mekhilta dR. Ismael, Amaleq</i>1, <i>Mekhilta dR. Simeon ben Iohai Epstein-Melamed</i> p. 121, <i>Tanḥuma Bešallaḥ</i>26.The list itself is attributed in most sources to Issy ben Jehudah. [A discussion in principle about this subject is found in ש. קוגוט, המקרא בין טעמים לפרשנות, י־ם תשנד; מ. ברויאר, מקראות שאין להם הכרע, לשוננו נח (תשנה) 189־199.]</i>, which are the following: “gift, cursed, tomorrow, almond shaped, rise”. <i>Behold if you choose well the gift</i> or <i>the gift if you do not choose well</i><sup class="footnote-marker">326</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Genesis 4:7" href="/Genesis.4.7"><i>Gen.</i>4:7</a>. They must have read הֲלוֹא אִם־תֵּיטִ֔יב שְׂאֵ֕ת וְאִם֙ לֹא תֵיטִ֔יב but also in the masoretic text הֲלוֹא אִם־תֵּיטִיב֙ שְׂאֵ֔ת there is a stop between תֵּיטִיב֙ and שְׂאֵ֔ת.</i>. <i>For in their rage they killed a man and by their will castrated a cursed ox</i> or <i>cursed be their rage for it is strong</i><sup class="footnote-marker">327</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Genesis 49:6-7" href="/Genesis.49.6-7"><i>Gen.</i>49:6–7</a>. It is a question whether to read one or two sentences.</i>. <i>Moses said to Joshua . .</i> <i>go fight Amaleq tomorrow</i> or <i>tomorrow I shall stand on top of the hill</i><sup class="footnote-marker">328</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 17:9" href="/Exodus.17.9"><i>Ex</i>. 17:9</a>.</i>. <i>On the candelabra four cups almond shaped</i> or <i>almond shaped their knobs and their flowers</i><sup class="footnote-marker">329</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 25:34" href="/Exodus.25.34"><i>Ex</i>. 25:34</a>.</i>.<i>The Eternal said to Moses, you will lie with your fathers and rise</i> or <i>rise will this people and whore</i><sup class="footnote-marker">330</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 31:16" href="/Deuteronomy.31.16"><i>Deut</i>. 31:16</a>.</i>. Rebbi Tanḥuma added the following: <i>Jacob’s sons came from the field when they heard</i> or <i>when they heard the men were offended</i><sup class="footnote-marker">331</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Genesis 34:7" href="/Genesis.34.7"><i>Gen</i>. 34:7</a>. Most translations follow the masoretic punctuation in choosing the first alternative but the German translation by Torczyner <i>et al</i>. (Berlin 1934) which opts for the second.</i>. Rebbi La said, there are things about which one kisses the mouth, as it is said, <i>may he kiss me with the kisses of his mouth</i><sup class="footnote-marker">332</sup><i class="footnote">This answers R. Ḥama bar Uqba’s question. The verse <a class="refLink" data-ref="Song of Songs 1:2" href="/Song_of_Songs.1.2"><i>Cant</i>. 1:2</a> was chosen because its first part, quoted now, tells R. Ismael to be silent since a person who is kissed on his mouth cannot speak at that time. The first part clearly refers to a male; nevertheless R. Ismael had a point reading the second part as addressing a female since the sentence switches from third to second person, possibly indicating a change of speaker.</i>. Rebbi Isaac said, <i>and me did the Eternal command</i><sup class="footnote-marker">333</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 4:14" href="/Deuteronomy.4.14"><i>Deut</i>. 4:14</a>. Here starts a rather defective Genizah fragment (Ginzberg pp. 276–277.)</i>. “Me, and me.” Things were said to me that were said to you. And things were said to me alone<sup class="footnote-marker">334</sup><i class="footnote">Not everything has to be told to everybody.</i>.
Rebbi Simeon ben Ḥalfuta, Rebbi Ḥaggai in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan. <i>Lambs for your clothing; the price of a field are rams</i><sup class="footnote-marker">335</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Proverbs 27:26" href="/Proverbs.27.26"><i>Prov</i>. 27:26</a>.</i>. It is written “suppressings<sup class="footnote-marker">336</sup><i class="footnote">Identifying שׁ and שׂ for the homily.</i>”. How is this? If the students are young, suppress before them the secrets of the Torah. If they grew and became like rams, reveal to them the secrets of the Torah. This lends support to what Rebbi Simoen ben Ioḥai stated: <i>And these are the laws which you shall put before them</i><sup class="footnote-marker">337</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 21:1" href="/Exodus.21.1"><i>Ex</i>. 21:1</a>.</i>. Just as this putting is not revealed to everybody<sup class="footnote-marker">338</sup><i class="footnote">The laws in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 21-22" href="/Exodus.21-22"><i>Ex</i>. 21–22</a> are not to be told to Gentiles nor applied to their suits.</i> so you do have permission to immerse yourself in words of the Torah only before legitimate people.

Halakhah 8

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> The following matters of Gentiles are forbidden but their prohibition is not of usufruct: Milk which the Gentile milked when no Jew was seeing him, bread, their oil<sup class="footnote-marker">339</sup><i class="footnote">Olive oil.</i>, and anything preserved by cooking<sup class="footnote-marker">340</sup><i class="footnote">Even if the Gentile cooked with the Jew’s vessels under his control, as long as no Jew participated in the cooking process.</i>. Rebbi and his court permitted oil. Preserves in which usually wine is used<sup class="footnote-marker">341</sup><i class="footnote">Preserves made with spices without cooking, such as <i>Sauerkraut</i>, where wine might have been added. The wine is forbidden but there is no danger that it might have been used for pagan rituals.</i>, and vinegar<sup class="footnote-marker">342</sup><i class="footnote">Vinegar is not wine, but vinegar made from grapes is forbidden.</i>, and pieces of <i>triton</i><sup class="footnote-marker">343</sup><i class="footnote">The Latin name of a kind of sea fish mentioned by Pliny; in Greek it is the name of sea gods. Since the fish is cut in little pieces one cannot ascertain that no forbidden fish (without fins and scales) was used.</i>, and fish sauce<sup class="footnote-marker">344</sup><i class="footnote">Fluid obtained by squeezing fish.</i> not containing a whole fish, and <i>hallec</i><sup class="footnote-marker">345</sup><i class="footnote">“Fish sauce (<i>garum</i>) prepared from small fish” (Lewis and Short’s Latin Dictionary).</i>, and a lump of <i>asa foetida</i><sup class="footnote-marker">205</sup><i class="footnote">A medical concoction on vegetal basis but for which (forbidden) wine might have been used.</i>, and <i>salqonarit</i> salt<sup class="footnote-marker">346</sup><i class="footnote">Musaphia reads סלקונדית and explains as <i>sal conditum</i>, spiced salt (mentioned by Pliny xxxi,87 and in Diocletian’s price edict. A recipe is given by Apicius.) Kohut proposes <i>salcenarium</i> Medieval Latin “used for pickling”. Also consider <i>sal candidum</i> “white salt” (E. G.).</i>; these are forbidden but their prohibition is not of usufruct:
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “The following matters of Gentiles are forbidden,” etc. <sup class="footnote-marker">347</sup><i class="footnote">Parallels to these two paragraphs are in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Terumot 8:3:22-23" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Terumot.8.3.22-23"><i>Terumot</i> 8:5 (Notes 135–142</a>) and <i>Šabbat</i> 1:(3d line 2). The text in <i>Terumot</i> is a copy of the text in <i>Šabbat</i>; the text here is somewhat different.</i> Why is Gentile’s milk forbidden? Rebbi Abba (son of) [in the name of]<sup class="footnote-marker">348</sup><i class="footnote">The text of the Leiden ms. in parentheses has to be corrected by the text of ג in brackets and the parallels.</i> Rav Jehudah, Rebbi Simon in the name of Rebbi Joshua ben Levi: Why is Gentile’s milk forbidden? Because of uncovering<sup class="footnote-marker">158</sup><i class="footnote">In both Talmudim it is assumed that a snake will drink from uncovered unattended fluids and in the process poison the drink for humans. Then the drink cannot be used, neither can it be sold since this would be “putting a stone in the path of a blind person.” This makes uncovered unattended wine almost as forbidden as wine used for pagan practices.</i>. Let him make cheese! Rebbi Samuel ben Rav Isaac said, because of the poison given between the holes<sup class="footnote-marker">349</sup><i class="footnote">One seems to assume that the poison will be separated out in the process of solidification making hard cheese. But if the cheese has holes the poison will accumulate there and there may be small holes not directly visible.</i>. It was stated so: There are three kinds of poison; one swims, one sinks down, and one is similar to a net<sup class="footnote-marker">350</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 30b" href="/Avodah_Zarah.30b">Babli 30b</a>.</i>. Workers were in the field. The water pitcher was uncovered. The first ones drank and were [not]<sup class="footnote-marker">350a</sup><i class="footnote">In brackets is the addition of ג.</i> disabled. The second ones died. I say that it was sinking poison. In the days of Rebbi Jeremiah the barrels of the great assembly<sup class="footnote-marker">351</sup><i class="footnote">The half-yearly reunion of all members of the Academy.</i> were uncovered. The first ones drank and were not disabled. The second ones died. I say that it was sinking poison. It was stated thus<sup class="footnote-marker">352</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Terumot (Lieberman) 7:17" href="/Tosefta_Terumot_(Lieberman).7.17">Tosephta <i>Terumot</i> 7:17</a>.</i>: “If a watermelon was pecked and ten people ate from it or wine was uncovered and ten people drank from it, it is forbidden to eat or drink after them; I say that there is sinking poison.”
Rebbi Jeremiah said, why is Gentile’s milk forbidden? Because of admixture from forbidden animals<sup class="footnote-marker">353</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 35b" href="/Avodah_Zarah.35b">Babli 35b</a>. This is the only explanation given in the Babli where also it is noted that making cheese from the milk does not solve the problem.</i>. It was stated so<sup class="footnote-marker">354</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Avodah Zarah 5:5-6" href="/Tosefta_Avodah_Zarah.5.5-6">Tosephta 4:11</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 30b" href="/Avodah_Zarah.30b">Babli 30b</a>.</i>: “There is nothing to worry if the Jew sits with the herd while the Gentile milks and brings to him.”
<sup class="footnote-marker">355</sup><i class="footnote">There are three parallels to this text, in <i>Ševi`it</i> 8:5 (Notes 57–62), <i>Ma`aser Šeni</i> 3:1 (Notes 2–3, <b>מ</b>), and <i>Šabbat</i>1 (3c l. 55, <b>ש</b>). The Genizah text here, and the texts in <i>Ma`aser Šeni</i> and <i>Šabbat</i> are clearly copies of one and the same original; the text in <i>Ševi`it</i> is slightly different in wording but identical in meaning.</i> Their bread. Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa in the name of Rebbi Jonathan. Bread is of the practices of obfuscation. [Rebbi Yose said, so I asked before Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa: What means “of the practices of obfuscation?]<sup class="footnote-marker">356</sup><i class="footnote">Added from the three identical texts; most of the paragraph is taken up by the questions of R. Yose and R. Mana; only at the end the correct answer is spelled out.<br>The practice of obfuscation is that the rabbis have to justify the popular disregard of their prohibition.</i> Do I say, at a place where Jewish bread is available it is logical that Gentile bread should be forbidden and they obfuscated about it and permitted it? Or at a place where Jewish bread is not available it is logical that Gentile bread should be permitted and they obfuscated about it and forbade it? Rebbi Mana said, is there any obfuscation for prohibition<sup class="footnote-marker">357</sup><i class="footnote">As shown in the Mishnah, rabbinic prohibitions do not need justification, only permissions which are inconsistent with general prohibition, such as to permit bread while prohibiting all other kinds of Gentile cooking.</i>? But is bread not like cooking of Gentiles? Are we saying: At a place where no Jewish cooking is available it is logical that Gentile cooking should be permitted<sup class="footnote-marker">358</sup><i class="footnote">The texts in <i>Ševi`it</i>, <i>Ma`aser Šeni</i>, and <i>Šabbat</i> add: “and they obfuscated about it and forbade it.” But the illegible space in <b>ג</b> is not sufficient for this addition; one has to assume that it never was in the <i>Avodah zarah</i> text.</i>? But it must be the following: At a place where Jewish bread is not available it is logical that Gentile bread should be forbidden and they obfuscated about it and permitted it because of the necessities of life. The rabbis of Caesarea in the name of Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa: Following the words of him who was permitting, but only from the store<sup class="footnote-marker">359</sup><i class="footnote">Greek πρατήρ “seller”. Lieberman reads πωλητήριον “retail store”, Kohut Latin <i>panetarius</i> “bread merchant”.</i>; but one does not do this<sup class="footnote-marker">360</sup><i class="footnote">But eats also privately baked Gentile bread; forbidden in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 35b" href="/Avodah_Zarah.35b">Babli 35b</a>.</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">361</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Šabbat</i> 1 (3c line 65). The Geniza fragment (ג) ends at the mention of Rav. Another fragment, in much better shape, edited by J. N. Epstein [<i>Tarbiz</i> 3(1932) 15-26 (G)] starts at the second mention of <i>Deut. 2:28.</i></i> They said before the Elder Rebbi Ḥiyya: Rebbi Simeon ben Ioḥai stated thus: <i>Food you shall buy from them with money and eat</i>, etc<sup class="footnote-marker">362</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 2:28" href="/Deuteronomy.2.28"><i>Deut.</i>2:28</a>. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 37b" href="/Avodah_Zarah.37b">Babli, 37b</a>, a statement of R. Joḥanan. The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 38a" href="/Avodah_Zarah.38a">Babli (38a</a>) negates a biblical base for the prohibition of Gentile cooking.</i>. As water is not changed from its natural state, so anything which was not changed from its natural state. They objected: Are there not their dry beans<sup class="footnote-marker">363</sup><i class="footnote">The list also appears in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Avodah Zarah 5:5-6" href="/Tosefta_Avodah_Zarah.5.5-6">Tosephta 4:11</a>. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 38b" href="/Avodah_Zarah.38b">Babli 38b</a> the word is explained as meaning either φάσηλος or שעועית, both meaning “bean”.</i>, פנקריסין<sup class="footnote-marker">365</sup><i class="footnote">The word is unexplained. In the Babli, <i>loc. cit</i>., and similarly in the Tosephta, it appears asקפריסין  “capers”.</i> and קובטיות<sup class="footnote-marker">366</sup><i class="footnote">In the Babli קפלוטות “leeks”. In Tosephta קפטאות, קפאטיות. N. Brüll (<i>Jahrbücher für jüdische Geschichte und Literatur</i> 5) proposes <i>copta</i> قُباط  “fig preserve”. This agrees well with the quote in the Babli since, as stated later also here, all preserves made from fruit which also is eaten raw are not under the prohibition of Gentile cooking. The only problem is that the next sentence implies that the word describes a dried fruit or vegetable which can be returned more or less to its original state but soaking.</i>, roasted grain, and hot water permitted? One understands all of these because they can be soaked and return to their former state. But roasted grain? Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun in the name of Rav<sup class="footnote-marker">367</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 38a" href="/Avodah_Zarah.38a">Babli 38a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 59a" href="/Avodah_Zarah.59a">59a</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shabbat 51a" href="/Shabbat.51a"><i>Šabbat</i>51a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 11a" href="/Bava_Kamma.11a"><i>Bava qamma</i>11a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Yevamot 12b" href="/Yevamot.12b"><i>Yebamot</i>12b</a>. Also <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 6:1:2-7" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.6.1.2-7">Yerushalmi <i>Nedarim</i>6:1</a> (39c l. 44).</i>: Any food which can be eaten alone raw is not in the category of Gentile cooking<sup class="footnote-marker">368</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Šabbat</i> text adds: And it may be used for <i>eruv tavšilin</i> (a symbolic act which turns a common courtyard or a dead-end alley into a private domain, or which lets one prepare cooked food for the Sabbath on a holiday which falls on a Friday); this formulation may be taken from the end of the Halakhah</i>. (With bread it is in the category of Gentile cooking<sup class="footnote-marker">369</sup><i class="footnote">This restriction is found neither in the <i>Šabbat</i> text nor the Babli. It also contradicts the insertion in the <i>Šabbat</i> text (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Avodah Zarah 2:8:13" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Avodah_Zarah.2.8.13">Note 368</a>). Either it is spurious or the text here disagrees with the text there.</i>.) How does Rebbi Ḥiyya explain <i>food you shall buy from them</i>? You shall buy with food. If you fed him you bought him. If he causes you trouble buy him with food or otherwise trade (ply him)<sup class="footnote-marker">370</sup><i class="footnote">Reading of the corrector of L. The scribe wrote as in the <i>Šabbat</i> text and G.</i> with him money. They said, that is what Rebbi Jonathan did. When a superior<sup class="footnote-marker">371</sup><i class="footnote">A superior Roman judge; cf. <i>Introduction to Tractate Neziqin</i> p. 4, remark about R. Jonathan.</i> came to [his]<sup class="footnote-marker">372</sup><i class="footnote">Readings of G and the <i>Šabbat</i> text.</i> town, he sent him an honor gift, that if a suit involving an orphan or a widow came he would have a way to mollify him.
<sup class="footnote-marker">373</sup><i class="footnote">The following also is in <i>Šabbat</i> I, 3d line 16 ff., <b>ש</b>. While the sentence order there differs from here, the fact that G while following the sentence order of L very often follows the formulation of <b>ש</b> indicates that the two texts are essentially the same.</i> Who forbade the oil? Rav Jehudah said, Daniel forbade it. <i>Daniel was careful</i>, etc<sup class="footnote-marker">374</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Daniel 1:8" href="/Daniel.1.8"><i>Dan</i>. 1:8</a>. The verse does not seem to mention olive oil. As the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 36a" href="/Avodah_Zarah.36a">Babli explains (36a</a>), the argument is from the continuation, <i>that he would not defile himself by the king’s solid food and the wine of his drinks</i>, where the plural <i>drinks</i> refers both to wine (whose prohibition is generally accepted) and oil (where Daniel’s action rather is an act of personal piety.) In any case, the prohibition of Gentile olive oil never was considered to have any biblical basis.</i>.
And who permitted it? Rebbi permitted it and his court<sup class="footnote-marker">375</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 36a" href="/Avodah_Zarah.36a"><i>Babli</i>36a</a>.</i>. At three places is Rebbi Jehudah the Prince called “our teachers,” in divorce documents<sup class="footnote-marker">376</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 7:3:2-4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.7.3.2-4"><i>Giṭtin</i>7:3, Notes 49–64</a>. The essence of the controversy is whether a divorce document subject to a certain condition is only valid after the condition has been satisfied or is valid immediately but not actionable unless the condition be satisfied. The simplest example is that of a sick childless man who wants to shield his wife from levirate marriage (or prevent her from marrying his brother) by writing a bill of divorce valid if he should die from his disease. If he stipulates that divorce be “from today if I shall die”, the divorce is unquestionably valid. If he omits “from today” then for Rebbi it is valid, for the rabbis it is invalid since a dead person cannot divorce.</i>, oil, and a sole<sup class="footnote-marker">377</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Niddah 3:4:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Niddah.3.4.3"><i>Niddah</i>3:4, Notes 93–98</a>. The “sole” is the result of a miscarriage where no features are recognizable in the aborted fetus. For the rabbis, the woman is subject to all disabilities of a woman who gave birth to a male and a female (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 12:1-8" href="/Leviticus.12.1-8"><i>Lev</i>. 12:1–8</a>), for Rebbi and his court only if human features were recognizable.</i>. They called him “the court which permitted oil.<sup class="footnote-marker">378</sup><i class="footnote">In the other two sources: Should he not be called “a permissive court” (a pejorative term) since …“</i>” Any court which permits three [previously forbidden] things is called “permissive court.” Rebbi Yudan said, his court disagreed with him about the bills of divorce. <sup class="footnote-marker">379</sup><i class="footnote">These two sentences really do not belong here; they are copied from the text in <i>Giṭtin</i>(Note 54) which thereby is shown to be the source of the preceding sentences, and refer to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 7:8:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.7.8.1">Mishnah <i>Giṭtin</i>7:10</a> about a man who gave his wife a bill of divorce valid if he should not return within 12 months, and notice was received of his death. Does she have to wait the full 12 months or not?</i> May she be allowed to remarry? Rebbi Ḥaggai said, she is allowed to remarry. Rebbi Yose said, she is forbidden to remarry.
Rebbi Aḥa, Rebbi Tanḥum bar Ḥiyya in the name of Rebbi Ḥanina, but some say in the name of Rebbi Joshua ben Levi: They were climbing up King’s Mountain and were killed about it<sup class="footnote-marker">380</sup><i class="footnote">In <b>ש</b>, this immediately follows the statement that Daniel forbade Gentile oil and invalidates it. It is not clear what danger there was in getting Gentile oil but since both Talmudim declare the prohibition of Gentile oil to be of the 18 prohibitions promulgated in the run-up to the first revolt against the Romans (<i>Šabbat</i> I, 3c l.37, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 17b" href="/Avodah_Zarah.17b">Babli 17b</a>) it must refer to the situation there. The exact meaning of “King’s mountain” as a geographical entity has not been determined.</i>. Isaac bar Samuel bar Martha went down to Nisibis. He met Simlai<sup class="footnote-marker">381</sup><i class="footnote">In the Babli and <b>ש</b>, as well as in the quote from the Yerushalmi in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosafot on Avodah Zarah 36a:6:1" href="/Tosafot_on_Avodah_Zarah.36a.6.1">Tosaphot 36a, s.v. אשר</a>, R. Simlai. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 37a" href="/Avodah_Zarah.37a">Babli 37a</a>. This dates “R. Jehudah” here as R. Jehudah II. For “Southerner” the Babli has “from Lydda”, which is taken as a pejorative term.</i> the Southerner who sat and expounded: Rebbi and his court permitted the oil. Samuel said, true<sup class="footnote-marker">382</sup><i class="footnote">In the two other sources, probably correctly, “Samuel accepted this and ate.”</i>! Rav did not accept on himself to eat. Samuel told him, eat! Otherwise I shall declare you a rebellious Elder<sup class="footnote-marker">383</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sanhedrin 8:6:5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sanhedrin.8.6.5"><i>Sanhedrin</i>8:6, Note 64</a>.</i>. He answered him, when I still was there<sup class="footnote-marker">384</sup><i class="footnote">When Rav was studying in Galilee under Rebbi (Jehudah I) and R. Ḥiyya the Elder.</i> I knew who complained about it, Simlai the Southerner. He told him<sup class="footnote-marker">385</sup><i class="footnote">Rav to Samuel. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 37a" href="/Avodah_Zarah.37a">Babli. 37a</a>, R. Simlai is described as being critical of the decision to allow Gentile oil.</i>, he said it in the name of himself, not in the name of Rebbi Jehudah the Prince. He bothered him and he ate<sup class="footnote-marker">386</sup><i class="footnote">Rav ate in order to sustain the authority of the Patriarchate.</i>.
Rebbi Joḥanan asked: Did we not state, “for no court may invalidate the words of another court unless it be greater in wisdom and numbers”<sup class="footnote-marker">387</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Eduyot 1:5" href="/Mishnah_Eduyot.1.5">Mishnah <i>Idiut</i>1:5</a>.</i>? And Rebbi and his court permit what Daniel and his companions forbade? Rebbi Joḥanan follows his own opinion: Rebbi Joḥanan said, I have a tradition in the name of Rebbi Eleazar ben Rebbi Ṣadoq that any restrictive edict passed by a court which is not accepted by the majority of the public is not an edict<sup class="footnote-marker">388</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 36a" href="/Avodah_Zarah.36a">Babli 36a</a>: It is forbidden to promulgate a decree that the majority of the public cannot accept.</i>. They checked and found in the matter of the edict about oil and did not find that a majority of the public followed it<sup class="footnote-marker">389</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 36a" href="/Avodah_Zarah.36a">Babli 36a</a>. This proves conclusively that the original prohibition of oil was relatively recent and purely rabbinic.</i>.
Their roasted egg, Bar Qappara permitted, Ḥizqiah forbade<sup class="footnote-marker">390</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 38b" href="/Avodah_Zarah.38b">Babli 38b</a>. The egg is roasted in its shell so that there are no problems of non-kosher admixtures. It is not impossible to eat an egg raw.</i>. So far if it was roasted intentionally. If it was roasted unintentionally? Let us hear from the following: There was a fire in an under-growth of reeds and a swamp of date palms where there were locusts which were roasted. The case came before Rebbi Mana<sup class="footnote-marker">391</sup><i class="footnote">R. Mana I.</i>, who forbade them. Rebbi Abbahu said, because of admixture of impure locusts<sup class="footnote-marker">392</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 38a" href="/Avodah_Zarah.38a">Babli 38a</a>.</i>. Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun said, and this is of the stringencies of Rav. Rav returned there and saw them taking things lightly and was restrictive with them<sup class="footnote-marker">393</sup><i class="footnote">He used his office as overseer of Jewish markets to enforce strict rabbinic rules.</i>. A man was carrying meat while walking on the market. There came a vulture, seized it from his hand, and it dropped off<sup class="footnote-marker">394</sup><i class="footnote">As recognized by J. N. Epstein, Aramaicאתֿר  is Af`el of Aramaicנתֿר  Hebrew נשר.</i> under him. He went and wanted to sell it. Rav told him, it is forbidden to you, for I am saying that it was carrying carcass meat, dropped it and took the other instead<sup class="footnote-marker">395</sup><i class="footnote">This is a case of “meat which had been lost sight of” which must be treated as non-kosher; it cannot be sold as kosher meat. Quoted in <i>Tosaphot Bava meṣi`a</i>24b <i>s. v</i>. אתא.</i>. A man went to wash a piece of meat<sup class="footnote-marker">396</sup><i class="footnote">The translation is a guess based on the context; the word itself is unexplained. It appears only here and in the exact parallel <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shekalim 7:2:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shekalim.7.2.3"><i>Šeqalim</i>7:3</a> (50c l. 47). Sokoloff in his <i>Dictionary of Jewish Palestinian Aramaic</i><sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup> accepts also Jastrow’s explanation “an object on which the meat was hung” but this seems to be excluded by the context.</i> in the river. He forgot it and left. Then he went and wanted to sell it. Rav told him, it is forbidden to you, for I am saying the other one was carried away by the river and it brought another piece of carcass meat in its place<sup class="footnote-marker">397</sup><i class="footnote">A similar story in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Chullin 95" href="/Chullin.95">Babli <i>Ḥulin</i>95a–b</a>.</i>.
What is the status of their lupines<sup class="footnote-marker">398</sup><i class="footnote">They cannot be eaten raw; according to Yerushalmi rules cooked lupines should be forbidden as Gentile’s cooking. But Ganiba was a Babylonian and the Babli has a second category of food exempted from the rules: coarse food not eaten in polite society (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 38a" href="/Avodah_Zarah.38a">Babli 38a</a>, rules of the autochthonous academy of Pumbedita.) The paragraph makes clear that this tradition never was accepted in the domain of influence of the Academy of Tiberias.</i>? Rebbi forbids, Ganiba permits. Rebbi said, I am an Elder and he is an Elder. It came to my mind to forbid; it was his opinion to permit. Rebbi Mana bar Tanḥum went to Tyre and permitted their lupines. Rebbi Ḥiyya bar Abba went to Tyre and found that Rebbi Mana had permitted their lupines. He returned to Rebbi Joḥanan who asked him, what case came to your hands? He told him, I found that Rebbi Mana had permitted their lupines. He asked him, did you not hit him? He answered, he is an important personality and knows how to sweeten the waters of the Great Sea. He told him<sup class="footnote-marker">399</sup><i class="footnote">Here ends the second Genizah fragment.</i>, no, my son. He knows how to compute the waters; when the waters come together to acclaim their Creator<sup class="footnote-marker">400</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <i>Midrash Tehillim</i> on <i>Ps.</i>93:4.</i> they sweeten themselves. Rebbi Isaac bar Eleazar said, he starts to speak his disgrace and tells his praise! Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun said, it is a difficult computation; Rebbi Zakkai of Alexandria knows it. He said, if you want, you may learn it from him.
What is the rule for their parboiled [food]<sup class="footnote-marker">401</sup><i class="footnote">Scalding is not boiling; while the food is prepared it is not cooked.</i>? Let us hear from the following: Rebbi Immi went with Rebbi Jehudah to the hot springs of Gadara and permitted their parboiled [food]. Rebbi Abba bar Mamal asked, what is the difference between parboiled and lupines? Rebbi Yose said, parboiled is missing action by fire; lupines are not missing action by fire.
<i>Ḥawarnas</i><sup class="footnote-marker">402</sup><i class="footnote">These two words are unexplained; they can be given sense only by radical emendation. Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun in the name of Rav: Any food which can be eaten alone raw is not in the category of Gentile cooking and one may use it for <i>eruv tavšilin</i><sup class="footnote-marker">368</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Šabbat</i> text adds: And it may be used for <i>eruv tavšilin</i> (a symbolic act which turns a common courtyard or a dead-end alley into a private domain, or which lets one prepare cooked food for the Sabbath on a holiday which falls on a Friday); this formulation may be taken from the end of the Halakhah</i>.

Halakhah 9

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> The following are permitted to eat: Milk which a Gentile milked while a Jew saw him, and honey, and broken grapes<sup class="footnote-marker">403</sup><i class="footnote">Table grapes transported in baskets, not vine grapes transported in barrels. In the first case, juice dripping from broken grape berries is lost. In the second case the juice is collected in the barrel and poured into the wine press; this juice follows all the rules of wine and becomes forbidden by the Gentile’s touch.</i> (even though they drip they do not prepare for impurity.<sup class="footnote-marker">404</sup><i class="footnote">Any vegetable food cannot become impure as long as it is connected to the ground. After harvest it can become impure once it comes into contact with water (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 11:38" href="/Leviticus.11.38"><i>Lev</i>. 11:38</a>). Juice is treated as water in this respect. But the verse reads, <i>if water is poured on vegetables</i>, this is read to imply that contact with water makes vegetables susceptible to impurity only if it was poured intentionally or at least if the wetting was agreeable to the owner (<i>Sifra Šemini Pereq</i> 11). Therefore juice which flows out of table grapes does not prepare for impurity since it is lost, but juice flowing from wine grapes does prepare for impurity since it is not lost and eases the work of wine pressing.</i>) Preserves in which usually wine or vinegar is not used, and uncut <i>triton</i><sup class="footnote-marker">343</sup><i class="footnote">The Latin name of a kind of sea fish mentioned by Pliny; in Greek it is the name of sea gods. Since the fish is cut in little pieces one cannot ascertain that no forbidden fish (without fins and scales) was used.</i>, and fish sauce containing a whole fish<sup class="footnote-marker">344</sup><i class="footnote">Fluid obtained by squeezing fish.</i>, and a leaf of <i>asa foetida</i><sup class="footnote-marker">205</sup><i class="footnote">A medical concoction on vegetal basis but for which (forbidden) wine might have been used.</i>, and rolled olives<sup class="footnote-marker">405</sup><i class="footnote">It seems that the correct reading is that of the <i>Arukh</i>: זיתי קלוסטא המגולגלים and the expression is a double Greek-Hebrew, the olives are “rolled” (or “apt to be rolled”) Greek κυλιστός and Hebrew “rolled”. Cf. E. and H. Guggenheimer, Notes on the Talmudic Vocabulary, <i>Lešonenu</i> 37 (1973) pp. 23–25.</i>; Rebbi Yose says, the pitted ones are forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">406</sup><i class="footnote">He thinks that standard technology is to soften the olives by vinegar before removing the pits (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Avodah Zarah 5:3" href="/Tosefta_Avodah_Zarah.5.3">Tosephta 4:8</a>).</i>. Locusts from the basket are forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">407</sup><i class="footnote">One assumes that the store will sprinkle the dead locusts on display with vinegar to keep them looking fresh (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Avodah Zarah 5:6" href="/Tosefta_Avodah_Zarah.5.6">Tosephta 4:12</a>).</i>, from storage permitted; similarly for heave<sup class="footnote-marker">408</sup><i class="footnote">A Cohen, who certainly is Jewish, has a food store and therefore can legally refreshen his locusts with vinegar or wine. If he is suspected of illegally using his heave wine or vinegar, which by biblical decree must be eaten by the Cohen in purity and should not be used commercially, to sprinkle on locusts for sale, he has to be treated the same as a Gentile seller of the same.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “The following are permitted for usufruct,<sup class="footnote-marker">409</sup><i class="footnote">This looks like a misquote from the Mishnah but probably is not since the following statement of R. Eleazar makes sense only if it relates to usufruct, not to food eaten by Jews.</i>” etc. Rebbi Eleazar said, about a dish in which usually one does not add wine or vinegar. Therefore if it is certain that he added, it is forbidden even for usufruct<sup class="footnote-marker">410</sup><i class="footnote">Since one refers to dishes produced by Gentiles they are forbidden as Gentile cooking. The only question is whether a Jew may use them as objects of trade. He may do that only if in the standard cook books no addition of wine or vinegar is required. In any case, if it is known that wine or vinegar was added it is forbidden for usufruct. If nothing is known but the standard recipe does not call for wine it is a legitimate object of trade.</i>. Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa, Rebbi Ḥiyya in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: If a Jew and a Gentile put a pot on the fire, the Jew puts it down and the Gentile stirs<sup class="footnote-marker">411</sup><i class="footnote">The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 38b" href="/Avodah_Zarah.38b">Babli 38b</a> declares that there is no prohibition of Gentile cooking if either the Jew puts it on the fire and the Gentile cooks or the Gentile puts it on the fire and the Jew cooks.</i>. Who puts it back? One would say that the Jew puts it back<sup class="footnote-marker">412</sup><i class="footnote">Since the Yerushalmi requires that the Jew put the dish on the fire it is obvious that he has to put it back if for some reason the pot was taken off the fire.</i>. Rebbi Benjamin bar Levai said, only if it is cooked like Ben-Drosai’s<sup class="footnote-marker">413</sup><i class="footnote">“The trampler”, a famous robber who always was on the run and therefore could eat his meat only rare. “Ben Derosai’s food” is rare, cooked one-third of normal cooking time.</i> food. Rebbi Yose asked, if it is cooked like Ben-Drosai’s food, why does the Jew have to put it back? Even the Gentile may put it back<sup class="footnote-marker">414</sup><i class="footnote">Any food cooked rare is no longer subject to the rules of Gentile cooking. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 38a" href="/Avodah_Zarah.38a">Babli 38a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shabbat 20a" href="/Shabbat.20a"><i>Šabbat</i> 20a</a>.</i>.
What is alec? Rav said, <i>soltanit</i><sup class="footnote-marker">415</sup><i class="footnote">There is no linguistic evidence for translating either sardine or herring. All one has to go on is a remark in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 29a" href="/Avodah_Zarah.29a">Babli 29a</a> that <i>soltanit</i> is forbidden because it is a small fish caught together with non-kosher fish. Cf. Latin <i>solea</i> “sole” (also the fish).</i>. Rebbi Joḥanan said, <i>alec</i> and cut-up triton follow the same rules. Rebbi Ze`ira, Cahana bar Taḥlifa, Ḥanan bar Abba in the name of Rav: Its mucus is forbidden because of admixture from prohibited fish. Rebbi Abba in the name of Rav Jehudah: This you say at a place were water is not running. But in running water no impure fish associates with pure fish. The Sea of Genezareth is similar to running water. Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun said, only at times when the fish bites. Samuel said, <i>ḥafitha</i><sup class="footnote-marker">416</sup><i class="footnote">This kind of fish likewise has not been identified. In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shir HaShirim Rabbah 1:4" href="/Shir_HaShirim_Rabbah.1.4"><i>Cant. rabba ad</i> 1:4</a> it is noted that it is a jumping fish. Arabic حٍفث is a large non poisonous snake.</i> one judges according to its shell. If it produces mucus it is permitted, otherwise forbidden.
What is uncut triton? Any where heads and spines exist<sup class="footnote-marker">417</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 40a" href="/Avodah_Zarah.40a">Babli 40a</a>, opinion of Rav Huna.</i>. Rebbi Eleazar in the name of Rebbi Ḥanina: It happened that on a ship of the House of Rebbi there were more than 300 amphoras. Rebbi checked them all out and found only one where heads and spines were found. He permitted all of them. Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa said, Rebbi Assi<sup class="footnote-marker">190</sup><i class="footnote">This is the Babli’s name of R. Yasa who later is quoted with his Galilean name. Note also in the first part Babylonian הנאה for Galilean הנייה.</i> asked, would it not be reasonable that one amphora be permitted but the other amphoras be forbidden? But it was by a pretext.<sup class="footnote-marker">418</sup><i class="footnote">Since all of the amphoras came from the same source, he had reason to assume that all of them were made by the same rule.</i> <sup class="footnote-marker">419</sup><i class="footnote">The following sentences are from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Terumot 10:5:6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Terumot.10.5.6"><i>Terumot</i> 10:7, Notes 91–94</a>. The common topic are subterfuges to stretch permissions over the reasonable limit.</i> (A sale)<sup class="footnote-marker">420</sup><i class="footnote">In <i>Terumot</i> one reads “cheeses”, ג for ז, a not infrequent misreading. While the principle of R. Abba bar Zavda refers to public property, here he applied it to loads on a common carrier. This is a subterfuge.</i> was mixed up. Rebbi Ḥaggai asked Rebbi Abba bar Zavda who said to him, the property of the public cannot be forbidden. Rebbi Jacob bar Zavdi said, Rebbi Isaac asked: Is not Cajus Lycus written on these documents, and you say, by a pretext<sup class="footnote-marker">421</sup><i class="footnote">Divorce documents can only be signed by Jews. In the diaspora, one must accept also Jewish witnesses with Latin and Greek names; therefore the appearance of foreign names on documents is no reason for attacking the genuineness of a document under any circumstance. This is a subterfuge.</i>?
Ulla the leatherworker<sup class="footnote-marker">422</sup><i class="footnote">Accadic <i>aškapu</i>.</i> stated before Rebbi Dosa, an impure fish has live births, a pure fish lays eggs<sup class="footnote-marker">423</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 40a" href="/Avodah_Zarah.40a">Babli 40a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bekhorot 7b" href="/Bekhorot.7b"><i>Bekhorot</i> 7b</a>.</i>. He started again and stated, fish intestines and fish innards are eaten only upon certification by an expert<sup class="footnote-marker">424</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 40a" href="/Avodah_Zarah.40a">Babli 40a</a></i>. He told him, change your statement! This one state or that one. Rebbi Ze`ira said to him, do not change your statement. They are finished only upon being laid<sup class="footnote-marker">425</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 40a" href="/Avodah_Zarah.40a">Babli 40a</a> the question is asked by Ulla (ben Ismael) to R. Dositheos of Bire and answered by R. Ze`ira correctly that all lay eggs but some impure fish hatch their eggs in their body (clearly excluding mammals). It is not impossible to read the same meaning into the statement here.</i>. Rebbi Abba in the name of Rav Jehudah, if one tells you, I salted them, he may be believed<sup class="footnote-marker">426</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 40b" href="/Avodah_Zarah.40b">Babli 40b</a>. If he asserts that he checked the fish and they were of the pure kind, he does not have to be an expert.</i> Nathan bar Abba said before Samuel, I know how to distinguish between fetuses of impure fish and fetuses of pure fish. The fetuses of impure fish are spherical, the fetuses of pure fish are elongated<sup class="footnote-marker">427</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 40a" href="/Avodah_Zarah.40a">Babli, 40a</a>, it is asserted that impure fish eggs are symmetrical ovaloid, pure fish eggs asymmetrical.</i>. He showed him a <i>salpe</i><sup class="footnote-marker">428</sup><i class="footnote">Greek σάλπη, Latin <i>salpa</i>, -<i>ae</i>, “stock-fish”.</i>; he asked him, what is its status? He answered, impure. He told him, not only am I aggravated that you said impure of what is pure but at the end you will say pure of what is impure.
It was stated: One buys fish intestines and fish innards only upon certification by an expert<sup class="footnote-marker">429</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Avodah Zarah 5:7" href="/Tosefta_Avodah_Zarah.5.7">Tosephta 4:13</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 39b" href="/Avodah_Zarah.39b">Babli 39b</a>.</i>. One buys dark blue wool only upon certification by an expert<sup class="footnote-marker">204,</sup><i class="footnote">Woolen threads for use in the <i>ṣiṣit</i>, which by tradition must be dyed with the blood of a marine snail which can easily be counterfeit by (much cheaper) indigo, and therefore needs certification. (In modern Hebrew תכלת is light blue but classically very dark blue.)</i><sup class="footnote-marker">430</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Menachot 42b" href="/Menachot.42b">Babli <i>Menaḥot</i> 42b</a>.</i>. One buys Bithynian<sup class="footnote-marker">431</sup><i class="footnote">The translation assumes that ותינייקי is a scribal error for בתינייקי “Bithynian”, which in the Mishnah in the Babli (Note 270) characterizes cheese which is forbidden for usufruct if produced by Gentiles. In the Tosephta <i>ed</i>. <i>princeps</i> בית היניקאי, Erfurt ms. ובית הינייקי, Vienna ms. missing. The Vienna text is the only one agreeing with the Mishnah as printed in the Yerushalmi editions.</i> cheese only upon certification by an expert<sup class="footnote-marker">429</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Avodah Zarah 5:7" href="/Tosefta_Avodah_Zarah.5.7">Tosephta 4:13</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 39b" href="/Avodah_Zarah.39b">Babli 39b</a>.</i>. One buys wine in Syria only upon certification by an expert<sup class="footnote-marker">429</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Avodah Zarah 5:7" href="/Tosefta_Avodah_Zarah.5.7">Tosephta 4:13</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 39b" href="/Avodah_Zarah.39b">Babli 39b</a>.</i>. One buys meat without a mark only upon certification by an expert<sup class="footnote-marker">429</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Avodah Zarah 5:7" href="/Tosefta_Avodah_Zarah.5.7">Tosephta 4:13</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 39b" href="/Avodah_Zarah.39b">Babli 39b</a>.</i>. But all one may eat at a person’s who is not an expert without worry<sup class="footnote-marker">429</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Avodah Zarah 5:7" href="/Tosefta_Avodah_Zarah.5.7">Tosephta 4:13</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 39b" href="/Avodah_Zarah.39b">Babli 39b</a>.</i>.
Rebbi Aha, Rebbi Tanḥum in the name of Rebbi Joshua ben Levi: If one sent <i>asa foetida</i> he is trustworthy<sup class="footnote-marker">432</sup><i class="footnote">If a person sends a gift to another of a substance whose kosher status might be questionable, the recipient may assume that the gift was kosher, otherwise it would not have been sent without proper warning. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 39b" href="/Avodah_Zarah.39b">Babli 39b</a>.</i>. Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa, Rebbi Jacob bar Idi in the name of Rebbi Joṣhua ben Levi: If one sent dark blue wool he is trustworthy. The lad of Levi of Ṣanbar was selling produce<sup class="footnote-marker">433</sup><i class="footnote">The lad was a slave. The question was whether the buyer of produce may assume that heave and tithes were taken from produce sold by the servant of a person who himself was known to be trustworthy in matters of heave and tithes. Since the slave’s owner would not tolerate untithed produce in his house, one must assume that any produce sold by his slave was tithed. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 39a" href="/Avodah_Zarah.39a">Babli 39a</a>.</i>. Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa in the name of Rebbi Yasa: The slave of a trustworthy person is trustworthy. Germanus the slave of Rebbi Jehudah the Prince had dark blue wool. Rebbi Yasa in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: The slave of an expert is like an expert<sup class="footnote-marker">434</sup><i class="footnote">This is parallel to the previous case. Since R. Jehudah the Prince would not tolerate fake blue wool on his property, one may buy blue wool threads from his slave even if the latter was uneducated.</i>.
“And <i>salqonarit</i> salt.” Some Tannaïm state, black one is forbidden but white permitted. But some Tannaïm state, white one is forbidden but black permitted<sup class="footnote-marker">435</sup><i class="footnote">One of these is R. Meïr, the other R. Jehudah, but <a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 39b" href="/Avodah_Zarah.39b">Babli 39b</a> and <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Avodah Zarah 5:1-2" href="/Tosefta_Avodah_Zarah.5.1-2">Tosephta 4</a>:12 give contradictory attributions. One has to assume that the editors of the Yerushalmi refrained from attaching names to the traditions since they knew about differing attributions.</i>. He who said black one is forbidden, because one puts a black crawling thing in it. He who said white one is forbidden, because one puts a white crawling thing in it. Rebbi Ḥanania ben Gamliel said in the name of Rebbi Jehudah ben Gamliel, both are forbidden. Rebbi Ḥanania<sup class="footnote-marker">436</sup><i class="footnote">He is the previously mentioned R. Ḥanania ben Gamliel, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 39b" href="/Avodah_Zarah.39b">Babli 39b</a>.</i> said, we had a neighbor who was mixing it with pig fat.
<sup class="footnote-marker">437</sup><i class="footnote">In this paragraph one gives example of technical terms used in Mishnaiot which are essentially the same. The list is made to explain the term זֵיתֵי קֻלוּסְקָא הַמְגוּלְגָּלִין which however is somewhat different in combining two expressions which are translations of one another (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Avodah Zarah 2:9:9" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Avodah_Zarah.2.9.9">Note 405</a>).</i> Coverings are the same as protuberances<sup class="footnote-marker">438</sup><i class="footnote">In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Oholot 8:2" href="/Mishnah_Oholot.8.2">Mishnah <i>Ahilut</i> 8:2</a> it is explained that סְכָכוֹת  refers to trees giving shade and פְּרָעוֹת to bushes growing out of a stone wall. Both transmit “tent” impurity.</i>. Veiled is the same as hulled<sup class="footnote-marker">439</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah <i>Šabbat</i> 6:6: On a Sabbath, a Jewish woman in Arabia may walk in the public domain veiled and in Persia hulled.</i>. Roofs are the same as covers<sup class="footnote-marker">440</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Kelim 8:9" href="/Mishnah_Kelim.8.9">Mishnah <i>Kelim</i> 8:9</a>. An oven (<i>furnus</i>) may become impure if according to R. Jehudah it has a roof (στέγη), according to Rabban Gamliel if it has a platform (שְׂפָיוֹת).</i>. Cups are the same as grooves<sup class="footnote-marker">441</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Parah 2:5" href="/Mishnah_Parah.2.5">Mishnah <i>Parah</i> 2:5</a>: Two black hairs growing in the same groove disqualify a red cow; R. Jehudah says in the same cup.</i>. A place under cups is a place for putting down pieces<sup class="footnote-marker">442</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Kelim 22:1" href="/Mishnah_Kelim.22.1">Mishnah <i>Kelim</i> 22:1</a>: A table or tripod (portable pieces of furniture which may become impure if whole) which were damaged but were covered with a marble top (which cannot become impure) still may become impure if the cover excludes a place for putting there cups (majority opinion) or pieces (R. Jehudah).</i>. Κυλιστόι olives are rolled olives,<sup class="footnote-marker">405</sup><i class="footnote">It seems that the correct reading is that of the <i>Arukh</i>: זיתי קלוסטא המגולגלים  and the expression is a double Greek-Hebrew, the olives are “rolled” (or “apt to be rolled”) Greek κυλιστός and Hebrew “rolled”. Cf. E. and H. Guggenheimer, Notes on the Talmudic Vocabulary, <i>Lešonenu</i> 37 (1973) pp. 23–25.</i>.
“The pitted are forbidden.” Rebbi Ḥiyya in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan said, it is a variety<sup class="footnote-marker">443</sup><i class="footnote">A kind of olives which can easily be freed of its pits. The name refers to the root שלח I “to strip off”.</i> and they put vinegar on them to be able to remove their pits.

Chapter 3



Halakhah 1

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> All statues are prohibited<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">Prohibited for usufruct. For R. Meïr there are no statues made for artistic value alone.</i> because they are worshipped once a year, the words of Rebbi Meïr, but the Sages say, prohibited are only those in whose hand is a rod<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">A symbol of command.</i>, or a bird<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">A symbol of having access everywhere.</i>, or a globe<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">A symbol of ruling the earth.</i>. Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel says, any in whose hand is anything.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “All statues are prohibited,” etc. Rebbi Ḥiyya bar Abba said, because they are worshipped in the great fortification of Rome twice in a Sabbatical period<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">This is given as R, Meïr’s reason.</i>. Then at a place where they are worshipped they should be forbidden, at a place where they are not worshipped they should be permitted. Rebbi Yose said, since they became forbidden at one place, their kind becomes forbidden anywhere<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 40b" href="/Avodah_Zarah.40b">Babli 40b</a> (following R. Meïr).</i>.
Where do we hold<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">Where is the disagreement between R. Meïr and the rabbis?</i>? If it is certain that they are of kings<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">Since Augustus introduced the worship of the Emperor in the provinces, any statue of an Emperor is an idol.</i>, according to everybody they are forbidden. If it is certain that they are of rulers<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">The statue of a procurator is not an idol.</i>, according to everybody they are permitted. But where we hold is if it is indeterminate. Rebbi Meïr says, indeterminate they are of kings but the rabbis say, indeterminate they are of rulers.
Ashian the woodworker in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: why are pictures forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">Flat pictures of pagan deities or pagan subjects, since the Second Commandment forbids only three-dimensional forms. Greek εἰκόνιον, “portrait”.</i>? Because one burns incense before them at the time they are hung. Rebbi Joḥanan said, one may look at them when they are taken down. What is the reason? <i>You may look when the evildoers are being destroyed</i><sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ps</i>. 34:37.</i>. One may not look at inscriptions under figures or under pictures on the Sabbath<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">Babli <i>Šabbat</i> 149a. Since the inscription explains what the picture is all about, reading it is for non-religious information which is forbidden on the Sabbath.</i>. Not only this, but even on a weekday one does not look at pictures. What is the reason? <i>Do not turn to idols</i><sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 19:4" href="/Leviticus.19.4"><i>Lev</i>. 19:4</a>.</i>, do not turn to worship them; Rebbi Jehudah says, do not turn actually to see them.
When Rebbi Naḥum bar Simai expired, the pictures were covered by bast mats<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">The entire paragraph in <i>Eccl</i>. <i>rabba</i> 9(9); cf. also Babli <i>Moˋed qatan</i> 25b.<br>They covered all icons along the route of the funeral procession by mats. (In the Babli, the icons turned into mats.)</i>. Just as he did not see them during his lifetime, so he should not see them in his death. But do they know anything<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">Babli <i>Berakhot</i> 18a.</i>? Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish said, the only difference between us and the just is speech<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Midrash Ps</i>. 30(3) [ed. Buber Note 6]; <i>Pesiqta rabbati</i> 2 (ed. Friedmann Note 17), 12 (Note 4). The name tradition changes from source to source.</i>. Rebbi Ze‘ira said, the dead person hears his eulogy as in a dream. Rebbi Ashian said, the dead person hears his eulogy<sup class="footnote-marker">16a</sup><i class="footnote">קִילוּס “praise” is derived from Greek καλῶς! “beautiful, excellent”. For the use as “eulogy” compare καλολογέω, same as ἐυλογέω (E. G.).</i> as in a dream. Why was he called “Naḥum the most holy man”? because he never in his life looked at the form on a coin<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sanhedrin</i> 10:6 Note 335, <i>Megillah</i> 1:13 (72b l. 57) 3:3 (74a l. 39); Babli <i>Šabbat</i> 118b, <i>Pesaḥim</i> 104a.</i>. Why was he called “our holy teacher”? Because he never looked at his circumcision<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sanhedrin</i> 10:6 Note 335, <i>Megillah</i> 1:13 (72b l. 57) 3:3 (74a l. 39); Babli <i>Šabbat</i> 118b, <i>Pesaḥim</i> 104a.</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">Most of these remarks about wonders which happened at the death of holy men are alluded to in the Babli, <i>Moˋed qaṭan</i> 25b.</i> When Rebbi Aḥa expired, a star was seen at high noon. When Rebbi Ḥanan expired, the statues were bent. When Rebbi Joḥanan expired, the pictures were bent. They said, there was no picture beauty like his<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">R. Joḥanan’s beauty is extolled in the Babli, <i>Bava meṣiˋa</i> 84a.</i>. When Rebbi Ḥanina from Hauran expired, Lake Tiberias was split. They said, when he was going to intercalate<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">Calendar intercalations, the proclamations of an additional month in the year, were done at the hot springs of Gadara, near the South-East comer of the Lake or Genezareth.</i>, the lake was split before him. When Rav Hoshaia expired, the shame<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">Usually קָלוֹן “shame” means a brothel (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Horayot 3:4:10" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Horayot.3.4.10"><i>Horaiot</i> 3:7 Note 259</a>), possibly government run. It is possible that here one should read קוֹלוּמן <i>columna</i> “a pillar” (commentary <i>Gilyon Efraim</i>.)</i> of Tiberias fell down. When Rebbi Isaac ben Elyashiv expired, 70 thresholds of Galilean houses were uprooted. They said, these were kept in place by his merit.
<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">This story appears also in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Peah 1:1:33" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Peah.1.1.33"><i>Peah</i> 1:1 (Note 139</a>) and twice in <i>Gen</i>. <i>rabba</i>. The different Yerushalmi versions are compared in Appendix 2 of M. Sokoloff, <i>The Geniza</i> <i>Fragments of Bereshit Rabba</i>, Jerusalem 1982. (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Ketubot 17a" href="/Ketubot.17a">Babli <i>Ketubot</i> 17a</a>.)</i> When Rebbi Samuel ben Rav Isaac expired, the cedars of the Land of Israel were uprooted. They said that he was taking branches and danced while singing<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">Arabic قلس “to dance while singing”. This קלס is not the verb קלס derived from Greek καλός “beautiful”.</i> before brides. The rabbis were complaining about him. Rebbi Zeˋira said to them, let him alone, the old man does not<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">J. N. Epstein deletes this word since in the parallels R. Zeˋira appears in a positive light.</i> know what he is doing. When he expired, fire came from Heaven and separated between his bier and the public; there were three hours of thunder and lightning in the world. They showed<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">Read חיוי instead of חייא.</i> what this branch did for the old man. There came a disembodied voice and said, woe that Samuel bar Rav Isaac, the doer of good deeds, has expired.
When Rebbi Yasa bar Ḥalfuta expired, the water pipes drew blood in Laodicea<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">In the Babli, R. Yose (ben Ḥalafta, the Tanna) at Sepphoris.</i>. They said, because he devoted his life to circumcisions.
When Rebbi Abbahu expired, the pillars of Caesarea were crying. The Samaritans said, they only are making a joyful noise<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">Since R. Abbahu was the driving force behind the permanent separation of Jews and Samaritans (Halakhah 5:3, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Chullin 6a" href="/Chullin.6a">Babli <i>Hulin</i> 6a</a>.)</i>. The Jews told them, do the outsiders know what sound the insiders make? When Rebbi Abbahu expired, they had thirteen rivers of balsamum flowing before him. He asked them<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">The angels. The next paragraph implies that he saw his reward before his death, and explains the euphemism “expired” (really “slept”) for “died”. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bereishit Rabbah 63:3" href="/Bereishit_Rabbah.63.3"><i>Gen</i>. <i>rabba</i>63(3</a>). A similar story is in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Taanit 25a" href="/Taanit.25a">Babli, <i>Taˋanit</i> 25a</a>, referring to R. Eleazar.</i>, for whom are these? They told him, they are yours. He said to them, all of this is for Abbahu, <i>and I said</i>. <i>in vain I labored</i><sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Isaiah 49:4" href="/Isaiah.49.4"><i>Is</i>. 49:4</a>. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bereishit Rabbah 63:3" href="/Bereishit_Rabbah.63.3"><i>Gen</i>. <i>rabba</i> 63(3</a>).</i>, etc.
<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Bereishit Rabbah 63:3" href="/Bereishit_Rabbah.63.3"><i>Gen</i>. <i>rabba</i> 63(3</a>), an enlarged version.</i> The Holy One, praise to Him, shows to the just their rewards in this world; their soul is satisfied and they go to sleep. [A parable] of a king who gave a dinner and pictured all kinds of dishes on a chart. When the guests entered they saw it, they were satiated and went to sleep.
<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Bereishit Rabbah 63:4" href="/Bereishit_Rabbah.63.4"><i>Gen</i>. <i>rabba</i> 63(4</a>), <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bereishit Rabbah 92:2" href="/Bereishit_Rabbah.92.2">92(2</a>), <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shemot Rabbah 22:3" href="/Shemot_Rabbah.22.3"><i>Ex</i>. <i>rabba</i> 22(3</a>), <a class="refLink" data-ref="Psalms 5:11" href="/Psalms.5.11"><i>Midrash Psalms</i> 5(11</a>).</i> Zavdai ben Levai, and Rebbi Yose bar Petrus, and Rebbi Joshua ben Levi said three verses when they expired. One of them said, <i>for this every pious one should pray to You</i><sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Psalms 32:6" href="/Psalms.32.6"><i>Ps</i>. 32:6</a>.</i> etc. And one said, <i>all who rely on You will enjoy</i><sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Psalms 5:12" href="/Psalms.5.12"><i>Ps</i>. 5:12</a>.</i> etc. And one said, <i>how great is Your bounty which You did hide for those who fear You</i><sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Psalms 31:20" href="/Psalms.31.20"><i>Ps</i>. 31:20</a>.</i> etc.
<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">This paragraph is from <i>Horaiot</i> 3:7, <i>Soṭah</i> 9:17, 9:13–14 (Notes 212–218), Tosephta <i>Soṭah</i> 13:3–4; parallels in the Babli <i>Berakhot</i>57a, <i>Soṭah</i> 48b, <i>Sanhedrin</i> 11a.</i> Rebbi Jacob bar Idi in the name of Rebbi Joshua ben Levi: It happened that the Elders assembled in the upper story of House Gadya at Jericho when there came a disembodied voice and told them, there are two among you worthy of the Holy Spirit and one of them is Hillel the Elder. They looked at Samuel Minor. Then the Elders assembled in the upper story at Jabneh when there came a disembodied voice and told them, there are two among you worthy of the Holy Spirit and one of them is Samuel Minor. They looked at Eliezer ben Hyrcanos. They were happy that their opinion coincided with that of the Holy Spirit.
One of the Patriarch’s court expired. The burial cave received him but people’s lives were endangered<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">Probably the cave was unsafe.</i>. Rebbi Yose<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">This name probably should read “Yasa” as in the following, since R. Yose was R. Zeˋira’s student’s student.</i> came and held a funeral oration, “happy the man who leaves the world in peace.” When Rebbi Yasa died, the sacred fountain<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">The usual interpretation as Latin <i>castellum</i> (New Greek καστέλλιον), expression used by Vitruvius for the water container on top of a water tower, fits <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Mikvaot 4:4" href="/Tosefta_Mikvaot.4.4">Tosephta <i>Miqwaot</i> 4:6</a> but not this passage. Fortification in the Yerushalmi always appears as <i>castra</i>. -<i>orum</i>, and has nothing to do with pagan worship. The word here seems to be κασταλία “a sacred fountain”. Compare Latin <i>castalius</i>, “holy” (E. G.).</i> of Tiberias fell down and those of the Patriarch’s court were happy<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">That something happened to the preacher who found fault with the court because something happened at one of its funerals.</i>. Rebbi Zeˋira told them, it is not comparable. There people were in danger, here nobody was in danger. There no pagan worship was uprooted, here pagan worship was uprooted.
<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 9:16:2-4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.9.16.2-4"><i>Soṭah</i> 9:17</a> (<b>ס</b>) Notes 283–284. The first paragraph is slightly reformulated, the second more radically except for the last sentence. Probably one should read <i>bar</i> Pazi instead of <i>Rebbi</i> Pazi. According to<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Horayot 3:5:15" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Horayot.3.5.15"><i>Horaiot</i> 3:7 (Note 329</a>) the family bar Pazi already was related to the Patriarchate but had no learned members.</i> Rebbi Jacob bar Idi in the name of Rebbi Joshua ben Levi: When Rabban Joḥanan ben Zakkai died, he said, clear the house because of impurity and prepare a chair for Hezekiah, the king of Judah. When his student Rebbi Eliezer died, he said, clear the house because of impurity and prepare a chair for Rabban Joḥanan ben Zakkai. But some say, what his teacher saw, he saw.
Some of the family of the Patriarch proposed a marriage connection to one of the house of Rebbi Pazi but he did not accept; he said that they should not be embarrassed by him. When he died he said, clear the house because of impurity and prepare a chair for Josaphat, the king of Judah. They said, the one who ran after honor should come for him who fled before honor.
“But the Sages say, prohibited are only those in whose hand is a rod, or a bird, or a globe.” A rod by which he rules the world<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 41a" href="/Avodah_Zarah.41a">Babli 41a</a>. The other two attributes are interpreted differently; the Yerushalmi interpretation also is in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bamidbar Rabbah 13:13" href="/Bamidbar_Rabbah.13.13"><i>Num</i>. <i>rabba</i> 13(13</a>).</i>. A bird, <i>my hand reaches to the riches of people as to a bird’s nest</i><sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Isaiah 10:14" href="/Isaiah.10.14"><i>Is</i>. 10:14</a>.</i>. A globe, for the world is globe-shaped.
<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Bamidbar Rabbah 13:13" href="/Bamidbar_Rabbah.13.13"><i>Num</i>. <i>rabba</i> 13(13</a>).</i> Rebbi Jonah said, when Alexander the Macedonian wanted to ascend he rose, and rose, and rose, until he saw the world as a globe and the ocean like a bowl. Therefore one represents him with a globe in his hand. Should one not represent him with a bowl in his hand? He does not rule over the Sea. But the Holy One, praise to Him, rules over Sea and dry land, He rescues on the Sea and on land.
<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">A slightly different version of this paragraph is in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Berakhot 9:1:22" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Berakhot.9.1.22"><i>Berakhot</i> 9:1 (Notes 56–58</a>).</i> Rebbi Zeˋira the son of Rebbi Abbahu preached before Rebbi Eleazar. <i>Happy is he whom the God of Jacob helps</i><sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Psalms 146:5-6" href="/Psalms.146.5-6"><i>Ps</i>. 146:5–6</a>.</i>. What is written after this? <i>The Maker of Heaven and Earth</i>. What has one to do with the other? But a king of flesh and blood needs a protector<sup class="footnote-marker">45*</sup><i class="footnote">Latin <i>patronus</i> “protector, defender”.</i>; in one district<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">Greek ἐπαρχία.</i> he is not a ruler, maybe in another one? If you say a universal ruler<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">Greek κοσμοκράτωρ, the Roman Emperor. A king in the Roman principate was a local ruler subject to orders from Rome.</i> rules over dry land, does he [rule] over the Sea? But the Holy One, praise to Him, is not like this; He rules over the Sea, He rules over dry land. Not only this, but if a sword is at a person’s neck the Holy One, praise to Him, saves him. That is what Moses said, <i>He saved me from Pharao’</i> s <i>sword</i><sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 18:4" href="/Exodus.18.4"><i>Ex</i>. 18:4</a>.</i>. It is written only <i>sword</i><sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">Since it does not say “from Pharao” but “from Pharao’s sword” Moses was condemned to death but escaped miraculously (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Berakhot 9:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Berakhot.9.1"><i>Berakhot</i> 9:1 Notes 42–47</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Devarim Rabbah 2:20" href="/Devarim_Rabbah.2.20"><i>Deut</i>. <i>rabba</i> 2(20</a>), <i>Midrash Cant</i>. 7(5), <i>Midrash Ps</i>. 4[3].)</i>. Even if a sword is at his neck the Holy One, praise to Him, saves him from it.
“They added to this the sword, the crown, and the ring.<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Avodah Zarah 6:1" href="/Tosefta_Avodah_Zarah.6.1">Tosephta 5:1</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 41" href="/Avodah_Zarah.41">Babli 41</a> a.</i>” The sword with which he kills, the crown with which he crowns himself, the ring with which he seals.
It is forbidden to seal with a seal on which there is an idol. Rebbi Jehudah says, if its seal was recessed it is forbidden to seal with it, if it protrudes it is permitted to seal with it<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 43b" href="/Avodah_Zarah.43b">Babli 43b</a>. A figure prohibited by the Second Commandment is a three-dimensional plastic image. A ring with a concave picture will result in a protruding seal which therefore is forbidden (but as the Babli points out, one is permitted to wear such a ring.) A ring with a protruding picture results in a recessed seal which is permitted (but one may not possess such a ring.)</i>. Rebbi Ḥanina ben Gamliel says, in my father’s family they were sealing with faces<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">Greek πρόσωπον, τό “face”.</i>. Rebbi Eleazar ben Rebbi Simeon says, all kinds of faces were in Jerusalem except human ones<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 42b" href="/Avodah_Zarah.42b">Babli (42b</a>): All faces are permitted except human ones. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Avodah Zarah 6:1" href="/Tosefta_Avodah_Zarah.6.1">Tosephta 5:2</a>.</i>.
“Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel says, any in whose hand is anything.” But only matters of honor<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 43b" href="/Avodah_Zarah.43b">Babli 43b</a>.</i>. A basket, laced shoes, and rags are matters of dishonor. Papyrus and reed pen<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">Greek καλαμάριον, τό.</i> are matters of honor. The pen-case<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">In the Babli and the independent Mishnah mss.: “of idols”. While idols are and remain biblically forbidden for all usufruct, a pagan (not a Jew) who damages or breaks an idol thereby removes the character of instrument of worship. If one finds shards of vessels decorated with pagan symbols then either (1) it simply is a decorated vessel never intended for pagan worship, or (2) even if it was an object of worship there is a likelihood that a pagan broke and discarded it. As a general principle such a case (called “the doubt of a doubt”) in which the object may be permitted in each of the cases clears the objected from any biblical prohibition.</i> is problematic.

Halakhah 2

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If one finds shards of vessels<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">In the Babli and the independent Mishnah mss.: “of idols”. While idols are and remain biblically forbidden for all usufruct, a pagan (not a Jew) who damages or breaks an idol thereby removes the character of instrument of worship. If one finds shards of vessels decorated with pagan symbols then either (1) it simply is a decorated vessel never intended for pagan worship, or (2) even if it was an object of worship there is a likelihood that a pagan broke and discarded it. As a general principle such a case (called “the doubt of a doubt”) in which the object may be permitted in each of the cases clears the objected from any biblical prohibition.</i> they are permitted. If one finds the figure of a hand or of a foot these are forbidden because similar ones are worshipped<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">In this case, possibility (1) is very remote; there is only one doubt which is not enough to remove the biblical prohibition.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If one finds shards of vessels,” etc. Rebbi Yose in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: Because most of them come from three-legged tables<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote">Greek δέλφιξ, -ικος, a (Delphian) tripod or small three-legged table. In this case the possibility of case (2) is remote.</i>. Then also figures of a hand or figures of a foot? There is a difference since similar ones are worshipped.
