<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote">Here starts a Geniza text (G), one leaf also edited by J. N. Epstein (Chapter 2, Note 361). The parallel in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sanhedrin 63b" href="/Sanhedrin.63b">Babli is <i>Sanhedrin</i> 63b</a>.</i> It is written<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="II Kings 17:30" href="/II_Kings.17.30"><i>2K</i>. 17:30</a>.</i>: <i>The people from Babylon made Sukkot Benot</i>, the chicken and its young, <i>and the people of Kuta made the Nergal</i>, the foot of Jacob and the foot of Joseph<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote">Some of the regions mentioned here and most of the deities are unidentified in cuneiform sources but Nergal is well identified as the god of the Underworld. Since the word does not correspond to any Semitic root, <i>Nun</i> is considered a prefix leaving a root רגל “foot” which in turn is taken of the source of גלל “because of” in analogy to עקב “heel, in consequence of”.</i>, <i>I divined that the Eternal blessed me for your sake</i><sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ge</i> n. 30:12.</i><i>;</i> <i>the Eternal blessed the Egyptian’s house for Joseph’s sake</i><sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ge</i> n. 39:5.</i>.<i>The people of Ḥamma made the Ashima</i>, a sheep, as you say, <i>the Cohen shall atone for him by the asham ram</i><sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Le</i> v. 19:22. G quotes 5:16 instead. In both cases there is acoustic similarity between <i>asham</i> and <i>ashima</i>.</i>. <i>The Awim made the Nibḥan</i><sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote">Both sources and the Babli read נבחן “the barker” against the MT נִבְחָז which together with תרתק are read as Elamite by modem interpreters.</i>, a dog, <i>and the Tartaq</i>, a donkey. <i>And the Sepharwites burned their sons in fire [to Adrammelekh and Annamelekh the gods of Sepharwaim]</i><sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote">Parentheses added from G; while the interpretations clearly refer to the deities mentioned in the addition, in talmudic style the explicit quote is not strictly necessary.</i>, peacock<sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote">Greek ταώς, ὁ. In Latin, <i>taos</i>, -<i>i</i>, <i>m</i>., is “a precious multi-colored stone”.</i> and pheasant<sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote">Latin <i>phasianus</i>, also <i>phasiana</i>, -<i>ae</i>, f. (Edict. Diocl.). According to myth, it is metamorphosed Itis, a son of Tereus. He was killed by his mother and served to his father, then changed into a pheasant.</i>.
An idol which was broken<sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 41b" href="/Avodah_Zarah.41b">Babli, 41b</a>, “if it broke spontaneously.” This is the reading of the correction in G over the line. Therefore one has to assume that the reading in the text simply means that a piece is before us and we do not know how it was broken. The argument in the text shows that the idol was a decoration of a vessel, not a statue.</i>, Rebbi Joḥanan said it is forbidden, Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish said it is permitted<sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 41b" href="/Avodah_Zarah.41b">Babli 41b</a>.</i>. Where do we hold? If in the future he would return it to its vessel, everybody agrees it is forbidden. If in the future he will not return it to its vessel, everybody agrees that it is permitted. But we hold if it is not indicated. Rebbi Joḥanan said, not indicated is as if he would return it to its vessel; Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish said, not indicated is as if he would not return it to its vessel. Rebbi Yudan the father of Rebbi Mattaniah said, if they were lying on their spot<sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote">All parts of the vessel are still available, so it can be glued back together.</i> it is if he would return it to its vessel. Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish objected to Rebbi [Joḥanan]<sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote">From G, missing in L.</i>: Is it not written, <i>therefore the priests of Dagon and all visitors to Dagon’s temple will not step on Dagon’s threshold</i><sup class="footnote-marker">73</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="I Samuel 5:5" href="/I_Samuel.5.5"><i>1S</i>. 5:5</a>. Since it does not mention Dagon’s hands and head, it proves that once a statue is broken it is not worshipped any longer by pagans. In the Babli the same verse serves to buttress R. Joḥanan’s position against R. Simeon ben Laqish.</i>? He told him, this teaches that they honored the threshold more than Dagon. Rebbi Jeremiah in the name of Rebbi Ḥiyya bar Abba, the peoples of the world made one threshold; Israel made many thresholds; what is the reason? <i>I shall visit on [any]</i><sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote">Added from G (and the verse in MT); needed for the point of the sermon.</i> <i>who skips over the</i> <i>threshold [on that day]</i><sup class="footnote-marker">75</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Zephaniah 1:9" href="/Zephaniah.1.9"><i>Zeph</i>. 1:9</a>.</i>.
If somebody finds an idol, Rav said, he breaks it limb by limb and it becomes insignificant, but Samuel said, it never can become insignificant<sup class="footnote-marker">76</sup><i class="footnote">Rav holds that an idol broken into little pieces cannot be reconstituted. Therefore it is no longer an idol and the shards may be used. Samuel holds that an idol which falls into the hands of a Jew is <i>ḥerem</i> and forbidden for any use under any circumstance (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 13:18" href="/Deuteronomy.13.18"><i>Deut</i>. 13:18</a>). For him the only way an idol or its parts can become useable for a Jew is that a Gentile (a pagan) breaks it. The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 49b" href="/Avodah_Zarah.49b">Babli (49b</a>) explains Samuel’s position away.</i>.
Rebbi Abin in the name of (Rebbi Simeon) [Samuel]<sup class="footnote-marker">77</sup><i class="footnote">Reading of L in (parentheses), of G in [brackets].</i>: This means<sup class="footnote-marker">78</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to the last statement of the Mishnah, about hands and feet. If these are complete, not broken off from a base, the Mishnah applies; these are complete objects of worship. But if they are broken off a basis they are shards. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 41" href="/Avodah_Zarah.41">Babli 41a/b</a>.</i> if they are without base. But if they have a base, I am saying they come from shards.

Halakhah 3

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If somebody finds vessels and on them the form of the sun, the form of the moon<sup class="footnote-marker">79</sup><i class="footnote">According to Maimonides, not circles representing sun and moon but their astrological representation.</i>, the form of a dragon<sup class="footnote-marker">80</sup><i class="footnote">Greek δράκων, -οντος, ὁ, also “dragon-shaped jewellery”.</i>, he should bring them to the Dead Sea<sup class="footnote-marker">81</sup><i class="footnote">Made from precious metals. All others fall under the category of “contemptible”.</i>. Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel says, on honored ones<sup class="footnote-marker">82</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 13:18" href="/Deuteronomy.13.18"><i>Deut</i>. 13:18</a>.</i> it is forbidden, on contemptible ones permitted. Rebbi Yose said, he grinds them and disperses them into the wind or throws them into the Sea. They told him, but then it becomes fertilizer and it was said, <i>nothing of the ban should cling to your hand</i><sup class="footnote-marker">83</sup><i class="footnote">While they are divinities, they are not actively worshipped.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If somebody finds vessels,” etc. They stated only “vessels and on them the form of the sun, the form of the moon,” therefore not any of the other planets<sup class="footnote-marker">83</sup><i class="footnote">While they are divinities, they are not actively worshipped.</i>. They only stated “dragon”, therefore not any snakes. What is a dragon? Any which has spikes on its neck. It was stated thus<sup class="footnote-marker">84</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Avodah Zarah 6:1" href="/Tosefta_Avodah_Zarah.6.1">Tosephta 5:2</a>.</i>: “Rebbi Simeon ben (Azzai) [Eleazar]<sup class="footnote-marker">85</sup><i class="footnote">The reading of Tosephta, G, and the Leiden ms. before correction is “Eleazar”. In addition, while ben Azzai’s name was Simeon, he was not ordained and never is called “rabbi”. The correction is a corruption.</i> says, what is a dragon? Any which has spikes on its neck. “If it<sup class="footnote-marker">86</sup><i class="footnote">The neck on the snake-shaped vessel.</i> is smooth it is permitted. If one finds a dragon in the form of a something crawling<sup class="footnote-marker">87</sup><i class="footnote">The word may mean either snake, or worm, or even a small rodent.</i> it is forbidden. Something crawling and on it a dragon, he takes the crawling thing and throws away the dragon.
Samuel said, a cup which serves as base for a dragon is forbidden, a dragon which serves as base for a cup is permitted<sup class="footnote-marker">88</sup><i class="footnote">In the first case it may be an object of worship, in the second the dragon is a decoration.</i>. Rebbi Abba in the name of Rav: Fragments of a dragon are permitted. If it was a whole dragon and broke, it is forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">89</sup><i class="footnote">Since a forbidden idol can become permitted only if a Gentile, not a Jew, breaks it (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Avodah Zarah 3:2:5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Avodah_Zarah.3.2.5">Note 76</a>).</i>.But did the fragments not come from a whole one? Rebbi Ḥizqiah in the name of Rav: So it is: If one saw them bowing down before a whole dragon and then it was broken, it is forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">90</sup><i class="footnote">Then there is an indication that the idol broke by accident, not by intention, and the shards remain forbidden.</i>.
Where do we hold? If it is certain that they are worshipped, even the contemptible ones are forbidden. If it is certain that they are not worshipped, even the honored ones are permitted. But we must deal with the indeterminate case. Rebbi Crispus said, those cups<sup class="footnote-marker">91</sup><i class="footnote">In G: “garment”. The following sentences show that the text of L is correct even though <i>Gen</i>.<i>r</i>. 81(3) quotes R. Joḥanan to the effect that garments may be forbidden as pagan (based on <a class="refLink" data-ref="Genesis 35:2" href="/Genesis.35.2"><i>Gen</i>. 35:2</a>).</i> belong to the contemptible category, for Rebbi Ḥiyya bar Abba had a pitcher<sup class="footnote-marker">92</sup><i class="footnote">A Syriac word as identified in Sokoloff’s <i>Dictionary of Jewish Palestinian Aramaic</i>.</i> and the (worth) [fortune]<sup class="footnote-marker">93</sup><i class="footnote">The reading of G, τύχη “fortune, destiny” is preferable over the reading of L, τιμή “worth, value”.</i> of Rome<sup class="footnote-marker">94</sup><i class="footnote">The vocalization is from G referring to <i>Romae</i>.</i> was pictured on it. He came to ask Rebbi Joḥanan who told him, since water swims on top of it, it is a contemptible one. A drinking cup<sup class="footnote-marker">95</sup><i class="footnote">Greek κῶθων, “Laconian drinking vessel used by soldiers; drinking bout; religious banquet”.</i> is contemptible since one immerses it in water. In the days of Rebbi Joḥanan they started painting on walls<sup class="footnote-marker">96</sup><i class="footnote">Since the paintings are flat, they are not prohibited by the Second Commandment.</i> and he did not protest against it. [In the days of Rebbi Abun they started depicting by mosaics and he did not protest against it.<sup class="footnote-marker">97</sup><i class="footnote">Text of G, missing in L. As J. N. Epstein notes, any synagogue mosaic floor which shows figures (as opposed to text) must be dated after R. Abun’s time (about the year 300).</i>]
<sup class="footnote-marker">98</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 43b-44a" href="/Avodah_Zarah.43b-44a">Babli 43b/44a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Avodah Zarah 4:3" href="/Tosefta_Avodah_Zarah.4.3">Tosephta 3:19</a>. The speaker is R. Yose the Tanna who objects to the statement that the idol has to be dumped into the Dead Sea; he brings examples of disposition of pagan idols in other ways.</i> Rebbi Yose objected to the rabbis, is it not written: <i>your sin which you had made</i>, <i>the calf</i><sup class="footnote-marker">99</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 9:21" href="/Deuteronomy.9.21"><i>Deut</i>. 9:21</a>.</i>? They told him, this teaches that he intended to examine them in the way one examines deviant wives: <i>he strewed it on the surface of the water</i><sup class="footnote-marker">100</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 32:20" href="/Exodus.32.20"><i>Ex</i>. 32:20</a>. As usual, the argument is from the part of the verse which is not quoted, <i>viz</i>., <i>he let the Children of Israel drink</i>. The deviant wife has to drink water mixed with dust from the floor of the Sanctaury, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 5:11-31" href="/Numbers.5.11-31"><i>Num</i>. 5:11–31</a>.</i>.He said to them, is it not written, <i>and also Maacah</i>, <i>king Asa’s mother</i>, <i>he removed from power</i><sup class="footnote-marker">101</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="I Kings 15:13" href="/I_Kings.15.13"><i>1K</i>. 15:13</a>.</i>? They told him, is there proof from there? <i>Asa cut down</i><sup class="footnote-marker">102</sup><i class="footnote">Since Asa burned his mother’s Ashera, there is no doubt that burning a wooden statue is as good as bringing it to the Dead Sea.</i> etc. He told them, <i>he beat the bronze serpent to pieces</i><sup class="footnote-marker">103</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="II Kings 18:4" href="/II_Kings.18.4"><i>2K</i>. 18:4</a>.</i>. Was this an object of worship? Did not Moses make it? This teaches that Israel was erring after it until Hezekias came and removed it<sup class="footnote-marker">104</sup><i class="footnote">Here ends the Genizah fragment G edited by Epstein; on the next line starts one (<b>ג</b>) edited by Ginzberg (pp. 278–279) of which, however, not very much is legible.</i>.
One verse says, <i>they abandoned their idols;</i> <i>David and his men carried them away</i><sup class="footnote-marker">105</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="II Samuel 5:21" href="/II_Samuel.5.21"><i>2S</i>. 5:21</a>. The verse implies that the idols were a valuable part of the booty. According to the Mishnah the idols should have been brought to the Dead Sea, not to Hebron.</i>. But another verse says, <i>David gave orders and they were burned in the.fire</i><sup class="footnote-marker">106</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="I Chronicles 14:12" href="/I_Chronicles.14.12"><i>1Chr</i>. 14:12</a>, another report about the same event.</i>. Rebbi Yose [ben Rebbi] Ḥalafta<sup class="footnote-marker">107</sup><i class="footnote">This is the reading of <b>ג</b>, which agrees with the name of the Tanna in other sources.</i> and the rabbis. Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Ḥalafta says, what was of metal, <i>David and his men carried them away</i>. And what was of wood, <i>David gave orders and they were burned in the fire</i>. But the rabbis say, what Itai from Gat<sup class="footnote-marker">108</sup><i class="footnote">They assume that he was a Gentile and therefore able to break the idols to remove the prohibition and turn them into valuable metal scrap. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 44a" href="/Avodah_Zarah.44a">Babli 44a</a></i> annulled, <i>David and his men carried them away</i>. And what he did not annul, <i>David gave orders and they were burned in fire</i>. How does Rebbi Yose [ben Rebbi] Ḥalafta confirm <i>David and his men carried them away</i>? They took them by their rims<sup class="footnote-marker">109</sup><i class="footnote">To grind them down as R. Yose prescribes in the Mishnah.</i>.

Halakhah 4

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> Proclos ben Ploslos<sup class="footnote-marker">110</sup><i class="footnote">In the Babli פלוספוס which can be read פִלוֹסֳפוֹס “philosopher”; in the Venice edition of Alfasi פלופלוס, but in most sources, including Maimonides’s autograph Mishnah Commentary the name is written as in the Yerushalmi Mishnah.</i> asked Rabban Gamliel in Acco when he was taking a bath in the Bath of Aphrodite<sup class="footnote-marker">111</sup><i class="footnote">According to Maimonides there is no problem since Aphrodite is the symbol of the planet Venus, which was exempted from the general prohibition of pagan symbols in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Avodah Zarah 3:3:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Avodah_Zarah.3.3.2">Halakhah 3 Note 83</a>.</i>. He said to him, it is written in your Torah, <i>nothing banned should stick to your hand</i><sup class="footnote-marker">112</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 13:18" href="/Deuteronomy.13.18"><i>Deut</i>. 13:18</a>.</i>: why are you bathing in the Bath of Aphrodite? He said to him, one does not answer in the bath<sup class="footnote-marker">113</sup><i class="footnote">One may not discuss religious subjects while naked. Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Berakhot 2:3:10" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Berakhot.2.3.10"><i>Berakhot</i> 2:3 Notes 116–119</a>.</i>. When he left he told him, I did not come into her domain, she came into my domain. One does not say, the bath was built as adornment for Aphrodite but one says, Aphrodite was made as adornment of the bath.<br>Another explanation. If one gave you lots of money, would you enter your temple naked, after an emission, or urinating before it? But this one stands over the sewer and everybody is urinating in its presence. It only says <i>their gods</i><sup class="footnote-marker">114</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 12:30" href="/Deuteronomy.12.30"><i>Deut</i>. 12:30</a>; the verse which is the basis of Mishnah 6.</i>; what is treated as a divinity is forbidden, what is not treated as a divinity is not forbidden.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “Proclos ben Ploslos asked,” etc. Where do we hold? If he asked him about practice in the bath he should have answered; if he did not ask him about practice in the bath he should not have answered<sup class="footnote-marker">115</sup><i class="footnote">Since the instruction that one does not answer in the bath, i. e., in the room where people are naked, is a religious instruction forbidden at that place.</i>. <sup class="footnote-marker">116</sup><i class="footnote">The main source for the following is <i>Šabbat</i> 3:3 (6a l.51, <b>ש</b>), Babli <i>Šabbat</i> 40b.</i> As Rebbi Jacob bar Idi said in the name of Rebbi Joshua ben Levi: One asks about practice in the bath while in the bathhouse, about practice in the toilet while in the toilet. As when Rebbi Simeon ben Eleazar went to bathe with Rebbi Meïr, he asked him, may one rinse? He told him, it is forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">117</sup><i class="footnote">As explained in <i>Šabbat</i>, they went to bathe in the hot springs of Tiberias on the Sabbath. It is forbidden to take a hot bath in water which was heated, but not in water which is naturally hot. But then one has to be careful not to rinse oneself outside the water flowing from the source since water spilled on the earth outside would fill in depressions in the ground, which is prohibited work.</i>. May one dry himself with a towel? He told him, it is forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">118</sup><i class="footnote">Similarly, one may not use a bath towel on the Sabbath since squeezing out water from a towel (or a sponge) is forbidden work.</i>. But did not Samuel ask Rav, may one say “Amen” at a dirty place? He told him, it is forbidden, and it is forbidden that I told you that it is forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">119</sup><i class="footnote">Since he was asked at a place with a bad smell. He disagrees with R. Jacob bar Idi.</i>. It was found stated, one asks<sup class="footnote-marker">120</sup><i class="footnote">In <i>Šabbat</i>: “One does not ask.” This supports Rav and seems to be the original version. Unfortunately, <b>ג</b> is damaged at this passage.</i> about practice in the bath while in the bathhouse, about practice in the toilet while in the toilet. Rebbi Yudan, the father of Rebbi Mattaniah, said, he asked him about practice in the bath but it is not done<sup class="footnote-marker">121</sup><i class="footnote">In <b>ג:</b>“is not polite”.</i> to answer in the bath. Rebbi Samuel bar Eudaimon said, he asked him about practice in the bath but the steam in the bath is bad for the teeth. The colleagues, Rebbi Ḥama bar Yose in the name of Rebbi Hoshaia, Rebbi Zeˋira in the name if Rebbi Joshua ben Levi: He answered him in an evasive answer<sup class="footnote-marker">122</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 44b" href="/Avodah_Zarah.44b">Babli 44b</a>.</i>, for he could have replied about Baal Peˋor whose worship only consists in exposure and defecation<sup class="footnote-marker">123</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sanhedrin 7:9:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sanhedrin.7.9.1">Mishnah <i>Sanhedrin</i> 7:12</a>.</i>. What about it<sup class="footnote-marker">124</sup><i class="footnote">What would be the correct answer?</i>? What is treated as a divinity is forbidden, what is not treated as divinity is not forbidden.

Halakhah 5

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If Gentiles worship mountains and hills; these are permitted<sup class="footnote-marker">125</sup><i class="footnote">For agricultural use; the ground and what grows on it is not forbidden as long as it is connected to the ground.</i> but what is on them is forbidden, as it is said<sup class="footnote-marker">126</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 7:25" href="/Deuteronomy.7.25"><i>Deut</i>. 7:25</a>. If a mountain top is covered in gold or silver, the cover has to be removed and destroyed; only the ground itself is permitted.</i>, <i>do not covet silver or gold on them and take for yourself</i>. Rebbi Yose the Galilean said, <i>their gods [are] on the mountains</i><sup class="footnote-marker">127</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 12:2" href="/Deuteronomy.12.2"><i>Deut</i>. 12:2</a>.</i>, the mountains are not their gods. <i>Their gods [are] on the hills</i>, the hills are not their gods. Then why is an <i>Ashera</i><sup class="footnote-marker">128</sup><i class="footnote">A holy tree (Mishnah 11), which is connected to the ground and by the argument of Note 125 should be permitted as building material or fire wood.</i> forbidden? Because it was held by human hand<sup class="footnote-marker">129</sup><i class="footnote">It was planted.</i>, and anything held by human hand is forbidden.<br>Rebbi Aqiba said, I shall explain it to you. Any place where you find a high mountain, or an elevated hill, or a sprightly tree, know that there is idolatry<sup class="footnote-marker">162</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 12:2" href="/Deuteronomy.12.2"><i>Deut</i>. 12:2</a> reads: <i>You shall certainly destroy all the places where the peoples from whom you will inherit worshipped their gods</i>, <i>on high mountains</i>, <i>and hills</i>, <i>and</i> <i>under each sprightly tree</i>. This explains clearly that not the place is forbidden but the idol and its temple to be found there.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If Gentiles worship mountains,” etc. Rebbi Zeˋira, Rebbi Yasa, Rebbi Yose bar Ḥanina in the name of Rebbi Hoshaia: One verse says<sup class="footnote-marker">126</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 7:25" href="/Deuteronomy.7.25"><i>Deut</i>. 7:25</a>. If a mountain top is covered in gold or silver, the cover has to be removed and destroyed; only the ground itself is permitted.</i>, <i>do not covet silver or gold on them and take for yourself</i>. And one verse says<sup class="footnote-marker">130</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 29:16" href="/Deuteronomy.29.16"><i>Deut</i>. 29:16</a>.</i>, <i>silver and gold which is with them</i>. If <i>on them</i> why <i>with them</i>, and if <i>with them</i> why <i>on them</i>? Since <i>on them</i> refers to something which is particular for them and an adornment for them<sup class="footnote-marker">131</sup><i class="footnote">Since the mountains themselves cannot become forbidden, only adornments made explicitly for pagan worship can be forbidden. Therefore also of the contents of a dwelling only idolatrous objects can be forbidden for all usufruct. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 51b" href="/Avodah_Zarah.51b">Babli 51b</a>.</i>, also <i>with them</i> refers to something which is particular for them and an adornment for them.
Rebbi Joḥanan in the name of Rebbi Yannai: Anything which one introduced inside the lattice work<sup class="footnote-marker">132</sup><i class="footnote">The inner part of a temple, cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Avodah Zarah 2:3:23" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Avodah_Zarah.2.3.23">Chapter 2:3, Note 219</a>.</i> he made forbidden; e. g., money pouches. Rebbi Yasa said, we had a note that (return) [this]<sup class="footnote-marker">133</sup><i class="footnote">The text of L (in parentheses) is clearly a scribal error; one has to follow the text of <b>ג</b> [in brackets].</i> of Rebbi Hoshaia disagreed with Rebbi Yannai, but it is not so; it supports it. Since <i>on them</i> refers to something which is particular for them and an adornment for them, also <i>with them</i> refers to something which is particular for them and an adornment for them; this excludes money pouches<sup class="footnote-marker">134</sup><i class="footnote">This seemingly contradicts R. Yannai’s statement.</i>. Rebbi Abba in the name of Rav Jehudah: Even water and salt. Salt to rub and water to rinse<sup class="footnote-marker">135</sup><i class="footnote">To polish the statues.</i>. And this is “even”? But e. g., water and salt<sup class="footnote-marker">136</sup><i class="footnote">It is obvious that in general Gentiles’ water and salt are permitted. They become forbidden only by being introduced into the inner temple for idolatrous purposes. So also a Gentile’s wallet and his money are permitted. Only by being introduced into the inner temple the money becomes forbidden. The statement of R. Yannai is a necessary complement to that of R. Hoshaia and vice-versa. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 51b" href="/Avodah_Zarah.51b">Babli 51b</a>.</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">137</sup><i class="footnote">This paragraph is from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kilayim 7:3:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kilayim.7.3.3"><i>Kilaim</i> 7:4, Notes 46–48</a> (<b>כ</b>).</i> An object of worship. Some Tannaim state, whether it was his or another person’s, it is forbidden. But some Tannaim state, his is forbidden, another person’s is permitted. He who said, whether it was his or another person’s, it is forbidden, [Rebbi Meïr and]<sup class="footnote-marker">138</sup><i class="footnote">Added from both parallel texts. The reference is to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kilayim 7:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kilayim.7.3.1">Mishnaiot <i>Kilaim</i> 7:4</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kilayim 7:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kilayim.7.3.1">5</a>. If a person grows vegetables or grain in his vineyard, everything becomes forbidden for usufruct. But if he causes such a growth on another person’s land, R. Meïr holds that the crop is forbidden and the owner of the land has to sue the culprit for damages; R. Yose and R. Simeon hold on the authority of R. Aqiba that “nobody can sanctify anything which is not his.” In our case, anybody who worships an animal or an object which is his, made it forbidden for usufruct for himself and everybody else. But if the object was not his property, for R. Yose and R. Simeon it cannot become forbidden for anybody.</i> Rebbi Jehudah. But he who said, his is forbidden, another person’s is permitted, Rebbi Yose and Rebbi Simeon. Rebbi Yose<sup class="footnote-marker">139</sup><i class="footnote">The fifth-generation Amora.</i> in the name of Rebbi Ila: It is everybody’s opinion. Just as you say there, anything living<sup class="footnote-marker">140</sup><i class="footnote">Any living animal which is worshipped is an animal and not an idol and cannot be forbidden.</i> while it cannot become forbidden for a private person it becomes forbidden for Heaven, so similarly anything which is not yours even though it cannot become forbidden for a private person it (does not)<sup class="footnote-marker">141</sup><i class="footnote">This word is not in the parallel text; the context and the following paragraph make it clear that it is a scribal error.</i> become forbidden for Heaven<sup class="footnote-marker">142</sup><i class="footnote">The comparison with <i>Kilaim</i> is misleading. The relevant Mishnah is <a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Temurah 6:1" href="/Mishnah_Temurah.6.1"><i>Temurah</i> 6:1</a> where it is stated that an animal used for idolatry (or bestiality) is unfit as sacrifice, irrespective of ownership. For sacrifices there is no dispute between R. Meïr and R. Yose. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Temurah 28a" href="/Temurah.28a">Babli <i>Temurah</i>28a</a>.</i>.
Rebbi Abun bar Ḥiyya said, even his fine flour becomes forbidden and another person’s is permitted<sup class="footnote-marker">143</sup><i class="footnote">While it is clear from our Mishnah that if anybody worships wild standing grain it will not be forbidden since it is connected to the ground (but if it was sown for idolatrous purposes then it is an <i>Ashera</i>.) It now is stated that cut and milled produce will only be forbidden if used illegitimately by its owner, not by any third person.</i>. Is this following him who said, whether it was his or another person’s, it is forbidden; did he say<sup class="footnote-marker">144</sup><i class="footnote">While this word is not in <b>ג</b>, it is better to retain it.</i> that they do not disagree about something which is not alive and Rebbi Abun bar Ḥiyya comes to tell you that the <i>baraita</i> refers to living things? If they disagree about something which is not alive; therefore a living thing is forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">145</sup><i class="footnote">If R. Abun bar Ḥiyya disagrees with R. Yose in the name of Rebbi Ila. He should have mentioned an animal, not fine flour. For if R. Meïr and R. Yose disagree about plants then one must assume that R. Yose agrees with R. Meïr about animals.</i>. Then something which is not alive should be forbidden <i>a fortiori</i><sup class="footnote-marker">146</sup><i class="footnote">Since animals have a privileged status; R. Yose cannot agree with R. Meïr about their status.</i>. This is that you should not say as you say there, anything living while it cannot become forbidden for a private person it becomes forbidden for Heaven, so similarly anything which is not yours even though it cannot become forbidden for a private person it becomes forbidden for Heaven<sup class="footnote-marker">147</sup><i class="footnote">R. Abun bar Ḥiyya disagrees with R. Yose in the name of Rebbi Ila in a matter of principle; he holds that worshipping something which is the private property of a third person without knowledge or acquiescence of that third person has no influence on the status of the property whether for profane or sacral purposes. Here ends the Genizah text.</i>
If somebody worshipped an egg<sup class="footnote-marker">148</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 46a" href="/Avodah_Zarah.46a">Babli 46a</a>. The egg is a symbol of resurrection; cf. J. J. Bachofen, <i>Versuch über die Gräbersymbolik der Alten</i>, Gesammelte Werke Bd. 4, Basel 1954.</i> Ḥizqiah said, he did not make it forbidden; Rebbi Joḥanan said, he made it forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">149</sup><i class="footnote">For Ḥizqiah the egg is considered under the rules of animals; for R. Joḥanan only the chick would qualify, not the egg.</i>. Rebbi Zeˋira said, they disagree about an egg. The colleagues say, they disagree about pebbles in a river bed<sup class="footnote-marker">150</sup><i class="footnote">These are egg shaped. While they are products of nature, they are no longer connected to the ground.</i>. Rebbi Huna said, a verse supports what the colleagues say:<i>Your part is with river pebbles;</i> <i>these are your destiny</i><sup class="footnote-marker">151</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Is</i>.57:6.</i>. The strength of Ḥizqiah is from the following: “Then why is an <i>Ashera</i> forbidden? Because it was held by human hand, and anything held by human hand is forbidden.” Everybody agrees that wheat is forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">151</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Is</i>.57:6.</i>; <sup class="footnote-marker">152</sup><i class="footnote">The remainder of the paragraph has no connection with the topic of the Halakhah and the preceding text; it also appears in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Berakhot 9:2:24" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Berakhot.9.2.24"><i>Berakhot</i>9:3 Notes 170–174</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Taanit 1:3:2-8" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Taanit.1.3.2-8"><i>Taˋaniot</i> 1:3</a> (64b 1.30); its source seems to be <i>Gen</i>. <i>rabba</i> 13(19) on <a class="refLink" data-ref="Genesis 2:6" href="/Genesis.2.6"><i>Gen</i>. 2:6</a>. The different name traditions are noted in the Notes to <i>Berakhot</i>.</i> as Rebbi Ḥanina bar Yasa said in the name of Rav Jehudah: The roots of wheat split the earth to a depth of 50 cubits; the soft roots of a fig tree split rock. Then what do the roots of the carob and the roots of the sycamore? Rebbi Ḥanina said, once every thirty days the abyss wells up and drenches them. What is the reason? <i>I</i>, <i>the</i> <i>Eternal</i>, <i>am watching over it and water it in moments</i><sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">Prohibited for usufruct. For R. Meïr there are no statues made for artistic value alone.</i><sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">Greek πρόσωπον, τό “face”.</i>. It was stated: Rebbi Simeon ben Eleazar says, this teaches that the earth drinks only according to its hardness.
<sup class="footnote-marker">154</sup><i class="footnote">These two paragraphs are also in <i>‘Orlah</i> 1:8, Notes 200–208, <b>ע.</b>The scribe of L wrote only the first and last few words and then: “one continues in <i>Avodah zarah</i> Chapter 3, Halakhah 6, ‘if Gentiles worship’, to the end.” The text was then added by the first corrector in a somewhat shoddy way, from a source different from the text here. The full text is in the Rome <i>Zeraˋim</i> ms.</i> An egg of pagan worship which turned into a chick. Rebbi Ḥaggai in the name of Rebbi Josia: Cahana and Ḥizqiah disagree. Cahana said, it is permitted, Ḥizqiah said, it is forbidden. There is a difficulty in the opinion of Ḥizqiah: how is it possible for an egg of pagan worship to become a chick? Where do we hold? If somebody squashed it, there is no chick. If he brought it inside the lattice enclosure, come and look: If he worshipped it it is not forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">149</sup><i class="footnote">For Ḥizqiah the egg is considered under the rules of animals; for R. Joḥanan only the chick would qualify, not the egg.</i>; because he brought it inside the grating it should be forbidden? Rebbi Yudan the father of Rebbi Mattaniah said, explain it if he used to tow<sup class="footnote-marker">155</sup><i class="footnote">This is the text here. In <i>‘Orlah</i>: “he fenced in”, confirmed by the Rome text. This text can be understood since it mentions the walnut which was planted at the beginning of the first paragraph, not the second as in the text here. If a walnut tree was planted to close an opening in the lattice work, this certainly makes it forbidden as an <i>Ashera</i>. It is hard to see how to tow anything using an egg; the difference between the two texts is that between גרר and גרר. Neither of the two expressions makes much sense if applied to an egg.</i> the idol.
If an <i>‘orlah</i> walnut was planted<sup class="footnote-marker">156</sup><i class="footnote">The walnut was forbidden for all usufruct; the status of the tree is in doubt.</i> or an egg dedicated to the Temple became a chick<sup class="footnote-marker">157</sup><i class="footnote">It is property of the Temple and cannot be used without redemption. This case is not in dispute.</i>. Rebbi Yasa said, Cahana and Rebbi Joḥanan disagree. Cahana said it is forbidden and Rebbi Joḥanan said it is permitted<sup class="footnote-marker">158</sup><i class="footnote">Everybody agrees that the new tree is not <i>‘orlah</i>. According to Cahana the original prohibition has not disappeared, according to R. Joḥanan the situation is parallel to that of heave grain (restricted as food to pure Cohanim) which was used as seed grain; the crop needs only token redemption (Mishnah <i>Terumot</i> 9:4).</i>. Rebbi Zeˋira said before Rebbi Yasa, did not Rebbi [Joḥanan]<sup class="footnote-marker">159</sup><i class="footnote">From the text in <i>‘Orlah</i>, missing here.</i> say it is permitted, does he not redeem it as from the time it is sown<sup class="footnote-marker">160</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to the walnut. If the tree may be used, it never was <i>‘orlah</i>; the redemption is symbolic and can be effected by a token payment.</i>? There came Rebbi Ḥanania and Rebbi Jonah, Rebbi Eleazar in the name of Cahana: He redeems it as from the time it is sown. Rebbi Ḥanania in the name of Rebbi Phineas corrects it: Cahana said it is forbidden and he redeems it as it is now<sup class="footnote-marker">161</sup><i class="footnote">It needs redemption for its full value; in the case of the <i>‘orlah</i> tree the redemption money has to be destroyed.</i>; Rebbi Joḥanan said it is permitted and he redeems it as from the time it is sown.
“Rebbi Aqiba said, I shall explain it to you,” etc. We have stated אוֹבִין. Some are stating אוֹבִיל. He who says אוֹבִין “we understand.” But he who says אוֹבִיל “we bring.<sup class="footnote-marker">163</sup><i class="footnote">The hapax אוֹבִין is derived from the root בין “to understand”, אוֹבִיל is from the root יבל “to transport”. Both verbs are used in <i>hifˋil</i>.</i>”
Rebbi Borqai stated before Rebbi Mana: This teaches that the Canaanites did not leave any mountain or hill where they did not worship. But do we not understand to say, anything alive, while not forbidden for a private person, is forbidden for Heaven<sup class="footnote-marker">165</sup><i class="footnote">If it was worshipped as pagan deity, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Avodah Zarah 3:5:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Avodah_Zarah.3.5.4">Note 142</a>.</i>? How could the Temple have been built<sup class="footnote-marker">166</sup><i class="footnote">While a mountaintop is perfectly good for agricultural use, it should be forbidden as a place for the Temple. Therefore the place could not have been selected by humans. It is not a mountain top.</i>? By the order of a prophet; <i>David went up following the word of Gad which he had said by the Hand of the Eternal</i><sup class="footnote-marker">167</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="I Chronicles 21:19" href="/I_Chronicles.21.19"><i>1Chr</i>. 21:19</a>, slightly misquoted.</i>.

Halakhah 6

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If one’s house was connected<sup class="footnote-marker">168</sup><i class="footnote">His house and the pagan temple share a wall.</i> to a house of pagan worship and collapsed, it is forbidden to rebuild it. What should he do? He restricts himself to his property<sup class="footnote-marker">169</sup><i class="footnote">In the Babli: 4 cubits. His wall should be separated from the temple by 4 cubits. The text of Maimonides is that of the Yerushalmi.</i> and builds. If it was his and the idolatry’s, it is judged half by half<sup class="footnote-marker">170</sup><i class="footnote">He may take half of the building material of the common wall for his own use; the remainder is forbidden for all usufruct for any Jew.</i> Its stones, its wood, and its dust make impure like a crawling animal<sup class="footnote-marker">171</sup><i class="footnote">As described in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 11:29-38" href="/Leviticus.11.29-38"><i>Lev</i>. 11: 29–38</a>, it makes impure by touch. This applies also to the stones which remain his property.</i>, as it is said<sup class="footnote-marker">172</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 7:26" href="/Deuteronomy.7.26"><i>Deut</i>. 7:26</a>. In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 11:40-41" href="/Leviticus.11.40-41"><i>Lev</i>. 11:40–41</a>, all crawling things which are forbidden as food are called <i>abomination</i>, including those which make impure by touch.</i>, <i>you shall treat it as an abomination</i>. Rebbi Aqiba says like a menstruating woman as it is said<sup class="footnote-marker">173</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Isaiah 30:22" href="/Isaiah.30.22"><i>Is</i>. 30:22</a>. In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 12:2" href="/Leviticus.12.2"><i>Lev</i>. 12:2</a> menstruation is called “the separation of her feeling miserable”.</i>, <i>you shall throw it away like feeling miserable;</i> <i>you will call it excrement</i>. Since the menstruating makes impure by load<sup class="footnote-marker">174</sup><i class="footnote">In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 15:20-21" href="/Leviticus.15.20-21"><i>Lev</i>. 15:20–21</a> it is stated that anything she lies on becomes an original source of impurity. This means that if a woman in her period lies on top of ten mattresses and somebody touches the lowest one, which the woman never touched, he becomes impure as if he touched the woman herself. For R. Aqiba anybody who carries an idol becomes impure even if he never touched the idol.</i>, also idolatry makes impure by load.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If one’s house was connected to a house of pagan worship,” etc. <sup class="footnote-marker">175</sup><i class="footnote">This Halakhah also is Halakhah 9:1 in <i>Šabbat</i>(<b>ש</b>). Evidence points to <i>Šabbat</i> as the primary source. Much of the argument is found in Babli <i>Šabbat</i> 82b–83b.</i> There is written <i>abomination</i> about the menstruating women, <i>abomination</i> about vermin, <i>abomination</i> about idolatry. About the menstruating woman, <i>for anybody who would commit any of these abominations</i><sup class="footnote-marker">176</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 18:29" href="/Leviticus.18.29"><i>Lev</i>. 18:29</a>. The verse refers to all prohibitions of a sexual nature.</i>. About vermin, <i>do not eat any abomination</i><sup class="footnote-marker">177</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 14:2" href="/Deuteronomy.14.2"><i>Deut</i>. 14: 2</a>. The verse refers to all food prohibitions.</i>. About idolatry, <i>and do not bring any abomination into your house</i><sup class="footnote-marker">178</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 7:26" href="/Deuteronomy.7.26"><i>Deut</i>. 7:26</a>. This verse refers uniquely to idols and idolatry.</i>. But I do not know for which purpose it was compared. Rebbi Aqiba says, it was compared to <i>abomination</i> regarding the menstruating woman. As the menstruating woman imparts impurity by load<sup class="footnote-marker">174</sup><i class="footnote">In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 15:20-21" href="/Leviticus.15.20-21"><i>Lev</i>. 15:20–21</a> it is stated that anything she lies on becomes an original source of impurity. This means that if a woman in her period lies on top of ten mattresses and somebody touches the lowest one, which the woman never touched, he becomes impure as if he touched the woman herself. For R. Aqiba anybody who carries an idol becomes impure even if he never touched the idol.</i>, also idolatry imparts impurity by load. Or since the menstruating woman imparts impurity through a cover stone<sup class="footnote-marker">179</sup><i class="footnote">Stone is impervious to impurity. In general, anything not susceptible to impurity cannot transmit impurity. The one and only exception is impurity caused by genital discharges where impurity by load (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Avodah Zarah 3:6:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Avodah_Zarah.3.6.2">Note 174</a>) applies to anything under the affected person and even a stone plate covering a mattress will not shield the mattress from impurity if a person afflicted by a genital discharge sits on the stone. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Niddah 69b" href="/Niddah.69b">Babli <i>Niddah</i> 69b</a>.</i>, does idolatry impart impurity through a cover stone? Rebbi Zeriqa in the name of Rebbi Ḥanina<sup class="footnote-marker">180</sup><i class="footnote">In, <i>Šabbat</i>: Rav Jehudah. On one hand, the tradent in <i>Šabbat</i> is mentioned as R. Zeriqan, the Yerushalmi form, not the Babli form Zeriqa as here; but this is to be explained by the babylonized spelling of the text of the present Tractate. On the other hand, the tradent in the Babli (<i>Šabbat</i> 82b) is R. Eleazar, a known student of R. Ḥanina.</i>, but some say in the name of Rav Ḥisda: Rebbi Aqiba agrees with the Sages that idolatry does not impart impurity through a cover stone. But the rabbis say it was compared to <i>abominations</i> of vermin. As vermin imparts impurity by motion<sup class="footnote-marker">181</sup><i class="footnote">Here one has a serious discrepancy between the technical terminology of the Babli and the Yerushalmi. In the Babli impurity by motion is a form of impurity by load: If a person suffering from a genital discharge moves something indirectly or is moved with it, he imparts impurity. In the Yerushalmi this is consistently designated by its Mishnaic name, מִדְרָס, “stepping on.” This kind of impurity emphatically does not exist for vermin, or anything other than genital discharges. Therefore היסט the “motion” mentioned here must be that of a person’s hand touching an impure object. Transfer of impurity by touch is the only one mentioned for the eight kinds of impure vermin.</i>, so also idolatry imparts impurity by motion. Or as vermin in the size of a lentil imparts impurity<sup class="footnote-marker">182</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Oholot 1:8" href="/Mishnah_Oholot.1.8">Mishnah <i>Ahilut</i> 1:8</a>. This minimum size for generation of impurity does not apply to complete limbs.</i> does also idolatry in the size of a lentil impart impurity? Rebbi Zeˋira, Rebbi Isaac bar Naḥman, Rebbi Eleazar, Rebbi Abbahu in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: <i>They were yoked to Baal Peor and ate sacrifices to the dead</i><sup class="footnote-marker">183</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Psalms 106:28" href="/Psalms.106.28"><i>Ps</i>. 106:28</a>.</i>. As the dead in the size of an olive impart impurity<sup class="footnote-marker">182</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Oholot 1:8" href="/Mishnah_Oholot.1.8">Mishnah <i>Ahilut</i> 1:8</a>. This minimum size for generation of impurity does not apply to complete limbs.</i> so idolatry in the size of an olive imparts impurity. Or since a corpse imparts impurity once a person puts his finger tips in<sup class="footnote-marker">184</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to “tent” impurity (<i>Ševuot</i> 2:1 Note 34) which is created by <i>any</i> part of a person’s body being under the same roof as a corpse, even if it is only a finger tip.</i>, could I think that idolatry imparts once a person puts his finger tips in? <i>Tearing down</i>, <i>tearing down</i> one infers from the leprous house<sup class="footnote-marker">185</sup><i class="footnote">A house afflicted with recurrent “leprosy” must be torn down (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 14:45" href="/Leviticus.14.45"><i>Lev</i>. 14:45</a>). Pagan altars must be torn down (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 12:3" href="/Deuteronomy.12.3"><i>Deut</i>. 12:3</a>). By the nature of the topics, the verb נתץ is used in the singular in the first case, in the plural in the second. Therefore this is a comparison (הקש), not an “equal cut” (גזירה שוה); the laws will be similiar, not exactly identical.</i>. Since in a leprous house when he entered with his head and most of his body<sup class="footnote-marker">186</sup><i class="footnote">Based on <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 14:46" href="/Leviticus.14.46"><i>Lev</i>. 14:46</a>, which decrees impurity for anybody coming <i>into</i> the house, <i>Sifra Meṣoraˋ Pereq</i> 5(4), <a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Negaim 13:8" href="/Mishnah_Negaim.13.8">Mishnah <i>Negaˋim</i> 13:8</a>.</i>, so idolatry when he entered with his head and most of his body. Rebbi Ḥanina<sup class="footnote-marker">187</sup><i class="footnote">In <i>Šabbat</i>: Ḥanania. The latter attribution is correct since he must have been a contemporary of R. Mana (II).</i> said, this means that the impurity of idolatry is not consistent<sup class="footnote-marker">188</sup><i class="footnote">Neither R. Aqiba nor the rabbis are consistent in their comparisons.</i>. For otherwise, why does one compare if for the facile [impurity] and does not compare for the strict? Rebbi Mana said, it is consistent. Why was it compared to a corpse and to vermin? To inform in both cases about the facile [impurity] attached to it<sup class="footnote-marker">189</sup><i class="footnote">The impurity of idols and idolatry should follow the rules common to impurities generated either by dead vermin or by bodily discharges. This argument is known in the Babli tradition as הַצַּד הַשָּׁוֶה “the equal part;” cf. H. Guggenheimer, <i>Logical Problems in Jewish Tradition</i>, in: “Confrontations with Judaism”, ed. Ph. Longworth, London 1966, p. 185.</i>. This is for a broken idol. But an entire one even in the most minute size<sup class="footnote-marker">190</sup><i class="footnote">This is consistent with the impurity of animals as food, where a complete creature always is biblically forbidden irrespective of size (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 6:1:7" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.6.1.7"><i>Nazir</i> 6:1 Note 64</a>).</i>, as Rebbi Yose hen Rebbi Abun<sup class="footnote-marker">191</sup><i class="footnote">In <b>ש</b>: R. Ḥuna.</i> said, Rav Ḥama bar Gorion in the name of Rav: Baal was the penis gland in the form of a bean. What is the reason? <i>They selected the Baal of circumcision as god</i><sup class="footnote-marker">192</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Judges 8:33" href="/Judges.8.33"><i>Jud</i>. 8:33</a>. Instead of “Baal of Covenant” one reads “Baal of circumcision” referring to the place of circumcision. This identifies the Semitic Baal with the Greek and Roman Priapus.</i>.
What is Rebbi Aqiba’s reason? <i>You should treat it as an abomination</i><sup class="footnote-marker">193</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 7:26" href="/Deuteronomy.7.26"><i>Deut</i>. 7:26</a>.</i>, like a menstruating woman. What is the rabbis’ reason? <i>You should detest it</i>, like vermin. How do the rabbis uphold Rebbi Aqiba’s reason, <i>you should treat it as abomination</i>? Treat it as excrement, make it vile<sup class="footnote-marker">194</sup><i class="footnote"><b>ש</b> has an addition: How does R. Aqiba interpret the rabbi’s reason, <i>you should detest it</i>? Detest it, make it vile. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 46a" href="/Avodah_Zarah.46a">Babli 46a</a>, <i>Mekhilta dR</i>. <i>Ismael Mišpatim</i> 20 (p. 332 ed. Horovitz-Rabin).</i>. What means “make it vile” for the rabbis? Rebbi Samuel, Rebbi Abbahu in the name of Rebbi Eleazar: <i>you shall call it excrement</i>. What is called God’s<sup class="footnote-marker">195</sup><i class="footnote">In <b>ש</b>: King’s face.</i>. Face is called Dog’s Face. Spring of the Cup is called Spring of the Thorn. The Place of Fortune is called the Place of Undress<sup class="footnote-marker">196</sup><i class="footnote">All these places and most of their idolatrous meanings are unknown. A similar list is as Tannaitic text in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 46a" href="/Avodah_Zarah.46a">Babli 46a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Avodah Zarah 7:3" href="/Tosefta_Avodah_Zarah.7.3">Tosephta 6:4</a></i>. Rebbi Tanḥuma (asked) [in the name of]<sup class="footnote-marker">197</sup><i class="footnote">The reading of <i>Avodah zarah</i> is in (parentheses), that of <i>Šabbat</i> in [brackets].</i> Rebbi Huna: It is written<sup class="footnote-marker">198</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Joshua 7:2" href="/Joshua.7.2"><i>Jos</i>. 7:2</a>.</i>, <i>The Ai which is near Bet-Awen East of Bethel</i>. Earlier it was called Bethel but now one calls it Bet-Awen<sup class="footnote-marker">199</sup><i class="footnote">It seems that the translator of the LXX read the verse in the way of the Talmud and eliminated the mention of Bet-Awen, “the House of Iniquity”, as a gloss, as accepted by the moderns. (However, E. Täubler in a marginal Note to his copy of M. Noth’s <i>Das Buch Josua</i>, Tübingen 1938, identifies Bet-Awen as the old name of “The Ai (ruin)”; cf. Arabic اون “calm, tranquillity”.)</i>. It was stated in the name of Rebbi Eleazar: If one does not want to call it ‘Omda one calls it ‘Amida since the flow of urine is called <i>‘amida</i><sup class="footnote-marker">200</sup><i class="footnote">This piece is truncated here but it is in bad shape both here and in <i>Šabbat</i> (and <i>Yalqut Jos</i>. <i>ad</i> 7:2). It is intelligible in <i>Gen</i>. <i>rabba</i> 39(24), on <a class="refLink" data-ref="Genesis 12:8" href="/Genesis.12.8"><i>Gen</i>. 12:8</a>, the first mention of The Ai in the Bible, following the interpretation of J. Levy:<br>ויעתק משם ההרה מקדם לבית אל. לשעבר היתה נקראת בית אל ועכשיו היא נקראת בית און. א״ר אלעזר לא זכתה להקראת בית העמל הרי היא הקראת בית העמד. תמן קריין לפועלא טבא עמילה ולהרהון של מימי רגלים עמידה.<br><i>He transferred from there to the mountain, East of Bethel</i>. In the past it was called Bethel but now it is called Bet Awen. Rebbi Eleazar said, it did not merit to be called the House of Work but it is called the House of Baptism. There, they are calling a good worker عَمِل but the chamber pot ἀμίδα.</i>. How (do the rabbis) [does Rebbi Aqiba]<sup class="footnote-marker">197</sup><i class="footnote">The reading of <i>Avodah zarah</i> is in (parentheses), that of <i>Šabbat</i> in [brackets].</i> interpret <i>you shall call it excrement</i>? Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abin, Rebbi Huna in the name of Rav Josef: from (where) [here]<sup class="footnote-marker">197</sup><i class="footnote">The reading of <i>Avodah zarah</i> is in (parentheses), that of <i>Šabbat</i> in [brackets].</i> that one does not tell anybody to leave unless he entered with his head and most of his body.
<sup class="footnote-marker">204</sup><i class="footnote">At some places, this paragraph has been shortened from the text in <i>Šabbat</i> to the extent as to become unintelligible without recourse to that text.</i> There exists a <i>baraita</i> which says, idols are like a menstruating woman and its appurtenances are like a menstruating woman. Also there exists a <i>baraita</i> which says, idols are like a menstruating woman and its appurtenances are like crawling animals<sup class="footnote-marker">205</sup><i class="footnote">The first opinion is the teaching of R. Aqiba in the Mishnah, the second is ascribed to R. Aqiba in the Babli, <i>Šabbat</i> 83a.</i>. The one who says, idols are like a menstruating woman and its appurtenances are like a menstruating woman, is understandable. But the one who says, idols are like a menstruating woman and its appurtenances are like crawling animals, is it not called “unwell” only for its appurtenances<sup class="footnote-marker">206</sup><i class="footnote">The full text of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Isaiah 30:22" href="/Isaiah.30.22"><i>Is</i>. 30:22</a> (quoted in <i>Šabbat</i>) which is the base of R. Aqiba’s argument reads: <i>You will defile the cover of your silver statues and the clothing of your golden casts; you shall throw it away like feeling miserable</i>, <i>you shall call it excrement</i>. Therefore the reference of “feeling miserable” which is the description of a female period refers to appurtenances only. The second version of the position of R. Aqiba seems untenable.</i>? Explain it if they were engraved on its body. As Rebbi Jacob of Kefar Ḥanan said, explain it if one worships the <i>ephod</i> itself, similar to what is written, <i>Gideon made an ephod</i><sup class="footnote-marker">207</sup><i class="footnote">The formulation in <i>Šabbat</i> is better: R. Jacob said (as an alternative explanation) that the statue and its ornamental vest were two separate objects of worship. Either explanation is possible.</i>. Our Mishnah follows him who said, idols are like a menstruating woman and its appurtenances are like a menstruating woman, and we have stated: “its stones, its wood, and its dust make impure like a crawling animal.<sup class="footnote-marker">208</sup><i class="footnote">Since this sentence in the Mishnah precedes the statement of R. Aqiba, it is implied that R. Aqiba disagrees and imposes the impurity of <i>niddah</i> also on the stones which form the shell of the house of worship but are not the object of worship.</i>” Explain it if he worshipped the house itself, as Rebbi Abba, Rab Huna said in the name of Rav: One who worships a house makes it forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">209</sup><i class="footnote">A comparison with the text in <i>Šabbat</i> shows that the sentence has to be split in two. First it is stated that the house will be strictly impure if it was worshipped. Then a question is missing, quoting the following Mishnah 9: Only a house originally built as a temple is permanently forbidden; all others can be cleansed by removing the idol and all installations and ornamentations made for it. How could one decree severe impurity which can be easily eliminated? On this Rav says that even a house not built for worship becomes permanently forbidden as if it had been built as a pagan temple, if itself was worshipped. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 47b" href="/Avodah_Zarah.47b">Babli 47b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Meilah 20a" href="/Meilah.20a"><i>Meˋilah</i> 20a</a>.</i>. Rebbi Zeˋira, Rebbi Abbahu in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: If somebody dedicates a house one does not commit larceny with it. And he who says, one does not commit larceny with it, can he make it forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">210</sup><i class="footnote">In <i>Šabbat</i> one reads: “R. Zeˋura, R. Abbahu in the name of R. Joḥanan, if one dedicates his house one commits larceny with it.” The reading here is confirmed by Halakhah 9.<br>One compares the rules concerning a pagan temple with those of a house dedicated to the Temple (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 27:14-15" href="/Leviticus.27.14-15"><i>Lev</i>. 27:14–15</a>). Improper use of dedicated things is larceny which must be expiated by a sacrifice and payment of a fine, (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 5:14-16" href="/Leviticus.5.14-16"><i>Lev</i>. 5:14–16</a>). Just as real estate cannot become forbidden by idolatry, larceny by improper use of dedicated objects does not apply to real estate. If a house is considered real estate, it cannot become forbidden by worship, and its improper use while in the possession of the Temple cannot trigger a fine for larceny. If it is not considered real estate since the building materials were movables before being used, it can become forbidden and improper use can trigger the fine.</i>? Rebbi Ḥaggai objected before Rebbi Yose, does not a Mishnah disagree with Rav? “A trough in a rock: one does not fill from it, and one does not sanctify with it, and one does not sprinkle with it, it does not have to be fastened with a tightened cover, and it does not invalidate a <i>miqweh</i>. If it was a vessel and he fixed it with lime, etc., up to it invalidates a <i>miqweh</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">211</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Parah 5:7" href="/Mishnah_Parah.5.7">Mishnah <i>Parah</i> 5:7</a>. The ashes of the Red Cow, used to purify a person from the impurity of the dead, must be strewn on “flowing water in a vessel” (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 19:17" href="/Numbers.19.17"><i>Num</i>. 19:17</a>). A vessel is movable; therefore a trough hewn into the rock is not a vessel. The water flowing from the source into the trough becomes standing water. Therefore it may be used neither (1) to fill a vessel for the ashes, nor (2) to put some ashes in the water, “to sanctify it”, nor (3) to sprinkle the water on impure persons to purify them.<br>In addition, a corpse in a “tent” makes everything in the tent impure including the contents of vessels whose cover is not tightly fastened (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 19:15" href="/Numbers.19.15"><i>Num</i>. 19:15</a>). Since the trough is no vessel, if it is under one roof with a corpse it only needs to be covered but the cover does not have to be fastened.<br>A <i>miqweh</i> (ritual bath) has to contain 40 <i>seah</i> of water. It becomes invalid if 3 <i>log</i> (⅛ <i>seah</i>) of water from a vessel is poured into it. If the trough is not a vessel, its water cannot invalidate the <i>miqweh</i>. On the other hand, if the trough was a vessel before it was fastened in the rock, it can be used for the ashes of the red cow, and its water will disqualify the <i>miqweh</i>.<br>Since a house was not a vessel before being connected to the ground, it should be considered real estate and not be subject to prohibition because of worship.</i>” Because he excavated it and after that combined it<sup class="footnote-marker">212</sup><i class="footnote">A vessel which is permanently fixed to the ground remains a vessel and can become forbidden.</i>. Then if he fixed it and after that excavated it, what do you do with it<sup class="footnote-marker">213</sup><i class="footnote">A piece of loose rock which was cemented to the ground and then a trough was hewn from it does not become a vessel. Then why should a house become forbidden by being worshipped since it becomes a house only after being connected to the ground?</i>? Explain it following Rebbi Joḥanan that the hewing of stones is the completion of work on them<sup class="footnote-marker">214</sup><i class="footnote">It is true that a finished house not built as a temple cannot become forbidden. But if the finished stones for a stone building were worshipped before being cemented in the house they already are forbidden and do not become permitted by use as building blocks.</i>. Does this<sup class="footnote-marker">215</sup><i class="footnote">. The Mishnah which subjects the building material of the collapsed wall to the rules of idolatry. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Meilah 20a" href="/Meilah.20a">Babli <i>Meˋilah</i> 20a</a>.</i> not disagree with Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish who said, an idol which was broken is permitted? And so we are thinking to say if in the future he can restore it to its vessel it is forbidden according to everybody<sup class="footnote-marker">216</sup><i class="footnote">The statement of R. Simeon ben Laqish and the opposing statement of R. Joḥanan only refer to situations where it is not clear whether the idol can be restored or not.</i>. Explain it if he worships every single stone and then builds with them<sup class="footnote-marker">217</sup><i class="footnote">Then each individual stone remains forbidden; there is no contradiction to Mishnah 10.</i>. Does this not disagree with Rebbi Joḥanan, as Rebbi Joḥanan said, an idol which was broken is forbidden? And so we are thinking to say if in the future he cannot restore it to its vessel it is permitted according to everybody<sup class="footnote-marker">216</sup><i class="footnote">The statement of R. Simeon ben Laqish and the opposing statement of R. Joḥanan only refer to situations where it is not clear whether the idol can be restored or not.</i>. Explain it if he worshipped a vine and afterwards planted it<sup class="footnote-marker">218</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to a holy tree which is worshipped as <i>Ashera</i> (Mishnah 11) but where no idol is found buried under it. Then as connected to the ground it should not be part of the real estate and not be forbidden. It becomes permanently forbidden only if it was planted as a holy shoot.</i>.
Levi said, one who worships a house makes it forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">209</sup><i class="footnote">A comparison with the text in <i>Šabbat</i> shows that the sentence has to be split in two. First it is stated that the house will be strictly impure if it was worshipped. Then a question is missing, quoting the following Mishnah 9: Only a house originally built as a temple is permanently forbidden; all others can be cleansed by removing the idol and all installations and ornamentations made for it. How could one decree severe impurity which can be easily eliminated? On this Rav says that even a house not built for worship becomes permanently forbidden as if it had been built as a pagan temple, if itself was worshipped. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 47b" href="/Avodah_Zarah.47b">Babli 47b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Meilah 20a" href="/Meilah.20a"><i>Meˋilah</i> 20a</a>.</i>, a cave he does not make forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">219</sup><i class="footnote">Even if the cave was excavated for purposes of idol worship it does not lose its character as real estate.</i>. What is between a house and a cave? Rebbi Ḥanina hen Rebbi Hillel said, a house was separated at some time; a cave never was separated.
<sup class="footnote-marker">220</sup><i class="footnote">This paragraph is not in <i>Šabbat</i>.</i> Rebbi Joḥanan explained the Mishnah<sup class="footnote-marker">221</sup><i class="footnote">To explain how the house of a Jew and a pagan place of worship may share a wall.</i> about a proselyte and a pagan who inherited from their pagan father. Why does he not explain it when idol worship came and built next to him? It was stated: If a house of idol worship was built next to him and then left it is permitted<sup class="footnote-marker">222</sup><i class="footnote">If the Jew’s house was there before the pagan, it is not touched by temporary use of the adjacent house as place of pagan worship. But if the Jew builds in disregard of the injunction of the Mishnah to construct a separate wall inside his real estate he is fined.</i>. But for a proselyte and a pagan who inherited from their pagan father, it is forbidden. It was stated: If a house of idol worship was built next to him and then left it is permitted. But if he came and built next to idol worship the entire house is for idol worship<sup class="footnote-marker">222</sup><i class="footnote">If the Jew’s house was there before the pagan, it is not touched by temporary use of the adjacent house as place of pagan worship. But if the Jew builds in disregard of the injunction of the Mishnah to construct a separate wall inside his real estate he is fined.</i>. Rebbi Yose said, our Mishnah says so, “If it was his and the idolatry’s, it is judged half by half.<sup class="footnote-marker">223</sup><i class="footnote">The Mishnah might be referring to a house built after the pagan place already existed, not a proselyte brother to a pagan.</i>”

Halakhah 7

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> There are three houses. A house which one built originally for idolatry is forbidden. If he whitewashed it or ornamented it for idolatry and renovated, one removes what he renovated. If he introduced there an idol and removed it, it is permitted.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “There are three houses,” etc. Rav and Rebbi Joḥanan both are saying, the Mishnah treats their entry into the Land<sup class="footnote-marker">224</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 7:5" href="/Deuteronomy.7.5"><i>Deut</i>. 7:5</a> is an unconditional commandment to destroy all pagan altars and Temples in existence at the time of the conquest. This agrees with the statement that the house in the first case is absolutely and irrevocably forbidden, since in all other cases a partial destruction of the house will remove the prohibition.</i>. The opinion of Rav, who said that one who worships a house makes it forbidden, is the reason that he explains it by their entry into the Land<sup class="footnote-marker">225</sup><i class="footnote">Since he considers a house as a kind of movable (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Avodah Zarah 3:6:5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Avodah_Zarah.3.6.5">Note 209</a>), his problem is that the first rule of the Mishnah should apply not only to a house built as a pagan temple but also to one which was worshipped. But the effect of worship can be undone (Halakhot 14–16); only the commandment <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 7:5" href="/Deuteronomy.7.5"><i>Deut</i>. 7:5</a> is absolute.</i>. Following the opinion of Rebbi Joḥanan, who said that if somebody dedicates a house one does not commit larceny with it, could he not have explained it by somebody who was worshipping the house<sup class="footnote-marker">226</sup><i class="footnote">Since a house is real estate, it cannot become forbidden by simply being worshipped. But if a Jew built it for purposes of idolatry, it becomes forbidden by an act of idolatry (Mishnah 4:4). Therefore the first case of the Mishnah could also be referred to a case possible today. One does not expect a rule in the Mishnah which became obsolete some 1500 years before its formulation. The answer to be given is the same as in the case of Rav.</i>? {The effect of] one who worships a house can be voided. At their entry into the Land it could not be voided<sup class="footnote-marker">225</sup><i class="footnote">Since he considers a house as a kind of movable (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Avodah Zarah 3:6:5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Avodah_Zarah.3.6.5">Note 209</a>), his problem is that the first rule of the Mishnah should apply not only to a house built as a pagan temple but also to one which was worshipped. But the effect of worship can be undone (Halakhot 14–16); only the commandment <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 7:5" href="/Deuteronomy.7.5"><i>Deut</i>. 7:5</a> is absolute.</i>. What could he do<sup class="footnote-marker">227</sup><i class="footnote">To desecrate the house and therefore nullify its pagan status.</i>? He removes its clapboards and it is permitted.
Rebbi Ila in the name of Rebbi Eleazar: Only if he brought an idol into it<sup class="footnote-marker">228</sup><i class="footnote">This now refers to the second case, a house renovated for the purpose of idolatry. As property of the Jew it becomes forbidden only by an act of pagan worship, not by intention (Mishnah 4:4).</i>. In the opinion of Rebbi [Eleazar]<sup class="footnote-marker">229</sup><i class="footnote">Missing in the text; added from Halakhah 10 as required by the context.</i> there are two houses. But have we not stated three? Here temporarily, there permanently<sup class="footnote-marker">230</sup><i class="footnote">Since the presence of an idol is required in both cases 2 and 3, what is the difference? In case 3 the idol is temporarily in the house; one removes it and the house is permitted. In case 2 the intention is a permanent installation of the idol; therefore not only the idol but all installation has to be removed. Also for R. Eleazar there are three distinct cases.</i>.

Halakhah 8

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> There are three kinds of stone. If he quarried them from the start as pedestal<sup class="footnote-marker">231</sup><i class="footnote">Greek βωμός. The expression is used exclusively for pedestals of idols (including statues of emperors). From the comparison with the next Mishnaiot one must assume that a pedestal in itself was an object of veneration.</i> it is forbidden. If he whitewashed it and ornamented it for idolatry and renovated, one removes what he renovated. If he put an idol on it and removed it, it is permitted.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “There are three kinds of stones,” etc. Rebbi Abba in the name of Rav: This<sup class="footnote-marker">232</sup><i class="footnote">The first part of the Mishnah is not only about stones quarried but about everything intentionally manufactured as an idol. Therefore if a metal statue was cast, the process of casting makes it forbidden even before it is polished and worshipped.</i> implies that what is cast for idolatry is immediately forbidden. Rebbi Jeremiah in the name of Rebbi Eleazar: This implies that if one casts a cup for idolatry it is immediately forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">233</sup><i class="footnote">A cup is an implement of worship; it is not an object of worship. The statement of R. Eleazar is stronger than that of Rav. The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 52a" href="/Avodah_Zarah.52a">Babli disagrees, 52a</a>.</i>.
Rebbi Ila in the name of Rebbi Eleazar: Only if he put an idol on it<sup class="footnote-marker">234</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to the second case in the Mishnah, where a stone quarried indifferently is adorned to become an altar.</i>. The opinions of Rav<sup class="footnote-marker">235</sup><i class="footnote">It seems that one has to read: R. Eleazar.</i> seem inconsistent. There he says, if one casts a cup for idolatry it is immediately forbidden, and here he says so<sup class="footnote-marker">236</sup><i class="footnote">Why for a cup it is immediately forbidden but for a decorated stone only by actually being used in idolatry?</i>? Explain it following what has been stated<sup class="footnote-marker">237</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah 4:4. It is presumed that only a Gentile would make something from the start for idolatry; the most a Jew would do is to adorn an object.</i>: “A Gentile’s idol is immediately forbidden, but a Jew’s when it was worshipped.” There is disagreement about an idol, maybe also for its appurtenances<sup class="footnote-marker">238</sup><i class="footnote">In Halakhah 4:4 there is an opinion to the reverse: the idol of a Gentile becomes forbidden only by being worshipped, the Jew’s idol immediately upon acquisition or manufacture.</i>? Rebbi Simeon in the name of Rebbi Eleazar: There is disagreement also about appurtenances<sup class="footnote-marker">239</sup><i class="footnote">Even though Mishnah 4:4 and the corresponding <i>baraita</i> are formulated for idols, they are valid for all implements of idolatry. The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 52a" href="/Avodah_Zarah.52a">Babli disagrees, 52a</a>.</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">240</sup><i class="footnote">This is completely parallel to the corresponding paragraph in Halakhah 9.</i> Rebbi Ila in the name of Rebbi Eleazar: Only if he put an idol on it. In the opinion of Rebbi Eleazar there are two stones. But have we not stated “there are three stones”? Here temporarily, there permanently.

Halakhah 9

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> There are three kinds of <i>Ashera</i><sup class="footnote-marker">128</sup><i class="footnote">A holy tree (Mishnah 11), which is connected to the ground and by the argument of Note 125 should be permitted as building material or fire wood.</i>. A tree which originally was planted for idolatry is forbidden. If he cut and shaped<sup class="footnote-marker">241</sup><i class="footnote">While the tree is still standing on the ground. A cut tree is not an <i>Ashera</i>.</i> it for idolatry and it grows new shoots, one removes what grew newly. If he puts an idol under it<sup class="footnote-marker">242</sup><i class="footnote">Under a tree not previously grown idolatry.</i>, when he makes it invalid it becomes permitted.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “There are three kinds of <i>Ashera</i>,” etc. Rebbi Immi in the name of Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish said, if he engraved engravings in it it cannot become invalidated. This is necessary: even if he removed the engravings<sup class="footnote-marker">243</sup><i class="footnote">Even though in the second case Mishnah it is sufficient to remove everything grown after the tree was shaped for idolatry, if an engraving was made it is irrevocable even if it was obliterated.</i>. It was stated: If he grafted, he has to remove what he grafted. Rebbi Yannai said, only if he grafted for idolatry<sup class="footnote-marker">244</sup><i class="footnote">If the grafted tree would have been used as <i>Asherah</i>. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 48a" href="/Avodah_Zarah.48a">Babli 48a</a>.</i>. Rebbi Hila in the name of Rebbi Eleazar<sup class="footnote-marker">245</sup><i class="footnote">The following argument shows that one has to read: R. Simeon ben Laqish.</i>: Only if he put an idol under it. The opinions of Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish seem inconsistent. There Rebbi Immi said in the name of Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish, if he engraved engravings on it it cannot become invalidated; and we interpreted this to mean, even if he removed the engravings. And here<sup class="footnote-marker">246</sup><i class="footnote">In the second case of the Mishnah he not only rules restrictively that an engraving is irrevocable but also leniently that shaping a tree as <i>Asherah</i> makes it forbidden only if an idol is placed under the tree.</i> he says so? It is more severe with engravings.
<sup class="footnote-marker">247</sup><i class="footnote">Dittography induced by the repetitious quote of the statements of R. Simeon ben Laqish.</i> It was stated: If he grafted, he has to remove what he grafted. Rebbi Yannai said, only if he grafted for idolatry. Rebbi Hila in the name of Rebbi Eleazar: Only if he put an idol under it.
<sup class="footnote-marker">240,</sup><i class="footnote">This is completely parallel to the corresponding paragraph in Halakhah 9.</i><sup class="footnote-marker">248</sup><i class="footnote">Babli 48a.</i>Rebbi Ila in the name of Rebbi Eleazar: Only if he put an idol under it. In the opinion of Rebbi Eleazar there are two <i>Asherot</i>. But have we not stated “there are three kinds of <i>Ashera</i>”? Here temporarily, there permanently.

Halakhah 10

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> What is an <i>Ashera</i><sup class="footnote-marker">249</sup><i class="footnote">Where one has no information to place it in one of the categories of Mishnah 11. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 48a" href="/Avodah_Zarah.48a">Babli 48a</a>.</i>?
