Any under which is an idol. Rebbi Simeon says, any which is worshipped. It happened in Sidon that they were worshipping one and there was a mound under it. Rebbi Joshua<sup class="footnote-marker">250</sup><i class="footnote">This is a copyist’s error as shown in the Halakhah. One has to read with all other Mishnah source: R. Simeon.</i> said to them, investigate the mound! They investigated it and found a figure in it. He told them, since they worship the figure we may permit the tree to them<sup class="footnote-marker">251</sup><i class="footnote">The rules of Mishnaiot 13–16 do not apply to this tree.</i>
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> What is an <i>Ashera</i>? Rav Ḥisda said, They disagree in the indeterminate case<sup class="footnote-marker">249</sup><i class="footnote">Where one has no information to place it in one of the categories of Mishnah 11. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 48a" href="/Avodah_Zarah.48a">Babli 48a</a>.</i>. Where do we hold? If it is certain that they worship both idol and tree, everybody agrees that it is forbidden. But if it is certain that they worship the idol and do not worship the tree, everybody agrees that it is permitted. But where do we hold? If it is indeterminate. Rebbi Simeon says, normally they worship both idol and tree; but the rabbis say, normally they worship the idol but do not worship the tree. And it is difficult. Rebbi Simeon disagrees with the Sages and acts in their sense<sup class="footnote-marker">252</sup><i class="footnote">Since in the interpretation of the Halakhah, R. Simeon should have forbidden both tree and idol. But there is nothing to wonder at since R. Simeon insists that practice follow the majority rule against his own opinion, <i>Seviˋit</i> 9:1, Notes 35–36.</i>!

Halakhah 11

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> One should not sit in its shadow<sup class="footnote-marker">253</sup><i class="footnote">It was noted earlier that idols impart “tent” impurity (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Avodah Zarah 3:6:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Avodah_Zarah.3.6.2">Note 184</a>). Therefore if one sits under the crown of an <i>Ashera</i> he is impure by the impurity of the dead. But if he sits in its shadow but not under its crown, he illegally profits from idolatry but is not impure. If he passes under an <i>Ashera</i> he becomes impure like anybody passing under a tent above a corpse. But walking through the shadow of an <i>Ashera</i> outside its crown is not considered profiting from idolatry.</i> but if one sat he is pure. One should not pass under it and if one passed under it he is impure. If it robbed the public<sup class="footnote-marker">254</sup><i class="footnote">It is clear from the earlier sources (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Avodah Zarah 3:6:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Avodah_Zarah.3.6.2">Note 184</a>) and the following Halakhah that the tent impurity generated by an idol is purely rabbinic. If the crown of the <i>Ashera</i> extended over a public road, the customary impurity of idols never was intended to deprive Jews from using public roads.</i> and one passed under it, he is pure. One may sow vegetables under it in the rainy season but not in summer<sup class="footnote-marker">255</sup><i class="footnote">In winter, the shade given by the tree inhibits the growth of the vegetables; the tree is a detriment, not a positive influence. But in summer the shade is necessary; it clearly is usufruct.</i>, but lettuce neither in summer nor in the rainy season. Rebbi Yose says not even vegetables in the rainy season since falling leaves drop down on it and become fertilizer.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “One should not sit in its shadow,” etc. There<sup class="footnote-marker">256</sup><i class="footnote">In Babylonia.</i> they say m the name of Rav Ḥisda: Its shadow is forbidden, its half-shadow is permitted. What is its shadow and what is its half-shadow? There they say, any place where if it would fall down would touch him is its shadow, but any place where if it would fall down would not touch him is its half-shadow<sup class="footnote-marker">257</sup><i class="footnote">It is clear that at any place not under the tree’s crown there is no question of impurity. The discussion is only about the statement that one should not sit in the <i>Ashera’s</i> shadow. It is now added that in the half-shadow one may sit, i. e., far enough away that if the tree fell down in the direction of the person sitting there the fallen crown would not reach the person.<br>In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 48b" href="/Avodah_Zarah.48b">Babli, 48b</a>, this is attributed to R. Joḥanan the Galilean.</i>
Why is its shadow forbidden? Because it is an enjoyment. But a grave is forbidden for usufruct and its shadow is permitted! But the Temple Hall is forbidden for usufruct and Rabban Joḥanan ben Zakkai was sitting and teaching in the shadow of the Temple Hall<sup class="footnote-marker">258</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 26a" href="/Pesachim.26a">Babli <i>Pesaḥim</i> 26a</a>. There it is argued that he could do this only because the Temple was built for its service and the Herodian Hall was 100 cubits high so that its shadow at times extended beyond the Temple Hill; it never was intended that the shadow should prevent people from being there. This disagrees with the statements here.</i>! Therefore the reason cannot be that it is forbidden for usufruct. Rav Abin in the name of the rabbis there<sup class="footnote-marker">256</sup><i class="footnote">In Babylonia.</i>: This implies that this impurity of the dead is not logically consistent<sup class="footnote-marker">259</sup><i class="footnote">Since as pointed out in the next sentence, a mausoleum whose roof extends over a public road will automatically impart impurity to any Jew walking under it (and force any Cohen to make a detour to avoid passing under that roof) the rules of the <i>Ashera</i> cannot be deduced from the rules of the impurity of the dead. They are customary rules and as such not susceptible to logical analysis.</i>. For otherwise if a grave were robbing the public and one passed under it one would be pure.
Gamliel the Twin was supporting<sup class="footnote-marker">260</sup><i class="footnote">In this paragraph, the second person mentioned always is the authority who in walking leans upon one of his students. A comparison with other occurrences shows that מיסתמיך ב means “was supporting” but מיסתמיך על is “leaning on.”</i> Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish. They came to this statue. He asked him, may we pass in front of it? He said, pass by it and blind its eye<sup class="footnote-marker">261</sup><i class="footnote">One “blinds the eye” of the idol by passing by it without taking any notice of it and the idol sees that it is not taken seriously.</i>. Rebbi Isaac bar Mattanah was supporting Rebbi Joḥanan. They came to the statue of the city council<sup class="footnote-marker">262</sup><i class="footnote">Greek βουλή. In Tiberias this probably was a statue of <i>divus</i> Tiberius.</i> He asked him, may we pass in front of it? He said, pass by it and blind its eye. Rebbi Jacob bar Idi was supporting Rebbi Joshua ben Levi. They came to the statue of Herod<sup class="footnote-marker">263</sup><i class="footnote">In <i>Moˋed qatan</i> 3:7 the spelling is as here. In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Berakhot 2:1:16" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Berakhot.2.1.16"><i>Berakhot</i> 2:1 (Note 57</a>) אהדורי, in <i>Šeqalim</i> 2:6 ארורא, in <i>Midrash Shemuel</i> 19[4], ed. Buber Note 17, הרודיס, in <i>Yalqut Šemuel</i> # 124 הרודוס. The last two readings “Herod” seem to be the correct one.</i>. He told him, Naḥum the most holy man<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sanhedrin 10:5:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sanhedrin.10.5.3"><i>Sanhedrin</i> 10:6 Note 335</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Megillah 1:11:2-8" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Megillah.1.11.2-8"><i>Megillah</i> 1:13</a> (72b l. 57) 3:3 (74a l. 39); Babli <i>Šabbat</i> 118b, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 104a" href="/Pesachim.104a"><i>Pesaḥim</i> 104a</a>.</i> passed by it and you do not want to pass by? Pass by it and blind its eye!
Does this<sup class="footnote-marker">264</sup><i class="footnote">The last sentence of the Mishnah, where R. Yose forbids planting anything. Why should the status of wilted leaves be different from broken pieces? It is not so much the statement of R. Joḥanan but the following statement that anything which cannot be repaired is permitted. Wilted leaves certainly cannot be restored.</i> not disagree with Rebbi Joḥanan, since Rebbi Joḥanan said, an idol which was broken is forbidden? And so we are thinking to say if in the future he cannot restore it whole it is permitted according to everybody<sup class="footnote-marker">216</sup><i class="footnote">The statement of R. Simeon ben Laqish and the opposing statement of R. Joḥanan only refer to situations where it is not clear whether the idol can be restored or not.</i>. Explain it about a Jew’s idol<sup class="footnote-marker">265</sup><i class="footnote">Which is permanently forbidden.</i>. Does this not disagree with Rav, since Rav said, for its purpose it is forbidden but its shavings are permitted<sup class="footnote-marker">266</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 49b" href="/Avodah_Zarah.49b">Babli, 49b</a>, this is a <i>baraita</i>: “A Gentile who worked on an idol with a plane, if it was for his benefit it and its shavings are permitted, for its benefit it is forbidden but the shavings are permitted. A Jew who worked on an idol with a plane, whether it was was for his or its benefit, both it and its shavings are forbidden.” Cf. Mishnah and Halakhah 16.</i>? Again explain it about a Jew’s idol. Why does everybody stay away from it<sup class="footnote-marker">267</sup><i class="footnote">Why does nobody ever take advantage of the permission given in the Mishnah to plant vegetables under an <i>Ashera</i> in the winter?</i>? Because its dropping leaves are bad.

Halakhah 12

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If he took wood from it<sup class="footnote-marker">268</sup><i class="footnote">From an <i>Ashera</i> tree.</i>, it is forbidden for usufruct. If he used it to heat an oven<sup class="footnote-marker">269</sup><i class="footnote">A portable earthenware cone.</i>, if it was new it must be destroyed<sup class="footnote-marker">270</sup><i class="footnote">These ovens were not finished in a kiln, they became hardened by being exposed to the fire kindled in them. Therefore a new oven would be finished by the <i>Ashera</i> wood; it must be destroyed.</i>, if it was old it must be cooled down. If he used it to bake bread, it is forbidden for usufruct. If it was mixed with other loaves, all are forbidden for usufruct<sup class="footnote-marker">271</sup><i class="footnote">Since loaves are sold by the piece, no loaf can be considered insignificant if mixed with others. Even without the special case of loaves of bread, “the ashes of everything burned are permitted except ashes coming from idolatry” (<i>‘Orlah</i> 3:3, Note 116).</i>. Rebbi Eliezer says, he should bring his profit to the Dead Sea<sup class="footnote-marker">272</sup><i class="footnote">Where the coins are corroded and destroyed. Obviously R. Eliezer must mean not only the profit but the entire value of the forbidden loaf.</i>. They said to him, idols cannot be redeemed.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If he took wood from it,” etc. Rav Ḥasdai said, they are disagreeing in the indeterminate case. Where do we hold? If to heat, everybody agrees that it is permitted. If to fumigate, everybody agrees that it is forbidden. Where do we hold? In the indeterminate case<sup class="footnote-marker">273</sup><i class="footnote">The Mishnah before R. Ḥasdai was not the text before us. He must have read: “If they (the pieces of wood) were mixed with others, …” Then R. Eliezer and the rabbis disagree about the value created by the wood coming from an <i>Ashera</i>. In this case the argument of Note 271 does not apply. Since idols are destroyed by burning (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Avodah Zarah 3:3:6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Avodah_Zarah.3.3.6">Note 106</a>) it is obvious that their wood may be burned. If it is used to cook or bake anything, the profit comes only after the wood has been reduced to ashes and is no longer forbidden. But in fumigating the burning itself is the valuable act and is forbidden. R. Eliezer permits to bring the profit to the Dead Sea as long as it is not clear that the action was forbidden. The rabbis forbid everything as long as it is not clear that the action is permitted.</i>.

Halakhah 13

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If he took from it [wood to make] a weaver’s shuttle<sup class="footnote-marker">274</sup><i class="footnote">Reading with the Halakhah (and Maimonides’s autograph Mishnah) כרכד, Greek κερκίς, -ίδος.</i>, it is forbidden for usufruct. If he used it to weave cloth the cloth is forbidden for usufruct. If it was mixed up with others, all are forbidden for usufruct<sup class="footnote-marker">275</sup><i class="footnote">Same argument as in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Avodah Zarah 3:12:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Avodah_Zarah.3.12.1">Note 271</a>.</i>. Rebbi Eliezer says, he should bring his profit to the Dead Sea<sup class="footnote-marker">272</sup><i class="footnote">Where the coins are corroded and destroyed. Obviously R. Eliezer must mean not only the profit but the entire value of the forbidden loaf.</i>. They said to him, idols cannot be redeemed.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If he took from it [wood to make] a weaver’s shuttle,” etc. <sup class="footnote-marker">276</sup><i class="footnote">A somewhat truncated version of a passage in <i>ˋOrlah</i>3:1, Notes 77–81.</i> Rebbi Ḥaggai said, when I descended from a ship I found Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa sitting and asked him. “If he took from it [wood to make] a weaver’s shuttle, it is forbidden for usufruct. If he used it to weave cloth the cloth is forbidden for usufruct.” But did we not state<sup class="footnote-marker">277</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah 5:13: “If libation wine (forbidden for usufruct by the idolatrous act) fell into a cistern (of wine), all is forbidden for usufruct. Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel (whom practice follows) says, it should all be sold to a Gentile except for the value of libation wine in it.” Why could the cloth not be sold except for the value of the one forbidden piece?</i>, “it should all be sold to a Gentile except for the value of the libation wine in it”? Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa said, Ḥaggai raised the question, Ḥaggai resolved it. What is it? There people do not buy<sup class="footnote-marker">278</sup><i class="footnote">In a society where Gentiles actually make libations to their gods it practically is impossible for Jews to buy wine from them. Therefore there is no danger that the forbidden wine would end up in the hands of a Jew. The strict rule of Mishnah 15 is not a consequence of the laws of forbidden usufruct but of the necessity to keep the forbidden items out of the hands of Jews.</i> from the Gentile; but here people buy cloth from the Gentile.

Halakhah 14

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> How does one make it insignificant? If he pruned dry branches, green branches, took from it a rod, or a twig, even a leaf, it became in significant<sup class="footnote-marker">279</sup><i class="footnote">Taking anything from a holy tree in a manner which a pagan never would do turns the <i>Ashera</i> into a common tree.</i>. If he planed it for its benefit it is forbidden, not for its benefit it is permitted<sup class="footnote-marker">266</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 49b" href="/Avodah_Zarah.49b">Babli, 49b</a>, this is a <i>baraita</i>: “A Gentile who worked on an idol with a plane, if it was for his benefit it and its shavings are permitted, for its benefit it is forbidden but the shavings are permitted. A Jew who worked on an idol with a plane, whether it was was for his or its benefit, both it and its shavings are forbidden.” Cf. Mishnah and Halakhah 16.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “How does one make it insignificant,” etc Rav said, for his benefit, both it and its shavings are permitted; for its benefit it is forbidden but its shavings are permitted. Samuel said, for its benefit both it and its shavings are forbidden, for his benefit it is forbidden but its shavings are permitted<sup class="footnote-marker">280</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Avodah Zarah 3:14:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Avodah_Zarah.3.14.1">Note 266</a>. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 49b" href="/Avodah_Zarah.49b">Babli, 49b</a>, the opinions given here as those of Rav and Samuel are reported as those of students of Rav.</i>. Rebbi Joḥanan said, for its benefit it is forbidden but its shavings are permitted.

Chapter 4



Halakhah 1

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> Rebbi Ismael said, three stones next to one another and next to a Mercury<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">A Hermes stele, where every passer-by is supposed to add a stone to the heap covering it. Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sanhedrin 5:1:11" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sanhedrin.5.1.11"><i>Sanhedrin</i>5:2 Note 36</a>, Mishnah 7:12. The stele is a phallic symbol (Herodotus II 54).</i> are forbidden, but two are permitted<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">If the stones are separated from the heap they do not belong to it. One starts a new heap with a triangle of stones, not with two stones.</i>. But the Sages say, what is seen with it is forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">Since the additional stones are thrown, even if they are found separately from the mound but near to it one may assume that they were thrown as homage to Mercury.</i>, what is not seen with it is permitted.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “Rebbi Ismael said,” etc. Rebbi Immi said, the reason of Rebbi Ismael is because of a small Mercurius next to a large Mercurius. But the rabbis say, because of the feet of Mercurius. How large are the feet of Mercurius? 50 cubits<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">Within 50 cubits of the center of the heap the stones “are seen with it.” <a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 50a" href="/Avodah_Zarah.50a">Babli 50a</a>.</i>.
The practice of Mercurius is the following: Two stones one touching the other and the third on top of them<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">This does not mean that the third stone lies vertically on top of the other two since this is a very unstable arrangement. It must be that the third stone forms a triangle together with the others; then the third stone is on the altitude of the triangle which is at a right angle to, i. e. “on”, the base line.</i>. If one put down the second one and they warned him because of “it and its young”<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 22:28" href="/Leviticus.22.28"><i>Lev</i>. 22:28</a>. The text is very elliptic here; it is explained by the following quote from <i>Terumot.</i><br>A person is starting to build a rudimentary Mercurius. When he had put down the second stone he decided to sacrifice to the yet unfinished idol and he chose for this purpose an animal and its young. As explained in <i>Sanhedrin</i>, a criminal conviction in rabbinic theory is possible only if criminal intent was proven by the testimony of two eye witnesses that the perpetrator had duly been warned of the criminal nature of his intended act. Also, for one act there can be only one punishment. Slaughtering an animal and its young on the same day is a simple criminal infraction for which no punishment is spelled out in the biblical text. The prescribed punishment for such an act is flogging.</i>, he is whipped. Because of idolatry he is stoned<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">One has to read “is not stoned”. Since two stones do not make an idol, even if there was criminal intent no crime was committed.</i>. If he put down the third<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">Then there is an idol and even though it is worshipped by throwing an additional stone, anything which would be part of the service in the Temple when done for an idol is a capital crime whether or not the idol is worshipped in this way.</i>; there is a disagreement between Rebbi Joḥanan and Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish. <sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">This text to the end of the paragraph is from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Terumot 7:1:13" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Terumot.7.1.13"><i>Terumot</i>7:1 Notes 64–66</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 3:1:2-19" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.3.1.2-19"><i>Ketubot</i>3:1</a> (27c l.21).</i> For they disagreed: If somebody slaughters an animal and its young for idolatrous purposes. Rebbi Joḥanan says, if he was cautioned about an animal and its young he is flogged, about idolatry he is stoned. Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish said, even if he is cautioned about an animal and its young he is not flogged since he would be stoned to death had he be cautioned about idolatry.
What is a Mercurius? Any which borders the ocean or roads<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">Indicating a landing spot on the shore or a road crossing on land.</i>. The practice of Mercurius is by throwing. What if he bowed down before it? Rebbi Yose said, essentially is is called Mercurius only for bowing down, <i>a maskit stone you shall not put up in your land to bow down on it</i><sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 26:1" href="/Leviticus.26.1"><i>Lev</i>. 26:1</a>. The connection to be made between משׂכית and מרקוליס is not clear.</i>. I could think that one may not put two stones next to one another and put his box down on them, the verse says <i>to bow down on it</i>, you do not bow down on it but you put your box down on it; you do not bow down on it but you bow down on the stones of the Temple<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Megillah 22b" href="/Megillah.22b">Babli <i>Megillah</i>22b</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sifra, Behar, Chapter 9 5" href="/Sifra,_Behar,_Chapter_9.5"><i>Sifra Behar Pereq</i>9(5</a>).</i>.
Rav commanded to the family of Rav Aḥa, Rebbi Immi commanded to his own family, if you go out on the fast day<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">Usually, “fast day” is one of the days of penitence called in case of a drought, as described in Tractate <i>Ta`anit</i>. However, since the Babli states (<i>Ta`anit</i>11b) in the name of Rav’s colleague R. Jeremiah bar Abba that this kind of fast day is not practiced in Babylonia, Rav’s instructions to the family of Rav Aḥa can only refer to the Fast Day, the Day of Atonement, where it is customary to prostrate oneself during the recitation of Rav’s composition <i>Alenu</i> at the mention that “we prostrate ourselves” and during the enactment of the Temple ceremony of the day. Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Avodah Zarah 4:1:5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Avodah_Zarah.4.1.5">Note 18</a>.</i> you should not incline normally<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">Face down on the floor. The examples following refer to the daily prayers which morning and afternoon have three parts. The first, the “eighteen benedictions” are said standing, the second, “falling down on one’s face”, is said while bending down, and the third, a recitation of biblical verses, is said sitting up straight. Following the majority opinion here and the unquestioned prescription of the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Megillah 22b" href="/Megillah.22b">Babli (<i>Megillah</i>22b</a>) one may not put down one’s face to the ground but must bend it sideways. This presumes that the synagogue had a stone floor (as shown by the archeological evidence in Galilee.) There would be no need to turn one’s head on a dirt floor or on carpets.</i>. Rebbi Jonah inclined on his side; Rebbi Aḥa inclined on his side. Rebbi Samuel said, I saw Rebbi Abbahu inclining normally. Rebbi Yose said, I pointed out a difficulty before Rebbi Abbahu, is it not written<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 26:1" href="/Leviticus.26.1"><i>Lev</i>. 26:1</a>. The connection to be made between משׂכית and מרקוליס is not clear.</i>, <i>a maskit stone you shall not put up in your land to bow down on it</i>. Explain it if he fixed a place for it. But is it not written<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="II Samuel 15:32" href="/II_Samuel.15.32"><i>2S</i>. 15:32</a>. A “high place” on the Mount of Olives which however had not to be destroyed since there was neither altar nor stone floor.</i>, <i>it was when David arrived at the mountain</i><i>top where one bows down before God</i>? Except bowing down which is not on the ground. Does there exist bowing down which is not on the ground? <i>They fell down with their faces to the ground and bowed down</i><sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="II Chronicles 7:3" href="/II_Chronicles.7.3"><i>2Chr.</i>7:3</a>.</i>. Rebbi Abbahu added up to <i>give thanks to the Eternal for He is good;</i> Rebbi Mana added up to <i>for eternal is His kindness to Israel</i><sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">The additions of RR. Abahu and Mana are in the verse, except for the addition “on Israel” which are not in this verse nor in any other biblical occurrence of the formula <i>give thanks to the Eternal for He is good</i>, <i>for eternal is His kindness.</i></i>. Rebbi Joḥanan said to Rebbi Ḥiyya bar Abba: Babylonian, two things came from you, the stretching out on the Fast Day<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">Prostrating oneself with outstretched arms and legs. Since the only day one did this in Babylonia was the day of Atonement, it must refer to that day.</i>, and the willow of the Seventh Day<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">Hitting the floor with a bunch of willow twigs on the Seventh Day of the Festival of Tabernacles; an ancient custom to induce ample rains in the coming winter.</i>. The rabbis of Caesarea say, also this blood letting<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">Traditions of good and bad days for blood letting.</i>. It follows what Rebbi Immi said, the Babylonians in the name of the rabbis there: They permitted prostrating only on a public fast, and only on the side<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">To avoid violating <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 26:1" href="/Leviticus.26.1"><i>Lev.</i>26:1</a>. These now are Babylonian instructions for the Palestinian fast days for rain which were not practiced in Babylonia.</i>. The younger Rebbi Yannai in the name of his fathers: Anybody who is not qualified like Joshua that if he falls on his face the Holy One, praise to Him, would tell him, <i>get up</i><sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Joshua 7:10" href="/Joshua.7.10"><i>Jos.</i>7:10</a>.</i>, should not fall down, in the case of an individual [praying] for the community<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">An individual praying in public for the relief of a public calamity may not prostrate himself unless he claims for himself a status at least equal to Joshua’s. A private person praying for private needs may prostrate himself as much and as long as he feels necessary. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Megillah 22b" href="/Megillah.22b">Babli <i>Megillah</i>22b</a> in the name of R. Eleazar.</i>.
“But the Sages say, what is seen with it is forbidden, what is not seen with it is permitted.” (What is seen with it, its body, is forbidden; what is not seen with it is permitted.)<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">The first time (in parentheses) the scribe mixed the Mishnah text with the intended explanation. The second time then he wrote correctly.</i> What is seen with it<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">Within 50 cubits of the center of the heap the stones “are seen with it.” <a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 50a" href="/Avodah_Zarah.50a">Babli 50a</a>.</i> is its body, what is not seen with it is not its body. <sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">A quote from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Avodah Zarah 3:6:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Avodah_Zarah.3.6.4">Chapter 3, Notes 215</a> ff.</i> Does this not disagree with Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish since Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish said, an idol which was broken is permitted? And so we are thinking to say if in the future he can restore it whole it is permitted<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">It is clear that one has to read with the text in 3:2 “forbidden”. The argument goes as follows: Since the stones lying around a Mercurius are just pebbles, should they not be considered shards? The answer is that the pebbles can easily be put back on the mound; therefore they are forbidden.</i> according to everybody. <sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">A quote from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Avodah Zarah 3:6:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Avodah_Zarah.3.6.4">Chapter 3, Notes 215</a> ff.</i> Does this not disagree with Rebbi Joḥanan, as Rebbi Joḥanan said, an idol which was broken is forbidden? And so we are thinking to say if in the future he cannot restore it whole it is permitted according to everybody. <sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Avodah Zarah 3:2:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Avodah_Zarah.3.2.4">Chapter 3, Note 71</a>.</i>
But Rebbi Yudan the father of Rebbi Mattaniah said, if they were lying on their spot it is as if he would return it to its wholeness. And these are at their place<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">Therefore they are forbidden according to everybody.</i>.
Rebbi Abba in the name of Rav: Sometimes you are saying, broken pieces of idols cannot be nullified. And sometimes you are saying, serving pieces of idols cannot be nullified<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">Both versions are reported as to be stated by Rav.</i> He who says serving pieces, certainly idols. But he who says idols, therefore not serving pieces. Rebbi Samuel, Rebbi Abbahu in the name of Rav: Mercurius stones which were scattered never can be nullified, because of worship of idols<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">Since the worship of Mercurius is by throwing a stone on the heap, all stones (except the core which formed it in the first place) are offerings to the idol which are permanently and irrevocably forbidden.</i>. Rebbi Joḥanan heard it and said, our teacher taught us well, since if one bows down before an idol of (mourners)<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">A scribal error. Instead of שלאבלים read שֶׁלָּאֹכְלִים “of food”.</i> they never can be nullified.
How does one nullify it? Rebbi Ḥiyya bar Ada said, by spit<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">By having a Gentile spit on some of the core stones.</i>. But did not Rebbi Ḥiyya state: Scattered stones of Mercurius have no nullification? Rebbi Phineas said, here those which where thrown at it; there those which were not thrown at it<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">As noted before, the original mound can be disassembled and its stones used; the accretions by passers-by cannot.</i>. As this: Rebbi Simeon ben Rebbi had a Mercurius on his field. He came to his <i>burgonar</i><sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">Probably a dweller or supervisor of a <i>burgus,</i>πυργός “tower”; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Avodah Zarah 1:9:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Avodah_Zarah.1.9.3">Chapter 1, Note 229</a>. The conjecture of M. Sokoloff, “tenant farmer” based on an interpretation of D. Sperber (<i>Essays on Greek and Latin in the Mishna, Talmud and Midrashic Literature</i>, Jerusalem 1982) of πυργός as “farm house” is not convincing. Also compare Greek πυρκαεύς. “fire-kindler” (E. G.).</i> and told him, since I heard that the magistrate<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">Greek ἄρχων.</i> wants to pass by here tomorrow, by your life lift these stones. After he<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>burgonar</i>.</i> had lifted them, he wanted to take them away. He<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">R. Simeon ben Rebbi.</i> told him, they are mine. Rebbi Ḥiyya bar Abba heard this and said, did not the Elder Rebbi Ḥiyya state this<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">As noted before, the original mound can be disassembled and its stones used; the accretions by passers-by cannot.</i>? But since he had heard it from him, he reinforced<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">R. H.I.D. Azulay reports (ככר לאדן) a ms. reading חזר “he repeated”.</i> and fixed it<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">He publicized what he had done in order to fix practice following R. Hiyya in the interpretation of R. Phineas.</i>.

Halakhah 2

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If one found on top of it<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">Of a Mercurius mound.</i> coins, garments, or vessels, they are permitted<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">Since only stones are offerings to the Mercurius. There is a dissenting opinion in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Avodah Zarah 7:6" href="/Tosefta_Avodah_Zarah.7.6">Tosephta 6:13</a> which forbids coins found on the top stone.</i>. Branches of vine<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">Greek φραγέλλιον “twig”.</i>, wreaths of ears of grain, wines, oils, and fine flours, and anything similar to what is offered on the altar<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">Anything of a kind acceptable for the altar in the Temple is forbidden if offered to any pagan deity even if the latter is not usually worshipped in this way. Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sanhedrin 7:9:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sanhedrin.7.9.1"><i>Sanhedrin</i>7:11, Note 239</a>.</i> is forbidden.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If one found coins on top of it,” etc. Rebbi Jonathan said, not only wreaths of ears of grain but even wreaths of roses<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">Which are forbidden as ornaments and also because they are used in Isis worship.</i>. Rebbi Yose said, not only a Mercurius and on top of it coins but even any pagan place which has no lattice work<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">Which separates the area reserved for priests from that of common worshippers, where everything is forbidden as appurtenances of idols (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Avodah Zarah 2:3:23" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Avodah_Zarah.2.3.23">Chapter 2 Note 219</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Avodah Zarah 3:5:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Avodah_Zarah.3.5.3">Chapter 3 Note 132</a>). If no such separation exists, nothing is reserved for the idol except what is on it. The Babli has the opposite rule, 51b.</i> and he found in it garments or vessels, they are permitted.

Halakhah 3

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If an idol had a garden or a bath house, one may benefit from them not with a favor<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">One does “a favor” either by paying or by incurring a debt of gratitude, i. e., the other party accumulates goodwill which obliges one to reciprocate on occasion.</i> but one may not benefit with a favor. If it belongs to it and a third party one benefits whether with or without a favor<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">Since one may credit the third party with either income or goodwill. As implied by the Halakhah, direct payments to a pagan temple are always excluded.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If an idol had a garden,” etc. So is the Mishnah: A favor to the priests or without a favor to the priests<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 51b" href="/Avodah_Zarah.51b">Babli 51b</a>.</i>. <sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Avodah Zarah 7:1" href="/Tosefta_Avodah_Zarah.7.1">Tosephta 6:1</a>.</i>“It is forbidden to sell<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">In the Tosephta: “to use for dirges.” Since a rental fee is mentioned, “rent” is the reading which is required here also.</i> fifes of idols, but if one pays the rental fee to the state if it is used to supply the needs of idol worship it is permitted to sell<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">In the Tosephta: “to use for dirges.” Since a rental fee is mentioned, “rent” is the reading which is required here also.</i> them<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">As stated at the end of the Halakhah, since the state will take its cut and not spend all the money for idolatry, one may suggest that the Jew’s money is income for the state. This is valid for all cases in the Tosephta.</i>. It is forbidden to rent a store from a pagan temple, but if one pays rent to the state even if it is used to supply the needs of idol worship it is permitted to rent it. It is forbidden to give to collectors for idol worship. If he pays a fee to the state even though he collects for idol worship it is permitted to give to him.” And the Mishnah said so, “if it belongs to it and a third party one benefits whether with or without a favor.”

Halakhah 4

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> A Gentile’s idol is immediately forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">Immediately when it was made since its manufacture is part of its worship.</i> but a Jew’s only when it is worshipped<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">Since he is not supposed to do it there is a chance that he will not worship it.</i>. A Gentile is able to annul his idol and that of a Jew; but a Jew cannot annul the idol of a Gentile<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">“To annul” an idol is to treat it in a way inconsistent with veneration, such as breaking off a piece. If the Gentile does this he transforms the idol into a piece of art which is permitted for usufruct. A Jew can do this to his own idol as part of his repentance but since he is under the obligation to destroy pagan idols without obtaining any gain he cannot transform Gentile objects forbidden for usufruct into those permitted for usufruct.</i>. If one annulled an idol he annulled its appurtenances; if he annulled its appurtenances the appurtenances are annulled but it is forbidden.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “A Gentile’s idol is immediately forbidden,” etc. Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish said, the Mishnah speaks about an artist who works to sell it on the market. When he finishes it, it is a sure thing that he bows down before it. Rebbi Yose asked, if it is a sure thing that he bows down before it, why did we state “a Gentile’s idol is immediately forbidden but a Jew’s only when it is worshipped<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">If he works for the trade and an idol is worth more if it was worshipped then there is no reason to exempt a Jewish-made idol from immediate prohibition.</i>”?
Rebbi Hila in the name of Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish: If one cuts an idol in stone, even though you say that nothing connected to the ground is forbidden, one warns him on every single chiseling and when it will be separated from the rock he will be flogged<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">While in modern Hebrew קורנס is a sledge hammer, and some dictionaries define it as a mallet, neither etymology nor meaning are clear. (In Arabic قرناس is “the back of an axe, mountain ridge”.) The sentence is not intelligible, but the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 19b" href="/Avodah_Zarah.19b">Babli (19b</a>) seems to disagree since it notes that an incomplete idol will not be worshipped and therefore has no commercial value in the idol trade; therefore it is the last application of the hammer or chisel which confers the status of idol.</i>. Rebbi Yose said, from this you understand that if somebody makes a statue for idol worship, even though you say that nothing breathing can be forbidden, one warns him about each single mallet<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote">It is not a logical consequence. The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Avodah Zarah 6:2" href="/Tosefta_Avodah_Zarah.6.2">Tosephta, 5:3</a>, notes that it is a logical consequence of the opposite opinion. If the idol made by a Jew is immediately forbidden even if he did not worship it then it follows that he cannot annul it (except maybe by melting it down) but the Gentile whose idol becomes forbidden only if worshipped can annul an idol by an act of non-worship.</i> and he will be flogged when it is finished<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 12:20" href="/Deuteronomy.12.20"><i>Deut</i>. 12:20</a>.</i>.
“A Gentile’s idol is immediately forbidden,” therefore it can be annulled. “But a Jew’s only when it is worshipped,” therefore it cannot be annulled. Rebbi Ze`ira said, there is no “therefore”<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote">It is not a logical consequence. The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Avodah Zarah 6:2" href="/Tosefta_Avodah_Zarah.6.2">Tosephta, 5:3</a>, notes that it is a logical consequence of the opposite opinion. If the idol made by a Jew is immediately forbidden even if he did not worship it then it follows that he cannot annul it (except maybe by melting it down) but the Gentile whose idol becomes forbidden only if worshipped can annul an idol by an act of non-worship.</i>, only “it cannot be annulled or it can be annulled”. “A Gentile’s idol is immediately forbidden,” as it is written<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 12:20" href="/Deuteronomy.12.20"><i>Deut</i>. 12:20</a>.</i>: <i>you certainly shall destroy.</i>“But a Jew’s only when it is worshipped,” as it is written<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 27:15" href="/Deuteronomy.27.15"><i>Deut</i>. 27:15</a>. The argument is from the later part of the verse, <i>cursed be the man who would make a hewn or molten image and put it up in secret</i>. As long as it is not put up there is no curse. The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 52a" href="/Avodah_Zarah.52a">Babli, 52a</a>, reads the verse as stating that the curse applies when the idol is made.</i>, <i>cursed be the man who makes a hewn or molten image</i>, etc., when he puts it up. But some are switching this<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Avodah Zarah 6:2" href="/Tosefta_Avodah_Zarah.6.2">Tosephta 5:4</a>.</i>. “A Jew’s idol is immediately forbidden,” for it is written, <i>cursed be the man</i>. “But a Gentile’s only when it was worshipped,” as it is written<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 12:20" href="/Deuteronomy.12.20"><i>Deut</i>. 12:20</a>.</i>, <i>you certainly shall destroy all the places where these peoples worshipped</i>. Rebbi Isaac bar Naḥman in the name of Samuel understood it from the following: If you inherited it when it was a god you shall burn it in fire<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote">This probably should be a quote of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 7:25" href="/Deuteronomy.7.25"><i>Deut</i>. 7:25</a>.</i>, otherwise <i>of which you will inherit their gods</i><sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote">The full verse reads: <i>you certainly shall destroy all the places where these peoples worshipped, whom you are going to inherit, their gods</i>. By taking only a fraction of the verse it is turned into permission to inherit the statues of the gods. This implies that if the Gentiles do not consider them as gods any more, the prohibition of usufruct is lifted.</i>. Rebbi Joḥanan in the name of Rebbi Yannai understood it from the following: <i>You should not covet silver or gold for them</i><sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote">` <i>Deut</i>. 8:25. If the Gentiles covet their silver and gold, you may take it. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 52a" href="/Avodah_Zarah.52a">Babli, 52a</a>, is a similar argument. Samuel reads the verse, <i>do not covet silver or gold for them and take for yourself</i> as: <i>you may not covet silver or gold for them but you may be permitted to take for yourself.</i></i>. You cannot covet and take, but others can covet and you are taking.
Rebbi Joḥanan said to Bar Drosai, go down and break all those statues in the public baths<sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote">Greek δημόσιον (βαλανεῖον) public (government run) bath.</i>. He went down and broke them all except one<sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote">Greek παρά. For this use of the word cf. E. and H. Guggenheimer, <i>On the Talmudic Vocabulary</i>12–15, Lešonenu 39(1975) pp. 62–69.</i>. Why this? Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun said, because another<sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote">One might read אחד “one” instead of אחר “another.”</i> Jew was suspected of burning incense for it.
Rebbi Ḥiyya bar Ashi in the name of Rav: <sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 52b" href="/Avodah_Zarah.52b">Babli 52b</a>, where, however, it is accepted that Rebbi in his advanced age changed his opinion.</i> Rebbi was sitting and teaching his son<sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote">Reading בְּרֵהּ “his son” instead of inappropriate בָּמָה “height, altar” or בַּמֶּה “why?”.</i> Rebbi Simeon. The Gentile annuls his own idol and that of his neighbor. He said to him, when you still were in full mental powers you quoted Tannaim stating that a Gentile annuls his own idol and that of a Jew. He answered him, no, my son. An idol which a Jew had worshipped can never be annulled. And it was stated so: “Rebbi Simeon ben Menassia says, an idol which a Jew had worshipped can never be annulled.”<sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 53a" href="/Avodah_Zarah.53a">Babli 53a</a>; as anonymous text <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Avodah Zarah 6:2" href="/Tosefta_Avodah_Zarah.6.2">Tosephta 5:3</a>.</i> Rav stated in the name of <sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote">A name is missing here.</i> the reason of this Tanna: <i>Cursed be the man who makes a hewn or molten image</i>, forever<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 27:15" href="/Deuteronomy.27.15"><i>Deut</i>. 27:15</a>. The argument is from the later part of the verse, <i>cursed be the man who would make a hewn or molten image and put it up in secret</i>. As long as it is not put up there is no curse. The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 52a" href="/Avodah_Zarah.52a">Babli, 52a</a>, reads the verse as stating that the curse applies when the idol is made.</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote">Another version of the same story is in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 43a" href="/Avodah_Zarah.43a">Babli, 43a</a>, leading to essentially the same conclusions.</i> Bar Qappara found a ring with an idol. An Aramean youth was running after him. He was hitting him<sup class="footnote-marker">73</sup><i class="footnote">Bar Qappara the Gentile youth.</i>, cursing him, and told him, spit on it! He did not accept. Urinate on it! He did not accept. This implies that a Non-Jew annuls his own and another person’s idols under duress, on condition that he knows the nature of this idol. An idol which had been worshipped by a Jew can never be annulled<sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote">This sentence does not seem to fit into the context here. It is not totally excluded that the translation should be: An idol which had been worshipped, a Jew can never annul.</i>. Could it not be like an idol which was abandoned by its worshippers and by that being annulled<sup class="footnote-marker">75</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah 6.</i>? Rebbi Ze`ira said, anything which becomes annulled by itself, a Jew can annul. But anything which does not become annulled by itself, a Jew cannot annul<sup class="footnote-marker">76</sup><i class="footnote">If the ring had been abandoned, by this fact it would become permitted for usufruct. Then the Jew could eliminate the idolatry aspect either by defacing it or by melting it down. But if the ring was lost and the owner did not realize his loss, he could not abandon it and it remains forbidden for all usufruct unless a Gentile treat it with contempt.</i>.
Rav said, a pedestal<sup class="footnote-marker">77</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Avodah Zarah 3:8:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Avodah_Zarah.3.8.1">Chapter 3:10, Note 231</a>.</i> is not annulled<sup class="footnote-marker">78</sup><i class="footnote">If the pedestal was made especially for an idol, removing the idol does not automatically make the pedestal permitted for usufruct. This implies that the pedestal is treated not as an appurtenance of the idol but as a separate idol.</i>. Therefore if one annulled it it is annulled<sup class="footnote-marker">79</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 53b" href="/Avodah_Zarah.53b">Babli, 53b</a>, this is the opinion of the Galilean authorities R. Joḥanan and R. Simeon ben Laqish, opposed by Rav and Samuel.</i>. The opinions of Rav seem inconsistent. There he says<sup class="footnote-marker">80</sup><i class="footnote">Halakhah 3:10. Since Rav deduces from the statement of the Mishnah about stones used for a pedestal rules about appurtenances of idols it follows that he treats the pedestal as an appurtenance.</i>, if one casts a cup for idolatry it is immediately forbidden, and here he says so? Here if he burned incense, there if he did not burn incense<sup class="footnote-marker">81</sup><i class="footnote">A pedestal which was not used as an altar is an appurtenance; if it was used as an altar it is an idol.</i>. And even if you are saying, in both cases if he burned incense, Rav follows his opinion since Rav said, if one casts a cup for idolatry it is immediately forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">82</sup><i class="footnote">Obviously this quote cannot be correct since it was the basis of the question raised; one must refer here to the statement of R. Abba in the name of Rav in Halakhah 3:10: “This implies that what is cast for idolatry is immediately forbidden.” The difference is whether the stone was quarried or the tree felled for the purpose of building a pedestal for idolatry or whether the material was bought on the market.</i>.
Rav said, a pedestal is not annulled. Therefore its appurtenances are annulled. The opinions of Rav seem inconsistent. There<sup class="footnote-marker">83</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to Mishnah 6, pedestal erected by the Augustales, the priests of the worship of the Emperor, for a visit of the Emperor in the region. If the Emperor did not pass by a certain pedestal it is permitted without being disassembled. The statement of Rav Sheshet is not otherwise recorded. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 53b" href="/Avodah_Zarah.53b">Babli 53b</a>.</i> Rav Sheshet said in the name of Rav, if the procession was using another way, and here he says so? There if he burned incense, here if he did not burn incense. And even if you are saying, in both cases if he burned incense, Rav follows his opinion since Rav said, if one casts a cup for idolatry it is immediately forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">82</sup><i class="footnote">Obviously this quote cannot be correct since it was the basis of the question raised; one must refer here to the statement of R. Abba in the name of Rav in Halakhah 3:10: “This implies that what is cast for idolatry is immediately forbidden.” The difference is whether the stone was quarried or the tree felled for the purpose of building a pedestal for idolatry or whether the material was bought on the market.</i>.
Rebbi Joḥanan said, a stele is a monolith, an altar has many stones<sup class="footnote-marker">84</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 53b" href="/Avodah_Zarah.53b">Babli 53b</a>. In the Babli, instead of <i>maṣseba</i> one uses βωμός, not consistent with the use of the term in the Yerushalmi.. This change in terminology is not usually recognized in the talmudic Dictionaries.</i>. Ḥizqiah said, a stele is annulled once it is damaged; of an altar one has to damage each single stone. A <i>baraita</i> of Ḥizqiah disagrees with him: If you tore down the altar, leave it alone; if you broke the stele, leave it alone <sup class="footnote-marker">85</sup><i class="footnote">The terminology is taken from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 12:3" href="/Deuteronomy.12.3"><i>Deut</i>. 12:3</a>.</i>. Rebbi Ze`ira, Rebbi Isaac bar Naḥman in the name of Rebbi Hoshaia; Rebbi Ḥiyya (Rebbi)<sup class="footnote-marker">86</sup><i class="footnote">Read בר for ר׳. The tradent is R. Ḥiyya bar Abba.</i> Abba, Rebbi Eleazar in the name of Rav Hoshaia: <i>When He turns all stones of the altar like smashed pieces of chalk</i><sup class="footnote-marker">87</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Isaiah 27:9" href="/Isaiah.27.9"><i>Is</i>. 27:9</a>. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 54a" href="/Avodah_Zarah.54a">Babli, 54a</a>, this is Ḥizqiah’s <i>baraita</i>; probably here also this is the statement which contradicts his earlier statement that simple damage, not complete annihilation, permits <i>maṣsebot</i> and altars for usufruct.</i>, persecute them until you eliminated its egg from the world. But here, if you broke a stele, leave it alone; if you tore down an altar, leave it alone<sup class="footnote-marker">88</sup><i class="footnote">Divine vengeance is different from rules prescribed for humans.</i>.
It is written<sup class="footnote-marker">89</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 26:1" href="/Leviticus.26.1"><i>Lev</i>. 26:1</a>.</i>, <i>do not make idols for yourselves, statue and stele you shall not erect for yourselves.</i> Is not making the same as erecting? Rebbi Hila said, making is from new; erecting, if it falls down one shall not re-erect it. It is written<sup class="footnote-marker">90</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 12:3" href="/Deuteronomy.12.3"><i>Deut</i>. 12:3</a>. The verse continues, <i>their statues you shall cut down.</i></i>, <i>you shall tear down their altars and break their steles.</i> From where to apply one to the other? Rebbi Abun bar Ḥiyya said, it was said to refer to both sides; either breaking, or cutting down, or tearing down for each of them.

Halakhah 5

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> How does one annul it<sup class="footnote-marker">91</sup><i class="footnote">An idol or one of its appurtenances.</i>? If he cut off the top of its earlobe, the tip of its nose, the tip of its finger. If he disfigured it even though nothing is missing<sup class="footnote-marker">92</sup><i class="footnote">E. g., by denting the statue with a hammer so that nothing is missing.</i>, he annulled it. If he spat in its face, urinated before it, dragged it, threw excrement on it, this is not annulled.<sup class="footnote-marker">93</sup><i class="footnote">Only permanent damage is acceptable.</i> If he sold or mortgaged it, Rebbi says it is annulled, but the Sages say it is not annulled.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “How does one annul it,” etc? Rebbi Ze`ira said, this is if he sold it in peace<sup class="footnote-marker">93</sup><i class="footnote">Only permanent damage is acceptable.</i>. But if he sold it in ill-will, everybody agrees that it is annulled; <i>it happens that he is hungry, falls into a rage, and curses his king</i><sup class="footnote-marker">94</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Isaiah 8:21" href="/Isaiah.8.21"><i>Is</i>. 8:21</a>. The Babli uses the same verse as a reason for which a contemptuous treatment of an idol is not an annulment; 53a.</i> etc. Ze`ur bar Ḥinena in the name of Rebbi Ḥanina: They disagreed in case he sold it to a goldsmith<sup class="footnote-marker">95</sup><i class="footnote">The disagreement between R. Joḥanan and Rav is formulated in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 53a" href="/Avodah_Zarah.53a">Babli (53a</a>) about whether the idol is sold to a Jewish or a Gentile goldsmith, without exact attribution of authorship.</i>. But if he sold it to its worshipper, everybody agrees that it is not annulled. Rav Jeremiah in the name of Rav: they disagreed if he sold it to its worshippers. But if he sold it to a goldsmith everybody agrees that it is annulled. Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: the opinion of everybody. Rebbi Hila in the name of Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish: in dispute<sup class="footnote-marker">96</sup><i class="footnote">It is not spelled out what they are referring to. The next sentence makes this clear.</i>. It turns out that Rebbi Ḥanina held with Rebbi Joḥanan and Rav Jeremiah with Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish. What about it? They disagreed in case he sold it to a goldsmith. But if he sold it to one of its worshippers, everybody agrees that it is not annulled<sup class="footnote-marker">97</sup><i class="footnote">The decision follows R, Joḥanan and the goldsmith must be a Jew who will melt down the idol.</i>.

Halakhah 6

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> An idol which was abandoned by its worshippers in times of peace is permitted, in times of war is forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">97a</sup><i class="footnote">Since there is no indication that the idol was permanently abandoned.</i>. Pedestals of kings are permitted since they put them up when the kings pass by<sup class="footnote-marker">98</sup><i class="footnote">This is a temporary situation; as soon as the Emperor has departed, the pedestal has lost its mission; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Avodah Zarah 4:4:9" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Avodah_Zarah.4.4.9">Note 83</a>.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “An idol which was abandoned by its worshippers,” etc. It was stated: The war of Joshua is forbidden, similar to the war of Joshua is permitted. The war of David is permitted, like the war of David is forbidden. The war of Joshua is forbidden, as it is written<sup class="footnote-marker">99</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 20:17" href="/Deuteronomy.20.17"><i>Deut</i>. 20:17</a>. This verse is quoted as justification of the first statement, that idols captured in the wars of Joshua were forbidden.</i>, <i>for certainly you should ban them</i>. Similar to the war of Joshua is permitted, for it is a short time<sup class="footnote-marker">100</sup><i class="footnote">Idols conquered in a war of limited duration are temporarily forbidden until it is ascertained after the war that no worshippers returned, as in the war of Joshua where in theory all Canaanites were killed (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 20:16" href="/Deuteronomy.20.16"><i>Deut</i>. 20:16</a>).</i>. The war of David is permitted, what Itai from Gat annulled<sup class="footnote-marker">101</sup><i class="footnote">Since David had Gentile mercenaries in his army, they immediately could annul all idols and articles of pagan worship which then became of use (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Avodah Zarah 3:3:6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Avodah_Zarah.3.3.6">Chapter 3:3 Note 108</a>).</i>. Like the war of David is forbidden, for it is permanent<sup class="footnote-marker">102</sup><i class="footnote">If a war is not ended with a peace but with an indefinite truce, the preceding arguments are not applicable.</i>.

Halakhah 7

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> The Elders were asked in Rome, if He does not approve of idol worship, why does He not eliminate it? They answered, if they would worship something which the world does not need, He would eliminate it. But they worship sun and moon, stars and planets, mountains and hills. Should He destroy His world because of the stupid? They told them, if it is so, He should eliminate things which are not needed in the world, and keep things necessary for the world. They answered, then we would strengthen the worshippers of the latter who would say, you should know that these are divinities since those were eliminated and these were not eliminated.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “The Elders were asked in Rome,” etc. If somebody stole seeds and sowed, would it not grow in the end? If somebody sleeps with a married woman, would he not in the end cause a bastard to be born? But let the world continue its usual way. The stupid who misbehave will in the future have to account for their deeds.
Rebbi Ze`ira said, if it were written, “like them should be their worshippers”, that would be difficult; the sun’s worshippers like the sun, the moon’s worshippers like the moon<sup class="footnote-marker">103</sup><i class="footnote">To compare people to sun and moon would be praise.</i>? But, <i>like them should be their makers</i><sup class="footnote-marker">104</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Psalms 115:8" href="/Psalms.115.8"><i>Ps</i>. 115:8</a>. This clearly refers to idols and their makers.</i>. Rebbi Mana said, if it were written, “like them should be their worshippers”, it would not be difficult, as it is written<sup class="footnote-marker">105</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Isaiah 24:23" href="/Isaiah.24.23"><i>Is</i>. 24:23</a>.</i>, <i>the moon will be ashamed and the sun abashed</i>. Rebbi Naḥman in the name of Rebbi Mana: In the future, an idol will come, spit in the face of its worshippers, make them ashamed, and disappear from the world. What is the reason? <i>All worshippers of statues will be ashamed</i><sup class="footnote-marker">106</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Psalms 97:7" href="/Psalms.97.7"><i>Ps.</i>97:7</a>. This homily ends the part of the Tractate devoted to idols.</i>. Rebbi Naḥman in the name of Rebbi Mana: In the future, an idol will come, bow down before the Holy One, praise to Him, and disappear from the world. What is the reason? <i>Bow down before Him, all gods</i><sup class="footnote-marker">106</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Psalms 97:7" href="/Psalms.97.7"><i>Ps.</i>97:7</a>. This homily ends the part of the Tractate devoted to idols.</i><i>.</i>

Halakhah 8

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> One may buy a used wine-press from a Non-Jew<sup class="footnote-marker">107</sup><i class="footnote">Since it is stated later in the Mishnah that grape juice becomes wine only when it is collected in the cistern below the wine press, a wine press used by a Gentile does not have to be cleansed from the remainders of the Gentile’s pressing before being used by the Jew to make kosher wine.</i>. Even if he<sup class="footnote-marker">108</sup><i class="footnote">This now starts a new topic. The Gentile may help the Jew to load the grapes into the wine press even if the grapes are already oozing juice..</i> takes it in his hands and puts it on the pile<sup class="footnote-marker">109</sup><i class="footnote">“The apple” or “the swollen”. Any heap of organic material is called by this expression, so also the heap of ashes at the center of the altar in the Temple courtyard. Here it denotes the heap of grapes to be pressed.</i>. It does not become libation wine until it flows down into the cistern. If it flowed into the cistern<sup class="footnote-marker">110</sup><i class="footnote">The place where the juice flowing from the wine press is collected. As Rashi points out, European wine presses are not built over cisterns; there the place of the cistern is taken by the barrel into which the juice is collected.<br>If the Gentile touches the fluid in the cistern, the contents of the cistern become forbidden, the fluid still in the press is permitted. The status of the fluid in the pipe leading from press to cistern has to be discussed in the Halakhah.</i>, what is in the cistern is forbidden and the remainder in permitted.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “One may buy a used wine-press,” etc. Rebbi Ḥanin stated: Only if the Jew did not turn his eyes away from him<sup class="footnote-marker">111</sup><i class="footnote">The Gentile can help in preparing the harvest for pressing only if at all times a Jew sees him so one is sure that he does not touch the cistern or any storage barrels of grape juice. Quoted in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosafot on Avodah Zarah 55b" href="/Tosafot_on_Avodah_Zarah.55b">Tosaphot 55b</a>, Roqeaḥ #495, Raviah # 1096, Mordekhai #845, Rosh (Note 113).</i>. But if he turned his eyes away from him, not then. So far if he<sup class="footnote-marker">112</sup><i class="footnote">The Gentile.</i> was taking juice and grapes together to put on the heap. If he gave these and those separately, (even from what was left in the fissures, what is under the bunches is forbidden, on the sides is permitted.)<sup class="footnote-marker">113</sup><i class="footnote">The reading of Rosh (<i>Avodah zarah</i>4, #50) in the version of R. Lipman Heller Wallerstein and R. ḤID Azulai seems preferable:<br>הָיָה נוֹתֵן אֵילּוּ בִפְנֵי עַצְמָן וְאֵילּוּ בִפְנֵי עַצְמָן אֲפִילוּ <i>כֵן</i><br>מוּתָּר. גוֹמָמִיוֹת שֶׁתַּחַת הָאֶשְׁכּוֹלוֹת <i>מוּתָּרוֹת.</i><br>שֶׁבְּצִידֵּיהֶן הֵן <i>אֲסוּרוֹת.</i><br>“If he gave these and those separately, <i>even so it is permitted</i>. The fissures under the bunches are <i>permitted</i>, on the sides they are <i>forbidden</i>.”<br>If the grapes are harvested into a vat, if the entire vat is emptied into the wine press one does not care if juice already oozed from the grapes since this is part of the pressing process. If the grapes are taken out of the vat and then the fluid is poured into the press separately, this also is acceptable. But if then some fluid is left in the fissures between the wooden planks which form the walls of the vat, it is grape juice in a separate vessel which is no different from a barrel standing under the pipe through which the juice flows from press to cistern; any touch by the Gentile will turn it into forbidden libation wine.</i> Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: Itself it becomes libation wine<sup class="footnote-marker">114</sup><i class="footnote">He objects to making a difference between places where some juice is left; any juice left in the vat has the same status as juice in the cistern.</i>.
A wine press on which a Gentile stopped a leak from the inside is forbidden, from the outside permitted, for it is impossible that there not be wet bast where he touches and turns the entire cistern into libation wine<sup class="footnote-marker">115</sup><i class="footnote">If the wine press were completely dry, there would be no difference whether the Gentile plugs the leak from the inside or the outside. But if it is moist, the bast which he uses to plug the leak retains some wine which then turns the wine press into a vessel like vat and cisterns, and the Gentile’s touch forbids the wine. But from the outside it never can be considered inside a vessel.</i>.
Rav Huna in the name of Rav: The jet is like the cistern<sup class="footnote-marker">116</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 55b" href="/Avodah_Zarah.55b">Babli 55b</a>. The case of the conduit from wine press to cistern which was not treated in the Mishnah is decided: The moment when a drop of grape juice passes the filter at the bottom of the press and enters the pipe leading to the cistern it turns into wine.</i>. Rebbi Ze`ira asked: Everywhere<sup class="footnote-marker">117</sup><i class="footnote">The basic text is <a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Tahorot 8:8" href="/Mishnah_Tahorot.8.8">Mishnah <i>Ṭahorot</i>8:8</a> which states that any flow downwards is no connection for impurity while standing fluid on a level surface is. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 72a" href="/Avodah_Zarah.72a">Babli 72a</a>.</i> you do not say that flow is connection, but here you are treating flow as connection? Rav Huna is of the opinion that if the cistern became libation wine, the flow became libation wine<sup class="footnote-marker">118</sup><i class="footnote">In these matters, any defect is transferred upwards but not downwards.</i>; if the flow became libation wine the cistern did not become libation wine<sup class="footnote-marker">119</sup><i class="footnote">If the flow was cut off and prevented from reaching the cistern.</i>. Rebbi Abba did not say so, but if the cistern became libation wine, the flow did not become libation wine; if the flow became libation wine the cistern became libation wine. What Rav Huna said in the name of Rav, the jet is like the cistern, for Rav Huna is of the opinion, just as the cistern becomes libation wine so the jet becomes libation wine. There, they say in the name of Rav, the touch of a Gentile makes libation wine. Rav Naḥman bar Jacob said, only when it is flowing<sup class="footnote-marker">120</sup><i class="footnote">A libation consists of shaking the wine as an homage to the gods. Therefore if the touch of the Gentile did not move the wine from its position at rest, no libation was made.</i>.
An Aramean fell into a cavity. The case came before Rebbi Huna who said, immobilize him<sup class="footnote-marker">121</sup><i class="footnote">Since libation wine is created by a Gentile moving the wine, the Non-Jew has to be immobilized until the cistern is emptied.</i> until you have emptied the cavity. Rebbi Ḥanina said, he is moving his hand. He said, bring willow baskets and approach from under his hand<sup class="footnote-marker">122</sup><i class="footnote">Hebrew and Aramaic זלף means “to sprinkle” (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Avodah Zarah 4:10:5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Avodah_Zarah.4.10.5">Note 161</a>). This does not make any sense here. The word is Arabic <b>زلف</b> “to approach, advance toward”. One brings baskets or mats of willow weave and approaches his arms from below so that his hands are in the air above the wine and cannot move the wine.</i>.
Simeon bar Ḥiyya was instructing Ḥiyya bar Rav. Starting when does a Gentile make libation wine<sup class="footnote-marker">123</sup><i class="footnote">This seems to be a question which Ḥiyya bar Rav asked Simeon bar Ḥiyya<i>.</i></i>? He told him, from when he knows the nature of idol worship. (Rav Joshia left) [Rav put out his head]<sup class="footnote-marker">124</sup><i class="footnote">The translation [in brackets] follows the argument of S. Lieberman (<i>Hayerushalmi Kiphshuto</i> New York 1995, p. 226) that one has to read not אפיק רב יאשיה but אפיק רב ראשיה as in <i>Eruvin</i>1:1, 18d line 33.</i> from the curtain and told him, you may say this if one buys grapes from the Gentile. But the Gentile proper makes libation wine when he is one day old<sup class="footnote-marker">125</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 57a" href="/Avodah_Zarah.57a">Babli 57a</a>, where Simeon bar Ḥiyya actively opposes Rav. Rav admits the leniency only for grape juice flowing from grapes in a vat. But in this case, even an adult Gentile was admitted as help. The leniency applies only to the juice left in the vat after it was emptied of grapes, cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Avodah Zarah 4:8:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Avodah_Zarah.4.8.2">Note 113</a>.</i>.
May a Gentile make libation wine with his mouth? Rebbi Ada in the name of Rebbi Eleazar: A Gentile cannot make libation wine with his mouth. Rebbi Jeremiah in the name of Rebbi Abbahu: A Gentile can make libation wine with his mouth. And we have stated thus: “If a market supervisor<sup class="footnote-marker">126</sup><i class="footnote">This should read אגרנימוס Greek ἀγορανόμος <b>“</b>market supervisor<b>”.</b></i> tasted from a cup or from a suction pipe<sup class="footnote-marker">127</sup><i class="footnote">To check the quality of the wine, whetrher the customer would not be overcharged.</i> and returned<sup class="footnote-marker">128</sup><i class="footnote">The remainder of the wine.</i> to the amphora it is forbidden.” In the opinion of Rebbi Ada in the name of Rebbi Eleazar, only if the Gentile returned it<sup class="footnote-marker">129</sup><i class="footnote">Since they hold that the motion of the wine induced by the overseer’s mouth cannot make libation wine, if the Gentile returned it to the amphora he causes waves in the wine which makes it libation wine forbidden for usufruct.</i>. In the opinion of Rebbi Jeremiah in the name of Rebbi Hoshaia, even if a Jew returned<sup class="footnote-marker">129a</sup><i class="footnote">For him the wine drawn by the overseer is libation wine in all cases; the person who returns the wine to the amphora will make it forbidden in all cases.</i>.

Halakhah 9

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> One may stamp with a Non-Jew in the wine press<sup class="footnote-marker">130</sup><i class="footnote">The grapes are pressed not by a mechanical device but by people stamping on a wooden plank on top of the grapes. All the wine produced is libation wine forbidden for all usufruct. Therefore one could think that a Jew would be forbidden to receive wages for pressing the grapes. But it was explained in the preceding Halakhah that the grape juice turns into wine only by flowing into the cistern below the press. Therefore the Jew may receive wages for pressing the grapes whose juice will not be forbidden at the moment of pressing.</i> but one may not harvest with him<sup class="footnote-marker">131</sup><i class="footnote">This Tanna is of the opinion that it is forbidden to cause impurity to fruits of the Holy Land. Since the grapes become impure by being handled by the Non-Jew, a Jewish agricultural worker observing the rules of purity may not help in the Non-Jew’s harvest (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Berakhot 1:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Berakhot.1.1.1"><i>Berakhot</i>1:1, Note 3</a> and <i>Introduction to Tractate Demay</i> p. 349–350).</i>. With a Jew who works in impurity<sup class="footnote-marker">132</sup><i class="footnote">Working at a time when it was still possible to follow the rules of impurity, a Jew making his wine in impurity was a sinner since he caused his heave and tithes (which are biblical obligations for grain, olive oil, and grape wine) to be impure and therefore forbidden to be consumed by the Cohen. Grapes harvested for wine making are placed in a vat for transport to the wine press. The fluid oozing out of injured grape berries are collected in the vat and also used for making wine. This fluid is welcome; therefore it prepares the grapes for impurity as explained in <i>Demay</i>2:3 Notes 136,137, 141. An observant Jew harvesting together with an unobservant one is guilty of helping to cause impurity to fruits of the Land. There really is no intrinsic reason to forbid pressing the grapes which already are impure; the prohibition to help in pressing is a general rule “not to support sinners”.</i> one neither stamps nor harvests with him but one transports with him amphoras to the wine press and brings with him from the wine press<sup class="footnote-marker">133</sup><i class="footnote">Transporting empty amphoras to the wine press is not connected with any impropriety; it is not even forbidden to fill impure wine into pure amphoras which thereby will become impure. Transporting amphoras filled with impure wine from the wine press is permitted since all forbidden action was in the past.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “One may stamp with a Non-Jew in the wine press,” etc. Rebbi Jonah said, that is, you say only if they walked over it crosswise<sup class="footnote-marker">134</sup><i class="footnote">Since Gentiles are considered impure under the rules of a sufferer from gonorrhea (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 36b" href="/Avodah_Zarah.36b">Babli 36b</a>), the moment a Gentile steps on the planks on top of the grapes everything is impure by “stepping on” (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Avodah Zarah 3:6:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Avodah_Zarah.3.6.2">Chapter 3:8, Note 181</a>). It is not really necessary that the Gentile walk on the plank in two different directions. Since then the entire contents of the wine press are impure: the Jew does not help to make them impure.</i>. But if they did not walk over it crosswise, it does not apply. Rebbi Yose asked, if they walked over it crosswise, even following the earlier teaching<sup class="footnote-marker">135</sup><i class="footnote">There seems to be no reason for R. Jonah’s rule since the entire harvest becomes impure at harvest time, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Avodah Zarah 4:9:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Avodah_Zarah.4.9.1">Note 132</a>. There never should be any reason not to press grapes with a Gentile.</i>? As it was stated:<sup class="footnote-marker">136</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Avodah Zarah 8:1" href="/Tosefta_Avodah_Zarah.8.1">Tosephta 7:1</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 55b" href="/Avodah_Zarah.55b">Babli 55b</a>.</i> “Originally they were saying that one harvests grapes with the Non-Jew and stamps with a Jew who works in impurity<sup class="footnote-marker">137</sup><i class="footnote">In both sources, one reads: “Originally they were saying that one <i>does not</i> harvest grapes with the Non-Jew and <i>does not</i> stamp with a Jew who works in impurity.” This text must be supposed also for the quote here. If one holds that it is forbidden to cause impurity to the fruits of the Land then one may not harvest with the Gentile because this is the moment when the grapes become impure.</i>. They changed and said, one does not harvest grapes with the Non-Jew nor stamp with a Jew who works in impurity, but one stamps with a Gentile and helps him as long as it did not disappear from sight. Once it disappeared from sight it became libation wine<sup class="footnote-marker">138</sup><i class="footnote">The second part of this <i>baraita</i> parallels the Mishnah; it is missing in the Tosephta.
