In the Babli text it reads “one <i>does not</i> stamp with the Non-Jew”; this is explained as a precaution because if the Gentile has a small harvest and decided to make red wine by leaving the skins in the fluid, the easiest way would be to plug the pipe leading to the cistern and use the press itself to let the wine ferment. Then the act of pressing makes libation wine and the Jew’s wages would be forbidden for usufruct. The Yerushalmi very reasonably does not consider this a possibility.</i>”.
What is Rebbi Jonah’s reason? Because it already became impure when it was prepared by the grape harvest<sup class="footnote-marker">139</sup><i class="footnote">As explained before, this is R. Yose’s argument.</i>. What is the reason of Rebbi Yose? They were pure by biblical standards. You are the one who decided that they be impure<sup class="footnote-marker">140</sup><i class="footnote">This is R. Jonah’s argument. There is no biblical impurity for Gentiles; the laws of impurity are uniquely formulated for Jews. The imputation of impurity (permanent and irrevocable) for Gentiles is rabbinical (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 36b" href="/Avodah_Zarah.36b">Babli 36b</a>). The exclusion of Gentiles from the Temple precinct is not because of impurity but because of their being disqualified.</i>. The strength of Rebbi Yose is the following, as Rebbi Yose said in the name of Rebbi Hila<sup class="footnote-marker">141</sup><i class="footnote">The source of this statement (in another context) is <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Challah 3:2:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Challah.3.2.2"><i>Ḥallah</i>3:2, Note 53</a>.</i> It should be logical that one may (free)<sup class="footnote-marker">142</sup><i class="footnote">This text makes no sense either by itself nor in this context. <i>Ṭevel</i> is produce under the obligation of heave from which heave was not yet taken; it may not be consumed. By all standards a person is not only permitted but obligated to free his <i>ṭevel</i> by separating the heave. The text in <i>Ḥallah</i> correctly reads: “a person may make his <i>ṭevel</i> impure by biblical standards.” The main point of the argument is given there but omitted here. Heave not only must be consumed in purity but it is stated in Num. 18:8 that the priests have a special duty to guard heave from impurity. It follows that this duty does not apply to <i>ṭevel</i>, which therefore may be allowed to become impure; disagreeing with the hypothesis underlying the Mishnah.<br> The quote here refers to a different subject. Heave given to Aaron in his quality of priest is pure heave for which he can exercise his duty to watch it and consume it in purity. But once produce has incurred even a doubt of impurity it can no longer be consumed by the Cohen; this eliminates not only the duty of guarding it but all rules made to safeguard it. If the rules of purity no longer can be observed there remain no restrictions on harvesting and pressing grapes with a Gentile.</i> his <i>ṭevel</i> as it is written, <i>you should give from it the Eternal’s heave to Aaron the priest</i><sup class="footnote-marker">143</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 18:27" href="/Numbers.18.27"><i>Num</i>. 18:27</a>.</i>. You have to give it to Aaron in his quality of priest. But here, since you cannot give it to Aaron the priest in his quality of priest, you may make it impure.

Halakhah 10

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If a baker works in impurity one does not knead with him<sup class="footnote-marker">144</sup><i class="footnote">For kneading one must wet the flour; this prepares it for impurity and, in an impure bakery, makes the dough impure.</i> nor form the bread with him<sup class="footnote-marker">145</sup><i class="footnote">The dough already is impure when it is formed; this prohibition falls under the general prohibition “to support sinners.”</i>, but one may bring bread to the seller<sup class="footnote-marker">146</sup><i class="footnote">Greek πρατήρ (Buxtorf) or πωλητήρ (Krauss) “seller”.</i> with him<sup class="footnote-marker">147</sup><i class="footnote">Since the bread is fully baked, transporting it is not “to support sinners.”</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If a baker works in impurity,” etc. One states about this: One does not moisten, nor grind, nor mix with him<sup class="footnote-marker">148</sup><i class="footnote">One either moistens the grain before grinding to facilitate the separation of chaff from flour or one washes the grain before grinding to eliminate impurities such as pebbles. In any case will the moistening make the grain susceptible to impurity. Even if the grain was dry milled, moistening the flour to make dough will bring on the impurity. Only grinding itself is solely forbidden not “to support sinners.”</i>. <sup class="footnote-marker">149</sup><i class="footnote">From here on this is taken from <i>Ševi`it</i>5:9 (Notes 92–97)7, 4:3 (Notes 52–57)*7.<br>Instead of the <i>baraita</i> quoted here, one states in <i>Ševi`it</i>: “One should neither cull nor grind nor sift with him.” These are all done dry when the grain still may be immune to impurity. The question is to explain when each of the <i>baraitot</i> applies.</i> Rebbi Hila said, here for profane [bread], there for heave. Can you say heave at a baker’s? The colleagues: one if he moistens, the other if he does not moisten<sup class="footnote-marker">150</sup><i class="footnote">Since it has been established that the Cohen has the duty to guard his heave, he has to bake his heave bread himself or at least have it baked under his strict supervision; he is forbidden to hand it over to a commercial baker. The explanation of the colleagues is the only acceptable one. Quoted in Tosaphot <i>Giṭtin</i>61a/b s.v. רישא.</i>. But did we not state<sup class="footnote-marker">151</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah <i>Ševi`it</i>5:9, speaking of an observant woman baking together with one who does not observe the rules of purity.</i>: “But she may not touch hers once water has been put in because one does not support transgressors.”
Rebbi Ḥiyya and Rebbi Immi. One said<sup class="footnote-marker">152</sup><i class="footnote">This explains the last sentence in Mishnah <i>Ševi`it</i>5:9 that one encourages Gentiles in the Sabbatical and that one greets Gentiles for communal peace. What is the difference between encouraging and greeting?</i>, “plough it well; then I will rent it from you after the Sabbatical.” The other one said, “may you succeed.” For him who said, plough it well; then I will rent it from you after the Sabbatical, what means “one greets them”? “May you succeed.<sup class="footnote-marker">153</sup><i class="footnote">This is the greeting of Gentiles endorsed by the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 62a" href="/Gittin.62a">Babli, <i>Giṭtin</i>62a</a>.</i>” But he who said that one says to a Gentile, may you succeed, what means that one greets Jews<sup class="footnote-marker">154</sup><i class="footnote">The text of <i>Ševi`it</i>5:9 is more convincing: One greets Gentiles in the same way one greets Jews.</i>? “Peace be on you.”
Explanation<sup class="footnote-marker">155</sup><i class="footnote">Greek δήλωμα.</i>. Rebbi Ḥanina bar Pappa and Rebbi Samuel bar Naḥman passed by one of the ploughmen in the Sabbatical. Rebbi Samuel bar Naḥman said to him, may you succeed. Rebbi Ḥanina bar Pappa said to him, did not Rebbi teach, <i>the passers-by did not say</i><sup class="footnote-marker">156</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Psalms 129:8" href="/Psalms.129.8"><i>Ps.</i>129:8</a>.</i>, etc., that it is forbidden to say “may you succeed” to one who ploughs in the Sabbatical? He told him, you know how to read but you do not know how to make a homily. <i>The passers-by did not say</i>, these are the nations who pass out of the world because they did not say to Israel, <i>the blessing of the Eternal is on you.</i> What does Israel say to them? <i>We bless you in the name of the Eternal</i>. Israel says to them, all the good things and happy circumstances that come into the world because of us are not enough for you; you do not say, come and take from these blessings for yourselves, but you roll over us pro-rated contributions<sup class="footnote-marker">157</sup><i class="footnote">Aramaic and Syriac פיסא “piece”, here used in the sense of individual contribution to a tax imposed on the community (I. Löw).</i> and fines<sup class="footnote-marker">158</sup><i class="footnote">Greek ζημία.</i>, head taxes and <i>annonae</i><sup class="footnote-marker">159</sup><i class="footnote">The annual contributions in kind for the upkeep of the Roman army.</i>.
Rebbi Immi instructed: It is forbidden to take the bread out of the oven with him<sup class="footnote-marker">160</sup><i class="footnote">The baker who works in impurity. The scribe in <i>Ševi`it</i>5:9 started to write this sentence, then he noticed that there it makes no sense. This determines <i>Avodah zarah</i> as origin of the preceding paragraph.</i>. Similarly, it is forbidden to sprinkle with him<sup class="footnote-marker">161</sup><i class="footnote">Moisten baking goods. It would be possible to translate “to load (the oven)”; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Avodah Zarah 4:8:5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Avodah_Zarah.4.8.5">Note 122</a>.</i>.

Halakhah 11

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> A Non-Jew who was found standing next to a wine cistern, if he has a loan secured by it<sup class="footnote-marker">162</sup><i class="footnote">The Jewish owner’s wine is mortgaged to a Gentile. Then it may be assumed that the Gentile touched the wine, which makes the wine forbidden for the Jew. The Gentile may take the wine as repayment of the loan.</i>, [the wine] is forbidden, if he has no loan secured by it, it is permitted. If he fell into the cistern and climbed out, or measured it with a measuring rod; if he tried to hit a wasp with a stick<sup class="footnote-marker">163</sup><i class="footnote">The stick did not hit the wasp but the wine in the amphora. This certainly is not libation wine but it was hit if not by the Gentile so by the force of his arm; the wine is forbidden as drink but permitted for usufruct.</i>, or was drumming on the mouth of a fermenting amphora<sup class="footnote-marker">164</sup><i class="footnote">There is a distinct possibility that the Gentile touched the foam on the mouth of the amphora. This is not a way of making libations.</i>; all these cases happened and they said, it should be sold<sup class="footnote-marker">165</sup><i class="footnote">To Gentiles since the wine is forbidden as drink but permitted for usufruct.</i>, but Rebbi Simeon permits. If he took the amphora and threw it into the cistern;<sup class="footnote-marker">166</sup><i class="footnote">In a rage; there is no libation.</i> this happened and they declared it kosher<sup class="footnote-marker">167</sup><i class="footnote">Even to drink.</i>..
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “A Non-Jew who was found standing,” etc. Rab Sheshet in the name of Rav: Only if he has a lien on this particular cistern<sup class="footnote-marker">168</sup><i class="footnote">The Mishnah could be read as applying to a loan secured by all wine in the possession of the debtor. It is now asserted that in the opinion of Rav (in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 60b" href="/Avodah_Zarah.60b">Babli, 60b</a>, of Samuel) only the holder of a lien on the particular cistern will consider the wine his property and therefore touch it without hesitation whereas a lender on a future harvest will only visit wine press and threshing floor to ascertain that the harvest is sufficient to secure his claim but he will not touch the produce or the wine since it is not his. In the Babli, this is the only opinion declared consistent with the following Mishnaiot; the discussion in the Yerushalmi shows that the opposing opinion is considered equally valid.</i>. But if he has no lien on this particular cistern, it is the way of creditors to stand near wine presses and threshing floors. They wanted to say, he who says, only if he has a lien on this particular cistern, within his outstretched arms<sup class="footnote-marker">169</sup><i class="footnote">The definition of “next to” is “within reach of his hands without moving his body.” This is the definition accepted for the distance in which a person not following the rules of impurity makes vessels and food impure by his presence (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Tahorot 7:3" href="/Mishnah_Tahorot.7.3">Mishnah <i>Ṭahorot</i>7:3</a>).</i>, but he who says, even if he has no lien on this particular cistern, even outside his outstretched arms<sup class="footnote-marker">170</sup><i class="footnote">Since he is more restrictive than the first opinion, one may assume that he is more restrictive in this matter also.</i>. But did not Rebbi Abba say in the name of Rav Sheshet<sup class="footnote-marker">171</sup><i class="footnote">Halakhah 5:6 (45a l. 1).</i>, just as they gave outstretched arms for purity so they gave it for libation wine? But it must be so: He who says, only if he has a lien on this particular cistern, within his outstretched arms, but he who says, even if he has no lien on this particular cistern, only outside his outstretched arms<sup class="footnote-marker">172</sup><i class="footnote">The definition of “next to” is independent of the circle of persons to which it is applied. In all circumstances, “next to” means what can be reached without moving one’s body.</i>.
“One who makes a Non-Jew’s wine in purity,” etc. <sup class="footnote-marker">177</sup><i class="footnote">This Halakhah in its entirety refers to Mishnah 11, not Mishnah 12.</i> Rebbi Abin in the name of Rebbi Samuel, practice follows Rebbi Simeon<sup class="footnote-marker">178</sup><i class="footnote">Who in Mishnah 11 permitted to drink wine which the Sages only permitted for usufruct. This tradition is quoted in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 60b" href="/Avodah_Zarah.60b">Babli 60b</a> but in the end rejected.</i>. Rebbi Abin in the name of Samuel. I am atoning for Rebbi Simeon<sup class="footnote-marker">179</sup><i class="footnote">This is an expression traditionally used by the son speaking of his father. He considers himself the spiritual son of R. Simeon (ben Ioḥai).</i>, either permit to drink or forbid for usufruct<sup class="footnote-marker">180</sup><i class="footnote">R. Simeon holds that any wine not forbidden for usufruct is not forbidden as drink.</i>. Rebbi Jeremiah said in the name of Rebbi Abbahu. There was a case and they permitted for drinking following Rebbi Simeon. Rebbi Samuel in the name of Rebbi Abbahu: A Gentile does not make libation wine by throwing<sup class="footnote-marker">181</sup><i class="footnote">If the Gentile never touched the wine but moved it by throwing the bottle in which it was stored, he cannot possibly have made it into libation wine. Quoted in Tosaphot 59a/b <i>s. v</i>. מהו.</i>. Does a Gentile make libation wine in his rage? Let us hear from the following: An Aramean<sup class="footnote-marker">182</sup><i class="footnote">A Gentile, cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Avodah Zarah 1:6:5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Avodah_Zarah.1.6.5">Halakhah 1:6, Note 177</a>.</i> had boxes in a wine press. There came a Jew and sprinkled wine on them. The Aramean came<sup class="footnote-marker">183</sup><i class="footnote">It might be better to read אַייָתִי “brought” instead of אֲתַא “came”.</i> [with] a pitcher and poured it out into the cistern. The case came before the rabbis who said, a Gentile does not make libation wine in his rage.
Rebbi Jeremiah in the name of Rebbi Ḥiyya bar Abba. One who mixes wine, with hot water it is forbidden, with cold water it is permitted. It should come over me, if I ever did this<sup class="footnote-marker">184</sup><i class="footnote">He never let a Gentile pour water in his cup of wine.</i>. Rebbi Yasa went to Tyre<sup class="footnote-marker">185</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 58b" href="/Avodah_Zarah.58b">Babli 58b</a> a similar story is told of R. Jeremiah himself. The problem with the text here is that R. Yasa was one of the teachers of R. Ḥiyya bar Abba, the teacher of R. Jeremiah’s teacher R. Ze`ira.</i>. He saw them drinking wine with hot water and an Aramean was mixing. He told them, what<sup class="footnote-marker">186</sup><i class="footnote">“What”, not “who”. He made it clear that he did not think that any rabbi would allow this.</i> did allow you?

Halakhah 12

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> One who makes a Non-Jew’s wine in purity<sup class="footnote-marker">173</sup><i class="footnote">The Jew uses a Gentile’s winery to make kosher wine to sell the wine to Jews.</i> and leaves it in his domain, in a house open to the public domain<sup class="footnote-marker">174</sup><i class="footnote">Where the public has open access. If a Jew saw the Gentile near the wine, he could not anymore sell the wine to Jews. Since this would be costly to him, one may assume that the minor oversight provided by the occasional Jewish visitor is sufficient.</i> in a city where there are Gentiles and Jews it is permitted, in a city where all are Gentiles it is forbidden unless he install there a watchman. But the watchman does not have to sit there and watch; even if he leaves and reenters it is permitted<sup class="footnote-marker">175</sup><i class="footnote">On condition that he not tell anybody when he would leave or come back.</i>. Rebbi Simeon ben Eleazar says, all Gentiles’ domains are the same<sup class="footnote-marker">176</sup><i class="footnote">There is no difference between the owner’s property or that of any other Gentile in this matter.</i>.<br>One who makes a Non-Jew’s wine in purity<sup class="footnote-marker">173</sup><i class="footnote">The Jew uses a Gentile’s winery to make kosher wine to sell the wine to Jews.</i> and leaves it in his domain<sup class="footnote-marker">187</sup><i class="footnote">Without a guard.</i>, if this one writes to him that I received the money, it is permitted<sup class="footnote-marker">188</sup><i class="footnote">Since the Gentile has recognized the wine as the Jew’s property he will not touch it and expose himself to a suit for damages.</i>. But if the Jew wants to remove it and he does not let him do it before he paid him his money<sup class="footnote-marker">189</sup><i class="footnote">The Gentile shows that he considers the wine his property until the rental fee for his wine press and wine cellar was paid. This makes the wine Gentile and forbidden.</i>, this was a case in Bet Shean and the Sages forbade.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “One who makes a Non-Jew’s wine in purity,” etc. <sup class="footnote-marker">190</sup><i class="footnote">This Halakhah discusses Mishnah 12.</i>Rebbi Abbahu in the name of Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Ḥanina: Not only a door but even a window wide four by four and high ten<sup class="footnote-marker">191</sup><i class="footnote">Since the numerals are masculine they denote hand-breadths, not quite 10cm. An opening of 4-by-4 and high 10 is considered a door also for the rules of impurity.</i>, on condition that it be roofed and glued closed<sup class="footnote-marker">192</sup><i class="footnote">Not the door but the amphora in which the wine is stored. It cannot be left open in the Gentile’s place under any circumstances but must have a cover, “be roofed”, and the cover must be glued to the amphora by clay which is left to dry. Then it is sufficient that an occasional visitor check that the clay seal is inviolate.</i>. If it was growing, we would say if he is seen climbing up and coming down it is forbidden, otherwise it is permitted<sup class="footnote-marker">193</sup><i class="footnote">If the wine is stored on a tree (a seemingly unlikely case but which is mentioned also in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 61a" href="/Avodah_Zarah.61a">Babli, 61a</a>). If an amphora is stored at a place reserved for it and the Gentile is seen going there where he has no reason to be, the wine will become forbidden since one must suspect him of removing the seal, taking some wine, and resealing.</i>. Rebbi Abba in the name of Rav Jehudah: The Mishnah speaks of a town “with double doors and bolts.<sup class="footnote-marker">194</sup><i class="footnote">A fortified city; the expression is Biblical (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 3:5" href="/Deuteronomy.3.5"><i>Deut</i>. 3:5</a>). In the Babli the author is Samuel, Rav Jehudah’s teacher and the statement is accepted as authoritative. According to Rashi, the reason is that there all inhabitants are known and easily checked out; therefore one does not need a permanent presence of the watchman.</i>” Was it stated in that case “a watchman even if he comes only at intervals”?
It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">196</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Avodah Zarah 8:3-4" href="/Tosefta_Avodah_Zarah.8.3-4">Tosephta 7:10</a>.</i> “A Jew and a Gentile who brought their wine into the surroundings<sup class="footnote-marker">197</sup><i class="footnote">According to H. L. Fleischer this is derived from Greek περιοχή in its meaning “circumference”. In the Tosephta, the corresponding word is (Persian) פַּרוָר “suburb”. This is translated here.</i> of the city, even if the watchmen are Gentile, even if the amphoras are open, they are permitted since one does not make a libation in this way.”
Rebbi Hila in the name of Rebbi Yannai: The buyer has the same status as the seller. It was stated thus<sup class="footnote-marker">198</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 61" href="/Avodah_Zarah.61">Babli, 61a/b</a>, “buyer and renter”. The Yerushalmi’s version is preferable since the Mishnah refers to the producer who is the seller. The rules about storage are identical for producer and buyer.</i>: Whether the seller or the buyer [stores] on the Gentile’s property. If the wine is the Jew’s and a Jew lives there it is permitted on condition that in his hand are key and seal. If the wine is the Gentile’s and a Jew lives there it is permitted on condition that in his hand are key and seal<sup class="footnote-marker">199</sup><i class="footnote">The key to the storage room and the seal either on the covers of the amphoras or, if the amphoras are open, on the door to the storage room.</i>; but if no Jew lives there it is forbidden even if key and seal are in his hand.
Rebbi Ilai in the name of Rebbi Yannai: They disagree with Rebbi Simeon ben Eleazar. Not on this was it said but on the following, “one who makes an Non-Jew’s wine in purity,”<sup class="footnote-marker">200</sup><i class="footnote">The disagreement is not about the statement of R. Simeon ben Eleazar applied to Mishnah 12, where everybody agrees that the identity of the Gentile is irrelevant, but about Mishnah 13 where the majority insists that it refers only to the owner of the wine press.</i> etc.

Chapter 5



Halakhah 1

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If somebody hires a worker to work for him on libation wine, his wages are forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">If a Gentile hires a Jewish worker specifically to work on his wine, the wages are forbidden to the worker for all usufruct. The rule which makes it impossible for the Jewish worker to be hired in this way is purely rabbinical; it is not implied by the fact that libation wine is forbidden for usufruct for a Jewish owner.</i>. If he hired him for other work, even though he told him, transport an amphora of libation wine for me from one place to another, his wages are permitted<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">The moment that libation wine was not mentioned at the time of the hiring, there is no obligation on the worker to refrain from being occupied with libation wine.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If somebody hires a worker,” etc. Does he not give him his wages? Rebbi Abbahu in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: They fined him a fine<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">A customary restriction without justification in law.</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">This paragraph is a shortened and somewhat corrupt copy of one in <i>Ševi`it</i>8:6, Notes 94–98.</i> It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 62a" href="/Avodah_Zarah.62a">Babli 62a</a>, Tosephta <i>Ševi`it</i>6:26.</i>: “The wages of donkey drivers, carriers, and all those working with Sabbatical produce are sabbatical.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">They should only be used to buy Sabbatical produce to be eaten, not processed.</i>” Rebbi Ze`ira said, this <i>baraita</i> deals with permitted produce. And what Rebbi Joḥanan taught those of the House of Rebbi Yannai that they should not accept in their hands<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">While the paragraph in general looks like a bad copy of the one in <i>Ševi`it,</i> the reading בידיהן “in their hands” is superior to the reading בדיהן “of their olive presses” of both <i>Ševi`it</i> mss. There is no question why one should be paid in wine for using an olive press.</i> wine as wages but money, he taught them following Rebbi Jehudah and Rebbi Neḥemiah<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">Who in <i>Ševi`it</i>(8:4 Note 69, Tosephta 6:21) forbid to pay for anything, even for food, with sabbatical produce.</i>. Rebbi Ilai said, this <i>baraita</i> deals with idolatrous produce<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">This reading is quite impossible; idolatrous produce does not exist. In <i>Ševi`it</i>: “forbidden produce”, stored sabbatical produce at a time when wild animals no longer can find produce on the fields, which has to be consumed immediately.</i>. As Rebbi Abbahu said in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan, in matters of libation wine they fined him a fine.
If he transported for him amphora against amphora<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">The language is not clear and the proposed interpretations are unfounded. It seems that the worker is not paid as a worker for his time but as a contractor by the piece, and is not paid in money but receives an (empty) amphora.<br>In the entire paragraph it is equally possible not to read the sentences as apodictic statements but as questions. If he is paid in land, is the land forbidden? Etc.</i>, you fine him in his own amphora up to the amount of his wages<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">That he might be forced to sell the amphora and destroy coins in the equivalent of his wages.</i>. If he paid him his wages in land. Everywhere you are saying that land is not forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Avodah Zarah 3:5:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Avodah_Zarah.3.5.1">Halakhah 3:6, Note 125</a>.</i>, here it is forbidden. If he paid him his wages in an animal. Everywhere you are saying that anything breathing is not forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Avodah Zarah 3:5:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Avodah_Zarah.3.5.4">Halakhah 3:6, Note 140</a>.</i>, here it is forbidden. If he gave him anything as his wages<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">Which clearly is worth more than the amount due for his wages.</i>, is everything forbidden or only up to the amount of his wages? If he worked for him for goodwill, do you impound his property in the value of his wages?
If he worked for him half a day in prohibition and half a day in permission. If he was selling profane [food] and Second Tithe together, [the buyer] has the choice of treating as profane what he chooses and as Second Tithe what he chooses<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">This statement is not found in any parallel source but no opposing opinion is noted. While it is necessary to “give a name” to the Second Tithe due for a harvest, it is not necessary bodily to separate it from the remainder of the harvest. If the farmer does not intend to bring his tithe bodily to Jerusalem, he may sell it and transfer its sanctity to the money. The buyer then has to separate tithe from profane food but is not restricted in his choice; cf. Introduction to Tractate <i>Ma`aser Šeni</i>.</i>. Is this the same here? Let us hear from the following<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ševi`it</i>8:4 Note 65. In the first case the worker is hired as a contractor, in the second as a worker who has to be paid for his time.</i>. If somebody hires a worker to bring wine to a sick person; if he delivered [the employer] has to pay him, otherwise he does not have to pay him. But if he told him, wine for a sick person from place X, an apple for a sick person from place X, whether or not he delivered he has to pay him, for he pays the wages of his feet. And here, does he not pay the wages of his feet<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">There is no reason to impound the wages for the time he worked in a permitted way.</i>?
Rebbi Jeremiah asked, if he hired him to break amphoras of libation wine; is it forbidden anyhow<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 63b" href="/Avodah_Zarah.63b">Babli 63b</a> the question was asked by Amoraim of the second generation and answered in the negative.</i>?
Assi said, the proceeds of libation wine in the hands of a Gentile are prohibited<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">They are not forbidden to the Gentile, only to a Jew who knows that this money was received as payment for wine whose usufruct is forbidden to Jews. From the argument in the text it seems possible that one should read; “Assi said, the proceeds of libation wine in the hands of a Gentile and the proceeds of an idol in the hand of a Gentile, Rebbi Jonathan said it is permitted, Rebbi Joḥanan said it is prohibited”, since in the proof texts wine and idols are always treated in parallel. On the other hand, the preceding text (Notes 11–12) notes that the prohibition of wine, a purely rabbinic custom, follows much stricter rules than the biblical prohibition of idols.</i>. The proceeds of an idol in the hand of a Gentile, Rebbi Jonathan said it is permitted, Rebbi Joḥanan said it is prohibited. A <i>baraita</i> supports one and a <i>baraita</i> supports the other. A <i>baraita</i> supports Rebbi Joḥanan: “A Gentile who owed money to a Jew should not tell him, wait until I sell libation wine, until I sell an idol, and I shall give it to you. I am saying, maybe he exchanged.<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 64a" href="/Avodah_Zarah.64a">Babli 64a</a>. If the Gentile tells the Jew that he gives him money received for forbidden wine or idols, the Jew may not accept since we must assume that the Gentile tells the truth. In the Babli’s tradition, this is accepted by everybody; in the Yerushalmi this is particular to R. Joḥanan.</i>” A <i>baraita</i> supports Rebbi Jonathan: “A Gentile who owes money to a Jew may sell libation wine and give to him, an idol and give to him. Because of the bad impression?<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">This is read as meaning that in all cases the Jew may accept the money and does not have to worry that people will say that he accepts idol money. The Babli would interpret the <i>baraita</i> as stating that the Jew may accept the money as long as the Gentile does not explicitly declare it as idol’s proceeds.</i>”
<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">The entire paragraph has a parallel in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 54b" href="/Avodah_Zarah.54b">Babli, 54b</a>. Anything received in barter for an idol must be destroyed as if it were the idol itself.</i> Everybody agrees that substitutes are forbidden. What about substitutes of substitutes? Rebbi Ḥanina said, a disagreement between Rebbi Ismael and the rabbis. Rebbi Ismael ben Rebbi Yose said, forbidden. But the rabbis say, permitted. Rebbi Eleazar ben Hoshaia, the reason of the rabbis, <i>you would be banned like itself</i><sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 7:26" href="/Deuteronomy.7.26"><i>Deut</i>. 7:26</a>.</i>. Anything which you can keep from it is like itself<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">Any proceeds are like the idol itself. But nothing is said about proceeds for barter of the proceeds.</i>. What about it? Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Bun said, <i>like itself, like itself</i> two times<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">The expression חֶרֶם כָּמוֹהוּ appears only once in the biblical text. The argument gives the reason for R. Ismael ben R. Yose’s position. If the proceeds obtained by barter for an idol are an idol, then the proceeds in barter for the proceeds are also an idol; in effect, the prohibition never stops.</i>, because of <i>nothing should cling to your hand from the ban</i><sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 13:18" href="/Deuteronomy.13.18"><i>Deut</i>. 13:18</a>.</i>.

Halakhah 2

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If somebody<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">A Gentile. The Mishnah is parallel to Mishnah 1; only rentals made for the explicit purpose of transporting libation wine (or idols) is forbidden, not occasional use if the hiring was for general purposes.</i> hires a donkey to transport libation wine, its wages are forbidden. If he hired it to sit on it, even though he put his barrel<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">Greek λάγυνος, λάγηνος, Latin <i>lagena</i>“bottle”, also a measure. Middle High German <i>lâgen</i>“small barrel; a measure of volume or weight”.</i> on it, its wages are permitted.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If somebody hires a donkey,” etc. How far? Up to when the donkey takes offense<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">If the weight of the rider and the barrel would be more than the normal load of a donkey, the contract would be for transporting the wine and illegal.</i>.

Halakhah 3

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If libation wine fell on grapes, one should wash it off and they are permitted; but if they were split they are forbidden. If happened that Boetius ben Zenon brought dried figs in a boat when amphoras of libation wine broke over them. The case came before the Sages and they permitted. This is the principle: Anything where the taste improves the use is forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">If the taste of the forbidden wine improves the taste of the food on which it fell.</i>; anything where the taste does not improve its use is permitted<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">If the spill was accidental, it is not necessary that the wine actually diminish the value or enjoyment of the food on which it fell.</i>, such as vinegar on broken beans<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">In the Halakhah both here and in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 67a" href="/Avodah_Zarah.67a">Babli (67a</a>) R. Joḥanan restricts this to wine falling on a dish of hot broken beans or peas.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If libation wine fell on grapes,” etc. Our Mishnah if their seal is not loosened. But if their seal was loosened they are as injured<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">Grapes which still sit on the vine with intact skin are sealed; if libation wine falls on them one washes the bunch and it is permitted. But loose grapes have a hole where they were sitting on the vine; the libation wine enters through this hole and cannot be removed.</i>.
How do you treat them<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">What is the reason that injured grapes on which libation wine fell are forbidden?</i>? Like wine in wine or wine in a dish<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">If wine of one kind is mixed with wine of another type, for wine cognoscenti this definitely is spoilage. But wine added to a cooked dish improves the taste.</i>? If you treat it like wine in a dish, according to everybody it is forbidden. If you treat it like wine in wine, there is the disagreement between Rebbi Meїr and the Sages<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">As noted later, if the forbidden admixture improves the taste, the mixture is forbidden according to everybody. But if it spoils the taste, the rabbis opposing Rebbi Meїr do permit consumption of the food.</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">This paragraph is an intrusion here; it should be placed at the end of the Halakhah since it refers to the last statement in the Mishnah, forbidden vinegar on broken beans. Ihe parallel is in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 67a" href="/Avodah_Zarah.67a">Babli, 67a</a>.</i> Rebbi Joḥanan said, this you say if it is hot. But cold it is forbidden, for the people of Sepphoris are used to make it and call it laxative<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">In the Babli, the dish of Sepphoris is called שחליים. This probably comes from the Babylonian pronunciation of ח as ה. As the Babli reports, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Megillah 24b" href="/Megillah.24b"><i>Megillah</i>24b</a>, Rebbi told R. Ḥiyya the Babylonian to be careful if he quotes <i>Is</i> 8:17, “I am waiting (וְחִכִּיתִי) for the Eternal”, not to blaspheme by saying “I am hitting (וְהִכִּיתִי).” Now שְׁחַלַיִים are watercresses, not making sense for the context. The root שהל is not documented in Hebrew or any of the Talmudic Aramaic dialects; but one of the many meanings of Arabic سمل is “to purge, to act as laxative.” This is taken as the meaning here. It cannot be determined whether the word was pronounced with שׁ or שׂ, even though שׁ is more likely.</i>. If it was hot and one cooled it down? Then even hot it should be forbidden since usually it cools down<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">An implied answer to this question is found in the Babli: Vinegar in the cold dish improves the taste and therefore makes it forbidden. If the dish then is heated the vinegar spoils the taste but this does not remove the prohibition. Cooling the dish after heating leaves the dish spoiled.</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">This paragraph is a corrupted version of one in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Terumot 10:4:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Terumot.10.4.2"><i>Terumot</i>10:2 (Notes 20–24</a>), <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Orlah 2:4:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Orlah.2.4.3"><i>Orlah</i>2:5 (Note 121</a>). It is the continuation of the discussion why and under which condition contamination with libation wine makes food prohibited. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 67b-68a" href="/Avodah_Zarah.67b-68a">Babli 67b/68a</a>.</i> As we have stated<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Terumot (Lieberman) 8:9" href="/Tosefta_Terumot_(Lieberman).8.9">Tosephta <i>Terumot</i>8:9</a>.</i>. “Everything that can be smelled is forbidden, whether it improves or spoils, the words of Rebbi Meїr. But the Sages say, if it improves it is forbidden, if it spoils it is permitted,” as for example vinegar which fell into broken beans. Rebbi Yasa in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: It is Rebbi Meïr’s, as we have stated there<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah <i>Terumot</i>10:2. The barley grains are heave, forbidden to anybody but a Cohen. Since they spoil the taste of the water, they impart no sanctity to it. The water remains permitted to everybody.</i>: “If barley grains fell into a cistern of water, even though they made it stink, [the water] is permitted.” About this Mishnah, Rebbi Meïr says it is in dispute, Rebbi Joḥanan and Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish: everybody’s opinion<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">This sentence and the following one are impossible; Rebbi Meïr the fourth-generation Tanna cannot engage in a discussion with the second generation Amoraim RR. Joḥanan and Simeon ben Laqish. The correct statements are from <i>Orlah</i> and <i>Terumot</i>: “About this Mishnah, Rebbi <i>Joḥanan</i> says it is in dispute, Rebbi Rebbi <i>Simeon ben Laqish</i> said it is everybody’s opinion. Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun said this tradition so: In the opinion of Rebbi <i>Joḥanan</i> it is in dispute, in the opinion of Rebbi <i>Simeon ben Laqish</i> it is everybody’s opinion.”</i>. Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun said this tradition so: In the opinion of Rebbi Meïr it is in dispute, in the opinions of Rebbi Joḥanan and Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish it is everybody’s opinion.

Halakhah 4

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> A Non-Jew who with a Jew was transporting wine jugs from one place to another, if it can be presumed to be watched it is permitted<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">The Jew transports kosher wine and hires a Gentile as help. Even if the Jew does not see the Gentile at every moment; the fact that the Jew is around will prevent the Gentile from touching the wine if he knows that he would lose his job and face a damage suit.</i>. If he told him that he was leaving<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">The Jew informed the Gentile that he was leaving for some time.</i>, the time needed to open, close, and fill the hole<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">The containers are of clay and are closed. If the expected time of absence of the owner is sufficient for the Gentile to drill a hole in the cover, close it again and seal it, the wine becomes forbidden.</i>; Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel says, to open, and close, and dry<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">He thinks that the owner upon his return would recognize a hole freshly filled. He therefore forbids the wine only if there was time for the Gentile to remove the old seal completely, make a new one and have it dry to become undetectable.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “A Non-Jew who was transporting,” etc. Rebbi Samuel said, it happened that a Non-Jew was transporting wine jugs with a Jew from one place to another. The case came before Rebbi Abbahu who forbade. They said, the case was about open [containers]<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">The ruling of R. Abbahu does not contradict the Mishnah. The wine containers are only “presumed to be watched” if on top they are closed with a clay cover. Open containers, where the wine could be touched in no time, are never “presumed to be watched”.</i>. Rebbi Zeriqan said, not only full ones but also short ones; for he suddenly could have touched with his hand and removed it<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">Even if the container has a narrow neck and is not full, so that even without a cover it is not possible for the Gentile to touch the wine and remove his hand in less than a second, the wine is forbidden.</i>.
You may be of the opinion that Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel’s statement was a leniency, but rather it is a stringency. For the opening of the rabbis leaves little of the cover<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">While Rabban Simeon requires time for the cover to dry, he envisages the possibility that the Gentile drill a small hole in the cover, whereas the rabbis only contemplate the removal of most of the old cover.</i>.
Rebbi Jehudah ben Pazi in the name of Rebbi Immi: A roasted egg of Samaritans is permitted<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">There is no suspicion that it not be kosher.</i>. Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa in the name of Rebbi Eleazar: Samaritans’ dishes are permitted. This is what you say about a dish usually made without wine or vinegar. Therefore if it is certain that he put some in, it is forbidden even for usufruct<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">Samaritan wine is forbidden as if it were Gentile wine.</i>. As what had been stated: In earlier times they used to say, why is the wine of Ogdor forbidden? Because of Kefar-Pagesh, and of Burgata because of the Saracen tower, and of the <i>Kushit</i> spring because of <i>Kefar Shalem</i><sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">These places are not identified; also it is not clear whether the wine of the first mentioned locality is forbidden because it is Samaritan close to the second one settled by Gentiles, or that the first locality is Jewish and the second Samaritan. <i>Kefar Shalem</i> may be the place mentioned in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Genesis 33:18" href="/Genesis.33.18"><i>Gen</i>. 33.18</a> as situated near Sichem.</i>. They changed to say, open it<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">Wine sold in open amphoras since there is no controlling who might have touched it.</i> is everywhere forbidden, sealed it is permitted. Perforated and repaired it is like sealed, Rebbi Isaac ben Ḥaqula said, it is like open. Rebbi Ḥanina said, I can confirm. If it has a cavity on it<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">This translation is tentative; it is offered as an educated guess. The proposal of <i>Pene Moshe</i> to read קירי “<i>cera,</i> wax” instead of יקיר is not very convincing; a wax seal can easily be counterfeit. The explanation of M. Sokoloff, to read here as at other places “worth, expense” is even less convincing; it seems illogical to assume that wine was not tampered with because it was expensive. The explanation given is based on Arabic وقر “cavity”. If the clay seal on top of the amphora is not plane it probably is original, if it is smooth it may have been tampered with.</i> it was not opened, otherwise it was opened. As the following: Rebbi Simeon ben Eleazar went to the city of Samaria<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Chullin 6a" href="/Chullin.6a">Babli, <i>Ḥulin</i>6a</a>, he was sent by R. Meїr to buy wine from Samaritans.</i>. The scribe came to him. He told him, bring me a closed jug<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">Of wine to drink.</i>. He answered him, there is a spring in front of you, drink. He importuned him; he said, there is a spring in front of you, drink. When he saw that he was going to importune him, he told him, if you are master of yourself, there is a spring in front of you, drink. But if your soul brings you to rebellion, <i>you are going to swallow a knife if you are too covetous</i><sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Proverbs 23:2" href="/Proverbs.23.2"><i>Prov</i>. 23:2</a>.</i>. The Samaritans already are degenerate<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote">This projects the Amoraic development of separation of Jews from Samaritans into Tannaitic times (and it was not complete even in Fatimid Egypt.)</i>.
Rebbi Ismael ben Rebbi Yose went to the wellknown Neapolis<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote">The former Sichem, today Nablus.</i>. The Samaritans came to him. He told them, I am seeing you bowing down not to this mountain but to the idols under it, as it is written, <i>he hid them under the terebinth near Sichem</i><sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Genesis 35:4" href="/Genesis.35.4"><i>Gen</i>. 35:4</a>.</i>. He heard their voices saying, let us get up early and gather those thorns. He understood that they intended to kill him; he got up early and left.
Rebbi Aḥa went to Emmaus and ate something made in boiling water<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote">Dumplings or bagels, any grain product prepared in hot water is called חָלוּט.</i>. Rebbi Jeremiah ate pickles. Rebbi Ḥizqiah ate locusts<sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote">All three considered Samaritan cooking as kosher. (Also Samaritan slaughter, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Chullin 5b" href="/Chullin.5b">Babli <i>Ḥulin</i>5b</a>).</i>. Rebbi Abbahu forbade their wine on the testimony of Rebbi Ḥiyya, Rebbi Assi, and Rebbi Immi who went up King’s Mountain and saw a Gentile who was suspected because of wine<sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote">He was suspected of selling Gentile wine to Samaritans. The latter, as Sadducees, had no reason to forbid Gentile wine which was not actually used in pagan exercises.</i>. They came and said it before him. He told them, but not as a pretext<sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote">He wanted to forbid Samaritan wine only upon solid evidence.</i>. But some say, one Sabbath eve no wine was found in all of Samaria<sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote">The district.</i>. When Sabbath ended, it was found full from what the Arameans brought and the Samaritans accepted from them<sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote">As noted earlier, they had no reason to forbid Gentile wine. But this made Samaritan wine equal to Gentile wine for rabbinic Jews. Of all reasons given, this seems the most plausible.</i>. But some say, when King Diocletian came here he decided and said, all peoples have to offer libations except the Jews. The Samaritans offered libations and their wine was forbidden. But some say, they have a kind of dove and they offer libations to it.
The Samaritans of Caesarea asked Rebbi Abbahu: Your fathers were providing themselves from us<sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote">They were buying Samaritan wine for personal use.</i>. Why are you not providing yourselves from us? He answered them, your fathers did not spoil their deeds<sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote">One could assume that in earlier times Samaritan wine was made according to rabbinic standards. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosafot on Chagigah 25a:3:1" href="/Tosafot_on_Chagigah.25a.3.1">Tosaphot <i>Ḥagigah</i>25a <i>s. v.</i>שרצועה</a>.</i>, you are spoiling your deeds.
There<sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Mikvaot 8:1" href="/Mishnah_Mikvaot.8.1">Mishnah <i>Miqwaot</i>8:1</a>.</i> we have stated: “The Land of Israel is pure and its ritual baths are pure<sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote">There is a universal presumption that every Jewish congregation in the Land of Israel will see to it that its ritual baths are built according to the rules (in particular, that the water is natural, ground water or rain water, not from vessels, and that the amount of water is at last 40 <i>seah</i>(a Roman <i>culleus</i>, 20 <i>amphorae</i>) as required by rabbinic tradition. A visitor may use any such <i>miqweh</i> without first investigating its status. Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Berakhot 3:4:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Berakhot.3.4.4"><i>Berakhot</i>3:4, Notes 164–166</a>.</i>.” <sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote">Tosephta <i>Miqwaot</i>6:1.</i>“The land of the Samaritans is pure; its ritual baths, and its dwellings<sup class="footnote-marker">73</sup><i class="footnote">Gentile dwellings and paths are impure even in the Land of Israel since one has to worry that Gentiles bury their stillborn in their dirt floors or on their paths. Anybody walking over such a spot would become impure by “tent impurity.” The opinion that Gentiles are not sources of “tent impurity” is not found in Yerushalmi sources (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Yevamot 61a" href="/Yevamot.61a">Babli <i>Yebamot</i>61a</a>, attributed to R. Simeon ben Ioḥai.)</i>, and its paths are pure.” Its paths are pure; it is a presumption that they would not select a path unless it was pure<sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote">They are assumed to be more strict in matters of impurity than rabbinic Jews.</i>. And its ritual baths are pure; Rebbi Eleazar ben Rebbi Yose<sup class="footnote-marker">75</sup><i class="footnote">The Tanna.</i>: that is, to believe them that they are not from drawn water<sup class="footnote-marker">76</sup><i class="footnote">Which is a source of impurity.</i>, but not for the measure of forty; for they explain, <i>only a spring or a cistern, a collection of water</i><sup class="footnote-marker">77</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 11:36" href="/Leviticus.11.36"><i>Lev</i>. 11:36</a>.</i>,” just as a spring cleanses in any amount, any collection of water cleanses in any amount<sup class="footnote-marker">78</sup><i class="footnote">In <i>Sifra Šemini Parašah</i>9(1) the argument attributed here to Samaritans (as Sadducees) is rejected only because of the rule that אַךְ “only” must indicate an exclusion. This characterizes the rule that flowing water purifies in any quantity but standing water only in 40 <i>seah</i> as rabbinical but in essence it is also found in the Damascus Document CD X 11..</i>.
They asked Rebbi Abbahu: What is the status of their parboiled food<sup class="footnote-marker">79</sup><i class="footnote">Which only could be forbidden if Samaritans were considered Gentiles.</i>? He told them, if only we also could prohibit their water! Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa in the name of Rebbi Ḥanina: It is permitted to lend to the Samaritans of Caesarea on interest<sup class="footnote-marker">80</sup><i class="footnote">He considers them as Gentiles.</i>. Rebbi Yose asked, if that is so, we should not be worried about their <i>ḥallah</i>; but we see that the rabbis do worry<sup class="footnote-marker">81</sup><i class="footnote">A Gentile baker does not have to give <i>ḥallah</i> and should he give it would not have any holiness. If the rabbis insist that Samaritan bread may be used only if <i>ḥallah</i> had duly been given, the attitude of R. Jacob bar Aḥa and R. Abbahu is rejected. Since R. Jacob bar Aḥa was one of the teachers of R. Yose, one has to assume that the latter’s position is that of his foremost teacher, R. Jeremiah.</i>.

Halakhah 5

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If somebody leaves his wine on a cart<sup class="footnote-marker">82</sup><i class="footnote">Latin <i>carrum</i>. The teamster transporting the wine (in sealed amphoras) is Gentile.</i> or a ship<sup class="footnote-marker">83</sup><i class="footnote">A ship on a river.</i> and went by a short-cut<sup class="footnote-marker">84</sup><i class="footnote">Latin <i>compendiarium</i>(<i>iter</i>); cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Berakhot 1:1:13" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Berakhot.1.1.13"><i>Berakhot</i>1:1, Note 36</a>.</i>, [even if] he entered a town and took a bath<sup class="footnote-marker">85</sup><i class="footnote">The Jew is out of sight and away for an extended period. Nevertheless the wine is permitted if the Gentile has to expect every minute that the owner of the wine might return.</i>, it is permitted. If he told him that he was leaving<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">The Jew informed the Gentile that he was leaving for some time.</i>, the time needed to open, close, and fill the hole<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">The containers are of clay and are closed. If the expected time of absence of the owner is sufficient for the Gentile to drill a hole in the cover, close it again and seal it, the wine becomes forbidden.</i>; Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel says, to open, and close, and dry<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">He thinks that the owner upon his return would recognize a hole freshly filled. He therefore forbids the wine only if there was time for the Gentile to remove the old seal completely, make a new one and have it dry to become undetectable.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If somebody leaves his wine on a cart,” etc. <sup class="footnote-marker">86</sup><i class="footnote">Quoted in <i>Or Zarua</i>240 (edition of Makhon Yerushalaim 2010, vol. 3 p. 650b); referred to in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shabbat 122a" href="/Shabbat.122a">Babli, <i>Šabbat</i>122a</a>.</i> Rebbi Ḥanina said, it happened that a cart of the family of Rebbi was leaving for more than four <i>mil</i><sup class="footnote-marker">87</sup><i class="footnote">The distance between the Jewish overseer and the Gentile driver. The <i>mil</i> referred to here probably is a quarter of a Greek <i>parasanges</i>, 1380 m.</i>. The case came before the rabbis who declared it kosher. It was on the road to Sidon populated by Jews<sup class="footnote-marker">88</sup><i class="footnote">In this interpretation, such a large distance is acceptable only if the cart at all times was seen by Jews.</i>. Rebbi Ḥanina<sup class="footnote-marker">89</sup><i class="footnote">In <i>Or Zarua</i>, R. Ḥama, a more likely reading. The attribution to R. Ḥanina of the original statement is confirmed by the Babli.</i> said, sometimes he sees a fence formed by thorn bushes<sup class="footnote-marker">90</sup><i class="footnote">Which may look like human shapes at a distance.</i> and he thinks that they are people and is afraid.

Halakhah 6

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If somebody leaves a Gentile in charge of his store, even though he [intermittently] leaves and returns, [the wine] is permitted. If he told him that he was leaving<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">The Jew informed the Gentile that he was leaving for some time.</i>, the time needed to open, close, and fill the hole<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">The containers are of clay and are closed. If the expected time of absence of the owner is sufficient for the Gentile to drill a hole in the cover, close it again and seal it, the wine becomes forbidden.</i>; Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel says, to open, and close, and dry<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">He thinks that the owner upon his return would recognize a hole freshly filled. He therefore forbids the wine only if there was time for the Gentile to remove the old seal completely, make a new one and have it dry to become undetectable.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If somebody leaves a Gentile in charge of his store,,” etc. Following Rebbi Meïr? Did not Rebbi Meïr say, “in any case the house is impure?<sup class="footnote-marker">91</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Tahorot 7:4" href="/Mishnah_Tahorot.7.4">Mishnah <i>Tahorot</i>7:4</a>. Since an anonymous Mishnah is supposed to follow R. Meïr’s formulation, an apparent contradiction between two statements attributed to R. Meïr must be resolved.<br>Since the wine in the store is for sale, it is stored in open amphoras. The wine would become forbidden for usufruct if touched by the Gentile. In our Mishnah it seems that R. Meïr assumes that the Gentile will not touch the open amphoras. But in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Tahorot 7:4" href="/Mishnah_Tahorot.7.4">Mishnah <i>Tahorot</i>7:4</a> he holds that if a woman keeping all rules of ritual purity invites one careless in these matters to help her in her home all vessels in the home become impure since one must assume that the helper is impure and touches everything in sight.</i>” Rebbi Ḥama in the name of Rebbi Yose bar Ḥanina: If a courtyard is subdivided by a low wall<sup class="footnote-marker">92</sup><i class="footnote">The explanation is Rashi’s (70b, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 2b" href="/Bava_Batra.2b">Bava batra 2b</a>). The etymology is unknown; the word certainly is different from פְּסֵיפָס ψῆφος “pebble”.</i>, for purities it is impure, in matters of libation wine it is pure<sup class="footnote-marker">93</sup><i class="footnote">A courtyard (in the back of houses or walled in, not directly open to the street) has been divided by a low wall as a sign that each party has only the use of its own part. Even though it is easy to step over the partition, the fact that it is raised is enough to create the presumption that the parties will abide by the rules and not trespass. If the dwellers in one part are scrupulous in observing the rules of purity while the others are not (or are Gentiles), the vessels standing in the courtyard of the observant party remain pure. Similarly, if one party is Jewish and the other Gentile, wine amphoras stored in the the Jewish part remain permitted since the presumption is that the Gentile will not enter the Jewish part.</i>, since Rebbi Meïr is restrictive for purities but lenient for libation wine; also the rabbis are restrictive for purities but lenient for libation wine<sup class="footnote-marker">94</sup><i class="footnote">While a store catering to customers observant in matters of purity certainly cannot have a Gentile employee, a grocery store for the general public can have such an employee. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 70b" href="/Avodah_Zarah.70b">Babli, 70b</a>, this is reported as the opinion of Rav, opposed by R. Joḥanan.</i>.

Halakhah 7

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If he was eating with him<sup class="footnote-marker">95</sup><i class="footnote">A Gentile.</i> at one table, he put a pitcher<sup class="footnote-marker">96</sup><i class="footnote">Latin <i>lagena</i>, Greek λάγηνος, λάγυνος; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Avodah Zarah 5:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Avodah_Zarah.5.2.1">Note 28</a>. The vessels contain wine.</i> on the table and one on a Delphic table<sup class="footnote-marker">97</sup><i class="footnote">Latin <i>delphica</i>(sc. <i>mensa</i>); <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Avodah Zarah 3:2:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Avodah_Zarah.3.2.2">Chapter 3:2 Note 58</a>. A small ornamental table in the form of the Pythia’s tripod.</i>, then let him stay there and left. Anything on the table is forbidden, on the Delphic table is permitted<sup class="footnote-marker">98</sup><i class="footnote">If the Delphic table is out of the Gentile’s reach when the latter is sitting down.</i>. But when he told him, mix with water and drink, also the one on the Delphic table is forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">99</sup><i class="footnote">Since the Gentile guest was given permission to move around.</i>. Open amphoras are forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">100</sup><i class="footnote">Since wine is taken out from the open top with a bucket. As the French wine making authorities of the Middle ages pointed out, these rules do not necessarily apply to barrels from which the wine is drawn from a spigot near the bottom.</i>, sealed after the time needed to open, and close, and dry<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">He thinks that the owner upon his return would recognize a hole freshly filled. He therefore forbids the wine only if there was time for the Gentile to remove the old seal completely, make a new one and have it dry to become undetectable.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “f he was eating with him,” etc. They wanted to say, “anything on the table is forbidden,” within reach of his outstretched arms; “on the Delphic table is permitted,” out of the reach of his outstretched arms<sup class="footnote-marker">101</sup><i class="footnote">In this first version, anything on a separate table is considered out of the reach of the Gentile’s hand even if physically it is within his reach.</i>. But did not Rebbi Abba say in the name of Rav Sheshet<sup class="footnote-marker">102</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Avodah Zarah 4:11:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Avodah_Zarah.4.11.2">Halakhah 4:11, Note 171</a>. Since an impure person is presumed to make anything within his reach impure, a Gentile must be presumed to make all wine within his reach forbidden.</i>, just as they gave outstretched arms for purity so they gave it for libation wine? But it must be the following. He who said, “anything on the table is forbidden,” within reach of his outstretched arms; “on the Delphic table is permitted,” only if<sup class="footnote-marker">103</sup><i class="footnote">Both prohibition and permission are conditional.</i> out of the reach of his outstretched arms.

Halakhah 8

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If an investigative unit<sup class="footnote-marker">104</sup><i class="footnote">Police or military who enter all houses.</i> came to a village in times of peace, open amphoras are forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">105</sup><i class="footnote">One may assume that they take from the wine whether or not it is legal.</i> and sealed ones permitted. In times of war these and those are permitted since there is no time to make libations<sup class="footnote-marker">106</sup><i class="footnote">The soldiers cannot risk getting drunk.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If an investigative unit came to a village,” etc. In times of a religious persecution all is forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">107</sup><i class="footnote">This does not refer to the Mishnah but to a text similar to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Avodah Zarah 6:2" href="/Tosefta_Avodah_Zarah.6.2">Tosephta 5:6</a>: Pedestals erected by Gentiles in times of religious persecution (as base for statues of the Emperor or of Rome, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Avodah Zarah 3:8:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Avodah_Zarah.3.8.1">Halakhah 3:10 Note 231</a>) are never annulled.</i>; it is impossible that there was not a single Jew who worshipped it under duress. This implies that an idol which was worshipped by a Jew is never invalidated<sup class="footnote-marker">108</sup><i class="footnote">This is a statement in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Avodah Zarah 4:1:8" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Avodah_Zarah.4.1.8">Halakhah 4:4, Note 30</a>, for which proof was not given there.</i>. Rebbi Yose said, you infer from this that an idol which was worshipped by a Jew under duress is never invalidated<sup class="footnote-marker">109</sup><i class="footnote">The statement is much stronger than the one stated in Halakhah 4:4 since there one speaks of idols genuinely worshipped by a Jew. But here it follows that the act of worship by a Jew makes it permanently forbidden even if the only intent of the Jew was to escape martyrdom. Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 54a" href="/Avodah_Zarah.54a">Babli 54a</a>.</i>.
Rebbi Joḥanan said, there is no “forbidden” here, only “permitted”. Rebbi Ze`ira said, there is “forbidden” here<sup class="footnote-marker">110</sup><i class="footnote">R. Joḥanan deletes the first part of the Mishnah; R. Ze`irah confirms the text of the Mishnah.</i>. For if he reaches it he will inform him; if he does reach it he will not inform him, lest he owe him a favor<sup class="footnote-marker">111</sup><i class="footnote">If the investigator opens the wine amphora, he will inform the owner. Then the wine will be forbidden under the general rules of wine touched by a pagan; the Mishnah is not necessary in this case. If he does not open the amphora the owner may assume that the investigator did not inform him out of malice; even if there was time for new clay to dry (following Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel) one may assume that the amphora was not touched. It is this later statement which R. Ze`ira objects to.</i>. Rebbi Immi in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: If the investigative unit comes in peaceful or warring manner<sup class="footnote-marker">112</sup><i class="footnote">It is not a question whether the investigators are sent by the villagers’ own government or a hostile power but whether they come with friendly or adversary intent, expecting collaboration or hostile reception.</i>.
A snake when pursuing him fell in a pit. The case came before the rabbis who declared it usable. They said, there was no time to deposit poison<sup class="footnote-marker">113</sup><i class="footnote">This is an unrelated story; its connection with the Mishnah is the expression “there was no time” to cause damage. The problem here is the general prohibition to drink water or wine that was left standing unsupervised. This is a sanitary, not a religious, rule since we are afraid that a poisonous snake could have drunk from the fluid and in the process left its own poison in the fluid; Halakhah 2:3. Here it is noted that if the snake fell into a cistern full of wine while attacking (a human?, an animal?) it is not intent on drinking but will try to get out, so as not to drown, as fast as possible. Therefore there is no health hazard in drinking the wine.</i>.

Halakhah 9

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> Jewish craftsmen whom a Gentile sent an amphora of libation wine<sup class="footnote-marker">114</sup><i class="footnote">Which is forbidden for usufruct to the Jew and therefore worthless to them.</i> as their wages are permitted to tell him, give us its worth<sup class="footnote-marker">115</sup><i class="footnote">They can refuse to accept the amphora and ask for payment in money. But they cannot accept the merchandise and then request that it be exchanged for money; this would be usufruct of forbidden wine, not payment for work done.</i>. After it came to their possession it is forbidden.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “Jewish craftsmen,” etc. Is it not forbidden because of libation wine? Bar Qappara stated, if he sends it to houses<sup class="footnote-marker">116</sup><i class="footnote">If the amphora were delivered by a Jewish teamster, he would act as the Jew’s agent and the wine would be forbidden. Similarly, if the wine were delivered by a Gentile to the property of the artisan, the delivery would effect the transfer of property rights and the wine would be forbidden. Therefore one must assume that the Jew rents his place of work in a building dedicated to artisans’ workshops; delivery there by a Gentile transporter does not transfer property rights and the artisan may refuse acceptance and require payment in coin.</i>.

Halakhah 10

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If somebody sells his wine to a Non-Jew, in case he fixed the price before that one measured, the proceeds are permitted<sup class="footnote-marker">117</sup><i class="footnote">The entire amphora is sold wholesale for a fixed price. By accepting the deal the Gentile becomes the owner and owes the money to the Jew. If then he takes the measuring rod and dips it into the wine, the wine already is his property and what he does with it is irrelevant for Jewish law.</i>; if that one measured before he fixed the price, the proceeds are forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">118</sup><i class="footnote">If the Gentile buys the wine by volume, the contents of the amphora have to be measured before the deal is concluded. It is essential that the measuring be done by a Jew since if the Gentile dips the rod into the amphora he is moving the wine and this turns it into libation wine forbidden for usufruct.</i>. If he took the funnel and measured into a Non-Jew’s vessel, then took it and measured into the vessel of a Jew, if it is contaminated with wine it is forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">119</sup><i class="footnote">If the wine is sold in retail quantities and a funnel is used to pour from the amphora into a smaller vessel, it is essential that the funnel be thoroughly cleansed after wine was sold to a Gentile. Since the wine becomes forbidden the moment it reaches the Gentile’s vessel, any drops clinging to the bottom of the funnel which was immersed in the Gentile’s wine also are forbidden. Since libation wine is forbidden in the most minute amounts, a single drop remaining on the funnel turns everything passing through the funnel into libation wine.</i>. If somebody pours from a vessel to another, what is poured from is permitted<sup class="footnote-marker">120</sup><i class="footnote">While we hold that fluids conduct impurity, so that continuous pouring from a pure into an impure vessel will transfer the impurity to the fluid remaining in the upper vessel, we do not hold so for the prohibition of libation wine. Therefore if wine is poured from an amphora into a smaller vessel, the status of the smaller vessel has no influence on the status of the wine remaining in the amphora.</i>, what is poured into is forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">121</sup><i class="footnote">The Yerushalmi does not discuss whether wine poured from an amphora into a Gentile’s vessel becomes forbidden when it reaches the vessel or when it leaves the amphora. The difference is relevant to the previous case that a funnel was used in the process. In the first case, the funnel has to be cleansed only in that part which was immersed in the lower vessel; in the second case it has to be cleansed and dried thoroughly from top to bottom.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If somebody sold his wine to a Non-Jew,” etc. <sup class="footnote-marker">122</sup><i class="footnote">The text is copied from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 1:4:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.1.4.3"><i>Qiddušin</i> 1:4, Notes 420–429</a>. The first paragraph belongs to the theory of transfer of property; it is irrelevant here. The origin of the second clearly is in <i>Qiddušin</i> since the Mishnah here is quoted as “there”, even though the text here is slightly better.</i> Rebbi Abba, Rav Huna in the name of Rav: One who handles a wine-skin and it splits in his hand is not responsible for it. Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun said, it is necessary even if he takes it in the store when he has no other intention than that no other person should take it. Can he take possession at a minimal rate<sup class="footnote-marker">123</sup><i class="footnote">If there is firm intention to buy one could argue that the buyer already agreed to pay no less than the lowest price recorded for a similar product within the last few days. Then it would be irrelevant whether the wine was measured before or after the price was fixed. Since prices are influenced by news, the question could have been dismissed out of hand. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 72a" href="/Avodah_Zarah.72a">Babli 72a</a>.</i>? Rebbi Ḥaggai said before Rebbi Yose, a Mishnah says that it cannot become his possession at a minimal rate, as we have stated there: “If somebody sells his wine to a Non-Jew, in case he fixed the price before that one measured, the proceeds are permitted; if that one measured before he fixed the price, the proceeds are forbidden.” If you say that it can come into his possession at a minimal price, even if he measured before he fixed the price should be as if he fixed the price before he measured and the proceeds would be permitted.
What means “contaminated with wine”<sup class="footnote-marker">124</sup><i class="footnote">The expression is uncommon and in the Mishnah in the Babli and the independent Mishnah mss. is replaced by עֲכֶּבֶת “a remainder”.</i>? If it is dirty, as one says, <i>immersed in blood</i><sup class="footnote-marker">125</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Hosea 6:8" href="/Hosea.6.8"><i>Hos</i>. 6:8</a>.</i>.
The following is obvious: If the Jew holds the funnel and the Gentile pours, everybody agrees that is it forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">126</sup><i class="footnote">If the Gentile tilts the Amphora, he moves the wine in it and the entire contents of the amphora become libation wine forbidden for all usufruct. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 60a" href="/Avodah_Zarah.60a">Babli 60a</a>.</i>. If the Gentile holds the funnel and the Jew pours, Rebbi Assi<sup class="footnote-marker">127</sup><i class="footnote">This is his Babli name; in the Yerushalmi he otherwise appears as R. Yasa. Possibly one also should read the Babylonian <i>Ammi</i> for Galilean <i>Immi</i>.</i> forbids and Rebbi Immi permits. If the Gentile holds the opening of the wineskin and the Jew pours<sup class="footnote-marker">128</sup><i class="footnote">The Gentile keeps the opening of the wineskin in a fixed position; the Jew lifts the skin to pour.</i>, Rebbi Jeremiah in the name of Rebbi Ze`ira: this is the disagreement. Rebbi Mana asked before Rebbi Yose, which disagreement? He told him, the disasgreement between Rebbi Assi and Rebbi Immi. If the Jew holds the wineskin and the Gentile pours, they wanted to say that according to everybody it is permitted. Rebbi Samuel: In itself it implies that it is forbidden. For sometimes the Jew will loosen his grip and then the entire pouring is done by the Gentile<sup class="footnote-marker">129</sup><i class="footnote">And even the wine remaining in the skin is forbidden.</i>. If somebody lets down<sup class="footnote-marker">130</sup><i class="footnote">It is not too clear what the <i>hapax</i> רגל (as Palestinian Aramaic verb) means. It is conjectured that it means “to turn upside down”. It is taken here in one of the meanings of Arabic رجل “to lower”. If a sealed kosher wineskin is handled by a Gentile it remains permitted.</i> a wineskin from top to bottom this is not pouring from vessel to vessel.

Halakhah 11

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> Libation wine is forbidden and makes forbidden in the most minute amount. Wine mixed with wine<sup class="footnote-marker">131</sup><i class="footnote">Libation wine mixed with kosher wine.</i> or water mixed with water<sup class="footnote-marker">132</sup><i class="footnote">Holy water used for pagan rituals.</i> in the most minute amount. Wine in water or water in wine, if it gives taste<sup class="footnote-marker">133</sup><i class="footnote">In the practice of the Babli, if the forbidden substance is less than one sixtieth of the permitted; cf. <i>Terumot</i> Chapter 10.</i>. This is the principle, kind in the same kind in the most minute amount, kind in another kind if it gives taste.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “Libation wine is forbidden,” etc. Ḥizqiah said, that is at a place where water is not sold by measure. But at a place where water is sold by measure it is like wine in wine<sup class="footnote-marker">134</sup><i class="footnote">If water is so precious that it is sold bottled, mixing wine and water is mixing two equally important fluids and treated as equals. This rule is not found in the Babli.</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">135</sup><i class="footnote">From here to the end of the Halakhah the text is from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Orlah 2:6:2-5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Orlah.2.6.2-5"><i>Orlah</i> 2:7</a> (<b>ע</b>), Notes 158–174. The text there is superior in the tradition both of text and of names.</i>Ḥizqiah said, if somebody mixed a cup from forbidden and from permitted [wine], if the forbidden fell in [last]<sup class="footnote-marker">136</sup><i class="footnote">Added from <b>ע</b>, needed for comprehension.</i> it makes it forbidden, permitted [last] makes it permitted. Rebbi Samuel [bar Rav Isaac] said, this follows Rebbi Eliezer, as Rebbi Eliezer said, I am going after the last one<sup class="footnote-marker">137</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah `<i>Orlah</i> 2:10.</i>. Rebbi Jeremiah said, this is a stringency about libation wine. Rebbi Yose asked, if it were a stringency about libation wine, even if the permitted fell in last it should be forbidden! Rebbi Assi<sup class="footnote-marker">127</sup><i class="footnote">This is his Babli name; in the Yerushalmi he otherwise appears as R. Yasa.
