Possibly one also should read the Babylonian <i>Ammi</i> for Galilean <i>Immi</i>.</i> in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: if somebody mixed a cup from forbidden and from permitted [wine], one considers the permitted as nonexistent; if the forbidden does impart taste it is forbidden, otherwise permitted. Rebbi Hoshaia said, only if the permitted fell in last. Rebbi Immi in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan, it does not make any difference whether the forbidden fell in first and the permitted last or the permitted first and the forbidden last, even water and wine, even if it was mixed perfectly from permitted, one considers the permitted as nonexistent; if the forbidden does impart taste it is forbidden, otherwise permitted<sup class="footnote-marker">138</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 73b" href="/Avodah_Zarah.73b">Babli 73b</a>.</i>. Rebbi Ze`ira said, this has been said about the preceding; how can it be? Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun, Rebbi Abbahu in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan, if a flask of libation wine fell into an amphora of (water)<sup class="footnote-marker">139</sup><i class="footnote">In <b>ע</b> “wine” (permitted wine). Only this makes sense; otherwise there would be no need to mention two different vessels.</i> and then it fell into a water cistern, one considers the permitted as nonexistent; if the forbidden does impart taste it is forbidden, otherwise permitted<sup class="footnote-marker">140</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 73a" href="/Avodah_Zarah.73a">Babli 73a</a>.</i>.
So far when they fell in one after the other. What can one say if they fell together? Let us hear from the following<sup class="footnote-marker">141</sup><i class="footnote">The text in parentheses (identical with that in `<i>Orlah</i>) was originally written by the scribe; the text in brackets is his correction.</i>: “When is leavened matter of Samaritans permitted after Passover? Of private persons after three weekly bakings, of city bakers after three days, of rural ones after three baking loads.” Rebbi Simeon ben Eleazar says, even when they said of private persons after three weekly bakings, if he was an important personality or he married off his son, if he baked three loads in one week one after the other it is permitted. Even when they said of (rural) [city]<sup class="footnote-marker">142</sup><i class="footnote">It is a practice universally accepted (probably by necessity when Sadducees were a viable Jewish sect), not a decision incorporated in a consistent theory of the law.</i> bakers after three days, if he was under pressure and baked three loads one after the other, it is permitted. It was stated: Rebbi Simeon says, even when they said of rural bakers after three baking loads, it is forbidden at least three days since in the morning he prepares sourdough for the entire day.” Does the second dough not become sour from forbidden and permitted? And Rebbi Jeremiah said in the name of Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish, who is the Tanna of “the leavened matter of the Samaritans”? Rebbi Eliezer! And Rebbi Yose said to Rebbi Ḥanina Eyntanaya: Do you remember that you and Rebbi Jeremiah were instructing in the name of Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish, who is the Tanna of “the leavened matter of the Samaritans”? Rebbi Eliezer! But here we do not instruct so but Rebbi Hila in the name of Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish, they treated leavened matter of the Samaritans following Rebbi Eliezer. In addition from the following:: Rebbi Ḥananiah bar Rebbi Abbahu said, my father had a case; he sent to Rebbi Ḥiyya, and Rebbi Immi, and Rebbi Yasa, and they instructed him following Rebbi Eliezer. How? Would they instruct following an isolated opinion? No, since they treated leavened matter of the Samaritans following Rebbi Eliezer<sup class="footnote-marker">142</sup><i class="footnote">It is a practice universally accepted (probably by necessity when Sadducees were a viable Jewish sect), not a decision incorporated in a consistent theory of the law.</i>.
Rebbi Mani said before Rebbi Yose: as you are saying there<sup class="footnote-marker">143</sup><i class="footnote">In matters of leavened matter; does this apply to all cases of food prohibited in the most minute amount? The answer is negative.</i>, “practice follows Rebbi Eliezer”, also here practice follows Rebbi Eliezer. He said to him, for everything<sup class="footnote-marker">144</sup><i class="footnote">The scribe also wrote דבה in `<i>Orlah</i> but there corrected to דבר. The form דִּבָּה, Accadic <i>dibbu</i>, should be acknowledged, but it is not clear whether this is learned Hebrew speech or popular Aramaic.</i>?

Halakhah 12

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> The following are forbidden and they forbid in the most minute amount:<sup class="footnote-marker">145</sup><i class="footnote">The rule that anything which cannot be tasted in a mixture is irrelevant does not apply to these items. An admixture of one of these even if less than one in a thousand will make everything forbidden.</i> Libation wine, idols, heart-removed skins<sup class="footnote-marker">146</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah 2:3.</i>, and an ox which was stoned<sup class="footnote-marker">147</sup><i class="footnote">Which killed a human, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 21:28" href="/Exodus.21.28"><i>Ex</i>. 21:28</a>.</i>, and the calf whose neck was broken<sup class="footnote-marker">148</sup><i class="footnote">To atone for an unsolved murder case, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 21:1-9" href="/Deuteronomy.21.1-9"><i>Deut</i>. 21:1–9</a>.</i>, and the birds of the sufferer from skin disease<sup class="footnote-marker">149</sup><i class="footnote">For purification of the healed person, Lev. 14:4-6.</i>, and the <i>nazir</i>’s hair<sup class="footnote-marker">150</sup><i class="footnote">Which has to be burned, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 6:18" href="/Numbers.6.18"><i>Num</i>. 6:18</a>.</i>, and the firstborn donkey<sup class="footnote-marker">151</sup><i class="footnote">Which is not redeemed by a lamb given to a Cohen, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 13:13" href="/Exodus.13.13"><i>Ex</i>. 13:13</a>.</i>, and meat in milk<sup class="footnote-marker">152</sup><i class="footnote">Meat cooked in milk, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 23:19" href="/Exodus.23.19"><i>Ex</i>. 23:19</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 34:26" href="/Exodus.34.26">34:26</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 14:21" href="/Deuteronomy.14.21"><i>Deut</i>. 14:21</a>.</i>, and profane animals slaughtered in the Temple precinct<sup class="footnote-marker">153</sup><i class="footnote">It is pointed out in the Halakhah that the biblical text forbids only the slaughter of dedicated animals outside the holy precinct, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 17:4" href="/Leviticus.17.4"><i>Lev</i>. 17:4</a>.</i>. These are forbidden and they forbid in the most minute amount.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “The following are forbidden and they forbid in the most minute amount,” etc. Libation wine, idols, heart-removed skins, because <i>nothing of the taboo should cling to your hand</i><sup class="footnote-marker">154</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 13:18" href="/Deuteronomy.13.18"><i>Deut</i>. 13:18</a>. <i>Sifry Deut</i>. 96.</i>.
An ox which was stoned. From the meaning of what was said, <i>the ox shall be stoned</i><sup class="footnote-marker">147</sup><i class="footnote">Which killed a human, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 21:28" href="/Exodus.21.28"><i>Ex</i>. 21:28</a>.</i> would I not know that it cannot be eaten? Why does the verse say, <i>it shall not be eaten</i>? From here that it is forbidden for usufruct<sup class="footnote-marker">155</sup><i class="footnote">This is a very short reference to a text in `<i>Orlah</i> 3:1 (Notes 26–28, <i>Pesaḥim</i> 2:1 28c), which is part of a lengthy discussion under which conditions a prohibition as food implies a prohibition of usufruct (`<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Orlah 3:1:3-9" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Orlah.3.1.3-9"><i>Orlah</i> 3:1, Notes 10–44</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 2:1:2-16" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.2.1.2-16"><i>Pesaḥim</i> 2:1</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 21b" href="/Pesachim.21b">Babli <i>Pesaḥim</i> 21b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Kiddushin 56b" href="/Kiddushin.56b"><i>Qiddušin</i> 56b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 41a" href="/Bava_Kamma.41a"><i>Bava qamma</i> 41a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Chullin 114b" href="/Chullin.114b"><i>Ḥulin</i> 114b</a>) Since the mention of the prohibition as food is unnecessary, the ox qualifies as a case where the prohibition as food means prohibition of usufruct. In the Babylonian sources (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 41a" href="/Bava_Kamma.41a"><i>Bava qamma</i> 41a</a>, <i>Mekhilta</i> <i>dR. Ismael Mišpatim</i> 10) it is agreed that it is obvious that a stoned animal was not ritually slaughtered and therefore is forbidden as meat but it is noted that the prohibition as food is necessary anyhow to show that if the ox was ritually slaughtered after conviction by the court, the slaughter is ineffective and does not permit the carcass to be eaten. Everybody agrees that if the ox was correctly slaughtered before a trial could be held the meat is kosher. The Babli has to find the prohibition of usufruct in another part of the verse which makes the prohibition one of rabbinic interpretation rather than biblical.<br>Louis Ginzberg in his <i>Yerushalmi Fragments from the Genizah</i> (New York 1909) p. 280 has published a leaf of selections from the entire tractate. The passage referring to the paragraph under discussion reads there (vocalization added):<br>וְשׁוֹר הַנִּסְקָל. סָקֹ֨ל יִסָּקֵ֜ל הַשּׁ֗וֹר וג׳ מִמַּשְׁמַע שנ׳ סָקוֹל יִסָּקֵל וְכִי אֵין אָנוּ יוֹדְעִין לֹא יֵאָכֵל. מַה תַלְמוּד לוֹמַר לֹא יֵֽאָכֵל֙. אֶלָּא שֶׁהוּא אָסוּר בַּאֲכִילָה וְאַסוּר בַּהֲנָייָה. ר׳ אַבָּ׳ בְשֶׁם ר׳ יוֹחָנָן כָּל־מָקוֹם שנ׳ לֹא יֵאָכֵל לֹא תֹאכְלוּ … אִסוּר<br>And an ox which was stoned. <i>The ox should certainly be stoned</i>. From the meaning of what was said, <i>shall certainly be stoned</i> would we not know that it cannot be eaten? Why does the verse say, <i>it shall not be eaten</i>? But that it is forbidden for eating and forbidden for usufruct. Rebbi Abba[hu] in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: Any place where it says <i>shall not be eaten, you shall not eat</i> [it is forbidden for eating and forbidden for usufruct.]<br>The addition to the text is from `<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Orlah 3:1:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Orlah.3.1.3"><i>Orlah</i> 3:1 (Note 10</a>) or <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 2:1:2-16" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.2.1.2-16"><i>Pesaḥim</i> 2:1</a> l. 15. In these and all Babli sources (quoted above) the statement is attributed to R. Eleazar, not R. Joḥanan (R. Eleazar’s teacher). The addition has to be considered a commentary. </i>.
And the calf whose neck was broken. <i>There, there</i>. Since <i>there</i> mentioned further describes a prohibition of usufruct, also <i>there</i> said here implies prohibition of usufruct<sup class="footnote-marker">156</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 29b" href="/Avodah_Zarah.29b">Babli 29b</a>, <i>Sifry Deut</i>. 207. This is a case of <i>equal cut</i>. It is written in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 21:4" href="/Deuteronomy.21.4"><i>Deut</i>. 21:4</a>,<i>and they shall break the calf’s neck</i> there <i>in the wadi</i>. Also it is written in <i>Num</i>.20:1, <i>there Miriam died and was buried</i> there. Since a grave and all its appurtenances are forbidden for usufruct, and since the mention of <i>there</i> in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 21:4" href="/Deuteronomy.21.4"><i>Deut</i>. 21:4</a> is not logically necessary, the prohibition of usufruct (and, as noted in the Babylonian sources, the obligation of burial) is transferred to the calf.</i>.
And the birds of the sufferer from skin disease. <i>Any pure bird you may eat</i><sup class="footnote-marker">157</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 14:11" href="/Deuteronomy.14.11"><i>Deut</i>. 14:11</a>.</i>, this is the living bird. <i>But the following you may not eat of them</i><sup class="footnote-marker">158</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 14:12" href="/Deuteronomy.14.12"><i>Deut</i>. 14:12</a>.</i>, that is the slaughtered one<sup class="footnote-marker">159</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sifry Deut</i>. 103, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Kiddushin 57a" href="/Kiddushin.57a">Babli <i>Qiddušin</i> 57a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Chullin 82a" href="/Chullin.82a"><i>Ḥulin</i> 82a</a>. Since first it is said, <i>any pure bird you may eat</i>, which is immediately followed by <i>but the following you may not eat of them</i>, this implies that not only may one not eat the impure birds listed later but also some pure birds. Of the two pure birds used for the purification ceremony of the healed sufferer from skin disease, one is slaughtered (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 14:5" href="/Leviticus.14.5"><i>Lev</i>. 14:5</a>). The other is released (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 14:7" href="/Leviticus.14.7">v. 7</a>); since it is nor recognizable it cannot be forbidden to anybody who might catch it. Therefore the slaughtered bird must be the one pure bird which was correctly slaughtered and nevertheless is forbidden as food. The parallel sources also presuppose that the remains of the slaughtered bird, after its blood has benn used, is forbidden for usufruct but no explicit reason for that is given. One must assume that this is one of the cases where a prohibition as food implies prohibition of usufruct (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Avodah Zarah 5:12:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Avodah_Zarah.5.12.3">Note 155</a>).</i>. Or the other way around? Rebbi Joḥanan in the name of Rebbi Ismael: The Torah has no prohibition of usufruct which applies to any living thing<sup class="footnote-marker">160</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Kiddushin 57a" href="/Kiddushin.57a">Babli <i>Qiddušin</i> 57a</a>, opposed there by R. Simeon ben Laqish.</i>.
And the <i>nazir</i>’s hair. <i>He shall put it in the fire which is under the well-being offering</i><sup class="footnote-marker">150,</sup><i class="footnote">Which has to be burned, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 6:18" href="/Numbers.6.18"><i>Num</i>. 6:18</a>.</i><sup class="footnote-marker">161</sup><i class="footnote">Using the hair as fuel is a kind of usufruct; since it is a positive commandment to burn the hair in this way, any <i>other</i> use is forbidden (but not sanctionable).</i>.
And the firstborn donkey. Breaking the neck, breaking the neck. Since there one buries it and it is forbidden for usufruct, also here one buries it and it is forbidden for usufruct<sup class="footnote-marker">162</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Bekhorot 9b" href="/Bekhorot.9b">Babli <i>Bekhorot</i> 9b</a>, <i>Mekhilta dR. Ismael Bo</i> 18, <i>dR. Simeon bar Ioḥai</i> 13:13 (l. 25). This is <i>heqqeš</i> rather than “equal cut”. Since the only other case where the neck of a four-legged animal has to be broken is the atonement for an unsolved murder case, the rules of the latter have to be applied to the case where a rancher refuses to redeem the first born of his donkey. Since the rule has moral justification in the parallel sources, “he refused to give value to the Cohen; therefore value has to be denied him”, the rule has to be considered rabbinic rather than biblical.</i>.
And meat in milk. It was stated: At three places it is written <i>do not cook a kid goat</i><sup class="footnote-marker">152</sup><i class="footnote">Meat cooked in milk, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 23:19" href="/Exodus.23.19"><i>Ex</i>. 23:19</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 34:26" href="/Exodus.34.26">34:26</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 14:21" href="/Deuteronomy.14.21"><i>Deut</i>. 14:21</a>.</i>, about eating, about usufruct, and about cooking<sup class="footnote-marker">163</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Chullin 115b" href="/Chullin.115b">Babli <i>Ḥulin</i> 115b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Kiddushin 57b" href="/Kiddushin.57b"><i>Qiddušin</i> 57b</a>; <i>Mekhilta dR. Ismael Mišpaṭim</i> 20 (opposed by <i>Mekhilta dR. Simeon ben Ioḥai</i> 23:19, <i>Sifry Re`eh</i> 104).</i>.
And profane animals slaughtered in the Temple precinct. Rebbi Joḥanan in the name of Rebbi Ismael: The Torah said, slaughter what is Mine in My place<sup class="footnote-marker">153</sup><i class="footnote">It is pointed out in the Halakhah that the biblical text forbids only the slaughter of dedicated animals outside the holy precinct, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 17:4" href="/Leviticus.17.4"><i>Lev</i>. 17:4</a>.</i> and what is yours in yours. Since Mine in yours is forbidden, also yours in Mine<sup class="footnote-marker">164</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Kiddushin 57b" href="/Kiddushin.57b">Babli <i>Qiddušin</i> 57b</a> where the argument if found unconvincing and other proofs are given.</i>. Is it punished by extirpation? Does not Rebbi Ismael say, one infers by an argument <i>de minore ad majus</i> but one does not punish by an argument <i>de minore ad majus</i><sup class="footnote-marker">165</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yevamot 11:1:11" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yevamot.11.1.11"><i>Yebamot</i> 11:1 Note 50</a>. There is no argument <i>de minore ad majus</i> involved here; more appropriate is the language of the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sanhedrin 54a" href="/Sanhedrin.54a">Babli (<i>Sanhedrin</i> 54a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Makkot 5b" href="/Makkot.5b"><i>Makkot</i> 5b</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Makkot 14a" href="/Makkot.14a">14a</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Makkot 17a" href="/Makkot.17a">17a</a>): One does not punish by a logical argument (only by an explicit verse). One cannot object that extirpation anyhow is a matter for the Heavenly Court not bound by human arguments since offenses punishable by extirpation automatically are punishable by flogging by the human court. The exclusion of extirpation implies removal of the offense from human criminal justice.</i>?
Why did we not state carcasses with them<sup class="footnote-marker">166</sup><i class="footnote">Carcass meat as meat forbids only if it can be tasted, i. e., if it is more than 1/60 in a mixture. Therefore the question must be about entire carcasses which usually are sold by the piece; nothing usually sold by the piece can become insignificant (`<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Orlah 3:5:2-5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Orlah.3.5.2-5"><i>Orlah</i> 3:6</a>, <i>Terumot</i> 10:6; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Beitzah 3b" href="/Beitzah.3b">Babli <i>Beṣah</i> 3b</a>).</i>? Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun said, it did state only things forbidden for usufruct. Carcass meat is permitted for usufruct<sup class="footnote-marker">167</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 14:21" href="/Deuteronomy.14.21"><i>Deut</i>. 14:21</a>.</i>. They objected, is there not leavened matter on Passover<sup class="footnote-marker">168</sup><i class="footnote">Here again one only considers loaves of bread which exclusively are sold by the piece and therefore cannot become insignificant.</i>? Leavened matter makes liable to extirpation<sup class="footnote-marker">169</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 12:15" href="/Exodus.12.15"><i>Ex</i>. 12:15</a> (but only for eating, not for possession.</i>, but for these<sup class="footnote-marker">170</sup><i class="footnote">The prohibited items enumerated in the Mishnah.</i> there is no extirpation.
Rebbi Yose asked in the name of Rebbi Ḥanina: May prohibitions of usufruct become insignificant in (combination) [by being shredded]<sup class="footnote-marker">171</sup><i class="footnote">The text in parentheses is the original text written by the scribe, printed in the <i>editio princeps</i> and the editions based on the latter. The text in brackets is a correction by the scribe himself.<br>In both cases the question is whether prohibitions of usufruct automatically imply the impossibility of disregarding minute amounts or whether the prohibition will be void if either the prohibited item is mixed with sufficiently many permitted items and is no longer recognizable or whether it reverts to the usual test of giving taste if shredded and mixed with others.</i>? But did we not state “the ox which was stoned”? Explain it in pieces<sup class="footnote-marker">172</sup><i class="footnote">The question is legitimate for meat from such an ox. This question then reads טירוף.</i>. But did we not state “the birds of the sufferer from skin disease”? Explain it as a bird among birds<sup class="footnote-marker">173</sup><i class="footnote">A slaughtered small bird among many of the same kind. Here one has to read צירוף.</i>. But did we not state “the <i>nazir</i>’s hair”? Can you say a bird among birds<sup class="footnote-marker">174</sup><i class="footnote">This is the one genuine question since hair is not sold by the unit; therefore the rabbinic prohibition of unufruct for admixtures of forbidden things sold by the piece (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Avodah Zarah 5:12:10" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Avodah_Zarah.5.12.10">Note 166</a>) does not apply. Here also the prohibition of usufruct is secondary; the question can arise only if the positive commandment of burning the hair in the fire cooking the well-being offering has not been followed.</i>? When they came to study `<i>Orlah</i> did Rebbi Jacob object before Rebbi Yose, did we not state<sup class="footnote-marker">175</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah `<i>Orlah</i> 3:3. Since a minimum length of a <i>si̇̄t</i> (`<i>Orlah</i> 3:3 <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Avodah Zarah 5:5:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Avodah_Zarah.5.5.1">Note 82</a>) is required, it is obvious that the prohibition of the <i>nazir</i>’s hair does not apply to minute amounts.</i>, “if somebody weaves the length of a <i>sīt</i> of firstling’s wool, the cloth must be burned. Of <i>nazir</i>’s hair or firstling donkey in sackcloth, the sackcloth must be burned.” He told him, (if the Tanna had said before us, you would have given)<sup class="footnote-marker">176</sup><i class="footnote">The text as written here can be translated word for word but does not make any sense. It is a corruption of a text in `<i>Orlah</i> 3:3 (Note 94): אִילּוּ אִיְתָּבָת תַּמָּן אִית הֲווָת יְאוּת׃ If you had objected there you would have been correct, i. e., if when I asked the question in <i>Avodah zarah</i> about prohibitions of usufruct becoming insignificant you pointed out the Mishnah in `<i>Orlah</i> it would have been relevant.</i> correctly.

Halakhah 13

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If libation wine fell into a cistern, the entire cistern is forbidden for usufruct. Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel said, all of it shall be sold to a Gentile except for the value of libation wine contained in it<sup class="footnote-marker">177</sup><i class="footnote">If the wine is sold by volume, the Gentile shall not be required to pay for the volume of libation wine contained in the cistern. Libation wine cannot become insignificant; there is no volume of the cistern which would make the wine kosher to drink. According to Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel the prohibition of usufruct does not extend to the mixture.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If libation wine fell into a cistern,” etc. Rebbi Yose, Rebbi Joḥanan in the name of ben Bathyra: If libation wine fell into a cistern, all of it shall be sold to a Gentile except for the value of libation wine<sup class="footnote-marker">178</sup><i class="footnote">The lenient ruling of Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel is a traditional Babylonian one.</i>. Rebbi Samuel bar Nathan in the name of Rebbi Ḥama: practice follows Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel<sup class="footnote-marker">179</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 74a" href="/Avodah_Zarah.74a">Babli, 74a</a>, this is a ruling of the prestigious Rav Naḥman. Here it is quoted in the name of obscure authorities because it is not unquestionable.</i>. Rebbi Yasa said, one of the rabbis came out from the house of assembly and said, Rebbi Joḥanan and Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish disagree. One said, practice follows Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel, and one said, practice does not follow Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel, but Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel agrees that as wine for cooking it is forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">180</sup><i class="footnote">As explained in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Avodah Zarah 5:13:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Avodah_Zarah.5.13.1">Note 177</a>, even for Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel libation wine remains forbidden even in the most minute amount; it is only that the mixture does not entirely become forbidden for all usufruct.</i>. Rebbi Bun in the name of Rav: The Sages agree with Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel if it was an amphora among amphoras that all of it shall be sold to a Gentile except for the value of libation wine<sup class="footnote-marker">181</sup><i class="footnote">This is Rav’s interpretation of Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel’s ruling in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 74a" href="/Avodah_Zarah.74a">Babli 74a</a>. If an amphora in a storage facility with at least two other amphoras was contaminated with libation wine and it can no longer be identified, all amphoras can be sold to Gentiles since for each single one the probability that it was the forbidden one is less than 50% which makes the sale acceptable by biblical standards.</i>. Rebbi Ze`ira said before Rebbi Immi: Before this was said did we not know that it was so<sup class="footnote-marker">182</sup><i class="footnote">That even a person rejecting the opinion of Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel could agrees with the ruling.</i>? Was it said that the Sages agree with Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel, or perhaps was only said that Rabban Gamliel agrees with the Sages? Rebbi Yudan said, Rebbi Ze`ira knew that it was so, but he is like a person who hears something and objects to it<sup class="footnote-marker">183</sup><i class="footnote">He does not question that practice follows Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel in all cases, also of a single cistern or a single amphora, following Samuel in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 74a" href="/Avodah_Zarah.74a">Babli 74a</a>. He only makes it clear that this is a practical ruling without theoretical underpinning.</i>.

Halakhah 14

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> A wine press of stone which a Gentile had pitched one has to wipe dry<sup class="footnote-marker">184</sup><i class="footnote">If the wine press is hewn into the local limestone, the walls of the vat under the press into which the pressed juice flows have to be made impermeable. If this is done by sealing with pitch, something has to be done that the pitch does not spoil the wine. The usual way of neutralizing the pitch was to wash it down with wine. The Yerushalmi does not specify how the wiping dry has to be done, probably because it was generally known, but the Halakhah implies that first everything has to be washed down with water for an extended period. The Babli specifies that in washing down an abrasive powder has to be used.</i> and it is pure. But one of wood, Rebbi said he shall wipe it dry but the Sages say he must remove the pitch<sup class="footnote-marker">185</sup><i class="footnote">Since wood is more absorbent than stone, a new insulation has to be applied.</i>. And one of pottery, even if he removed the pitch, remains forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">186</sup><i class="footnote">Pottery absorbs the wine; the walls of the vat become forbidden and cannot be repaired (except by standing empty for 12 months, by which time all organic material will have decomposed.)</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “A wine press of stone which a Gentile had pitched,” etc. Our Mishnah is Rebbi’s. “One of wood, Rebbi said he shall wipe it dry but the Sages say he must remove the pitch.” For pottery it is not following Rebbi, as it was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">187</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 74b" href="/Avodah_Zarah.74b">Babli 74b</a>. A comparison with the text of the Babli shows that the text here is elliptic. It must read: The Mishnah follows Rebbi for stone and wood vessels, but does not follow Rebbi for pottery.</i>: A Gentile’s wine-press, bucket, and funnel, Rebbi permits but the Sa (say)<sup class="footnote-marker">188</sup><i class="footnote">It is obvious that for אוֹמְרִים “say” one has to read אוֹסְרִים “forbid”. What Rebbi permits and the Sages forbid is cleansing the earthenware vessels by wiping them dry.</i>. Rebbi agrees that pitchers are forbidden. Why are these forbidden and those permitted? These one fills with it, the others one does not fill with it<sup class="footnote-marker">189</sup><i class="footnote">The text of the Babli is more explicit: In pitchers wine is stored, in other vessels it is filled only temporarily.</i>. Rebbi Yose in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: practice follows Rebbi<sup class="footnote-marker">190</sup><i class="footnote">The Babli does not discuss which opinion to follow but, since at other places it states the rule that practice follows Rebbi only if his opinion is opposed by a single dissenter, not against a majority opinion, the codifiers of Talmudic law follow the Sages in this matter.</i>. If a Gentile pitched a papyrus vessel<sup class="footnote-marker">191</sup><i class="footnote">Not paper cups but wicker work made with papyrus rope or stems; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kilayim 6:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kilayim.6.3.1"><i>Kilaim</i> 6:3 Note 38</a>. The discussion is interrupted by the next sentence; it continues with “is the disagreement …”</i>. Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun in the name of Samuel, practice follows Rebbi<sup class="footnote-marker">192</sup><i class="footnote">There is Babylonian authority for following Rebbi; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Avodah Zarah 5:14:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Avodah_Zarah.5.14.2">Note 190</a>.</i>. It is the disagreement of Rebbi and the Sages, as we have stated<sup class="footnote-marker">193</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Avodah Zarah 9:3" href="/Tosefta_Avodah_Zarah.9.3">Tosephta 8:3</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 75a" href="/Avodah_Zarah.75a">Babli 75a</a>.</i>: “If somebody’s wine-press was impure<sup class="footnote-marker">194</sup><i class="footnote">Nothing connected to the ground can be impure except a leprous house. One speaks here of the movable parts and the implements belonging to a wine-press which in a standard sale contract would be sold with the building.</i> and he wants to purify it, the brooms, the planks, and the lentil<sup class="footnote-marker">195</sup><i class="footnote">Any ovaloid implement is called “lentil”; this may be a vessel (a hot water bottle, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Shabbat (Lieberman) 3:7" href="/Tosefta_Shabbat_(Lieberman).3.7">Tosephta <i>Šabbat</i> 3:7</a>) or a roller.</i> one rubs dry and they are pure. The wicker-work of wood splinters, or of hemp, or of wood, or of bark, of threads, of bast, one lets age a full twelve months; Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel said, from one olive pressing to the next, from one wine pressing to the next<sup class="footnote-marker">196</sup><i class="footnote">If one has to wait a full 12 months one loses the use of the press for one full harvest.</i>. Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel said<sup class="footnote-marker">197</sup><i class="footnote">In the Babylonian sources, Babli and Tosephta: Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel in the name of R. Yose.</i>, if he wants to purify it immediately, he puts it for a period in a pipe where water streams, or in a brook whose water flows. Just as one wipes dry for purity<sup class="footnote-marker">198</sup><i class="footnote">Even though one uses the expression “to make the wine in purity” as equivalent to “making kosher wine” (Halakhah 4:12), here one speaks of purity in the exact biblical meaning.</i> so one wipes dry for libation wine.” How much is a period? Rebbi Yose in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan, half a day or half a night. Rebbi Ḥiyya stated, either a day or a night. Do they disagree? But one when day and night are equal, the other when day and night are unequal<sup class="footnote-marker">199</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 75a" href="/Avodah_Zarah.75a">Babli 75a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 113b" href="/Pesachim.113b"><i>Pesaḥim</i> 113b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Yevamot 62b" href="/Yevamot.62b"><i>Yebamot</i> 62b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shevuot 18b" href="/Shevuot.18b"><i>Ševuot</i> 18b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Niddah 63b" href="/Niddah.63b"><i>Niddah</i> 63b</a>. Either day or night at the equinox or half a day during the summer solstice, half a night during the winter solstice.</i>.

Halakhah 15

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If somebody buys crockery<sup class="footnote-marker">200</sup><i class="footnote">Any vessels and utensils used for the preparation or serving of food.</i> from a Gentile, what usually is immersed one immerses<sup class="footnote-marker">201</sup><i class="footnote">The basic source is <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 31:21-23" href="/Numbers.31.21-23"><i>Num</i>. 31:21–23</a>. If the item has not been used for non-kosher food and is of metal, it is immersed in water.</i>, cleansed in boiling water one cleanses in boiling water, making glowing white in fire one makes glowing white<sup class="footnote-marker">202</sup><i class="footnote">The rules for cleansing vessels from non-kosher food are identical with those for cleansing kosher vessels from leavened matter for Passover and discussed in Tractate <i>Pesaḥim</i>. As a general principle, what was used only cold simply has to be cleansed, what was used with hot fluids must be cleansed with boiling water, and what was used in the fire must be exposed to fire.</i>. The spit and the grill<sup class="footnote-marker">203</sup><i class="footnote">Greek ἐσχάρα, with change of the liquid.</i> one makes glowing white in fire but a knife one rubs clean<sup class="footnote-marker">204</sup><i class="footnote">As explained in the Halakhah, one pushes it three times into hard soil. This should eliminate all fat particles clinging to the knife.</i> and it is pure.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If somebody buys crockery from a Gentile,” etc. “<sup class="footnote-marker">205</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 75b" href="/Avodah_Zarah.75b">Babli 75b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Avodah Zarah 9:2" href="/Tosefta_Avodah_Zarah.9.2">Tosephta 8:2</a>. In these texts, the formulation is much more clear. If a vessel is new, one simply immerses it. If it was used cold like cups, one washes them clean. If it was used hot or for cooking, it has to be cleansed by boiling water.</i> If somebody buys crockery from a Gentile, something of which he knows that it is used for human food, he washes them clean and they are pure. But pots and boiling kettles<sup class="footnote-marker">206</sup><i class="footnote">Latin <i>cucuma.</i></i> one cleanses in boiling water. All of these, if he used them even if he did not immerse them, or cleansed them in boiling water, or rubbed them clean, or made them glowing white in fire, they are pure.” Rebbi Hoshaia said, one has to immerse them<sup class="footnote-marker">207</sup><i class="footnote">One has to immerse any vessel bought from a Gentile to convert it to a Jewish vessel. In the Babli (Note 205) this is one version of the preceding <i>baraita</i>; another version denies the obligation.</i>, as by the following. Rebbi Immi and Rebbi Jehudah the Prince went down to the hot springs of Gadara<sup class="footnote-marker">208</sup><i class="footnote">The place were calendar computations and proclamations were held and all leading scholars of the time came together. The names of persons involved here are probably corrupt. R. Immi cannot ask for instruction from R. Jeremiah, the student of his colleagues R. Yasa’s student R. Ze`ira.</i> and borrowed silverware from the family Osinos<sup class="footnote-marker">209</sup><i class="footnote">From the context it appears that these were Gentiles.</i>. They asked Rebbi Jeremiah who said, one must immerse them because they left the impurity of Gentiles and entered the holiness of Israel<sup class="footnote-marker">210</sup><i class="footnote">This opinion is not found in any other source.</i>. He went out and we want to learn<sup class="footnote-marker">211</sup><i class="footnote">While the meaning of the sentence is clear, the text is corrupt and cannot be vocalized to make sense as it stands. The people who asked R. Jeremiah did not accept his ruling, they decided to look around for other opinions. Possibly then the sentence was נֵצֵא לַחוּץ וְנִלְמַד “let us go out and study.”</i>. They left and heard Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa, Simeon bar Abba in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: they said only “who buys”, but the borrower is permitted<sup class="footnote-marker">212</sup><i class="footnote">In the Babli, <i>loc. cit</i>., a statement of R. Naḥman (bar Jacob).</i>. Then even vessels<sup class="footnote-marker">213</sup><i class="footnote">New earthenware vessels, not covered by reference to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 31:21-24" href="/Numbers.31.21-24"><i>Num</i>. 31:21–24</a>.</i>. Rebbi Hoshaia bought vessels and immersed them<sup class="footnote-marker">214</sup><i class="footnote">As a matter of practice, not theoretical obligation.</i>.
Here you say, one washes them clean and they are pure. But there you say, one cleanses them in boiling water. One is more stringent with the abominations of Gentiles<sup class="footnote-marker">215</sup><i class="footnote">It is difficult to make sense of this passage. Most probably the explanation of <i>Pene Moshe</i> is correct that the Mishnah here requires that spit and grill be cleansed in fire whereas in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Zevachim 11:7" href="/Mishnah_Zevachim.11.7">Mishnah <i>Zevaḥim</i> 11:7</a> it is only required that spit and grill used in the Temple be cleaned by boiling water. The answer is that while the cleansing of the utensils in the Temple is a biblical requirement (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 6:21" href="/Leviticus.6.21"><i>Lev</i>. 6:21</a>) the rabbinic requirement of cleansing from forbidden cooking follows rules that are more strict.</i>.
A knife one sticks into the ground three times and it is enough<sup class="footnote-marker">216</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 76b" href="/Avodah_Zarah.76b">Babli, 76b</a>, ten times in hard ground, and only for a knife without depressions.</i>. Rebbi Abba in the name of Rav Jehudah: That is for a small knife. But a large knife needs to become white hot, and in its being white hot it is necessary that sparks fly from it<sup class="footnote-marker">217</sup><i class="footnote">The Babli, <i>loc. cit</i>., makes a difference between a knife used for cold food which can be cleansed in the ground and one used hot which needs cleansing in boiling water.</i>.
